<document>
<heading>Pride and Prejudice </heading>
<heading>By Jane Austen </heading>
<header>Ebd </header>
<header>E-BooksDirectory.com </header>
<heading>Chapter 1. </heading>
<p>It is a truth universally acknowledged, that a single man in </p>
<p>possession of a good fortune, must be in want of a wife. </p>
<p>However little known the feelings or views of such a man </p>
<p>may be on his first entering a neighbourhood, this truth is so </p>
<p>well fixed in the minds of the surrounding families, that he is </p>
<p>considered the rightful property of some one or other of their </p>
<p>daughters. </p>
<p>"My dear Mr. Bennet," said his lady to him one day, "have </p>
<p>you heard that Netherfield Park is let at last?" </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet replied that he had not. </p>
<p>"But it is," returned she; "for Mrs. Long has just been here, </p>
<p>and she told me all about it." </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet made no answer. </p>
<p>"Do you not want to know who has taken it?" cried his </p>
<p>wife impatiently. </p>
<p>"You want to tell me, and I have no objection to hearing </p>
<p>it." </p>
<p>This was invitation enough. </p>
<p>"Why, my dear, you must know, Mrs. Long says that </p>
<p>Netherfield is taken by a young man of large fortune from the </p>
<p>north of England; that he came down on Monday in a chaise </p>
<p>and four to see the place, and was so much delighted with it, </p>
<p>that he agreed with Mr. Morris immediately; that he is to take </p>
<p>possession before Michaelmas, and some of his servants are to </p>
<p>be in the house by the end of next week." </p>
<p>"What is his name?" </p>
<p>"Bingley." </p>
<p>"Is he married or single?" </p>
<p>"Oh! Single, my dear, to be sure! A single man of large </p>
<p>fortune; four or five thousand a year. What a fine thing for our </p>
<p>girls!" </p>
<p>"How so? How can it affect them?" </p>
<p>"My dear Mr. Bennet," replied his wife, "how can you be </p>
<p>so tiresome! You must know that I am thinking of his </p>
<p>marrying one of them." </p>
<p>"Is that his design in settling here?" </p>
<p>"Design! Nonsense, how can you talk so! But it is very </p>
<p>likely that he MAY fall in love with one of them, and </p>
<p>therefore you must visit him as soon as he comes." </p>
<p>"I see no occasion for that. You and the girls may go, or </p>
<p>you may send them by themselves, which perhaps will be still </p>
<p>better, for as you are as handsome as any of them, Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley may like you the best of the party." </p>
<p>"My dear, you flatter me. I certainly HAVE had my share </p>
<p>of beauty, but I do not pretend to be anything extraordinary </p>
<p>now. When a woman has five grown-up daughters, she ought </p>
<p>to give over thinking of her own beauty." </p>
<p>"In such cases, a woman has not often much beauty to </p>
<p>think of." </p>
<p>"But, my dear, you must indeed go and see Mr. Bingley </p>
<p>when he comes into the neighbourhood." </p>
<p>"It is more than I engage for, I assure you." </p>
<p>"But consider your daughters. Only think what an </p>
<p>establishment it would be for one of them. Sir William and </p>
<p>Lady Lucas are determined to go, merely on that account, for </p>
<p>in general, you know, they visit no newcomers. Indeed you </p>
<p>must go, for it will be impossible for US to visit him if you do </p>
<p>not." </p>
<p>"You are over-scrupulous, surely. I dare say Mr. Bingley </p>
<p>will be very glad to see you; and I will send a few lines by </p>
<p>you to assure him of my hearty consent to his marrying </p>
<p>whichever he chooses of the girls; though I must throw in a </p>
<p>good word for my little Lizzy." </p>
<p>"I desire you will do no such thing. Lizzy is not a bit better </p>
<p>than the others; and I am sure she is not half so handsome as </p>
<p>Jane, nor half so good-humoured as Lydia. But you are always </p>
<p>giving her the preference." </p>
<p>"They have none of them much to recommend them," </p>
<p>replied he; "they are all silly and ignorant like other girls; but </p>
<p>Lizzy has something more of quickness than her sisters." </p>
<p>"Mr. Bennet, how can you abuse your own children in </p>
<p>such a way? You take delight in vexing me. You have no </p>
<p>compassion for my poor nerves." </p>
<p>"You mistake me, my dear. I have a high respect for your </p>
<p>nerves. They are my old friends. I have heard you mention </p>
<p>them with consideration these last twenty years at least." </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet was so odd a mixture of quick parts, sarcastic </p>
<p>humour, reserve, and caprice, that the experience of three-and </p>
<p>twenty </p>
<p>years had been insufficient to make his wife </p>
<p>understand his character. Her mind was less difficult to </p>
<p>develop. She was a woman of mean understanding, little </p>
<p>information, and uncertain temper. When she was </p>
<p>discontented, she fancied herself nervous. The business of her </p>
<p>life was to get her daughters married; its solace was visiting </p>
<p>and news. </p>
<heading>Chapter 2. </heading>
<p>Mr. Bennet was among the earliest of those who waited on </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley. He had always intended to visit him, though to </p>
<p>the last always assuring his wife that he should not go; and till </p>
<p>the evening after the visit was paid she had no knowledge of </p>
<p>it. It was then disclosed in the following manner. Observing </p>
<p>his second daughter employed in trimming a hat, he suddenly </p>
<p>addressed her with: </p>
<p>"I hope Mr. Bingley will like it, Lizzy." </p>
<p>"We are not in a way to know what Mr. Bingley likes," </p>
<p>said her mother resentfully, "since we are not to visit." </p>
<p>"But you forget, mamma," said Elizabeth, "that we shall </p>
<p>meet him at the assemblies, and that Mrs. Long promised to </p>
<p>introduce him." </p>
<p>"I do not believe Mrs. Long will do any such thing. She </p>
<p>has two nieces of her own. She is a selfish, hypocritical </p>
<p>woman, and I have no opinion of her." </p>
<p>"No more have I," said Mr. Bennet; "and I am glad to find </p>
<p>that you do not depend on her serving you." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet deigned not to make any reply, but, unable to </p>
<p>contain herself, began scolding one of her daughters. </p>
<p>"Don't keep coughing so, Kitty, for Heaven's sake! Have a </p>
<p>little compassion on my nerves. You tear them to pieces." </p>
<p>"Kitty has no discretion in her coughs," said her father; </p>
<p>"she times them ill." </p>
<p>"I do not cough for my own amusement," replied Kitty </p>
<p>fretfully. "When is your next ball to be, Lizzy?" </p>
<p>"To-morrow fortnight." </p>
<p>"Aye, so it is," cried her mother, "and Mrs. Long does not </p>
<p>come back till the day before; so it will be impossible for her </p>
<p>to introduce him, for she will not know him herself." </p>
<p>"Then, my dear, you may have the advantage of your </p>
<p>friend, and introduce Mr. Bingley to her." </p>
<p>"Impossible, Mr. Bennet, impossible, when I am not </p>
<p>acquainted with him myself; how can you be so teasing?" </p>
<p>"I honour your circumspection. A fortnight's acquaintance </p>
<p>is certainly very little. One cannot know what a man really is </p>
<p>by the end of a fortnight. But if we do not venture somebody </p>
<p>else will; and after all, Mrs. Long and her daughters must </p>
<p>stand their chance; and, therefore, as she will think it an act of </p>
<p>kindness, if you decline the office, I will take it on myself." </p>
<p>The girls stared at their father. Mrs. Bennet said only, </p>
<p>"Nonsense, nonsense!" </p>
<p>"What can be the meaning of that emphatic exclamation?" </p>
<p>cried he. "Do you consider the forms of introduction, and the </p>
<p>stress that is laid on them, as nonsense? I cannot quite agree </p>
<p>with you there. What say you, Mary? For you are a young </p>
<p>lady of deep reflection, I know, and read great books and </p>
<p>make extracts." </p>
<p>Mary wished to say something sensible, but knew not how. </p>
<p>"While Mary is adjusting her ideas," he continued, "let us </p>
<p>return to Mr. Bingley." </p>
<p>"I am sick of Mr. Bingley," cried his wife. </p>
<p>"I am sorry to hear that; but why did not you tell me that </p>
<p>before? If I had known as much this morning I certainly </p>
<p>would not have called on him. It is very unlucky; but as I have </p>
<p>actually paid the visit, we cannot escape the acquaintance </p>
<p>now." </p>
<p>The astonishment of the ladies was just what he wished; </p>
<p>that of Mrs. Bennet perhaps surpassing the rest; though, when </p>
<p>the first tumult of joy was over, she began to declare that it </p>
<p>was what she had expected all the while. </p>
<p>"How good it was in you, my dear Mr. Bennet! But I knew </p>
<p>I should persuade you at last. I was sure you loved your girls </p>
<p>too well to neglect such an acquaintance. Well, how pleased I </p>
<p>am! and it is such a good joke, too, that you should have gone </p>
<p>this morning and never said a word about it till now." </p>
<p>"Now, Kitty, you may cough as much as you choose," said </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet; and, as he spoke, he left the room, fatigued with </p>
<p>the raptures of his wife. </p>
<p>"What an excellent father you have, girls!" said she, when </p>
<p>the door was shut. "I do not know how you will ever make </p>
<p>him amends for his kindness; or me, either, for that matter. At </p>
<p>our time of life it is not so pleasant, I can tell you, to be </p>
<p>making new acquaintances every day; but for your sakes, we </p>
<p>would do anything. Lydia, my love, though you are the </p>
<p>youngest, I dare say Mr. Bingley will dance with you at the </p>
<p>next ball." </p>
<p>"Oh!" said Lydia stoutly, "I am not afraid; for though I am </p>
<p>the youngest, I'm the tallest." </p>
<p>The rest of the evening was spent in conjecturing how </p>
<p>soon he would return Mr. Bennet's visit, and determining </p>
<p>when they should ask him to dinner. </p>
<heading>Chapter 3. </heading>
<p>Not all that Mrs. Bennet, however, with the assistance of </p>
<p>her five daughters, could ask on the subject, was sufficient to </p>
<p>draw from her husband any satisfactory description of Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley. They attacked him in various ways--with barefaced </p>
<p>questions, ingenious suppositions, and distant surmises; but he </p>
<p>eluded the skill of them all, and they were at last obliged to </p>
<p>accept the second-hand intelligence of their neighbour, Lady </p>
<p>Lucas. Her report was highly favourable. Sir William had </p>
<p>been delighted with him. He was quite young, wonderfully </p>
<p>handsome, extremely agreeable, and, to crown the whole, he </p>
<p>meant to be at the next assembly with a large party. Nothing </p>
<p>could be more delightful! To be fond of dancing was a certain </p>
<p>step towards falling in love; and very lively hopes of Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley's heart were entertained. </p>
<p>"If I can but see one of my daughters happily settled at </p>
<p>Netherfield," said Mrs. Bennet to her husband, "and all the </p>
<p>others equally well married, I shall have nothing to wish for." </p>
<p>In a few days Mr. Bingley returned Mr. Bennet's visit, and </p>
<p>sat about ten minutes with him in his library. He had </p>
<p>entertained hopes of being admitted to a sight of the young </p>
<p>ladies, of whose beauty he had heard much; but he saw only </p>
<p>the father. The ladies were somewhat more fortunate, for they </p>
<p>had the advantage of ascertaining from an upper window that </p>
<p>he wore a blue coat, and rode a black horse. </p>
<p>An invitation to dinner was soon afterwards dispatched; </p>
<p>and already had Mrs. Bennet planned the courses that were to </p>
<p>do credit to her housekeeping, when an answer arrived which </p>
<p>deferred it all. Mr. Bingley was obliged to be in town the </p>
<p>following day, and, consequently, unable to accept the honour </p>
<p>of their invitation, etc. Mrs. Bennet was quite disconcerted. </p>
<p>She could not imagine what business he could have in town so </p>
<p>soon after his arrival in Hertfordshire; and she began to fear </p>
<p>that he might be always flying about from one place to </p>
<p>another, and never settled at Netherfield as he ought to be. </p>
<p>Lady Lucas quieted her fears a little by starting the idea of his </p>
<p>being gone to London only to get a large party for the ball; </p>
<p>and a report soon followed that Mr. Bingley was to bring </p>
<p>twelve ladies and seven gentlemen with him to the assembly. </p>
<p>The girls grieved over such a number of ladies, but were </p>
<p>comforted the day before the ball by hearing, that instead of </p>
<p>twelve he brought only six with him from London--his five </p>
<p>sisters and a cousin. And when the party entered the assembly </p>
<p>room it consisted of only five altogether--Mr. Bingley, his two </p>
<p>sisters, the husband of the eldest, and another young man. </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley was good-looking and gentlemanlike; he had </p>
<p>a pleasant countenance, and easy, unaffected manners. His </p>
<p>sisters were fine women, with an air of decided fashion. His </p>
<p>brother-in-law, Mr. Hurst, merely looked the gentleman; but </p>
<p>his friend Mr. Darcy soon drew the attention of the room by </p>
<p>his fine, tall person, handsome features, noble mien, and the </p>
<p>report which was in general circulation within five minutes </p>
<p>after his entrance, of his having ten thousand a year. The </p>
<p>gentlemen pronounced him to be a fine figure of a man, the </p>
<p>ladies declared he was much handsomer than Mr. Bingley, </p>
<p>and he was looked at with great admiration for about half the </p>
<p>evening, till his manners gave a disgust which turned the tide </p>
<p>of his popularity; for he was discovered to be proud; to be </p>
<p>above his company, and above being pleased; and not all his </p>
<p>large estate in Derbyshire could then save him from having a </p>
<p>most forbidding, disagreeable countenance, and being </p>
<p>unworthy to be compared with his friend. </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley had soon made himself acquainted with all the </p>
<p>principal people in the room; he was lively and unreserved, </p>
<p>danced every dance, was angry that the ball closed so early, </p>
<p>and talked of giving one himself at Netherfield. Such amiable </p>
<p>qualities must speak for themselves. What a contrast between </p>
<p>him and his friend! Mr. Darcy danced only once with Mrs. </p>
<p>Hurst and once with Miss Bingley, declined being introduced </p>
<p>to any other lady, and spent the rest of the evening in walking </p>
<p>about the room, speaking occasionally to one of his own </p>
<p>party. His character was decided. He was the proudest, most </p>
<p>disagreeable man in the world, and everybody hoped that he </p>
<p>would never come there again. Amongst the most violent </p>
<p>against him was Mrs. Bennet, whose dislike of his general </p>
<p>behaviour was sharpened into particular resentment by his </p>
<p>having slighted one of her daughters. </p>
<p>Elizabeth Bennet had been obliged, by the scarcity of </p>
<p>gentle men, to sit down for two dances; and during part of that </p>
<p>time, Mr. Darcy had been standing near enough for her to hear </p>
<p>a conversation between him and Mr. Bingley, who came from </p>
<p>the dance for a few minutes, to press his friend to join it. </p>
<p>"Come, Darcy," said he, "I must have you dance. I hate to </p>
<p>see you standing about by yourself in this stupid manner. You </p>
<p>had much better dance." </p>
<p>"I certainly shall not. You know how I detest it, unless I </p>
<p>am particularly acquainted with my partner. At such an </p>
<p>assembly as this it would be insupportable. Your sisters are </p>
<p>engaged, and there is not another woman in the room whom it </p>
<p>would not be a punishment to me to stand up with." </p>
<p>"I would not be so fastidious as you are," cried Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, "for a kingdom! Upon my honour, I never met with </p>
<p>so many pleasant girls in my life as I have this evening; and </p>
<p>there are several of them you see uncommonly pretty." </p>
<p>"You are dancing with the only handsome girl in the </p>
<p>room," said Mr. Darcy, looking at the eldest Miss Bennet. </p>
<p>"Oh! She is the most beautiful creature I ever beheld! But </p>
<p>there is one of her sisters sitting down just behind you, who is </p>
<p>very pretty, and I dare say very agreeable. Do let me ask my </p>
<p>partner to introduce you." </p>
<p>"Which do you mean?" and turning round he looked for a </p>
<p>moment at Elizabeth, till catching her eye, he withdrew his </p>
<p>own and coldly said: "She is tolerable, but not handsome </p>
<p>enough to tempt me; I am in no humour at present to give </p>
<p>consequence to young ladies who are slighted by other men. </p>
<p>You had better return to your partner and enjoy her smiles, for </p>
<p>you are wasting your time with me." </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley followed his advice. Mr. Darcy walked off; </p>
<p>and Elizabeth remained with no very cordial feelings toward </p>
<p>him. She told the story, however, with great spirit among her </p>
<p>friends; for she had a lively, playful disposition, which </p>
<p>delighted in anything ridiculous. </p>
<p>The evening altogether passed off pleasantly to the whole </p>
<p>family. Mrs. Bennet had seen her eldest daughter much </p>
<p>admired by the Netherfield party. Mr. Bingley had danced </p>
<p>with her twice, and she had been distinguished by his sisters. </p>
<p>Jane was as much gratified by this as her mother could be, </p>
<p>though in a quieter way. Elizabeth felt Jane's pleasure. Mary </p>
<p>had heard herself mentioned to Miss Bingley as the most </p>
<p>accomplished girl in the neighbourhood; and Catherine and </p>
<p>Lydia had been fortunate enough never to be without partners, </p>
<p>which was all that they had yet learnt to care for at a ball. </p>
<p>They returned, therefore, in good spirits to Longbourn, the </p>
<p>village where they lived, and of which they were the principal </p>
<p>inhabitants. They found Mr. Bennet still up. With a book he </p>
<p>was regardless of time; and on the present occasion he had a </p>
<p>good deal of curiosity as to the events of an evening which </p>
<p>had raised such splendid expectations. He had rather hoped </p>
<p>that his wife's views on the stranger would be disappointed; </p>
<p>but he soon found out that he had a different story to hear. </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear Mr. Bennet," as she entered the room, "we </p>
<p>have had a most delightful evening, a most excellent ball. I </p>
<p>wish you had been there. Jane was so admired, nothing could </p>
<p>be like it. Everybody said how well she looked; and Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley thought her quite beautiful, and danced with her </p>
<p>twice! Only think of that, my dear; he actually danced with </p>
<p>her twice! and she was the only creature in the room that he </p>
<p>asked a second time. First of all, he asked Miss Lucas. I was </p>
<p>so vexed to see him stand up with her! But, however, he did </p>
<p>not admire her at all; indeed, nobody can, you know; and he </p>
<p>seemed quite struck with Jane as she was going down the </p>
<p>dance. So he inquired who she was, and got introduced, and </p>
<p>asked her for the two next. Then the two third he danced with </p>
<p>Miss King, and the two fourth with Maria Lucas, and the two </p>
<p>fifth with Jane again, and the two sixth with Lizzy, and the </p>
<heading>boulanger--" </heading>
<p>"If he had had any compassion for me," cried her husband </p>
<p>impatiently, "he would not have danced half so much! For </p>
<p>God's sake, say no more of his partners. O that he had </p>
<p>sprained his ankle in the first place!" </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear, I am quite delighted with him. He is so </p>
<p>excessively handsome! And his sisters are charming women. I </p>
<p>never in my life saw anything more elegant than their dresses. </p>
<p>I dare say the lace upon Mrs. Hurst's gown--" </p>
<p>Here she was interrupted again. Mr. Bennet protested </p>
<p>against any description of finery. She was therefore obliged to </p>
<p>seek another branch of the subject, and related, with much </p>
<p>bitterness of spirit and some exaggeration, the shocking </p>
<p>rudeness of Mr. Darcy. </p>
<p>"But I can assure you," she added, "that Lizzy does not </p>
<p>lose much by not </p>
<p>suiting his fancy; for he is a most </p>
<p>disagreeable, horrid man, not at all worth pleasing. So high </p>
<p>and so conceited that there was no enduring him! He walked </p>
<p>here, and he walked there, fancying himself so very great! Not </p>
<p>handsome enough to dance with! I wish you had been there, </p>
<p>my dear, to have given him one of your set-downs. I quite </p>
<p>detest the man." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 4. </heading>
<p>When Jane and Elizabeth were alone, the former, who had </p>
<p>been cautious in her praise of Mr. Bingley before, expressed </p>
<p>to her sister just how very much she admired him. </p>
<p>"He is just what a young man ought to be," said she, </p>
<p>"sensible, good-humoured, lively; and I never saw such happy </p>
<p>man-ners!--so much ease, with such perfect good breeding!" </p>
<p>"He is also handsome," replied Elizabeth, "which a young </p>
<p>man ought likewise to be, if he possibly can. His character is </p>
<p>thereby complete." </p>
<p>"I was very much flattered by his asking me to dance a </p>
<p>second time. I did not expect such a compliment." </p>
<p>"Did not you? I did for you. But that is one great </p>
<p>difference between us. Compliments always take you by </p>
<p>surprise, and me never. What could be more natural than his </p>
<p>asking you again? He could not help seeing that you were </p>
<p>about five times as pretty as every other woman in the room. </p>
<p>No thanks to his gallantry for that. Well, he certainly is very </p>
<p>agreeable, and I give you leave to like him. You have liked </p>
<p>many a stupider person." </p>
<p>"Dear Lizzy!" </p>
<p>"Oh! you are a great deal too apt, you know, to like people </p>
<p>in general. You never see a fault in anybody. All the world are </p>
<p>good and agreeable in your eyes. I never heard you speak ill </p>
<p>of a human being in your life." </p>
<p>"I would not wish to be hasty in censuring anyone; but I </p>
<p>always speak what I think." </p>
<p>"I know you do; and it is that which makes the wonder. </p>
<p>With your good sense, to be so honestly blind to the follies </p>
<p>and nonsense of others! Affectation of candour is common </p>
<p>enough--one meets with it everywhere. But to be candid </p>
<p>without ostentation or design--to take the good of everybody's </p>
<p>character and make it still better, and say nothing of the bad- </p>
<p>belongs to you alone. And so you like this man's sisters, too, </p>
<p>do you? Their manners are not equal to his." </p>
<p>"Certainly not--at first. But they are very pleasing women </p>
<p>when you converse with them. Miss Bingley is to live with </p>
<p>her brother, and keep his house; and I am much mistaken if </p>
<p>we shall not find a very charming neighbour in her." </p>
<p>Elizabeth listened in silence, but was not convinced; their </p>
<p>behaviour at the assembly had not been calculated to please in </p>
<p>general; and with more quickness of observation and less </p>
<p>pliancy of temper than her sister, and with a judgement too </p>
<p>unassailed by any attention to herself, she was very little </p>
<p>disposed to approve them. They were in fact very fine ladies; </p>
<p>not deficient in good humour when they were pleased, nor in </p>
<p>the power of making themselves agreeable when they chose it, </p>
<p>but proud and conceited. They were rather handsome, had </p>
<p>been educated in one of the first private seminaries in town, </p>
<p>had a fortune of twenty thousand pounds, were in the habit of </p>
<p>spending more than they ought, and of associating with </p>
<p>people of rank, and were therefore in every respect entitled to </p>
<p>think well of themselves, and meanly of others. They were of </p>
<p>a respectable family in the north of England; a circumstance </p>
<p>more deeply impressed on their memories than that their </p>
<p>brother's fortune and their own had been acquired by trade. </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley inherited property to the amount of nearly a </p>
<p>hundred thousand pounds from his father, who had intended </p>
<p>to purchase an estate, but did not live to do it. Mr. Bingley </p>
<p>intended it likewise, and sometimes made choice of his </p>
<p>county; but as he was now provided with a good house and </p>
<p>the liberty of a manor, it was doubtful to many of those who </p>
<p>best knew the easiness of his temper, whether he might not </p>
<p>spend the remainder of his days at Netherfield, and leave the </p>
<p>next generation to purchase. </p>
<p>His sisters were anxious for his having an estate of his </p>
<p>own; but, though he was now only established as a tenant, </p>
<p>Miss Bingley was by no means unwilling to preside at his </p>
<p>table--nor was Mrs. Hurst, who had married a man of more </p>
<p>fashion than fortune, less disposed to consider his house as </p>
<p>her home when it suited her. Mr. Bingley had not been of age </p>
<p>two years, when he was tempted by an accidental </p>
<p>recommendation to look at Netherfield House. He did look at </p>
<p>it, and into it for half-an hour--was pleased with the situation </p>
<p>and the principal rooms, satisfied with what the owner said in </p>
<p>its praise, and took it immediately. </p>
<p>Between him and Darcy there was a very steady </p>
<p>friendship, in spite of great opposition of character. Bingley </p>
<p>was endeared to Darcy by the easiness, openness, and </p>
<p>ductility of his temper, though no disposition could offer a </p>
<p>greater contrast to his own, and though with his own he never </p>
<p>appeared dissatisfied. On the strength of Darcy's regard, </p>
<p>Bingley had the firmest reliance, and of his judgement the </p>
<p>highest opinion. In understanding, Darcy was the superior. </p>
<p>Bingley was by no means deficient, but Darcy was clever. He </p>
<p>was at the same time haughty, reserved, and fastidious, and </p>
<p>his manners, though well-bred, were not inviting. In that </p>
<p>respect his friend had greatly the advantage. Bingley was sure </p>
<p>of being liked wherever he appeared, Darcy was continually </p>
<p>giving offense. </p>
<p>The manner in which they spoke of the Meryton assembly </p>
<p>was sufficiently characteristic. Bingley had never met with </p>
<p>more pleasant people or prettier girls in his life; everybody </p>
<p>had been most kind and attentive to him; there had been no </p>
<p>formality, no stiffness; he had soon felt acquainted with all the </p>
<p>room; and, as to Miss Bennet, he could not conceive an angel </p>
<p>more beautiful. Darcy, on the contrary, had seen a collection </p>
<p>of people in whom there was little beauty and no fashion, for </p>
<p>none of whom he had felt the smallest interest, and from none </p>
<p>received either attention or pleasure. Miss Bennet he </p>
<p>acknowledged to be pretty, but she smiled too much. </p>
<p>Mrs. Hurst and her sister allowed it to be so--but still they </p>
<p>admired her and liked her, and pronounced her to be a sweet </p>
<p>girl, and one whom they would not object to know more of. </p>
<p>Miss Bennet was therefore established as a sweet girl, and </p>
<p>their brother felt authorized by such commendation to think of </p>
<p>her as he chose. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 5. </heading>
<p>Within a short walk of Longbourn lived a family with </p>
<p>whom the Bennets were particularly intimate. Sir William </p>
<p>Lucas had been formerly in trade in Meryton, where he had </p>
<p>made a tolerable fortune, and risen to the honour </p>
<p>of </p>
<p>knighthood by an address to the king during his mayoralty. </p>
<p>The distinction had perhaps been felt too strongly. It had </p>
<p>given him a disgust to his business, and to his residence in a </p>
<p>small market town; and, in quitting them both, he had </p>
<p>removed with his family to a house about a mile from </p>
<p>Meryton, denominated from that period Lucas Lodge, where </p>
<p>he could think with pleasure of his own importance, and, </p>
<p>unshackled by business, occupy himself solely in being civil </p>
<p>to all the world. For, though elated by his rank, it did not </p>
<p>render him supercilious; on the contrary, he was all attention </p>
<p>to everybody. By nature inoffensive, friendly, and obliging, </p>
<p>his presentation at St. James's had made him courteous. </p>
<p>Lady Lucas was a very good kind of woman, not too </p>
<p>clever to be a valuable neighbour to Mrs. Bennet. They had </p>
<p>several children. The eldest of them, a sensible, intelligent </p>
<p>young woman, about twenty-seven, was Elizabeth's intimate </p>
<p>friend. </p>
<p>That the Miss Lucases and the Miss Bennets should meet </p>
<p>to talk over a ball was absolutely necessary; and the morning </p>
<p>after the assembly brought the former to Longbourn to hear </p>
<p>and to communicate. </p>
<p>"You began the evening well, Charlotte," said Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>with civil self-command to Miss Lucas. "You were Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley's first choice." </p>
<p>"Yes; but he seemed to like his second better." </p>
<p>"Oh! you mean Jane, I suppose, because he danced with </p>
<p>her twice. To be sure that did seem as if he admired her- </p>
<p>indeed I rather believe he did--I heard something about it-but I </p>
<p>hardly know what--something about Mr. Robinson." </p>
<p>"Perhaps you mean what I overheard between him and Mr. </p>
<p>Robinson; did not I mention it to you? Mr. Robinson's asking </p>
<p>him how he liked our Meryton assemblies, and whether he did </p>
<p>not think there were a great many pretty women in the room, </p>
<p>and which he thought the prettiest? and his answering </p>
<p>immediately to the last question: 'Oh! the eldest Miss Bennet, </p>
<p>beyond a doubt; there cannot be two opinions on that point.'" </p>
<p>"Upon my word! Well, that is very decided indeed--that </p>
<p>does seem as if--but, however, it may all come to nothing, you </p>
<p>know." </p>
<p>"My overhearings were more to the purpose than yours, </p>
<p>Eliza," said Charlotte. "Mr. Darcy is not so well worth </p>
<p>listening to as his friend, is he?--poor Eliza!--to be only just </p>
<heading>tolerable." </heading>
<p>"I beg you would not put it into Lizzy's head to be vexed </p>
<p>by his ill-treatment, for he is such a disagreeable man, that it </p>
<p>would be quite a misfortune to be liked by him. Mrs. Long </p>
<p>told me last night that he sat close to her for half-an-hour </p>
<p>without once opening his lips." </p>
<p>"Are you quite sure, ma'am?--is not there a little mistake?" </p>
<p>said Jane. "I certainly saw Mr. Darcy speaking to her." </p>
<p>"Aye--because she asked him at last how he liked </p>
<p>Netherfield, and he could not help answering her; but she said </p>
<p>he seemed quite angry at being spoke to." </p>
<p>"Miss Bingley told me," said Jane, "that he never speaks </p>
<p>much, unless among his intimate acquaintances. With them he </p>
<p>is remarkably agreeable." </p>
<p>"I do not believe a word of it, my dear. If he had been so </p>
<p>very agreeable, he would have talked to Mrs. Long. But I can </p>
<p>guess how it was; everybody says that he is eat up with pride, </p>
<p>and I dare say he had heard somehow that Mrs. Long does not </p>
<p>keep a carriage, and had come to the ball in a hack chaise." </p>
<p>"I do not mind his not talking to Mrs. Long," said Miss </p>
<p>Lucas, "but I wish he had danced with Eliza." </p>
<p>"Another time, Lizzy," said her mother, "I would not dance </p>
<p>with him, if I were you." </p>
<p>"I believe, ma'am, I may safely promise you never to dance </p>
<p>with him." </p>
<p>"His pride," said Miss Lucas, "does not offend me so much </p>
<p>as pride often does, because there is an excuse for it. One </p>
<p>cannot wonder that so very fine a young man, with family, </p>
<p>fortune, everything in his favour, should think highly of </p>
<p>himself. If I may so express it, he has a right to be proud." </p>
<p>"That is very true," replied Elizabeth, "and I could easily </p>
<p>forgive his pride, if he had not mortified mine." </p>
<p>"Pride," observed Mary, who piqued herself upon the </p>
<p>solidity of her reflections, "is a very common failing, I </p>
<p>believe. By all that I have ever read, I am convinced that it is </p>
<p>very common indeed; that human nature is particularly prone </p>
<p>to it, and that there are very few of us who do not cherish a </p>
<p>feeling of self-complacency on the score of some quality or </p>
<p>other, real or imaginary. Vanity and pride are different things, </p>
<p>though the words are often used synonymously. A person may </p>
<p>be proud without being vain. Pride relates more to our opinion </p>
<p>of ourselves, vanity to what we would have others think of </p>
<p>us." </p>
<p>"If I were as rich as Mr. Darcy," cried a young Lucas, who </p>
<p>came with his sisters, "I should not care how proud I was. I </p>
<p>would keep a pack of foxhounds, and drink a bottle of wine a </p>
<p>day." </p>
<p>"Then you would drink a great deal more than you ought," </p>
<p>said Mrs. Bennet; "and if I were to see you at it, I should take </p>
<p>away your bottle directly." </p>
<p>The boy protested that she should not; she continued to </p>
<p>declare that she would, and the argument ended only with the </p>
<p>visit. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 6. </heading>
<p>The ladies of Longbourn soon waited on those of </p>
<p>Netherfield. The visit was soon returned in due form. Miss </p>
<p>Bennet's pleasing manners grew on the goodwill of Mrs. </p>
<p>Hurst and Miss Bingley; and though the mother was found to </p>
<p>be intolerable, and the younger sisters not worth speaking to, a </p>
<p>wish of being better acquainted with THEM was expressed </p>
<p>towards the two eldest. By Jane, this attention was received </p>
<p>with the greatest pleasure, but </p>
<p>Elizabeth still saw </p>
<p>superciliousness in their treatment of everybody, hardly </p>
<p>excepting even her sister, and could not like them; though </p>
<p>their kindness to Jane, such as it was, had a value as arising in </p>
<p>all probability from the influence of their brother's admiration. </p>
<p>It was generally evident whenever they met, that he DID </p>
<p>admire her and to HER it was equally evident that Jane was </p>
<p>yielding to the preference which she had begun to entertain </p>
<p>for him from the first, and was in a way to be very much in </p>
<p>love; but she considered with pleasure that it was not likely to </p>
<p>be discovered by the world in general, since Jane united, with </p>
<p>great strength of feeling, a composure of temper and a </p>
<p>uniform cheerfulness of manner which would guard her from </p>
<p>the suspicions of the impertinent. She mentioned this to her </p>
<p>friend Miss Lucas. </p>
<p>"It may perhaps be pleasant," replied Charlotte, "to be able </p>
<p>to impose on the public in such a case; but it is sometimes a </p>
<p>disadvantage to be so very guarded. If a woman conceals her </p>
<p>affection with the same skill from the object of it, she may </p>
<p>lose the opportunity of fixing him; and it will then be but poor </p>
<p>consolation to believe the world equally in the dark. There is </p>
<p>so much of gratitude or vanity in almost every attachment, </p>
<p>that it is not safe to leave any to itself. We can all BEGIN </p>
<p>freely--a slight preference is natural enough; but there are </p>
<p>very few of us who have heart enough to be really in love </p>
<p>without encouragement. In nine cases out of ten a women had </p>
<p>better show MORE affection than she feels. Bingley likes </p>
<p>your sister undoubtedly; but he may never do more than like </p>
<p>her, if she does not help him on." </p>
<p>"But she does help him on, as much as her nature will </p>
<p>allow. If I can perceive her regard for him, he must be a </p>
<p>simpleton, indeed, not to discover it too." </p>
<p>"Remember, Eliza, that he does not know Jane's </p>
<p>disposition as you do." </p>
<p>"But if a woman is partial to a man, and does not </p>
<p>endeavour to conceal it, he must find it out." </p>
<p>"Perhaps he must, if he sees enough of her. But, though </p>
<p>Bingley and Jane meet tolerably often, it is never for many </p>
<p>hours together; and, as they always see each other in large </p>
<p>mixed parties, it is impossible that every moment should be </p>
<p>employed in conversing together. Jane should therefore make </p>
<p>the most of every half-hour in which she can command his </p>
<p>attention. When she is secure of him, there will be more </p>
<p>leisure for falling in love as much as she chooses." </p>
<p>"Your plan is a good one," replied Elizabeth, "where noth </p>
<p>ing is in question but the desire of being well married, and if I </p>
<p>were determined to get a rich husband, or any husband, I dare </p>
<p>say I should adopt it. But these are not Jane's feelings; she is </p>
<p>not acting by design. As yet, she cannot even be certain of the </p>
<p>degree of her own regard nor of its reasonableness. She has </p>
<p>known him only a fortnight. She danced four dances with him </p>
<p>at Meryton; she saw him one morning at his own house, and </p>
<p>has since dined with him in company four times. This is not </p>
<p>quite enough to make her understand his character." </p>
<p>"Not as you represent it. Had she merely DINED with him, </p>
<p>she might only have discovered whether he had a good </p>
<p>appetite; but you must remember that four evenings have also </p>
<p>been spent together--and four evenings may do a great deal." </p>
<p>"Yes; these four evenings have enabled them to ascertain </p>
<p>that they both like Vingt-un better than Commerce; but with </p>
<p>respect to any other leading characteristic, I do not imagine </p>
<p>that much has been unfolded." </p>
<p>"Well," said Charlotte, "I wish Jane success with all my </p>
<p>heart; and if she were married to him to-morrow, I should </p>
<p>think she had as good a chance of happiness as if she were to </p>
<p>be studying his character for a twelvemonth. Happiness in </p>
<p>marriage is entirely a matter of chance. If the dispositions of </p>
<p>the parties are ever so well known to each other or ever so </p>
<p>similar beforehand, it does not advance their felicity in the </p>
<p>least. They always continue to grow sufficiently unlike </p>
<p>afterwards to have their share of vexation; and it is better to </p>
<p>know as little as possible of the defects of the person with </p>
<p>whom you are to pass your life." </p>
<p>"You make me laugh, Charlotte; but it is not sound. You </p>
<p>know it is not sound, and that you would never act in this way </p>
<p>yourself." </p>
<p>Occupied in observing Mr. Bingley's attentions to her </p>
<p>sister, Elizabeth was far from suspecting that she was herself </p>
<p>becoming an object of some interest in the eyes of his friend. </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy had at first scarcely allowed her to be pretty; he </p>
<p>had looked at her without admiration at the ball; and when </p>
<p>they next met, he looked at her only to criticise. But no sooner </p>
<p>had he made it clear to himself and his friends that she hardly </p>
<p>had a good feature in her face, than he began to find it was </p>
<p>rendered uncommonly intelligent by the beautiful expression </p>
<p>of her dark eyes. To this discovery succeeded some others </p>
<p>equally mortifying. Though he had detected with a critical eye </p>
<p>more than one failure of perfect symmetry in her form, he was </p>
<p>forced to acknowledge her figure to be light and pleasing; and </p>
<p>in spite of his asserting that her manners were not those of the </p>
<p>fashionable world, he was caught by their easy playfulness. </p>
<p>Of this she was perfectly unaware; to her he was only the man </p>
<p>who made himself agreeable nowhere, and who had not </p>
<p>thought her handsome enough to dance with. </p>
<p>He began to wish to know more of her, and as a step </p>
<p>towards conversing with </p>
<p>her himself, attended </p>
<p>to </p>
<p>her </p>
<p>conversation with others. His doing so drew her notice. It was </p>
<p>at Sir William Lucas's, where a large party were assembled. </p>
<p>"What does Mr. Darcy mean," said she to Charlotte, "by </p>
<p>listening to my conversation with Colonel Forster?" </p>
<p>"That is a question which Mr. Darcy only can answer." </p>
<p>"But if he does it any more I shall certainly let him know </p>
<p>that I see what he is about. He has a very satirical eye, and if I </p>
<p>do not begin by being impertinent myself, I shall soon grow </p>
<p>afraid of him." </p>
<p>On his approaching them soon afterwards, though without </p>
<p>seeming to have any intention of speaking, Miss Lucas defied </p>
<p>her friend to mention such a subject to him; which </p>
<p>immediately provoking Elizabeth to do it, she turned to him </p>
<p>and said: </p>
<p>"Did you not think, Mr. Darcy, that I expressed myself </p>
<p>uncommonly well just now, when I was teasing Colonel </p>
<p>Forster to give us a ball at Meryton?" </p>
<p>"With great energy; but it is always a subject which makes </p>
<p>a lady energetic." </p>
<p>"You are severe on us." </p>
<p>"It will be HER turn soon to be teased," said Miss Lucas. </p>
<p>"I am going to open the instrument, Eliza, and you know what </p>
<p>follows." </p>
<p>"You are a very strange creature by way of a friend!-- </p>
<p>always wanting me to play and sing before anybody and </p>
<p>everybody! If my vanity had taken a musical turn, you would </p>
<p>have been invaluable; but as it is, I would really rather not sit </p>
<p>down before those who must be in the habit of hearing the </p>
<p>very best performers." On Miss Lucas's persevering, however, </p>
<p>she added, "Very well, if it must be so, it must." And gravely </p>
<p>glancing at Mr. Darcy, "There is a fine old saying, which </p>
<p>everybody here is of course familiar with: 'Keep your breath </p>
<p>to cool your porridge'; and I shall keep mine to swell my </p>
<p>song." </p>
<p>Her performance was pleasing, though by no means </p>
<p>capital. After a song or two, and before she could reply to the </p>
<p>entreaties of several that she would sing again, she was </p>
<p>eagerly succeeded at the instrument by her sister Mary, who </p>
<p>having, in consequence of being the only plain one in the </p>
<p>family, worked hard for knowledge and accomplishments, </p>
<p>was always impatient for display. </p>
<p>Mary had neither genius nor taste; and though vanity had </p>
<p>given her application, it had given her likewise a pedantic air </p>
<p>and conceited manner, which would have injured a higher </p>
<p>degree of excellence than she had reached. Elizabeth, easy </p>
<p>and unaffected, had been listened to with much more pleasure, </p>
<p>though not playing half so well; and Mary, at the end of a </p>
<p>long concerto, was glad to purchase praise and gratitude by </p>
<p>Scotch and Irish airs, at the request of her younger sisters, </p>
<p>who, with some of the Lucases, and two or three officers, </p>
<p>joined eagerly in dancing at one end of the room. </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy stood near them in silent indignation at such a </p>
<p>mode of passing the evening, to the exclusion of all </p>
<p>conversation, and was too much engrossed by his thoughts to </p>
<p>perceive that Sir William Lucas was his neighbour, till Sir </p>
<p>William thus began: </p>
<p>"What a charming amusement for young people this is, </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy! There is nothing like dancing after all. I consider </p>
<p>it as one of the first refinements of polished society." </p>
<p>"Certainly, sir; and it has the advantage also of being in </p>
<p>vogue amongst the less polished societies of the world. Every </p>
<p>savage can dance." </p>
<p>Sir William only smiled. "Your friend performs </p>
<p>delightfully," he continued after a pause, on seeing Bingley </p>
<p>join the group; "and I doubt not that you are an adept in the </p>
<p>science yourself, Mr. Darcy." </p>
<p>"You saw me dance at Meryton, I believe, sir." </p>
<p>"Yes, indeed, and received no inconsiderable pleasure </p>
<p>from the sight. Do you often dance at St. James's?" </p>
<p>"Never, sir." </p>
<p>"Do you not think it would be a proper compliment to the </p>
<p>place?" </p>
<p>"It is a compliment which I never pay to any place if I can </p>
<p>avoid it." </p>
<p>"You have a house in town, I conclude?" </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy bowed. </p>
<p>"I had once had some thought of fixing in town myself-for </p>
<p>I am fond of superior society; but I did not feel quite certain </p>
<p>that the air of London would agree with Lady Lucas." </p>
<p>He paused in hopes of an answer; but his companion was </p>
<p>not disposed to make any; and Elizabeth at that instant </p>
<p>moving towards them, he was struck with the action of doing </p>
<p>a very gallant thing, and called out to her: </p>
<p>"My dear Miss Eliza, why are you not dancing? Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, you must allow me to present this young lady to you as </p>
<p>a very desirable partner. You cannot refuse to dance, I am </p>
<p>sure when so much beauty is before you." And, taking her </p>
<p>hand, he would have given it to Mr. Darcy who, though </p>
<p>extremely surprised, was not unwilling to receive it, when she </p>
<p>instantly drew back, and said with some discomposure to Sir </p>
<p>William: </p>
<p>"Indeed, sir, I have not the least intention of dancing. I </p>
<p>entreat you not to suppose that I moved this way in order to </p>
<p>beg for a partner." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, with grave propriety, requested to be allowed </p>
<p>the honour of her hand, but in vain. Elizabeth was determined; </p>
<p>nor did Sir William at all shake her purpose by his attempt at </p>
<p>persuasion. </p>
<p>"You excel so much in the dance, Miss Eliza, that it is </p>
<p>cruel to deny me the happiness of seeing you; and though this </p>
<p>gentleman dislikes the amusement in general, he can have no </p>
<p>objection, I am sure, to oblige us for one half-hour." </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy is all politeness," said Elizabeth, smiling. </p>
<p>"He is, indeed; but, considering the inducement, my dear </p>
<p>Miss Eliza, we cannot wonder at his complaisance--for who </p>
<p>would object to such a partner?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth looked archly, and turned away. Her resistance </p>
<p>had not injured her with the gentleman, and he was thinking </p>
<p>of her with some complacency, when thus accosted by Miss </p>
<p>Bingley: </p>
<p>"I can guess the subject of your reverie." </p>
<p>"I should imagine not." </p>
<p>"You are considering how insupportable it would be to </p>
<p>pass many evenings in this manner--in such society; and </p>
<p>indeed I am quite of your opinion. I was never more annoyed! </p>
<p>The insipidity, and yet the noise--the nothingness, and yet the </p>
<p>self-importance of all those people! What would I give to hear </p>
<p>your strictures on them!" </p>
<p>"You conjecture is totally wrong, I assure you. My mind </p>
<p>was more agreeably engaged. I have been meditating on the </p>
<p>very great pleasure which a pair of fine eyes in the face of a </p>
<p>pretty woman can bestow." </p>
<p>Miss Bingley immediately fixed her eyes on his face, and </p>
<p>desired he would tell her what lady had the credit of inspiring </p>
<p>such reflections. Mr. Darcy replied with great intrepidity: </p>
<p>"Miss Elizabeth Bennet." </p>
<p>"Miss Elizabeth Bennet!" repeated Miss Bingley. "I am all </p>
<p>astonishment. How long has she been such a favourite?--and </p>
<p>pray, when am I to wish you joy?" </p>
<p>"That is exactly the question which I expected you to ask. </p>
<p>A lady's imagination is very rapid; it jumps from admiration </p>
<p>to love, from love to matrimony, in a moment. I knew you </p>
<p>would be wishing me joy." </p>
<p>"Nay, if you are serious about it, I shall consider the matter </p>
<p>is absolutely settled. You will be having a charming motherin- </p>
<p>law, indeed; and, of course, she will always be at Pemberley </p>
<p>with you." </p>
<p>He listened to her with perfect indifference while she </p>
<p>chose to entertain herself in this manner; and as his </p>
<p>composure convinced her that all was safe, her wit flowed </p>
<p>long. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 7. </heading>
<p>Mr. Bennet's property consisted almost entirely in an estate </p>
<p>of two thousand a year, which, unfortunately for his </p>
<p>daughters, was entailed, in default of heirs male, on a distant </p>
<p>relation; and their mother's fortune, though ample for her </p>
<p>situation in life, could but ill supply the deficiency of his. Her </p>
<p>father had been an attorney in Meryton, and had left her four </p>
<p>thousand pounds. </p>
<p>She had a sister married to a Mr. Phillips, who had been a </p>
<p>clerk to their father and succeeded him in the business, and a </p>
<p>brother settled in London in a respectable line of trade. </p>
<p>The village of Longbourn was only one mile from </p>
<p>Meryton; a most convenient distance for the young ladies, </p>
<p>who were usually tempted thither three or four times a week, </p>
<p>to pay their duty to their aunt and to a milliner's shop just over </p>
<p>the way. The two youngest of the family, Catherine and </p>
<p>Lydia, were particularly frequent in these attentions; their </p>
<p>minds were more vacant than their sisters', and when nothing </p>
<p>better offered, a walk to Meryton was necessary to amuse </p>
<p>their morning hours and furnish conversation for the evening; </p>
<p>and however bare of news the country in general might be, </p>
<p>they always contrived to learn some from their aunt. At </p>
<p>present, indeed, they were well supplied both with news and </p>
<p>happiness by the recent arrival of a militia regiment in the </p>
<p>neighbourhood; it was to remain the whole winter, and </p>
<p>Meryton was the headquarters. </p>
<p>Their visits to Mrs. Phillips were now productive of the </p>
<p>most interesting intelligence. Every day added something to </p>
<p>their knowledge of the officers' names and connections. Their </p>
<p>lodgings were not long a secret, and at length they began to </p>
<p>know the officers themselves. Mr. Phillips visited them all, </p>
<p>and this opened to his nieces a store of felicity unknown </p>
<p>before. They could talk of nothing but officers; and Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley's large fortune, the mention of which gave animation </p>
<p>to their mother, was worthless in their eyes when opposed to </p>
<p>the regimentals of an ensign. </p>
<p>After listening one morning to their effusions on this </p>
<p>subject, Mr. Bennet coolly observed: </p>
<p>"From all that I can collect by your manner of talking, you </p>
<p>must be two of the silliest girls in the country. I have </p>
<p>suspected it some time, but I am now convinced." </p>
<p>Catherine was disconcerted, and made no answer; but </p>
<p>Lydia, with perfect indifference, continued to express her </p>
<p>admiration of Captain Carter, and her hope of seeing him in </p>
<p>the course of the day, as he was going the next morning to </p>
<p>London. </p>
<p>"I am astonished, my dear," said Mrs. Bennet, "that you </p>
<p>should be so ready to think your own children silly. If I </p>
<p>wished to think slightingly of anybody's children, it should </p>
<p>not be of my own, however." </p>
<p>"If my children are silly, I must hope to be always sensible </p>
<p>of it." </p>
<p>"Yes--but as it happens, they are all of them very clever." </p>
<p>"This is the only point, I flatter myself, on which we do not </p>
<p>agree. I had hoped that our sentiments coincided in every </p>
<p>particular, but I must so far differ from you as to think our two </p>
<p>youngest daughters uncommonly foolish." </p>
<p>"My dear Mr. Bennet, you must not expect such girls to </p>
<p>have the sense of their father and mother. When they get to </p>
<p>our age, I dare say they will not think about officers any more </p>
<p>than we do. I remember the time when I liked a red coat </p>
<p>myself very well--and, indeed, so I do still at my heart; and if </p>
<p>a smart young colonel, with five or six thousand a year, </p>
<p>should want one of my girls I shall not say nay to him; and I </p>
<p>thought Colonel Forster looked very becoming the other night </p>
<p>at Sir William's in his regimentals." </p>
<p>"Mamma," cried Lydia, "my aunt says that Colonel Forster </p>
<p>and Captain Carter do not go so often to Miss Watson's as </p>
<p>they did when they first came; she sees them now very often </p>
<p>standing in Clarke's library." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was prevented replying by the entrance of the </p>
<p>footman with a note for Miss Bennet; it came from </p>
<p>Netherfield, and the servant waited for an answer. Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet's eyes sparkled with pleasure, and she was eagerly </p>
<p>calling out, while her daughter read, </p>
<p>"Well, Jane, who is it from? What is it about? What does </p>
<p>he say? Well, Jane, make haste and tell us; make haste, my </p>
<p>love." </p>
<p>"It is from Miss Bingley," said Jane, and then read it aloud. </p>
<p>"MY DEAR FRIEND,- </p>
<p>"If you are not so compassionate as to dine to-day with </p>
<p>Louisa and me, we shall be in danger of hating each other for </p>
<p>the rest of our lives, for a whole day's tete-a-tete between two </p>
<p>women can never end without a quarrel. Come as soon as you </p>
<p>can on receipt of this. My brother and the gentlemen are to </p>
<p>dine with the officers.--Yours ever, </p>
<p>"CAROLINE BINGLEY" </p>
<p>"With the officers!" cried Lydia. "I wonder my aunt did </p>
<p>not tell us of THAT." </p>
<p>"Dining out," said Mrs. Bennet, "that is very unlucky." </p>
<p>"Can I have the carriage?" said Jane. </p>
<p>"No, my dear, you had better go on horseback, because it </p>
<p>seems likely to rain; and then you must stay all night." </p>
<p>"That would be a good scheme," said Elizabeth, "if you </p>
<p>were sure that they would not offer to send her home." </p>
<p>"Oh! but the gentlemen will have Mr. Bingley's chaise to </p>
<p>go to Meryton, and the Hursts have no horses to theirs." </p>
<p>"I had much rather go in the coach." </p>
<p>"But, my dear, your father cannot spare the horses, I am </p>
<p>sure. They are wanted in the farm, Mr. Bennet, are they not?" </p>
<p>"They are wanted in the farm much oftener than I can get </p>
<p>them." </p>
<p>"But if you have got them to-day," said Elizabeth, "my </p>
<p>mother's purpose will be answered." </p>
<p>She did at last extort from her father an acknowledgment </p>
<p>that the horses were engaged. Jane was therefore obliged to go </p>
<p>on horseback, and her mother attended her to the door with </p>
<p>many cheerful prognostics of a bad day. Her hopes were </p>
<p>answered; Jane had not been gone long before it rained hard. </p>
<p>Her sisters were uneasy for her, but her mother was delighted. </p>
<p>The rain continued the whole evening without intermission; </p>
<p>Jane certainly could not come back. </p>
<p>"This was a lucky idea of mine, indeed!" said Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>more than once, as if the credit of making it rain were all her </p>
<p>own. Till the next morning, however, she was not aware of all </p>
<p>the felicity of her contrivance. Breakfast was scarcely over </p>
<p>when a servant from Netherfield brought the following note </p>
<p>for Elizabeth: </p>
<p>"MY DEAREST LIZZY,- </p>
<p>"I find myself very unwell this morning, which, I suppose, </p>
<p>is to be imputed to my getting wet through yesterday. My </p>
<p>kind friends will not hear of my returning till I am better. </p>
<p>They insist also on my seeing Mr. Jones--therefore do not </p>
<p>be alarmed if you should hear of his having been to me-- </p>
<p>and, excepting a sore throat and headache, there is not </p>
<p>much the matter with me.--Yours, etc." </p>
<p>"Well, my dear," said Mr. Bennet, when Elizabeth had </p>
<p>read the note aloud, "if your daughter should have a </p>
<p>dangerous fit of illness--if she should die, it would be a </p>
<p>comfort to know that it was all in pursuit of Mr. Bingley, and </p>
<p>under your orders." </p>
<p>"Oh! I am not afraid of her dying. People do not die of </p>
<p>little trifling colds. She will be taken good care of. As long as </p>
<p>she stays there, it is all very well. I would go an see her if I </p>
<p>could have the carriage." </p>
<p>Elizabeth, feeling really anxious, was determined to go to </p>
<p>her, though the carriage was not to be had; and as she was no </p>
<p>horsewoman, walking was her only alternative. She declared </p>
<p>her resolution. </p>
<p>"How can you be so silly," cried her mother, "as to think of </p>
<p>such a thing, in all this dirt! You will not be fit to be seen </p>
<p>when you get there." </p>
<p>"I shall be very fit to see Jane--which is all I want." </p>
<p>"Is this a hint to me, Lizzy," said her father, "to send for </p>
<p>the horses?" </p>
<p>"No, indeed, I do not wish to avoid the walk. The distance </p>
<p>is nothing when one has a motive; only three miles. I shall be </p>
<p>back by dinner." </p>
<p>"I admire the activity of your benevolence," observed </p>
<p>Mary, "but every impulse of feeling should be guided by </p>
<p>reason; and, in my opinion, exertion should always be in </p>
<p>proportion to what is required." </p>
<p>"We will go as far as Meryton with you," said Catherine </p>
<p>and Lydia. Elizabeth accepted their company, and the three </p>
<p>young ladies set off together. </p>
<p>"If we make haste," said Lydia, as they walked along, </p>
<p>"perhaps we may see something of Captain Carter before he </p>
<p>goes." </p>
<p>In Meryton they parted; the two youngest repaired to the </p>
<p>lodgings of one of the officers' wives, and Elizabeth continued </p>
<p>her walk alone, crossing field after field at a quick pace, jump </p>
<p>ing over stiles and springing over puddles with impatient </p>
<p>activity, and finding herself at last within view of the house, </p>
<p>with weary ankles, dirty stockings, and a face glowing with </p>
<p>the warmth of exercise. </p>
<p>She was shown into the breakfast-parlour, where all but </p>
<p>Jane were assembled, and where her appearance created a </p>
<p>great deal of surprise. That she should have walked three </p>
<p>miles so early in the day, in such dirty weather, and by </p>
<p>herself, was almost incredible to Mrs. Hurst and Miss </p>
<p>Bingley; and Elizabeth was convinced that they held her in </p>
<p>contempt for it. She was received, however, very politely by </p>
<p>them; and in their brother's manners there was something </p>
<p>better than politeness; there was good humour and kindness. </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy said very little, and Mr. Hurst nothing at all. The </p>
<p>former was divided between admiration of the brilliancy </p>
<p>which exercise had given to her complexion, and doubt as to </p>
<p>the occasion's justifying her coming so far alone. The latter </p>
<p>was thinking only of his breakfast. </p>
<p>Her inquiries after her sister were not very favourably </p>
<p>answered. Miss Bennet had slept ill, and though up, was very </p>
<p>feverish, and not well enough to leave her room. Elizabeth </p>
<p>was glad to be taken to her immediately; and Jane, who had </p>
<p>only been </p>
<p>withheld by the fear of giving alarm </p>
<p>or </p>
<p>inconvenience from expressing in her note how much she </p>
<p>longed for such a visit, was delighted at her entrance. She was </p>
<p>not equal, however, to much conversation, and when Miss </p>
<p>Bingley left them together, could </p>
<p>attempt little besides </p>
<p>expressions of gratitude for the extraordinary kindness she </p>
<p>was treated with. Elizabeth silently attended her. </p>
<p>When breakfast was over they were joined by the sisters; </p>
<p>and Elizabeth began to like them herself, when she saw how </p>
<p>much affection and solicitude they showed for Jane. The </p>
<p>apothecary came, and having examined his patient, said, as </p>
<p>might be supposed, that she had caught a violent cold, and </p>
<p>that they must endeavour to get the better of it; advised her to </p>
<p>return to bed, and promised her some draughts. The advice </p>
<p>was followed readily, for the feverish symptoms increased, </p>
<p>and her head ached acutely. Elizabeth did not quit her room </p>
<p>for a moment; nor were the other ladies often absent; the </p>
<p>gentlemen being out, they had, in fact, nothing to do </p>
<p>elsewhere. </p>
<p>When the clock struck three, Elizabeth felt that she must </p>
<p>go, and very unwillingly said so. Miss Bingley offered her the </p>
<p>carriage, and she only wanted a little pressing to accept it, </p>
<p>when Jane testified such concern in parting with her, that Miss </p>
<p>Bingley was obliged to convert the offer of the chaise to an </p>
<p>invitation to remain at Netherfield for the present. Elizabeth </p>
<p>most thankfully consented, and a servant was dispatched to </p>
<p>Longbourn to acquaint the family with her stay and bring back </p>
<p>a supply of clothes. </p>
<heading>Chapter 8. </heading>
<p>At five o'clock the two ladies retired to dress, and at half- </p>
<p>past six Elizabeth was summoned to dinner. To the civil </p>
<p>inquiries which then poured in, and amongst which she had </p>
<p>the pleasure of distinguishing the much superior solicitude of </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley's, she could not make a very favourable answer. </p>
<p>Jane was by no means better. The sisters, on hearing this, </p>
<p>repeated three or four times how much they were grieved, </p>
<p>how shocking it was to have a bad cold, and how excessively </p>
<p>they disliked being ill themselves; and then thought no more </p>
<p>of the matter: and their indifference towards Jane when not </p>
<p>immediately before them restored Elizabeth to the enjoyment </p>
<p>of all her former dislike. </p>
<p>Their brother, indeed, was the only one of the party whom </p>
<p>she could regard with any complacency. His anxiety for Jane </p>
<p>was evident, and his attentions to herself most pleasing, and </p>
<p>they prevented her feeling herself so much an intruder as she </p>
<p>believed she was considered by the others. She had very little </p>
<p>notice from any but him. Miss Bingley was engrossed by Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, her sister scarcely less so; and as for Mr. Hurst, by </p>
<p>whom Elizabeth sat, he was an indolent man, who lived only </p>
<p>to eat, drink, and play at cards; who, when he found her to </p>
<p>prefer a plain dish to a ragout, had nothing to say to her. </p>
<p>When dinner was over, she returned directly to Jane, and </p>
<p>Miss Bingley began abusing her as soon as she was out of the </p>
<p>room. Her manners were pronounced to be very bad indeed, a </p>
<p>mixture of pride and impertinence; she had no conversation, </p>
<p>no style, no beauty. Mrs. Hurst thought the same, and added: </p>
<p>"She has nothing, in short, to recommend her, but being an </p>
<p>excellent walker. I shall never forget her appearance this </p>
<p>morning. She really looked almost wild." </p>
<p>"She did, indeed, Louisa. I could hardly keep my </p>
<p>countenance. Very nonsensical to come at all! Why must SHE </p>
<p>be scampering about the country, because her sister had a </p>
<p>cold? Her hair, so untidy, so blowsy!" </p>
<p>"Yes, and her petticoat; I hope you saw her petticoat, six </p>
<p>inches deep in mud, I am absolutely certain; and the gown </p>
<p>which had been let down to hide it not doing its office." </p>
<p>"Your picture may be very exact, Louisa," said Bingley; </p>
<p>"but this was all lost upon me. I thought Miss Elizabeth </p>
<p>Bennet looked remarkably well when she came into the room </p>
<p>this morning. Her dirty petticoat quite escaped my notice." </p>
<p>"YOU observed it, Mr. Darcy, I am sure," said Miss </p>
<p>Bingley; "and I am inclined to think that you would not wish </p>
<p>to see YOUR sister make such an exhibition." </p>
<p>"Certainly not." </p>
<p>"To walk three miles, or four miles, or five miles, or </p>
<p>whatever it is, above her ankles in dirt, and alone, quite alone! </p>
<p>What could she mean by it? It seems to me to show an </p>
<p>abominable sort of conceited independence, a most country- </p>
<p>town indifference to decorum." </p>
<p>"It shows an affection for her sister that is very pleasing," </p>
<p>said Bingley. </p>
<p>"I am afraid, Mr. Darcy," observed Miss Bingley in a half </p>
<p>whisper, "that this adventure has rather affected your </p>
<p>admiration of her fine eyes." </p>
<p>"Not at all," he replied; "they were brightened by the </p>
<p>exercise." A short pause followed this speech, and Mrs. Hurst </p>
<p>began again: </p>
<p>"I have a excessive regard for Miss Jane Bennet, she is </p>
<p>really a very sweet girl, and I wish with all my heart she were </p>
<p>well settled. But with such a father and mother, and such low </p>
<p>connections, I am afraid there is no chance of it." </p>
<p>"I think I have heard you say that their uncle is an attorney </p>
<p>on Meryton." </p>
<p>"Yes; and they have another, who lives somewhere near </p>
<p>Cheapside." </p>
<p>"That is capital," added her sister, and they both laughed </p>
<p>heartily. </p>
<p>"If they had uncles enough to fill ALL Cheapside," cried </p>
<p>Bingley, "it would not make them one jot less agreeable." </p>
<p>"But it must very materially lessen their chance of </p>
<p>marrying men of any consideration in the world," replied </p>
<p>Darcy. </p>
<p>To this speech Bingley made no answer; but his sisters </p>
<p>gave it their hearty assent, and indulged their mirth for some </p>
<p>time at the expense of their dear friend's vulgar relations. </p>
<p>With a renewal of tenderness, however, they returned to </p>
<p>her room on leaving the dining-parlour, and sat with her till </p>
<p>summoned to coffee. She was still very poorly, and Elizabeth </p>
<p>would not quit her at all, till late in the evening, when she had </p>
<p>the comfort of seeing her sleep, and when it seemed to her </p>
<p>rather right than pleasant that she should go downstairs </p>
<p>herself. On entering the drawing-room she found the whole </p>
<p>party at loo, and was immediately invited to join them; but </p>
<p>suspecting them to be playing high she declined it, and </p>
<p>making her sister the excuse, said she would amuse herself for </p>
<p>the short time she could stay below, with a book. Mr. Hurst </p>
<p>looked at her with astonishment. </p>
<p>"Do you prefer reading to cards?" said he; "that is rather </p>
<p>singular." </p>
<p>"Miss Eliza Bennet," said Miss Bingley, "despises cards. </p>
<p>She is a great reader, and has no pleasure in anything else." </p>
<p>"I deserve neither such praise nor such censure," cried </p>
<p>Elizabeth; "I am NOT a great reader, and I have pleasure in </p>
<p>many things." </p>
<p>"In nursing your sister I am sure you have pleasure," said </p>
<p>Bingley; "and I hope it will be soon increased by seeing her </p>
<p>quite well." </p>
<p>Elizabeth thanked him from her heart, and then walked </p>
<p>towards the table where a few books were lying. He </p>
<p>immediately offered to fetch her others--all that his library </p>
<p>afforded. </p>
<p>"And I wish my collection were larger for your benefit and </p>
<p>my own credit; but I am an idle fellow, and though I have not </p>
<p>many, I have more than I ever looked into." </p>
<p>Elizabeth assured him that she could suit herself perfectly </p>
<p>with those in the room. </p>
<p>"I am astonished," said Miss Bingley, "that my father </p>
<p>should have left so small a collection of books. What a </p>
<p>delightful library you have at Pemberley, Mr. Darcy!" </p>
<p>"It ought to be good," he replied, "it has been the work of </p>
<p>many generations." </p>
<p>"And then you have added so much to it yourself, you are </p>
<p>always buying books." </p>
<p>"I cannot comprehend the neglect of a family library in </p>
<p>such days as these." </p>
<p>"Neglect! I am sure you neglect nothing that can add to the </p>
<p>beauties of that noble place. Charles, when you build YOUR </p>
<p>house, I wish it may be half as delightful as Pemberley." </p>
<p>"I wish it may." </p>
<p>"But I would really advise you to make your purchase in </p>
<p>that neighbourhood, and take Pemberley for a kind of model. </p>
<p>There is not a finer county in England than Derbyshire." </p>
<p>"With all my heart; I will buy Pemberley itself if Darcy </p>
<p>will sell it." </p>
<p>"I am talking of possibilities, Charles." </p>
<p>"Upon my word, Caroline, I should think it more possible </p>
<p>to get Pemberley by purchase than by imitation." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was so much caught with what passed, as to </p>
<p>leave her very little attention for her book; and soon laying it </p>
<p>wholly aside, she drew near the card-table, and stationed </p>
<p>herself between Mr. Bingley and his eldest sister, to observe </p>
<p>the game. </p>
<p>"Is Miss Darcy much grown since the spring?" said Miss </p>
<p>Bingley; "will she be as tall as I am?" </p>
<p>"I think she will. She is now about Miss Elizabeth Bennet's </p>
<p>height, or rather taller." </p>
<p>"How I long to see her again! I never met with anybody </p>
<p>who delighted me </p>
<p>so much. Such a countenance, such </p>
<p>manners! And so extremely accomplished for her age! Her </p>
<p>performance on the pianoforte is exquisite." </p>
<p>"It is amazing to me," said Bingley, "how young ladies can </p>
<p>have patience to be so very accomplished as they all are." </p>
<p>"All young ladies accomplished! My dear Charles, what do </p>
<p>you mean?" </p>
<p>"Yes, all of them, I think. They all paint tables, cover </p>
<p>screens, and net purses. I scarcely know anyone who cannot </p>
<p>do all this, and I am sure I never heard a young lady spoken of </p>
<p>for the first time, without being informed that she was very </p>
<p>accomplished." </p>
<p>"Your list of the common extent of accomplishments," said </p>
<p>Darcy, "has too much truth. The word is applied to many a </p>
<p>woman who deserves it no otherwise than by netting a purse </p>
<p>or covering a screen. But I am very far from agreeing with </p>
<p>you in your estimation of ladies in general. I cannot boast of </p>
<p>knowing more than half-a-dozen, in the whole range of my </p>
<p>acquaintance, that are really accomplished." </p>
<p>"Nor I, I am sure," said Miss Bingley. </p>
<p>"Then," observed Elizabeth, "you must comprehend a great </p>
<p>deal in your idea of an accomplished woman." </p>
<p>"Yes, I do comprehend a great deal in it." </p>
<p>"Oh! certainly," cried his faithful assistant, "no one can be </p>
<p>really esteemed accomplished who does not greatly surpass </p>
<p>what is usually met with. A woman must have a thorough </p>
<p>knowledge of music, singing, drawing, dancing, and the </p>
<p>modern languages, to deserve the word; and besides all this, </p>
<p>she must possess a certain something in her air and manner of </p>
<p>walking, the tone of her voice, her address and expressions, or </p>
<p>the word will be but half-deserved." </p>
<p>"All this she must possess," added Darcy, "and to all this </p>
<p>she must yet add something more substantial, in the </p>
<p>improvement of her mind by extensive reading." </p>
<p>"I am no longer surprised at your knowing ONLY six </p>
<p>accomplished women. I rather wonder now at your knowing </p>
<p>ANY." </p>
<p>"Are you so severe upon your own sex as to doubt the </p>
<p>possibility of all this?" </p>
<p>"I never saw such a woman. I never saw such capacity, and </p>
<p>taste, and application, and elegance, as you describe united." </p>
<p>Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley both cried out against the </p>
<p>injustice of her implied doubt, and were both protesting that </p>
<p>they knew many women who answered this description, when </p>
<p>Mr. Hurst called them to order, with bitter complaints of their </p>
<p>inattention to what was going forward. As all conversation </p>
<p>was thereby at an end, Elizabeth soon afterwards left the </p>
<p>room. </p>
<p>"Elizabeth Bennet," said Miss Bingley, when the door was </p>
<p>closed on her, "is one of those young ladies who seek to </p>
<p>recommend themselves to the other sex by undervaluing their </p>
<p>own; and with many men, I dare say, it succeeds. But, in my </p>
<p>opinion, it is a paltry device, a very mean art." </p>
<p>"Undoubtedly," replied Darcy, to whom this remark was </p>
<p>chiefly addressed, "there is a meanness in ALL the arts which </p>
<p>ladies sometimes condescend to employ for captivation. </p>
<p>Whatever bears affinity to cunning is despicable." </p>
<p>Miss Bingley was not so entirely satisfied with this reply </p>
<p>as to continue the subject. </p>
<p>Elizabeth joined them again only to say that her sister was </p>
<p>worse, and that she could not leave her. Bingley urged Mr. </p>
<p>Jones being sent for immediately; while his sisters, convinced </p>
<p>that no country advice could be of any service, recommended </p>
<p>an express to town for one of the most eminent physicians. </p>
<p>This she would not hear of; but she was not so unwilling to </p>
<p>comply with their brother's proposal; and it was settled that </p>
<p>Mr. Jones should be sent for early in the morning, if Miss </p>
<p>Bennet were not decidedly better. </p>
<p>Bingley was quite </p>
<p>uncomfortable; his sisters declared that they were miserable. </p>
<p>They solaced their wretchedness, however, by duets after </p>
<p>supper, while he could find no better relief to his feelings than </p>
<p>by giving his housekeeper directions that every attention </p>
<p>might be paid to the sick lady and her sister. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 9. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth passed the chief of the night in her sister's room, </p>
<p>and in the morning had the pleasure of being able to send a </p>
<p>tolerable answer to the inquiries which she very early received </p>
<p>from Mr. Bingley by a housemaid, and some time afterwards </p>
<p>from the two elegant ladies who waited on his sisters. In spite </p>
<p>of this amendment, however, she requested to have a note sent </p>
<p>to Longbourn, desiring her mother to visit Jane, and form her </p>
<p>own judgement of her situation. The note was immediately </p>
<p>dispatched, and its contents as quickly complied with. Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, accompanied by her two youngest girls, reached </p>
<p>Netherfield soon after the family breakfast. </p>
<p>Had she found Jane in any apparent danger, Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>would have been very miserable; but being satisfied on seeing </p>
<p>her that her illness was not alarming, she had no wish of her </p>
<p>recovering immediately, as her restoration to health would </p>
<p>probably remove her from Netherfield. She would not listen, </p>
<p>therefore, to her daughter's proposal of being carried home; </p>
<p>neither did the apothecary, who arrived about the same time, </p>
<p>think it at all advisable. After sitting a little while with Jane, </p>
<p>on Miss Bingley's appearance and invitation, the mother and </p>
<p>three daughter all attended her into the breakfast parlour. </p>
<p>Bingley met them with hopes that Mrs. Bennet had not found </p>
<p>Miss Bennet worse than she expected. </p>
<p>"Indeed I have, sir," was her answer. "She is a great deal </p>
<p>too ill to be moved. Mr. Jones says we must not think of </p>
<p>moving her. We must trespass a little longer on your </p>
<p>kindness." </p>
<p>"Removed!" cried Bingley. "It must not be thought of. My </p>
<p>sister, I am sure, will not hear of her removal." </p>
<p>"You may depend upon it, Madam," said Miss Bingley, </p>
<p>with cold civility, "that Miss Bennet will receive every </p>
<p>possible attention while she remains with us." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was profuse in her acknowledgments. </p>
<p>"I am sure," she added, "if it was not for such good friends </p>
<p>I do not know what would become of her, for she is very ill </p>
<p>indeed, and suffers a vast deal, though with the greatest </p>
<p>patience in the world, which is always the way with her, for </p>
<p>she has, without exception, the sweetest temper I have ever </p>
<p>met with. I often tell my other girls they are nothing to HER. </p>
<p>You have a sweet room here, Mr. Bingley, and a charming </p>
<p>prospect over the gravel walk. I do not know a place in the </p>
<p>country that is equal to Netherfield. You will not think of </p>
<p>quitting it in a hurry, I hope, though you have but a short </p>
<p>lease." </p>
<p>"Whatever I do is done in a hurry," replied he; "and </p>
<p>therefore if I should resolve to quit Netherfield, I should </p>
<p>probably be off in five minutes. At present, however, I </p>
<p>consider myself as quite fixed here." </p>
<p>"That is exactly what I should have supposed of you," said </p>
<p>Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"You begin to comprehend me, do you?" cried he, turning </p>
<p>towards her. </p>
<p>"Oh! yes--I understand you perfectly." </p>
<p>"I wish I might take this for a compliment; but to be so </p>
<p>easily seen through I am afraid is pitiful." </p>
<p>"That is as it happens. It does not follow that a deep, </p>
<p>intricate character is more or less estimable than such a one as </p>
<p>yours." </p>
<p>"Lizzy," cried her mother, "remember where you are, and </p>
<p>do not run on in the wild manner that you are suffered to do at </p>
<p>home." </p>
<p>"I did not know before," continued Bingley immediately, </p>
<p>"that your were a studier of character. It must be an amusing </p>
<p>study." </p>
<p>"Yes, but intricate characters are the MOST amusing. They </p>
<p>have at least that advantage." </p>
<p>"The country," said Darcy, "can in general supply but a </p>
<p>few subjects for such a study. In a country neighbourhood you </p>
<p>move in a very confined and unvarying society." </p>
<p>"But people themselves alter so much, that there is </p>
<p>something new to be observed in them for ever." </p>
<p>"Yes, indeed," cried Mrs. Bennet, offended by his manner </p>
<p>of mentioning a country neighbourhood. "I assure you there is </p>
<p>quite as much of THAT going on in the country as in town." </p>
<p>Everybody was surprised, and Darcy, after looking at her </p>
<p>for a moment, turned silently away. Mrs. Bennet, who fancied </p>
<p>she had gained a complete victory over him, continued her </p>
<p>triumph. </p>
<p>"I cannot see that London has any great advantage over the </p>
<p>country, for my part, except the shops and public places. The </p>
<p>country is a vast deal pleasanter, is it not, Mr. Bingley?" </p>
<p>"When I am in the country," he replied, "I never wish to </p>
<p>leave it; and when I am in town it is pretty much the same. </p>
<p>They have each their advantages, and I can be equally happy </p>
<p>in either." </p>
<p>"Aye--that is because you have the right disposition. But </p>
<p>that gentleman," looking at Darcy, "seemed to think the </p>
<p>country was nothing at all." </p>
<p>"Indeed, Mamma, you are mistaken," said Elizabeth, </p>
<p>blushing for her mother. "You quite mistook Mr. Darcy. He </p>
<p>only meant that there was not such a variety of people to be </p>
<p>met with in the country as in the town, which you must </p>
<p>acknowledge to be true." </p>
