<p>"Certainly, my dear, nobody said there were; but as to not </p>
<p>meeting with many people in this neighbourhood, I believe </p>
<p>there are few neighbourhoods larger. I know we dine with </p>
<p>four and-twenty families." </p>
<p>Nothing but concern for Elizabeth could enable Bingley to </p>
<p>keep his countenance. His sister was less delicate, and </p>
<p>directed her eyes towards Mr. Darcy with a very expressive </p>
<p>smile. Elizabeth, for the sake of saying something that might </p>
<p>turn her mother's thoughts, now asked her if Charlotte Lucas </p>
<p>had been at Longbourn since HER coming away. </p>
<p>"Yes, she called yesterday with her father. What an </p>
<p>agreeable man Sir William is, Mr. Bingley, is not he? So </p>
<p>much the man of fashion! So genteel and easy! He had always </p>
<p>something to say to everybody. THAT is my idea of good </p>
<p>breeding; and those persons who fancy themselves very </p>
<p>important, and never open their mouths, quite mistake the </p>
<p>matter." </p>
<p>"Did Charlotte dine with you?" </p>
<p>"No, she would go home. I fancy she was wanted about the </p>
<p>mince-pies. For my part, Mr. Bingley, I always keep servants </p>
<p>that can do their own work; MY daughters are brought up </p>
<p>very differently. But everybody is to judge for themselves, </p>
<p>and the Lucases are a very good sort of girls, I assure you. It is </p>
<p>a pity they are not handsome! Not that I think Charlotte so </p>
<p>VERY plain--but then she is our particular friend." </p>
<p>"She seems a very pleasant young woman." </p>
<p>"Oh! dear, yes; but you must own she is very plain. Lady </p>
<p>Lucas herself has often said so, and envied me Jane's beauty. I </p>
<p>do not like to boast of my own child, but to be sure, Jane--one </p>
<p>does not often see anybody better looking. It is what </p>
<p>everybody says. I do not trust my own partiality. When she </p>
<p>was only fifteen, there was a man at my brother Gardiner's in </p>
<p>town so much in love with her that my sister-in-law was sure </p>
<p>he would make her an offer before we came away. But, </p>
<p>however, he did not. Perhaps he thought her too young. </p>
<p>However, he wrote some verses on her, and very pretty they </p>
<p>were." </p>
<p>"And so ended his affection," said Elizabeth impatiently. </p>
<p>"There has been many a one, I fancy, overcome in the same </p>
<p>way. I wonder who first discovered the efficacy of poetry in </p>
<p>driving away love!" </p>
<p>"I have been used to consider poetry as the FOOD of </p>
<p>love," said Darcy. </p>
<p>"Of a fine, stout, healthy love it may. Everything nourishes </p>
<p>what is strong already. But if it be only a slight, thin sort of </p>
<p>inclination, I am convinced that one good sonnet will starve it </p>
<p>entirely away." </p>
<p>Darcy only smiled; and the general pause which ensued </p>
<p>made Elizabeth tremble lest her mother should be exposing </p>
<p>herself again. She longed to speak, but could think of nothing </p>
<p>to say; and after a short silence Mrs. Bennet began repeating </p>
<p>her thanks to Mr. Bingley for his kindness to Jane, with an </p>
<p>apology for troubling him also with Lizzy. Mr. Bingley was </p>
<p>unaffectedly civil in his answer, and forced his younger sister </p>
<p>to be civil also, and say what the occasion required. She </p>
<p>performed her part indeed without much graciousness, but </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was satisfied, and soon afterwards ordered her </p>
<p>carriage. Upon this signal, the youngest of her daughters put </p>
<p>herself forward. The two girls had been whispering to each </p>
<p>other during the whole visit, and the result of it was, that the </p>
<p>youngest should tax Mr. Bingley with having promised on his </p>
<p>first coming into the country to give a ball at Netherfield. </p>
<p>Lydia was a stout, well-grown girl of fifteen, with a fine </p>
<p>complexion and good-humoured countenance; a favourite </p>
<p>with her mother, whose affection had brought her into public </p>
<p>at an early age. She had high animal spirits, and a sort of </p>
<p>natural self-consequence, which the attention of the officers, </p>
<p>to whom her uncle's good dinners, and her own easy manners </p>
<p>recommended her, had increased into assurance. She was very </p>
<p>equal, therefore, to address Mr. Bingley on the subject of the </p>
<p>ball, and abruptly reminded him of his promise; adding, that it </p>
<p>would be the most shameful thing in the world if he did not </p>
<p>keep it. His answer to this sudden attack was delightful to </p>
<p>their mother's ear: </p>
<p>"I am perfectly ready, I assure you, to keep my </p>
<p>engagement; and when your sister is recovered, you shall, if </p>
<p>you please, name the very day of the ball. But you would not </p>
<p>wish to be dancing when she is ill." </p>
<p>Lydia declared herself satisfied. "Oh! yes--it would be </p>
<p>much better to wait till Jane was well, and by that time most </p>
<p>likely Captain Carter would be at Meryton again. And when </p>
<p>you have given YOUR ball," she added, "I shall insist on their </p>
<p>giving one also. I shall tell Colonel Forster it will be quite a </p>
<p>shame if he does not." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet and her </p>
<p>daughters then departed, </p>
<p>and </p>
<p>Elizabeth returned instantly to Jane, leaving her own and her </p>
<p>relations' behaviour to the remarks of the two ladies and Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy; the latter of whom, however, could not be prevailed on </p>
<p>to join in their censure of HER, in spite of all Miss Bingley's </p>
<p>witticisms on FINE EYES. </p>
<heading>Chapter 10. </heading>
<p>The day passed much as the day before had done. Mrs. </p>
<p>Hurst and Miss Bingley had spent some hours of the morning </p>
<p>with the invalid, who continued, though slowly, to mend; and </p>
<p>in the evening Elizabeth joined their party in the drawing- </p>
<p>room. The loo-table, however, did not appear. Mr. Darcy was </p>
<p>writing, and Miss Bingley, seated near him, was watching the </p>
<p>progress of his letter and repeatedly calling off his attention </p>
<p>by messages to his sister. Mr. Hurst and Mr. Bingley were at </p>
<p>piquet, and Mrs. Hurst was observing their game. </p>
<p>Elizabeth took up some needlework, and was sufficiently </p>
<p>amused in attending to what passed between Darcy and his </p>
<p>companion. The perpetual commendations of the lady, either </p>
<p>on his handwriting, or on the evenness of his lines, or on the </p>
<p>length of his letter, with the perfect unconcern with which her </p>
<p>praises were received, formed a curious dialogue, and was </p>
<p>exactly in union with her opinion of each. </p>
<p>"How delighted Miss Darcy will be to receive such a </p>
<p>letter!" </p>
<p>He made no answer. </p>
<p>"You write uncommonly fast." </p>
<p>"You are mistaken. I write rather slowly." </p>
<p>"How many letters you must have occasion to write in the </p>
<p>course of a year! Letters of business, too! How odious I </p>
<p>should think them!" </p>
<p>"It is fortunate, then, that they fall to my lot instead of </p>
<p>yours." </p>
<p>"Pray tell your sister that I long to see her." </p>
<p>"I have already told her so once, by your desire." </p>
<p>"I am afraid you do not like your pen. Let me mend it for </p>
<p>you. I mend pens remarkably well." </p>
<p>"Thank you--but I always mend my own." </p>
<p>"How can you contrive to write so even?" </p>
<p>He was silent. </p>
<p>"Tell your sister I am delighted to hear of her improvement </p>
<p>on the harp; and pray let her know that I am quite in raptures </p>
<p>with her beautiful little design for a table, and I think it </p>
<p>infinitely superior to Miss Grantley's." </p>
<p>"Will you give me leave to defer your raptures till I write </p>
<p>again? At present I have not room to do them justice." </p>
<p>"Oh! it is of no consequence. I shall see her in January. But </p>
<p>do you always write such charming long letters to her, Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy?" </p>
<p>"They are generally long; but whether always charming it </p>
<p>is not for me to determine." </p>
<p>"It is a rule with me, that a person who can write a long </p>
<p>letter with ease, cannot write ill." </p>
<p>"That will not do for a compliment to Darcy, Caroline," </p>
<p>cried her brother, "because he does NOT write with ease. He </p>
<p>studies too much for words of four syllables. Do not you, </p>
<p>Darcy?" </p>
<p>"My style of writing is very different from yours." </p>
<p>"Oh!" cried Miss Bingley, "Charles writes in the most </p>
<p>careless way imaginable. He leaves out half his words, and </p>
<p>blots the rest." </p>
<p>"My ideas flow so rapidly that I have not time to express </p>
<p>them--by which means my letters sometimes convey no ideas </p>
<p>at all to my correspondents." </p>
<p>"Your humility, Mr. Bingley," said Elizabeth, "must </p>
<p>disarm reproof." </p>
<p>"Nothing is more deceitful," said </p>
<p>Darcy, "than the </p>
<p>appearance of humility. It is often only carelessness of </p>
<p>opinion, and sometimes an indirect boast." </p>
<p>"And which of the two do you call MY little recent piece </p>
<p>of modesty?" </p>
<p>"The indirect boast; for you are really proud of your </p>
<p>defects in writing, because you consider them as proceeding </p>
<p>from a rapidity of thought and carelessness of execution, </p>
<p>which, if not estimable, you think at least highly interesting. </p>
<p>The power of doing anything with quickness is always prized </p>
<p>much by the possessor, and often without any attention to the </p>
<p>imperfection of the performance. When you told Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>this morning that if you ever resolved upon quitting </p>
<p>Netherfield you should be gone in five minutes, you meant it </p>
<p>to be a sort of panegyric, of compliment to yourself--and yet </p>
<p>what is there so very laudable in a precipitance which must </p>
<p>leave very necessary business undone, and can be of no real </p>
<p>advantage to yourself or anyone else?" </p>
<p>"Nay," cried Bingley, "this is too much, to remember at </p>
<p>night all the foolish things that were said in the morning. And </p>
<p>yet, upon my honour, I believe what I said of myself to be </p>
<p>true, and I believe it at this moment. At least, therefore, I did </p>
<p>not assume the character of needless precipitance merely to </p>
<p>show off before the ladies." </p>
<p>"I dare say you believed it; but I am by no means </p>
<p>convinced that you would be gone with such celerity. Your </p>
<p>conduct would be quite as dependent on chance as that of any </p>
<p>man I know; and if, as you were mounting your horse, a friend </p>
<p>were to say, 'Bingley, you had better stay till next week,' you </p>
<p>would probably do it, you would probably not go--and at </p>
<p>another word, might stay a month." </p>
<p>"You have only proved by this," cried Elizabeth, "that Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley did not do justice to his own disposition. You have </p>
<p>shown him off now much more than he did himself." </p>
<p>"I am exceedingly gratified," said Bingley, "by your </p>
<p>converting what my friend says into a compliment on the </p>
<p>sweetness of my temper. But I am afraid you are giving it a </p>
<p>turn which that gentleman did by no means intend; for he </p>
<p>would certainly think </p>
<p>better of me, if under such a </p>
<p>circumstance I were to give a flat denial, and ride off as fast as </p>
<p>I could." </p>
<p>"Would Mr. Darcy then consider the rashness of your </p>
<p>original intentions as atoned for by your obstinacy in adhering </p>
<p>to it?" </p>
<p>"Upon my word, I cannot exactly explain the matter; </p>
<p>Darcy must speak for himself." </p>
<p>"You expect me to account for opinions which you choose </p>
<p>to call mine, but which I have never acknowledged. Allowing </p>
<p>the case, however, to stand according to your representation, </p>
<p>you must remember, Miss Bennet, that the friend who is </p>
<p>supposed to desire his return to the house, and the delay of his </p>
<p>plan, has merely desired it, asked it without offering one </p>
<p>argument in favour of its propriety." </p>
<p>"To yield readily--easily--to the PERSUASION of a friend </p>
<p>is no merit with you." </p>
<p>"To yield without conviction is no compliment to the </p>
<p>understanding of either." </p>
<p>"You appear to me, Mr. Darcy, to allow nothing for the </p>
<p>influence of friendship and affection. A regard for the </p>
<p>requester would often make one readily yield to a request, </p>
<p>without waiting for arguments to reason one into it. I am not </p>
<p>particularly speaking of such a case as you have supposed </p>
<p>about Mr. Bingley. We may as well wait, perhaps, till the </p>
<p>circumstance occurs before we discuss the discretion of his </p>
<p>behaviour thereupon. But in general and ordinary cases </p>
<p>between friend and friend, where one of them is desired by the </p>
<p>other to change a resolution of no very great moment, should </p>
<p>you think ill of that person for complying with the desire, </p>
<p>without waiting to be argued into it?" </p>
<p>"Will it not be advisable, before we proceed on this </p>
<p>subject, to arrange with rather more precision the degree of </p>
<p>importance which is to appertain to this request, as well as the </p>
<p>degree of intimacy subsisting between the parties?" </p>
<p>"By all means," cried Bingley; "let us hear all the </p>
<p>particulars, not forgetting their comparative height and size; </p>
<p>for that will have more weight in the argument, Miss Bennet, </p>
<p>than you may be aware of. I assure you, that if Darcy were not </p>
<p>such a great tall fellow, in comparison with myself, I should </p>
<p>not pay him half so much deference. I declare I do not know a </p>
<p>more awful object than Darcy, on particular occasions, and in </p>
<p>particular places; at his own house especially, and of a Sunday </p>
<p>evening, when he has nothing to do." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy smiled; but Elizabeth thought she could </p>
<p>perceive that he was rather offended, and therefore checked </p>
<p>her laugh. Miss Bingley warmly resented the indignity he had </p>
<p>received, in an expostulation with her brother for talking such </p>
<p>nonsense. </p>
<p>"I see your design, Bingley," said his friend. "You dislike </p>
<p>an argument, and want to silence this." </p>
<p>"Perhaps I do. Arguments are too much like disputes. If </p>
<p>you and Miss Bennet will defer yours till I am out of the </p>
<p>room, I shall be very thankful; and then you may say whatever </p>
<p>you like of me." </p>
<p>"What you ask," said Elizabeth, "is no sacrifice on my </p>
<p>side; and Mr. Darcy had much better finish his letter." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy took her advice, and did finish his letter. </p>
<p>When that business was over, he applied to Miss Bingley </p>
<p>and Elizabeth for an indulgence of some music. Miss Bingley </p>
<p>moved with some alacrity to the pianoforte; and, after a polite </p>
<p>request that Elizabeth would lead the way which the other as </p>
<p>politely and more earnestly negatived, she seated herself. </p>
<p>Mrs. Hurst sang with her sister, and while they were thus </p>
<p>employed, Elizabeth could not help observing, as she turned </p>
<p>over some music-books that lay on the instrument, how </p>
<p>frequently Mr. Darcy's eyes were fixed on her. She hardly </p>
<p>knew how to suppose that she could be an object of </p>
<p>admiration to so great a man; and yet that he should look at </p>
<p>her because he disliked her, was still more strange. She could </p>
<p>only imagine, however, at last that she drew his notice </p>
<p>because there was something more wrong and reprehensible, </p>
<p>according to his ideas of right, than in any other person </p>
<p>present. The supposition did not pain her. She liked him too </p>
<p>little to care for his approbation. </p>
<p>After playing some Italian songs, Miss Bingley varied the </p>
<p>charm by a lively Scotch air; and soon afterwards Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>drawing near Elizabeth, said to her: </p>
<p>"Do not you feel a great inclination, Miss Bennet, to seize </p>
<p>such an opportunity of dancing a reel?" </p>
<p>She smiled, but made no answer. He repeated the question, </p>
<p>with some surprise at her silence. </p>
<p>"Oh!" said she, "I heard you before, but I could not </p>
<p>immediately determine what to say in reply. You wanted me, I </p>
<p>know, to say 'Yes,' that you might have the pleasure of </p>
<p>despising my taste; but I always delight in overthrowing those </p>
<p>kind of schemes, and cheating a person of their premeditated </p>
<p>contempt. I have, therefore, made up my mind to tell you, that </p>
<p>I do not want to dance a reel at all--and now despise me if you </p>
<p>dare." </p>
<p>"Indeed I do not dare." </p>
<p>Elizabeth, having rather expected to affront him, was </p>
<p>amazed at his gallantry; but there was a mixture of sweetness </p>
<p>and archness in her manner which made it difficult for her to </p>
<p>affront anybody; and Darcy had never been so bewitched by </p>
<p>any woman as he was by her. He really believed, that were it </p>
<p>not for the inferiority of her connections, he should be in some </p>
<p>danger. </p>
<p>Miss Bingley saw, or suspected enough to be jealous; and </p>
<p>her great anxiety for the recovery of her dear friend Jane </p>
<p>received some assistance from her desire of getting rid of </p>
<p>Elizabeth. </p>
<p>She often tried to provoke Darcy into disliking her guest, </p>
<p>by talking of their supposed marriage, and planning his </p>
<p>happiness in such an alliance. </p>
<p>"I hope," said she, as they were walking together in the </p>
<p>shrubbery the next day, "you will give your mother-in-law a </p>
<p>few hints, when this desirable event takes place, as to the </p>
<p>advantage of holding her tongue; and if you can compass it, </p>
<p>do sure the younger girls of running after officers. And, if I </p>
<p>may mention so delicate a subject, endeavour to check that </p>
<p>little something, </p>
<p>bordering on conceit and impertinence, </p>
<p>which your lady possesses." </p>
<p>"Have you anything else to propose for my domestic </p>
<p>felicity?" </p>
<p>"Oh! yes. Do let the portraits of your uncle and aunt </p>
<p>Phillips be placed in the gallery at Pemberley. Put them next </p>
<p>to your great-uncle the judge. They are in the same profession, </p>
<p>you know, only in different lines. As for your Elizabeth's </p>
<p>picture, you must not have it taken, for what painter could do </p>
<p>justice to those beautiful eyes?" </p>
<p>"It would not be easy, indeed, to catch their expression, but </p>
<p>their colour and shape, and the eyelashes, so remarkably fine, </p>
<p>might be copied." </p>
<p>At that moment they were met from another walk by Mrs. </p>
<p>Hurst and Elizabeth herself. </p>
<p>"I did not know that you intended to walk," said Miss </p>
<p>Bingley, in some confusion, lest they had been overheard. </p>
<p>"You used us abominably </p>
<p>ill," answered Mrs. Hurst, </p>
<p>"running away without telling us that you were coming out." </p>
<p>Then taking the disengaged arm of Mr. Darcy, she left </p>
<p>Elizabeth to walk by herself. The path just admitted three. Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy felt their rudeness, and immediately said: </p>
<p>"This walk is not wide enough for our party. We had better </p>
<p>go into the avenue." </p>
<p>But Elizabeth, who had not the least inclination to remain </p>
<p>with them, laughingly answered: </p>
<p>"No, no; stay where you are. You are charmingly grouped, </p>
<p>and appear to uncommon advantage. The picturesque would </p>
<p>be spoilt by admitting a fourth. Good-bye." </p>
<p>She then ran gaily off, rejoicing as she rambled about, in </p>
<p>the hope of being at home again in a day or two. Jane was </p>
<p>already so much recovered as to intend leaving her room for a </p>
<p>couple of hours that evening. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 11. </heading>
<p>When the ladies removed after dinner, Elizabeth ran up to </p>
<p>her sister, and seeing her well guarded from cold, attended her </p>
<p>into the drawing-room, where she was welcomed by her two </p>
<p>friends with many professions of pleasure; and Elizabeth had </p>
<p>never seen them so agreeable as they were during the hour </p>
<p>which passed before the gentlemen appeared. Their powers of </p>
<p>conversation were considerable. They could describe an </p>
<p>entertainment with accuracy, relate an anecdote with humour, </p>
<p>and laugh at their acquaintance with spirit. </p>
<p>But when the gentlemen entered, Jane was no longer the </p>
<p>first object; Miss Bingley's eyes were instantly turned toward </p>
<p>Darcy, and she had something to say to him before he had </p>
<p>advanced many steps. He addressed himself to Miss Bennet, </p>
<p>with a polite congratulation; Mr. Hurst also made her a slight </p>
<p>bow, and said he was "very glad;" but diffuseness and warmth </p>
<p>remained for Bingley's salutation. He was full of joy and </p>
<p>attention. The first half-hour was spent in piling up the fire, </p>
<p>lest she should suffer from the change of room; and she </p>
<p>removed at his desire to the other side of the fireplace, that </p>
<p>she might be further from the door. He then sat down by her, </p>
<p>and talked scarcely to anyone else. Elizabeth, at work in the </p>
<p>opposite corner, saw it all with great delight. </p>
<p>When tea was over, Mr. Hurst reminded his sister-in-law </p>
<p>of the card-table--but in vain. She had obtained private </p>
<p>intelligence that Mr. Darcy did not wish for cards; and Mr. </p>
<p>Hurst soon found even his open petition rejected. She assured </p>
<p>him that no one intended to play, and the silence of the whole </p>
<p>party on the subject seemed to justify her. Mr. Hurst had </p>
<p>therefore nothing to do, but to stretch himself on one of the </p>
<p>sofas and go to sleep. Darcy took up a book; Miss Bingley did </p>
<p>the same; and Mrs. Hurst, principally occupied in playing </p>
<p>with her bracelets and rings, joined now and then in her </p>
<p>brother's conversation with Miss Bennet. </p>
<p>Miss Bingley's attention was quite as much engaged in </p>
<p>watching Mr. Darcy's progress through HIS book, as in </p>
<p>reading her own; and she was perpetually either making some </p>
<p>inquiry, or looking at his page. She could not win him, </p>
<p>however, to any conversation; he merely answered her </p>
<p>question, and read on. At length, quite exhausted by the </p>
<p>attempt to be amused with her own book, which she had only </p>
<p>chosen because it was the second volume of his, she gave a </p>
<p>great yawn and said, "How pleasant it is to spend an evening </p>
<p>in this way! I declare after all there is no enjoyment like </p>
<p>reading! How much sooner one tires of anything than of a </p>
<p>book! When I have a house of my own, I shall be miserable if </p>
<p>I have not an excellent library." </p>
<p>No one made any reply. She then yawned again, threw </p>
<p>aside her book, and cast her eyes round the room in quest for </p>
<p>some amusement; when hearing her brother mentioning a ball </p>
<p>to Miss Bennet, she turned suddenly towards him and said: </p>
<p>"By the bye, Charles, are you really serious in meditating a </p>
<p>dance at Netherfield? I would advise you, before you deter </p>
<p>mine on it, to consult the wishes of the present party; I am </p>
<p>much mistaken if there are not some among us to whom a ball </p>
<p>would be rather a punishment than a pleasure." </p>
<p>"If you mean Darcy," cried her brother, "he may go to bed, </p>
<p>if he chooses, before it begins--but as for the ball, it is quite a </p>
<p>settled thing; and as soon as Nicholls has made white soup </p>
<p>enough, I shall send round my cards." </p>
<p>"I should like balls infinitely better," she replied, "if they </p>
<p>were carried on in a different manner; but there is something </p>
<p>insufferably tedious in the usual process of such a meeting. It </p>
<p>would surely be much more rational if conversation instead of </p>
<p>dancing were made the order of the day." </p>
<p>"Much more rational, my dear Caroline, I dare say, but it </p>
<p>would not be near so much like a ball." </p>
<p>Miss Bingley made no answer, and soon afterwards she </p>
<p>got up and walked about the room. Her figure was elegant, </p>
<p>and she walked well; but Darcy, at whom it was all aimed, </p>
<p>was still inflexibly studious. In the desperation of her feelings, </p>
<p>she resolved on one effort more, and, turning to Elizabeth, </p>
<p>said: </p>
<p>"Miss Eliza Bennet, let me persuade you to follow my </p>
<p>example, and take a turn about the room. I assure you it is </p>
<p>very refreshing after sitting so long in one attitude." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was surprised, but agreed to it immediately. Miss </p>
<p>Bingley succeeded no less in the real object of her civility; </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy looked up. He was as much awake to the novelty of </p>
<p>attention in that quarter as Elizabeth herself could be, and </p>
<p>unconsciously closed his book. He was directly invited to join </p>
<p>their party, but he declined it, observing that he could imagine </p>
<p>but two motives for their choosing to walk up and down the </p>
<p>room together, with either of which motives his joining them </p>
<p>would interfere. "What could he mean? She was dying to </p>
<p>know what could be his meaning?"--and asked Elizabeth </p>
<p>whether she could at all understand him? </p>
<p>"Not at all," was her answer; "but depend upon it, he </p>
<p>means to be severe on us, and our surest way of disappointing </p>
<p>him will be to ask nothing about it." </p>
<p>Miss Bingley, however, was incapable of disappointing </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy in anything, and persevered therefore in requiring </p>
<p>an explanation of his two motives. </p>
<p>"I have not the smallest objection to explaining them," said </p>
<p>he, as soon as she allowed him to speak. "You either choose </p>
<p>this method of passing the evening because you are in each </p>
<p>other's confidence, and have secret affairs to discuss, or </p>
<p>because you are conscious that your figures appear to the </p>
<p>greatest advantage in walking; if the first, I would be </p>
<p>completely in your way, and if the second, I can admire you </p>
<p>much better as I sit by the fire." </p>
<p>"Oh! shocking!" cried Miss Bingley. "I never heard </p>
<p>anything so abominable. How shall we punish him for such a </p>
<p>speech?" </p>
<p>"Nothing so easy, if you have but the inclination," said </p>
<p>Elizabeth. "We can all plague and punish one another. Tease </p>
<p>him- laugh at him. Intimate as you are, you must know how it </p>
<p>is to be done." </p>
<p>"But upon my honour, I do NOT. I do assure you that my </p>
<p>intimacy has not yet taught me THAT. Tease calmness of </p>
<p>manner and presence of mind! No, no--feel he may defy us </p>
<p>there. And as to laughter, we will not expose ourselves, if you </p>
<p>please, by attempting to laugh without a subject. Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>may hug himself." </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy is not to be laughed at!" cried Elizabeth. "That </p>
<p>is an uncommon advantage, and uncommon I hope it will </p>
<p>continue, for it would be a great loss to ME to have many </p>
<p>such acquaintances. I dearly love a laugh." </p>
<p>"Miss Bingley," said he, "has given me more credit than </p>
<p>can be. The wisest and the best of men--nay, the wisest and </p>
<p>best of their actions--may be rendered ridiculous by a person </p>
<p>whose first object in life is a joke." </p>
<p>"Certainly," replied Elizabeth--"there are such people, but I </p>
<p>hope I am not one of THEM. I hope I never ridicule what is </p>
<p>wise and good. Follies and nonsense, whims and </p>
<p>inconsistencies, DO divert me, I own, and I laugh at them </p>
<p>whenever I can. But these, I suppose, are precisely what you </p>
<p>are without." </p>
<p>"Perhaps that is not possible for anyone. But it has been </p>
<p>the study of my life to avoid those weaknesses which often </p>
<p>expose a strong understanding to ridicule." </p>
<p>"Such as vanity and pride." </p>
<p>"Yes, vanity is a weakness indeed. But pride--where there </p>
<p>is a real superiority of mind, pride will be always under good </p>
<p>regulation." </p>
<p>Elizabeth turned away to hide a smile. </p>
<p>"Your examination of Mr. Darcy is over, I presume," said </p>
<p>Miss Bingley; "and pray what is the result?" </p>
<p>"I am perfectly convinced by it that Mr. Darcy has no </p>
<p>defect. He owns it himself without disguise." </p>
<p>"No," said Darcy, "I have made no such pretension. I have </p>
<p>faults enough, but they are not, I hope, of understanding. My </p>
<p>temper I dare not vouch for. It is, I believe, too little yielding- </p>
<p>certainly too little for the convenience of the world. I cannot </p>
<p>forget the follies and vices of other so soon as I ought, nor </p>
<p>their offenses against myself. My feelings are not puffed </p>
<p>about with every attempt to move them. My temper would </p>
<p>perhaps be called resentful. My good opinion once lost, is lost </p>
<p>forever." </p>
<p>"THAT is a failing indeed!" cried Elizabeth. "Implacable </p>
<p>resentment IS a shade in a character. But you have chosen </p>
<p>your fault well. I really cannot LAUGH at it. You are safe </p>
<p>from me." </p>
<p>"There is, I believe, in every disposition a tendency to </p>
<p>some particular evil--a natural defect, which not even the best </p>
<p>education can overcome." </p>
<p>"And YOUR defect is to hate everybody." </p>
<p>"And yours," he replied with a smile, "is willfully to </p>
<p>misunderstand them." </p>
<p>"Do let us have a little music," cried Miss Bingley, tired of </p>
<p>a conversation in which she had no share. "Louisa, you will </p>
<p>not mind my waking Mr. Hurst?" </p>
<p>Her sister had not the smallest objection, and the piano </p>
<p>forte was opened; and Darcy, after a few moments' </p>
<p>recollection, was not sorry for it. He began to feel the danger </p>
<p>of paying Elizabeth too much attention. </p>
<heading>Chapter 12. </heading>
<p>In consequence of an agreement between the sisters, </p>
<p>Elizabeth wrote the next morning to their mother, to beg that </p>
<p>the carriage might be sent for them in the course of the day. </p>
<p>But Mrs. Bennet, who had calculated on her daughters </p>
<p>remaining at Netherfield till the following Tuesday, which </p>
<p>would exactly finish Jane's week, could not bring herself to </p>
<p>receive them with pleasure before. Her answer, therefore, was </p>
<p>not propitious, at least not to Elizabeth's wishes, for she was </p>
<p>impatient to get home. Mrs. Bennet sent them word that they </p>
<p>could not possibly have the carriage before Tuesday; and in </p>
<p>her postscript it was added, that if Mr. Bingley and his sister </p>
<p>pressed them to stay longer, she could spare them very well. </p>
<p>Against staying longer, however, Elizabeth was positively </p>
<p>resolved--nor did she much expect it would be asked; and </p>
<p>fearful, on the contrary, as being considered as intruding </p>
<p>themselves needlessly long, she urged Jane to borrow Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley's carriage immediately, and at length it was settled </p>
<p>that their original design of leaving Netherfield that morning </p>
<p>should be mentioned, and the request made. </p>
<p>The communication excited many professions of concern; </p>
<p>and enough was said of wishing them to stay at least till the </p>
<p>following day to work on Jane; and till the morrow their going </p>
<p>was deferred. Miss Bingley was then sorry that she had </p>
<p>proposed the delay, for her jealousy and dislike of one sister </p>
<p>much exceeded her affection for the other. </p>
<p>The master of the house heard with real sorrow that they </p>
<p>were to go so soon, and repeatedly tried to persuade Miss </p>
<p>Bennet that it would not be safe for her--that she was not </p>
<p>enough recovered; but Jane was firm where she felt herself to </p>
<p>be right. </p>
<p>To Mr. Darcy it was welcome intelligence--Elizabeth had </p>
<p>been at Netherfield long enough. She attracted him more than </p>
<p>he liked--and Miss Bingley was uncivil to HER, and more </p>
<p>teasing than usual to himself. He wisely resolved to be </p>
<p>particularly careful that no sign of admiration should NOW </p>
<p>escape him, nothing that could elevate her with the hope of </p>
<p>influencing his felicity; sensible that if such an idea had been </p>
<p>suggested, his behaviour during the last day must have </p>
<p>material weight in confirming or crushing it. Steady to his </p>
<p>purpose, he scarcely spoke ten words to her through the whole </p>
<p>of Saturday, and though they were at one time left by </p>
<p>themselves for half-an-hour, he adhered most conscientiously </p>
<p>to his book, and would not even look at her. </p>
<p>On Sunday, </p>
<p>after morning service, the separation, so </p>
<p>agreeable to almost all, took place. Miss Bingley's civility to </p>
<p>Elizabeth increased at last very rapidly, as well as her </p>
<p>affection for Jane; and when they parted, after assuring the </p>
<p>latter of the pleasure it would always give her to see her either </p>
<p>at Longbourn or Netherfield, and embracing her most </p>
<p>tenderly, she even shook hands with the former. Elizabeth </p>
<p>took leave of the whole party in the liveliest of spirits. </p>
<p>They were not welcomed home very cordially by their </p>
<p>mother. Mrs. Bennet wondered at their coming, and thought </p>
<p>them very wrong to give so much trouble, and was sure Jane </p>
<p>would have caught cold again. But their father, though very </p>
<p>laconic in his expressions of pleasure, was really glad to see </p>
<p>them; he had felt their importance in the family circle. The </p>
<p>evening conversation, when they were all assembled, had lost </p>
<p>much of its animation, and almost all its sense by the absence </p>
<p>of Jane and Elizabeth. </p>
<p>They found Mary, as usual, deep in the study of thorough- </p>
<p>bass and human nature; and had some extracts to admire, and </p>
<p>some new observations of threadbare morality to listen to. </p>
<p>Catherine and Lydia had information for them of a different </p>
<p>sort. Much had been done and much had been said in the </p>
<p>regiment since the preceding Wednesday; several of the </p>
<p>officers had dined lately with their uncle, a private had been </p>
<p>flogged, and it had actually been hinted that Colonel Forster </p>
<p>was going to be married. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 13. </heading>
<p>"I hope, my dear," said Mr. Bennet to his wife, as they </p>
<p>were at breakfast the next morning, "that you have ordered a </p>
<p>good dinner to-day, because I have reason to expect an </p>
<p>addition to our family party." </p>
<p>"Who do you mean, my dear? I know of nobody that is </p>
<p>coming, I am sure, unless Charlotte Lucas should happen to </p>
<p>call in--and I hope MY dinners are good enough for her. I do </p>
<p>not believe she often sees such at home." </p>
<p>"The person of whom I speak is a gentleman, and a </p>
<p>stranger." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet's eyes sparkled. "A gentleman and a stranger! </p>
<p>It is Mr. Bingley, I am sure! Well, I am sure I shall be </p>
<p>extremely glad to see Mr. Bingley. But--good Lord! how </p>
<p>unlucky! There is not a bit of fish to be got to-day. Lydia, my </p>
<p>love, ring the bell--I must speak to Hill this moment." </p>
<p>"It is NOT Mr. Bingley," said her husband; "it is a person </p>
<p>whom I never saw in the whole course of my life." </p>
<p>This roused a general astonishment; and he had the </p>
<p>pleasure of being eagerly questioned by his wife and his five </p>
<p>daughters at once. </p>
<p>After amusing himself some time with their curiosity, he </p>
<p>thus explained: </p>
<p>"About a month ago I received this letter; and about a </p>
<p>fortnight ago I answered it, for I thought it a case of some </p>
<p>delicacy, and requiring early attention. It is from my cousin, </p>
<p>Mr. Collins, who, when I am dead, may turn you all out of </p>
<p>this house as soon as he pleases." </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear," cried his wife, "I cannot bear to hear that </p>
<p>mentioned. Pray do not talk of that odious man. I do think it is </p>
<p>the hardest thing in the world, that your estate should be </p>
<p>entailed away from your own children; and I am sure, if I had </p>
<p>been you, I should have tried long ago to do something or </p>
<p>other about it." </p>
<p>Jane and Elizabeth tried to explain to her the nature of an </p>
<p>entail. They had often attempted to do it before, but it was a </p>
<p>subject on which Mrs. Bennet was beyond the reach of reason, </p>
<p>and she continued to rail bitterly against the cruelty of settling </p>
<p>an estate away from a family of five daughters, in favour of a </p>
<p>man whom nobody cared anything about. </p>
<p>"It certainly is a most iniquitous affair," said Mr. Bennet, </p>
<p>"and nothing can clear Mr. Collins from the guilt of inheriting </p>
<p>Longbourn. But if you will listen to his letter, you may </p>
<p>perhaps be a little softened by his manner of expressing </p>
<p>himself." </p>
<p>"No, that I am sure I shall not; and I think it is very </p>
<p>impertinent of him to write to you at all, and very </p>
<p>hypocritical. I hate such false friends. Why could he not keep </p>
<p>on quarreling with you, as his father did before him?" </p>
<p>"Why, indeed; he does seem to have had some filial </p>
<p>scruples on that head, as you will hear." </p>
<p>"Hunsford, near Westerham, Kent, 15th October. </p>
<p>"Dear Sir,- </p>
<p>"The disagreement subsisting between yourself and my </p>
<p>late honoured father always gave me much uneasiness, and </p>
<p>since I have had the misfortune to lose him, I have frequently </p>
<p>wished to heal the breach; but for some time I was kept back </p>
<p>by my own doubts, fearing lest it might seem disrespectful to </p>
<p>his memory for me to be on good terms with anyone with </p>
<p>whom it had always pleased him to be at variance.--'There, </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet.'--My mind, however, is now made up on the </p>
<p>subject, for having received ordination at Easter, I have been </p>
<p>so fortunate as to be distinguished by the patronage of the </p>
<p>Right Honourable Lady Catherine de Bourgh, widow of Sir </p>
<p>Lewis de Bourgh, whose bounty and beneficence has </p>
<p>preferred me to the valuable rectory of this parish, where it </p>
<p>shall be my earnest endeavour to demean myself with grateful </p>
<p>respect towards her ladyship, and be ever ready to perform </p>
<p>those rites and ceremonies which are instituted by the Church </p>
<p>of England. As a clergyman, moreover, I feel it my duty to </p>
<p>promote and establish the blessing of peace in all families </p>
<p>within in the reach of my influence; and on these grounds I </p>
<p>flatter myself that my present overtures are highly </p>
<p>commendable, and that the circumstance of my being next in </p>
<p>the entail of Longbourn estate will be kindly overlooked on </p>
<p>your side, and not lead you to reject the offered olive-branch. </p>
<p>I cannot be otherwise than concerned at being the means of </p>
<p>injuring your amiable daughters, and beg leave to apologise </p>
<p>for it, as well as to assure you of my readiness to make them </p>
<p>every possible amends--but of this hereafter. If you should </p>
<p>have no objection to receive me into your house, I propose </p>
<p>myself the satisfaction of waiting on you and your family, </p>
<p>Monday, November 18th, by four o'clock, and shall probably </p>
<p>trespass on your hospitality till the Saturday night following, </p>
<p>which I can do without any inconvenience, as Lady Catherine </p>
<p>is far from objecting to my occasional absence on a Sunday, </p>
<p>provided that some other clergyman is engaged to do the duty </p>
<p>of the day.--I remain, dear sir, with respectful compliments to </p>
<p>your lady and daughters, your well-wisher and friend, </p>
<p>"WILLIAM COLLINS" </p>
<p>"At four o'clock, therefore, we may expect this peace- </p>
<p>making gentleman," said Mr. Bennet, as he folded up the </p>
<p>letter. "He seems to be a most conscientious and polite young </p>
<p>man, upon my word, and I doubt not will prove a valuable </p>
<p>acquaintance, especially if Lady Catherine should be so </p>
<p>indulgent as to let him come to us again." </p>
<p>"There is some sense in what he says about the girls, </p>
<p>however, and if he is disposed to make them any amends, I </p>
<p>shall not be the person to discourage him." </p>
<p>"Though it is difficult," said Jane, "to guess in what way he </p>
<p>can mean to make us the atonement he thinks our due, the </p>
<p>wish is certainly to his credit." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was chiefly struck by his extraordinary deference </p>
<p>for Lady Catherine, and his kind intention of christening, </p>
<p>marrying, and burying his parishioners whenever it were </p>
<p>required. </p>
<p>"He must be an oddity, I think," said she. "I cannot make </p>
<p>him out.--There is something very pompous in his style.--And </p>
<p>what can he mean by apologising for being next in the entail?- </p>
<p>We cannot suppose he would help it if he could.--Could he be </p>
<p>a sensible man, sir?" </p>
<p>"No, my dear, I think not. I have great hopes of finding </p>
<p>him quite the reverse. There is a mixture of servility and self- </p>
<p>importance in his letter, which promises well. I am impatient </p>
<p>to see him." </p>
<p>"In point of composition," said Mary, "the letter does not </p>
<p>seem defective. The idea of the olive-branch perhaps is not </p>
<p>wholly new, yet I think it is well expressed." </p>
<p>To Catherine and Lydia, neither the letter nor its writer </p>
<p>were in any degree interesting. It was next to impossible that </p>
<p>their cousin should come in a scarlet coat, and it was now </p>
<p>some weeks since they had received pleasure from the society </p>
<p>of a man in any other colour. As for their mother, Mr. </p>
<p>Collins's letter had done away much of her ill-will, and she </p>
<p>was preparing to see him with a degree of composure which </p>
<p>astonished her husband and daughters. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins was punctual to his time, and was received </p>
<p>with great politeness by the whole family. Mr. Bennet indeed </p>
<p>said little; but the ladies were ready enough to talk, and Mr. </p>
<p>Collins seemed neither in need of encouragement, nor </p>
<p>inclined to be silent himself. He was a tall, heavy-looking </p>
<p>young man of fiveand-twenty. His air was grave and stately, </p>
<p>and his manners were very formal. He had not been long </p>
<p>seated before he complimented Mrs. Bennet on having so fine </p>
<p>a family of daughters; said he had heard much of their beauty, </p>
<p>but that in this instance fame had fallen short of the truth; and </p>
<p>added, that he did not doubt her seeing them all in due time </p>
<p>disposed of in marriage. This gallantry was not much to the </p>
<p>taste of some of his hearers; but Mrs. Bennet, who quarreled </p>
<p>with no compliments, answered most readily. </p>
<p>"You are very kind, I am sure; and I wish with all my heart </p>
<p>it may prove so, for else they will be destitute enough. Things </p>
<p>are settled so oddly." </p>
<p>"You allude, perhaps, to the entail of this estate." </p>
<p>"Ah! sir, I do indeed. It is a grievous affair to my poor </p>
<p>girls, you must confess. Not that I mean to find fault with </p>
<p>YOU, for such things I know are all chance in this world. </p>
<p>There is no knowing how estates will go when once they </p>
<p>come to be entailed." </p>
<p>"I am very sensible, madam, of the hardship to my fair </p>
<p>cousins, and could say much on the subject, but that I am </p>
<p>cautious of appearing forward and precipitate. But I can </p>
<p>assure the young ladies that I come prepared to admire them. </p>
<p>At present I will not say more; but, perhaps, when we are </p>
<p>better acquainted--" </p>
<p>He was interrupted by a summons to dinner; and the girls </p>
<p>smiled on each other. They were not the only objects of Mr. </p>
<p>Collins's admiration. The hall, the dining-room, and all its </p>
<p>furniture, were examined and praised; and his commendation </p>
<p>of everything would have touched Mrs. Bennet's heart, but for </p>
<p>the mortifying supposition of his viewing it all as his own </p>
<p>future property. The dinner too in its turn was highly admired; </p>
<p>and he begged to know to which of his fair cousins the </p>
<p>excellency of its cooking was owing. But he was set right </p>
<p>there by Mrs. Bennet, who assured him with some asperity </p>
<p>that they were very well able to keep a good cook, and that </p>
<p>her daughters had nothing to do in the kitchen. He begged </p>
<p>pardon for having displeased her. In a softened tone she </p>
<p>declared herself not at all offended; but he continued to </p>
<p>apologise for about a quarter of an hour. </p>
<header>Ebd </header>
<header>E-BooksDirectory.com </header>
<heading>Chapter 14. </heading>
<p>During dinner, Mr. Bennet scarcely spoke at all; but when </p>
<p>the servants were withdrawn, he thought it time to have some </p>
<p>conversation with his guest, and therefore started a subject in </p>
<p>which he expected him to shine, by observing that he seemed </p>
<p>very fortunate in his patroness. Lady Catherine de Bourgh's </p>
<p>attention to his wishes, and consideration for his comfort, </p>
<p>appeared very remarkable. Mr. Bennet could not have chosen </p>
<p>better. Mr. Collins was eloquent in her praise. The subject </p>
<p>elevated him to more than usual solemnity of manner, and </p>
<p>with a most important aspect he protested that "he had never </p>
<p>in his life witnessed such behaviour in a person of rank--such </p>
<p>affability and condescension, as he had himself experienced </p>
<p>from Lady Catherine. She had been graciously pleased to </p>
<p>approve of both of the discourses which he had already had </p>
<p>the honour of preaching before her. She had also asked him </p>
<p>twice to dine at Rosings, and had sent for him only the </p>
<p>Saturday before, to make up her pool of quadrille in the </p>
<p>evening. Lady Catherine was reckoned proud by many people </p>
<p>he knew, but HE had never seen anything but affability in her. </p>
<p>She had always spoken to him as she would to any other </p>
<p>gentleman; she made not the smallest objection to his joining </p>
<p>in the society of the neighbourhood nor to his leaving the </p>
<p>parish occasionally for a week or two, to visit his relations. </p>
<p>She had even condescended to advise him to marry as soon as </p>
<p>he could, provided he chose with discretion; and had once </p>
<p>paid him a visit in his humble parsonage, where she had </p>
<p>perfectly approved all the alterations he had been making, and </p>
<p>had even vouchsafed to suggest some herself--some shelves in </p>
<p>the closet upstairs." </p>
<p>"That is all very proper and civil, I am sure," said Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, "and I dare say she is a very agreeable woman. It is a </p>
<p>pity that great ladies in general are not more like her. Does </p>
<p>she live near you, sir?" </p>
<p>"The garden in which stands my humble abode is </p>
<p>separated only by a lane from Rosings Park, her ladyship's </p>
<p>residence." </p>
<p>"I think you said she was a widow, sir? Has she any </p>
<p>family?" </p>
<p>"She has only one daughter, the heiress of Rosings, and of </p>
<p>very extensive property." </p>
<p>"Ah!" said Mrs. Bennet, shaking her head, "then she is </p>
<p>better off than many girls. And what sort of young lady is </p>
<p>she? Is she handsome?" </p>
<p>"She is a most charming young lady indeed. Lady </p>
<p>Catherine herself says that, in point of true beauty, Miss de </p>
<p>Bourgh is far superior to the handsomest of her sex, because </p>
<p>there is that in her features which marks the young lady of </p>
<p>distinguished </p>
<p>birth. </p>
<p>She </p>
<p>is unfortunately of a sickly </p>
<p>constitution, which has prevented her from making that </p>
<p>progress in many accomplishments which she could not have </p>
<p>otherwise failed of, as I am informed by the lady who </p>
<p>superintended her education, and who still resides with them. </p>
<p>But she is perfectly amiable, and often condescends to drive </p>
<p>by my humble abode in her little phaeton and ponies." </p>
<p>"Has she been presented? I do not remember her name </p>
<p>among the ladies at court." </p>
<p>"Her indifferent state of health unhappily prevents her </p>
<p>being in town; and by that means, as I told Lady Catherine </p>
<p>one day, has deprived the British court of its brightest </p>
<p>ornaments. Her ladyship seemed pleased with the idea; and </p>
<p>you may imagine that I am happy on every occasion to offer </p>
<p>those little delicate compliments which are always acceptable </p>
<p>to ladies. I have more than once observed to Lady Catherine, </p>
<p>that her charming daughter seemed born to be a duchess, and </p>
<p>that the most elevated rank, instead of giving </p>
<p>her </p>
<p>consequence, would be adorned by her. These are the kind of </p>
<p>little things which please her ladyship, and it is a sort of </p>
<p>attention which I conceive myself peculiarly bound to pay." </p>
<p>"You judge very properly," said Mr. Bennet, "and it is </p>
<p>happy for you that you possess the talent of flattering with </p>
<p>delicacy. May I ask whether these pleasing attentions proceed </p>
<p>from the impulse of the moment, or are the result of previous </p>
<p>study?" </p>
<p>"They arise chiefly from what is passing at the time, and </p>
<p>though I sometimes amuse myself with suggesting and </p>
<p>arranging such little elegant compliments as may be adapted </p>
<p>to ordinary occasions, I always wish to give them as unstudied </p>
<p>an air as possible." </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet's expectations were fully answered. His cousin </p>
<p>was as absurd as he had hoped, and he listened to him with the </p>
<p>keenest enjoyment, maintaining at the same time the most </p>
<p>resolute composure of countenance, and, except in an </p>
<p>occasional glance at Elizabeth, requiring no partner in his </p>
<p>pleasure. </p>
<p>By tea-time, however, the dose had been enough, and Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet was glad to take his guest into the drawing-room </p>
<p>again, and, when tea was over, glad to invite him to read </p>
<p>aloud to the ladies. Mr. Collins readily assented, and a book </p>
<p>was produced; but, on beholding it (for everything announced </p>
<p>it to be from a circulating library), he started back, and </p>
<p>begging pardon, protested that he never read novels. Kitty </p>
<p>stared at him, and Lydia exclaimed. Other books were </p>
<p>produced, and after some deliberation he chose Fordyce's </p>
<p>Sermons. Lydia gaped as he opened the volume, and before he </p>
<p>had, with very monotonous solemnity, read three pages, she </p>
<p>interrupted him with: </p>
<p>"Do you know, mamma, that my uncle Phillips talks of </p>
<p>turning away Richard; and if he does, Colonel Forster will </p>
<p>hire him. My aunt told me so herself on Saturday. I shall walk </p>
<p>to Meryton to-morrow to hear more about it, and to ask when </p>
<p>Mr. Denny comes back from town." </p>
<p>Lydia was bid by her two eldest sisters to hold her tongue; </p>
<p>but Mr. Collins, much offended, laid aside his book, and said: </p>
<p>"I have often observed how little young ladies are </p>
<p>interested by books of a serious stamp, though written solely </p>
<p>for their benefit. It amazes me, I confess; for, certainly, there </p>
<p>can be nothing so advantageous to them as instruction. But I </p>
<p>will no longer importune my young cousin." </p>
<p>Then turning to Mr. Bennet, he offered himself as his </p>
<p>antagonist at backgammon. Mr. Bennet accepted the </p>
<p>challenge, observing that he acted very wisely in leaving the </p>
<p>girls to their own trifling amusements. Mrs. Bennet and her </p>
<p>daughters apologised most civilly for Lydia's interruption, and </p>
<p>promised that it should not occur again, if he would resume </p>
<p>his book; but Mr. Collins, after assuring them that he bore his </p>
<p>young cousin no ill-will, and should never resent her </p>
<p>behaviour as any affront, seated himself at another table with </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet, and prepared for backgammon. </p>
<heading>Chapter 15. </heading>
<p>Mr. Collins was not a sensible man, and the deficiency of </p>
<p>nature had been but little assisted by education or society; the </p>
<p>greatest part of his life having been spent under the guidance </p>
<p>of an illiterate and miserly father; and though he belonged to </p>
<p>one of the universities, he had merely kept the necessary </p>
<p>terms, without forming at it any useful acquaintance. The </p>
<p>subjection in which his father had brought him up had given </p>
<p>him originally great humility of manner; but it was now a </p>
<p>good deal counteracted by the self-conceit of a weak head, </p>
<p>living in retirement, and the consequential feelings of early </p>
<p>and unexpected prosperity. A fortunate chance had </p>
<p>recommended him to Lady Catherine de Bourgh when the </p>
<p>living of Hunsford was vacant; and the respect which he felt </p>
<p>for her high rank, </p>
<p>and his veneration for her as his patroness, mingling with a </p>
<p>very good opinion of himself, of his authority as a clergyman, </p>
<p>and his right as a rector, made him altogether a mixture of </p>
<p>pride and obsequiousness, self-importance and humility. </p>
<p>Having now a good house and a very sufficient income, he </p>
<p>intended to marry; and in seeking a reconciliation with the </p>
<p>Longbourn family he had a wife in view, as he meant to </p>
<p>choose one of the daughters, if he found them as handsome </p>
<p>and amiable as they were represented by common report. This </p>
<p>was his plan of amends--of atonement--for inheriting their </p>
<p>father's estate; and he thought it an excellent one, full of </p>
<p>eligibility and suitableness, and excessively generous and </p>
<p>disinterested on his own part. </p>
<p>His plan did not vary on seeing them. Miss Bennet's lovely </p>
<p>face confirmed his views, and established all his strictest </p>
<p>notions of what was due to seniority; and for the first evening </p>
<p>SHE was his settled choice. The next morning, however, </p>
<p>made an alteration; for in a quarter of an hour's tete-a-tete </p>
<p>with Mrs. Bennet before breakfast, a conversation beginning </p>
<p>with his parsonage-house, and leading naturally to the avowal </p>
<p>of his hopes, that a mistress might be found for it at </p>
<p>Longbourn, produced from her, amid very complaisant smiles </p>
<p>and general encouragement, a caution against the very Jane he </p>
<p>had fixed on. "As to her YOUNGER daughters, she could not </p>
<p>take upon her to say--she could not positively answer--but she </p>
<p>did not KNOW of any prepossession; her ELDEST daughter, </p>
<p>she must just mention--she felt it incumbent on her to hint, </p>
<p>was likely to be very soon engaged." </p>
<p>Mr. Collins had only to change from Jane to Elizabeth-and </p>
<p>it was soon done--done while Mrs. Bennet was stirring the </p>
<p>fire. Elizabeth, equally next to Jane in birth and beauty, </p>
<p>succeeded her of course. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet treasured up the hint, and trusted that she </p>
<p>might soon have two daughters married; and the man whom </p>
<p>she could not bear to speak of the day before was now high in </p>
<p>her good graces. </p>
<p>Lydia's intention of walking to Meryton was not forgotten; </p>
<p>every sister except Mary agreed to go with her; and Mr. </p>
<p>Collins was to attend them, at the request of Mr. Bennet, who </p>
<p>was most anxious to get rid of him, and have his library to </p>
<p>himself; for thither Mr. Collins had followed him after </p>
<p>breakfast; and there he would continue, nominally engaged </p>
<p>with one of the largest folios in the collection, but really </p>
<p>talking to Mr. Bennet, with little cessation, of his house and </p>
<p>garden at Hunsford. Such doings discomposed Mr. Bennet </p>
<p>exceedingly. In his library he had been always sure of leisure </p>
<p>and tranquillity; and though prepared, as he told Elizabeth, to </p>
<p>meet with folly and conceit in every other room of the house, </p>
<p>he was used to be free from them there; his civility, therefore, </p>
<p>was most prompt in inviting Mr. Collins to join his daughters </p>
<p>in their walk; and Mr. Collins, being in fact much better fitted </p>
<p>for a walker than a reader, was extremely pleased to close his </p>
<p>large book, and go. </p>
<p>In pompous nothings on his side, and civil assents on that </p>
<p>of his cousins, their time passed till they entered Meryton. The </p>
<p>attention of the younger ones was then no longer to be gained </p>
<p>by him. Their eyes were immediately wandering up in the </p>
<p>street in quest of the officers, and nothing less than a very </p>
<p>smart bonnet indeed, or a really new muslin in a shop </p>
<p>window, could recall them. </p>
<p>But the attention of every lady was soon caught by a young </p>
<p>man, whom they </p>
<p>had never seen before, of most </p>
<p>gentlemanlike appearance, walking with another officer on the </p>
<p>other side of the way. The officer was the very Mr. Denny </p>
<p>concerning whose return from London Lydia came to inquire, </p>
<p>and he bowed as they passed. All were struck with the </p>
<p>stranger's air, all wondered who he could be; and Kitty and </p>
<p>Lydia, determined if possible to find out, led the way across </p>
<p>the street, under pretense of wanting something in an opposite </p>
<p>shop, and fortunately had just gained the pavement when the </p>
<p>two gentlemen, turning back, had reached the same spot. Mr. </p>
<p>Denny addressed them directly, and entreated permission to </p>
<p>introduce his friend, Mr. Wickham, who had returned with </p>
<p>him the day before from town, and he was happy to say had </p>
<p>accepted a commission in their corps. This was exactly as it </p>
<p>should be; for the young man wanted only regimentals to </p>
<p>make him completely charming. His appearance was greatly </p>
<p>in his favour; he had all the best part of beauty, a fine </p>
<p>countenance, a good figure, and very pleasing address. The </p>
<p>introduction was followed up on his side by a happy readiness </p>
<p>of conversation--a readiness at the same time perfectly correct </p>
<p>and unassuming; and the whole party were still standing and </p>
<p>talking together very agreeably, when the sound of horses </p>
<p>drew their notice, and Darcy and Bingley were seen riding </p>
<p>down the street. On distinguishing the ladies of the group, the </p>
<p>two gentlemen came directly towards them, and began the </p>
<p>usual civilities. Bingley was the principal spokesman, and </p>
<p>Miss Bennet the principal object. He was then, he said, on his </p>
<p>way to Longbourn on purpose to inquire after her. Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>corroborated it with a bow, and was beginning to determine </p>
<p>not to fix his eyes on Elizabeth, when they were suddenly </p>
<p>arrested by the sight of the stranger, and Elizabeth happening </p>
<p>to see the countenance of both as they looked at each other, </p>
<p>was all astonishment at the effect of the meeting. Both </p>
<p>changed colour, one looked white, the other red. Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham, after a few moments, touched his hat--a salutation </p>
<p>which Mr. Darcy just deigned to return. What could be the </p>
<p>meaning of it? It was impossible to imagine; it was impossible </p>
<p>not to long to know. </p>
<p>In another minute, Mr. Bingley, but without seeming to </p>
<p>have noticed what passed, took leave and rode on with his </p>
<p>friend. </p>
<p>Mr. Denny and Mr. Wickham walked with the young </p>
<p>ladies to the door of Mr. Phillip's house, and then made their </p>
<p>bows, in spite of Miss Lydia's pressing entreaties that they </p>
<p>should come in, and even in spite of Mrs. Phillips's throwing </p>
<p>up the parlour window and loudly seconding the invitation. </p>
<p>Mrs. Phillips was always glad to see her nieces; and the </p>
<p>two eldest, from their recent absence, were </p>
<p>particularly </p>
<p>welcome, and she was eagerly expressing her surprise at their </p>
<p>sudden return home, which, as their own carriage had not </p>
<p>fetched them, she should have known nothing about, if she </p>
<p>had not happened to see Mr. Jones's shop-boy in the street, </p>
<p>who had told her that they were not to send any more draughts </p>
<p>to Netherfield because the Miss Bennets were come away, </p>
<p>when her civility was claimed towards Mr. Collins by Jane's </p>
<p>introduction of him. She received him with her very best </p>
<p>politeness, which he returned with as much more, apologising </p>
<p>for his intrusion, without any previous acquaintance with her, </p>
<p>which he could not help flattering himself, however, might be </p>
<p>justified by his relationship to the young ladies who </p>
<p>introduced him to her notice. Mrs. Phillips was quite awed by </p>
<p>such an excess of good breeding; but her contemplation of one </p>
<p>stranger was soon put to an end by exclamations and inquiries </p>
<p>about the other; of whom, however, she could only tell her </p>
<p>nieces what they already knew, that Mr. Denny had brought </p>
<p>him from London, and that he was to have a lieutenant's </p>
<p>commission in the ---shire. She had been watching him the </p>
<p>last hour, she said, as he walked up and down the street, and </p>
<p>had Mr. Wickham appeared, Kitty and Lydia would certainly </p>
<p>have continued the occupation, but unluckily no one passed </p>
<p>windows now except a few of the officers, who, in </p>
<p>comparison with the stranger, were become "stupid, </p>
<p>disagreeable fellows." Some of them were to dine with the </p>
<p>Phillipses the next day, and their aunt promised to make her </p>
<p>husband call on Mr. Wickham, and give him an invitation </p>
<p>also, if the family from Longbourn would come in the </p>
<p>evening. This was agreed to, and Mrs. Phillips protested that </p>
<p>they would have a nice comfortable noisy game of lottery </p>
<p>tickets, and a little bit of hot supper afterwards. The prospect </p>
<p>of such delights was very cheering, and they parted in mutual </p>
<p>good spirits. Mr. Collins repeated his apologies in quitting the </p>
<p>room, and was assured with unwearying civility that they </p>
<p>were perfectly needless. </p>
<p>As they walked home, Elizabeth related to Jane what she </p>
<p>had seen pass between the two gentlemen; but though Jane </p>
<p>would have defended either or both, had they appeared to be </p>
<p>in the wrong, she could no more explain such behaviour than </p>
<p>her sister. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins on his return highly gratified Mrs. Bennet by </p>
<p>admiring Mrs. Phillips's manners and politeness. He protested </p>
<p>that, except Lady Catherine and her daughter, he had never </p>
<p>seen a more elegant woman; for she had not only received </p>
<p>him with the utmost civility, but even pointedly included him </p>
<p>in her invitation for the next evening, although utterly </p>
<p>unknown to her before. Something, he supposed, might be </p>
<p>attributed to his connection with them, but yet he had never </p>
<p>met with so much attention in the whole course of his life. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 16. </heading>
<p>As no objection was made to the young people's </p>
<p>engagement with their aunt, and all Mr. Collins's scruples of </p>
<p>leaving Mr. and Mrs. Bennet for a single evening during his </p>
<p>visit were most steadily resisted, the coach conveyed him and </p>
<p>his five cousins at a suitable hour to Meryton; and the girls </p>
<p>had the pleasure of hearing, as they entered the drawing-room, </p>
<p>that Mr. Wickham had accepted their uncle's invitation, and </p>
<p>was then in the house. </p>
<p>When this information was given, and they had all taken </p>
<p>their seats, Mr. Collins was at leisure to look around him and </p>
<p>admire, and he was so much struck with the size and furniture </p>
<p>of the apartment, that he declared he might almost have </p>
<p>supposed himself in the small summer breakfast parlour at </p>
<p>Rosings; a comparison that did not at first convey much </p>
<p>gratification; but when Mrs. Phillips understood from him </p>
<p>what Rosings was, and who was its proprietor--when she had </p>
<p>listened to the description of only one of Lady Catherine's </p>
<p>drawing-rooms, and found that the chimney-piece alone had </p>
<p>cost eight hundred pounds, she felt all the force of the </p>
<p>compliment, and would hardly have resented a comparison </p>
<p>with the housekeeper's room. </p>
<p>In describing to her all the grandeur of Lady Catherine and </p>
<p>her mansion, with occasional digressions in praise of his own </p>
<p>humble abode, and the improvements it was receiving, he was </p>
<p>happily employed until the gentlemen joined them; and he </p>
<p>found in Mrs. Phillips a very attentive listener, whose opinion </p>
<p>of his consequence increased with what she heard, and who </p>
<p>was resolving to retail it all among her neighbours as soon as </p>
<p>she could. To the girls, who could not listen to their cousin, </p>
<p>and who had nothing to do but to wish for an instrument, and </p>
<p>examine their own indifferent imitations of china on the </p>
<p>mantelpiece, the interval of waiting appeared very long. It was </p>
<p>over at last, however. The gentlemen did approach, and when </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham walked into the room, Elizabeth felt that she </p>
<p>had neither been seeing him before, nor thinking of him since, </p>
<p>with the smallest degree of unreasonable admiration. The </p>
<p>officers of the ----shire were in general a very creditable, </p>
<p>gentlemanlike set, and the best of them were of the present </p>
<p>party; but Mr. Wickham was as far beyond them all in person, </p>
<p>countenance, air, and walk, as THEY were superior to the </p>
<p>broad-faced, stuffy uncle Phillips, breathing port wine, who </p>
<p>followed them into the room. </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham was the happy man towards whom almost </p>
<p>every female eye was turned, and Elizabeth was the happy </p>
<p>woman by whom he finally seated himself; and the agreeable </p>
<p>manner in which he immediately fell into conversation, </p>
<p>though it was only on its being a wet night, made her feel that </p>
<p>the commonest, dullest, most threadbare topic might be </p>
<p>rendered interesting by the skill of the speaker. </p>
<p>With such rivals for the notice of the fair as Mr. Wickham </p>
<p>and the officers, Mr. Collins seemed to sink into </p>
<p>insignificance; to the young ladies he certainly was nothing; </p>
<p>but he had still at intervals a kind listener in Mrs. Phillips, and </p>
<p>was by her watchfulness, most abundantly supplied with </p>
<p>coffee and muffin. When the card-tables were placed, he had </p>
<p>the opportunity of obliging her in turn, by sitting down to </p>
<p>whist. </p>
<p>"I know little of the game at present," said he, "but I shall </p>
<p>be glad to improve myself, for in my situation in life--" Mrs. </p>
<p>Phillips was very glad for his compliance, but could not wait </p>
<p>for his reason. </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham did not play at whist, and with ready delight </p>
<p>was he received at the other table between Elizabeth and </p>
<p>Lydia. At first there seemed danger of Lydia's engrossing him </p>
<p>entirely, for she was a most determined talker; but being </p>
<p>likewise extremely fond of lottery tickets, she soon grew too </p>
<p>much interested in the game, too eager in making bets and </p>
<p>exclaiming after prizes to have attention for anyone in </p>
<p>particular. Allowing for the common demands of the game, </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham was therefore at leisure to talk to Elizabeth, and </p>
<p>she was very willing to hear him, though what she chiefly </p>
<p>wished to hear she could not hope to be told--the history of </p>
<p>his acquaintance with Mr. Darcy. She dared not even mention </p>
<p>that gentleman. Her curiosity, however, was unexpectedly </p>
<p>relieved. Mr. Wickham began the subject himself. He inquired </p>
<p>how far Netherfield was from Meryton; and, after receiving </p>
<p>her answer, asked in a hesitating manner how long Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>had been staying there. </p>
<p>"About a month," said Elizabeth; and then, unwilling to let </p>
<p>the subject drop, added, "He is a man of very large property in </p>
<p>Derbyshire, I understand." </p>
<p>"Yes," replied Mr. Wickham; "his estate there is a noble </p>
<p>one. A clear ten thousand per annum. You could not have met </p>
<p>with a person more capable of giving you certain information </p>
<p>on that head than myself, for I have been connected with his </p>
<p>family in a particular manner from my infancy." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not but look surprised. </p>
<p>"You may well be surprised, Miss Bennet, at such an </p>
<p>assertion, after seeing, as you probably might, the very cold </p>
<p>manner of our meeting yesterday. Are you much acquainted </p>
<p>with Mr. Darcy?" </p>
<p>"As much as I ever wish to be," cried Elizabeth very </p>
<p>warmly. "I have spent four days in the same house with him, </p>
<p>and I think him very disagreeable." </p>
<p>"I have no right to give MY opinion," said Wickham, "as </p>
<p>to his being agreeable or otherwise. I am not qualified to form </p>
<p>one. I have known him too long and too well to be a fair </p>
<p>judge. It is impossible for ME to be impartial. But I believe </p>
<p>your opinion of him would in general astonish--and perhaps </p>
<p>you would not express it quite so strongly anywhere else. </p>
<p>Here you are in your own family." </p>
<p>"Upon my word, I say no more HERE than I might say in </p>
<p>any house in the neighbourhood, except Netherfield. He is not </p>
<p>at all liked in Hertfordshire. Everybody is disgusted with his </p>
<p>pride. You will not find him more favourably spoken of by </p>
<p>anyone." </p>
<p>"I cannot pretend to be sorry," said Wickham, after a short </p>
<p>interruption, "that he or that any man should not be estimated </p>
<p>beyond their deserts; but with HIM I believe it does not often </p>
<p>happen. The world is blinded by his fortune and consequence, </p>
<p>or frightened by his high and imposing manners, and sees him </p>
<p>only as he chooses to be seen." </p>
<p>"I should take him, even on MY slight acquaintance, to be </p>
<p>an ill-tempered man." Wickham only shook his head. </p>
<p>"I wonder," said he, at the next opportunity of speaking, </p>
<p>"whether he is likely to be in this country much longer." </p>
<p>"I do not at all know; but I HEARD nothing of his going </p>
<p>away when I was at Netherfield. I hope your plans in favour </p>
<p>of the ----shire will not be affected by his being in the </p>
<p>neighbourhood." </p>
<p>"Oh! no--it is not for ME to be driven away by Mr. Darcy. </p>
<p>If HE wishes to avoid seeing ME, he must go. We are not on </p>
<p>friendly terms, and it always gives me pain to meet him, but I </p>
<p>have no reason for avoiding HIM but what I might proclaim </p>
<p>before all the world, a sense of very great ill-usage, and most </p>
<p>painful regrets at his being what he is. His father, Miss </p>
<p>Bennet, the late Mr. Darcy, was one of the best men that ever </p>
<p>breathed, and the truest friend I ever had; and I can never be </p>
<p>in company with this Mr. Darcy without being grieved to the </p>
<p>soul by a thousand tender recollections. His behaviour to </p>
<p>myself has been scandalous; but I verily believe I could </p>
<p>forgive him anything and everything, rather than his </p>
<p>disappointing the hopes and disgracing the memory of his </p>
<p>father." </p>
<p>Elizabeth found the interest of the subject increase, and </p>
<p>listened with all her heart; but the delicacy of it prevented </p>
<p>further inquiry. </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham began to speak on more general topics, </p>
<p>Meryton, the neighbourhood, the society, appearing highly </p>
<p>pleased with all that he had yet seen, and speaking of the latter </p>
<p>with gentle but very intelligible gallantry. </p>
<p>"It was the prospect of constant society, and good society," </p>
<p>he added, "which was my chief inducement to enter the --- </p>
<p>shire. I knew it to be a most respectable, agreeable corps, and </p>
<p>my friend Denny tempted me further by his account of their </p>
<p>present quarters, and the very great attentions and excellent </p>
<p>acquaintances Meryton had procured them. Society, I own, is </p>
<p>necessary to me. I have been a disappointed man, and my </p>
<p>spirits will not bear solitude. I MUST have employment and </p>
<p>society. A military life is not what I was intended for, but </p>
<p>circumstances have now made it eligible. The church OUGHT </p>
<p>to have been my profession--I was brought up for the church, </p>
<p>and I should at this time have been in possession of a most </p>
<p>valuable living, had it pleased the gentleman we were </p>
<p>speaking of just now." </p>
<p>"Indeed!" </p>
<p>"Yes--the late Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>bequeathed me the next </p>
<p>presentation of the best living in his gift. He was my </p>
<p>godfather, and excessively attached to me. I cannot do justice </p>
<p>to his kindness. He meant to provide for me amply, and </p>
<p>thought he had done it; but when the living fell, it was given </p>
<p>elsewhere." </p>
<p>"Good heavens!" cried Elizabeth; "but how could THAT </p>
<p>be? How could his will be disregarded? Why did you not seek </p>
<p>legal redress?" </p>
<p>"There was just such an informality in the terms of the </p>
<p>bequest as to give me no hope from law. A man of honour </p>
<p>could not have doubted the intention, but Mr. Darcy chose to </p>
<p>doubt </p>
<p>it--or </p>
<p>to treat it as a merely conditional </p>
<p>recommendation, and to assert that I had forfeited all claim to </p>
<p>it by extravagance, imprudence--in short anything or nothing. </p>
<p>Certain it is, that the living became vacant two years ago, </p>
<p>exactly as I was of an age to hold it, and that it was given to </p>
<p>another man; and no less certain is it, that I cannot accuse </p>
<p>myself of having really done anything to deserve to lose it. I </p>
<p>have a warm, unguarded temper, and I may have spoken my </p>
<p>opinion OF him, and TO him, too freely. I can recall nothing </p>
<p>worse. But the fact is, that we are very different sort of men, </p>
<p>and that he hates me." </p>
<p>"This is quite shocking! He deserves to be publicly </p>
<p>disgraced." </p>
<p>"Some time or other he WILL be--but it shall not be by </p>
<p>ME. Till I can forget his father, I can never defy or expose </p>
<p>HIM." </p>
<p>Elizabeth honoured him for such feelings, and thought him </p>
<p>handsomer than ever as he expressed them. </p>
<p>"But what," said she, after a pause, "can have been his </p>
<p>motive? What can have induced him to behave so cruelly?" </p>
<p>"A thorough, determined dislike of me--a dislike which I </p>
<p>cannot but attribute in some measure to jealousy. Had the late </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy liked me less, his son might have borne with me </p>
<p>better; but his father's uncommon attachment to me irritated </p>
<p>him, I believe, very early in life. He had not a temper to bear </p>
<p>the sort of competition in which we stood--the sort of </p>
<p>preference which was often given me." </p>
<p>"I had not thought Mr. Darcy so bad as this--though I have </p>
<p>never liked him. I had not thought so very ill of him. I had </p>
<p>supposed him to be despising his fellow-creatures in general, </p>
<p>but did not suspect him of descending to such malicious </p>
<p>revenge, such injustice, such inhumanity as this." </p>
<p>After a few minutes' reflection, however, she continued, "I </p>
<p>DO remember his boasting one day, at Netherfield, of the </p>
<p>implacability of his resentments, of his having an unforgiving </p>
<p>temper. His disposition must be dreadful." </p>
<p>"I will not trust myself on the subject," replied Wickham; </p>
<p>"I can hardly be just to him." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was again deep in thought, and after a time </p>
<p>exclaimed, "To treat in such a manner the godson, the friend, </p>
<p>the favourite of his father!" She could have added, "A young </p>
<p>man, too, like YOU, whose very countenance may vouch for </p>
<p>your being amiable"--but she contented herself with, "and </p>
<p>one, too, who had probably been his companion from </p>
<p>childhood, connected together, as I think you said, in the </p>
<p>closest manner!" </p>
<p>"We were born in the same parish, within the same park; </p>
<p>the greatest part of our youth was passed together; inmates of </p>
<p>the same house, sharing the same amusements, objects of the </p>
<p>same parental care. MY father began life in the profession </p>
<p>which your uncle, Mr. Phillips, appears to do so much credit </p>
<p>to--but he gave up everything to be of use to the late Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy and devoted all his time to the care of the Pemberley </p>
<p>property. He was most highly esteemed by Mr. Darcy, a most </p>
<p>intimate, confidential friend. Mr. Darcy often acknowledged </p>
<p>himself to be under the greatest obligations to my father's </p>
<p>active superintendence, and when, immediately before my </p>
<p>father's death, Mr. Darcy gave him a voluntary promise of </p>
<p>providing for me, I am convinced that he felt it to be as much </p>
<p>a debt of gratitude to HIM, as of his affection to myself." </p>
<p>"How strange!" cried Elizabeth. "How abominable! I </p>
<p>wonder that the very pride of this Mr. Darcy has not made </p>
<p>him just to you! If from no better motive, that he should not </p>
<p>have been too proud to be dishonest--for dishonesty I must </p>
<p>call it." </p>
<p>"It IS wonderful," replied Wickham, "for almost all his </p>
<p>actions may be traced to pride; and pride had often been his </p>
<p>best friend. It has connected him nearer with virtue than with </p>
<p>any other feeling. But we are none of us consistent, and in his </p>
<p>behaviour to me there were stronger impulses even than </p>
<p>pride." </p>
<p>"Can such abominable pride as his have ever done him </p>
<p>good?" </p>
<p>"Yes. It has often led him to be liberal and generous, to </p>
<p>give his money freely, to display hospitality, to assist his </p>
<p>tenants, and relieve the poor. Family pride, and FILIAL pride- </p>
<p>-for he is very proud of what his father was--have done this. </p>
<p>Not to appear to disgrace his family, to degenerate from the </p>
<p>popular qualities, or lose the influence of the Pemberley </p>
<p>House, is a powerful motive. He has also BROTHERLY </p>
<p>pride, which, with SOME brotherly affection, makes him a </p>
<p>very kind and careful guardian of his sister, and you will hear </p>
<p>him generally cried up as the most attentive and best of </p>
<p>brothers." </p>
<p>"What sort of girl is Miss Darcy?" </p>
<p>He shook his head. "I wish I could call her amiable. It </p>
