<p>gives me pain to speak ill of a Darcy. But she is too much like </p>
<p>her brother--very, very proud. As a child, she was affectionate </p>
<p>and pleasing, and extremely fond of me; and I have devoted </p>
<p>hours and hours to her amusement. But she is nothing to me </p>
<p>now. She is a handsome girl, about fifteen or sixteen, and, I </p>
<p>understand, highly accomplished. Since her father's death, her </p>
<p>home has been London, where a lady lives with her, and </p>
<p>superintends her education." </p>
<p>After many pauses and many trials of other subjects, </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not help reverting once more to the first, and </p>
<p>saying: </p>
<p>"I am astonished at his intimacy with Mr. Bingley! How </p>
<p>can Mr. Bingley, who seems good humour itself, and is, I </p>
<p>really believe, truly amiable, be in friendship with such a </p>
<p>man? How can they suit each other? Do you know Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley?" </p>
<p>"Not at all." </p>
<p>"He is a sweet-tempered, amiable, charming man. He </p>
<p>cannot know what Mr. Darcy is." </p>
<p>"Probably not; but Mr. Darcy can please where he chooses. </p>
<p>He does not want abilities. He can be a conversible </p>
<p>companion if he thinks it worth his while. Among those who </p>
<p>are at all his equals in consequence, he is a very different man </p>
<p>from what he is to the less prosperous. His pride never deserts </p>
<p>him; but with the rich he is liberal-minded, just, sincere, </p>
<p>rational, honourable, and perhaps </p>
<p>agreeable--allowing </p>
<p>something for fortune and figure." </p>
<p>The whist party soon afterwards breaking up, the players </p>
<p>gathered round the other table and Mr. Collins took his station </p>
<p>between his cousin Elizabeth and Mrs. Phillips. The usual </p>
<p>inquiries as to his success was made by the latter. It had not </p>
<p>been very great; he had lost every point; but when Mrs. </p>
<p>Phillips began to express her concern thereupon, he assured </p>
<p>her with much earnest gravity that it was not of the least </p>
<p>importance, that he considered the money as a mere trifle, and </p>
<p>begged that she would not make herself uneasy. </p>
<p>"I know very well, madam," said he, "that when persons sit </p>
<p>down to a card-table, they must take their chances of these </p>
<p>things, and happily I am not in such circumstances as to make </p>
<p>five shillings any object. There are undoubtedly many who </p>
<p>could not say the same, but thanks to Lady Catherine de </p>
<p>Bourgh, I am removed far beyond the necessity of regarding </p>
<p>little matters." </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham's attention was caught; and after observing </p>
<p>Mr. Collins for a few moments, he asked Elizabeth in a low </p>
<p>voice whether her relation was very intimately acquainted </p>
<p>with the family of de Bourgh. </p>
<p>"Lady Catherine de Bourgh," she replied, "has very lately </p>
<p>given him a living. I hardly know how Mr. Collins was first </p>
<p>introduced to her notice, but he certainly has not known her </p>
<p>long." </p>
<p>"You know of course that Lady Catherine de Bourgh and </p>
<p>Lady Anne Darcy were sisters; consequently that she is aunt </p>
<p>to the present Mr. Darcy." </p>
<p>"No, indeed, I did not. I knew nothing at all of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine's connections. I never heard of her existence till the </p>
<p>day before yesterday." </p>
<p>"Her daughter, Miss de Bourgh, will have a very large </p>
<p>fortune, and it is believed that she and her cousin will unite </p>
<p>the two estates." </p>
<p>This information made Elizabeth smile, as she thought of </p>
<p>poor Miss Bingley. Vain indeed must be all her attentions, </p>
<p>vain and useless her affection for his sister and her praise of </p>
<p>himself, if he were already self-destined for another. </p>
<p>"Mr. Collins," said she, "speaks highly both of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine and her daughter; but from some particulars that he </p>
<p>has related of her ladyship, I suspect his gratitude misleads </p>
<p>him, and that in spite of her being his patroness, she is an </p>
<p>arrogant, conceited woman." </p>
<p>"I believe her to be both in a great degree," replied </p>
<p>Wickham; "I have not seen her for many years, but I very well </p>
<p>remember that I never liked her, and that her manners were </p>
<p>dictatorial and insolent. She has the reputation of being </p>
<p>remarkably sensible and clever; but I rather believe she </p>
<p>derives part of her abilities from her rank and fortune, part </p>
<p>from her authoritative manner, and the rest from the pride for </p>
<p>her nephew, who chooses that everyone connected with him </p>
<p>should have an understanding of the first class." </p>
<p>Elizabeth allowed that he had given a very rational account </p>
<p>of it, and they continued talking together, with mutual </p>
<p>satisfaction till supper put an end to cards, and gave the rest of </p>
<p>the ladies their share of Mr. Wickham's attentions. There </p>
<p>could be no conversation in the noise of Mrs. Phillips's supper </p>
<p>party, but his manners recommended him to everybody. </p>
<p>Whatever he said, was said well; and whatever he did, done </p>
<p>gracefully. Elizabeth went away with her head full of him. </p>
<p>She could think of nothing but of Mr. Wickham, and of what </p>
<p>he had told her, all the way home; but there was not time for </p>
<p>her even to mention his name as they went, for neither Lydia </p>
<p>nor Mr. Collins were once silent. Lydia talked incessantly of </p>
<p>lottery tickets, of the fish she had lost and the fish she had </p>
<p>won; and Mr. Collins in describing the civility of Mr. and </p>
<p>Mrs. Phillips, protesting that he did not in the least regard his </p>
<p>losses at whist, enumerating all the dishes at supper, and </p>
<p>repeatedly fearing that he crowded his cousins, had more to </p>
<p>say than he could well man age before the carriage stopped at </p>
<p>Longbourn House. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 17. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth related to Jane the next day what had passed </p>
<p>between Mr. Wickham and herself. Jane listened with </p>
<p>astonishment and concern; she knew not how to believe that </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy could be so unworthy of Mr. Bingley's regard; and </p>
<p>yet, it was not in her nature to question the veracity of a young </p>
<p>man of such amiable appearance as Wickham. The possibility </p>
<p>of his having endured such unkindness, was enough to interest </p>
<p>all her tender feelings; and nothing remained therefore to be </p>
<p>done, but to think well of them both, to defend the conduct of </p>
<p>each, and throw into the account of accident or mistake </p>
<p>whatever could not be otherwise explained. </p>
<p>"They have both," said she, "been deceived, I dare say, in </p>
<p>some way or other, of which we can form no idea. Interested </p>
<p>people have perhaps misrepresented each to the other. It is, in </p>
<p>short, impossible for us to conjecture the causes or </p>
<p>circumstances which may have alienated them, without actual </p>
<p>blame on either side." </p>
<p>"Very true, indeed; and now, my dear Jane, what have you </p>
<p>got to say on behalf of the interested people who have </p>
<p>probably been concerned in the business? Do clear THEM </p>
<p>too, or we shall be obliged to think ill of somebody." </p>
<p>"Laugh as much as you choose, but you will not laugh me </p>
<p>out of my opinion. My dearest Lizzy, do but consider in what </p>
<p>a disgraceful light it places Mr. Darcy, to be treating his </p>
<p>father's favourite in such a manner, one whom his father had </p>
<p>promised to provide for. It is impossible. No man of common </p>
<p>humanity, no man who had any value for his character, could </p>
<p>be capable of it. Can his most intimate friends be so </p>
<p>excessively deceived in him? Oh! no." </p>
<p>"I can much more easily believe Mr. Bingley's being </p>
<p>imposed on, than that Mr. Wickham should invent such a </p>
<p>history of himself as he gave me last night; names, facts, </p>
<p>everything mentioned without ceremony. If it be not so, let </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy contradict it. Besides, there was truth in his looks." </p>
<p>"It is difficult indeed--it is distressing. One does not know </p>
<p>what to think." </p>
<p>"I beg your pardon; one knows exactly what to think." </p>
<p>But Jane could think with certainty on only one point-that </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley, if he HAD been imposed on, would have much </p>
<p>to suffer when the affair became public. </p>
<p>The two young ladies were summoned from the shrubbery, </p>
<p>where this conversation passed, by the arrival of the very </p>
<p>persons of whom they had been speaking; Mr. Bingley and his </p>
<p>sisters came to give their personal invitation for the long- </p>
<p>expected ball at Netherfield, which was fixed for the </p>
<p>following Tuesday. The two ladies were delighted to see their </p>
<p>dear friend again, called it an age since they had met, and </p>
<p>repeatedly asked what she had been doing with herself since </p>
<p>their separation. To the rest of the family they paid little </p>
<p>attention; avoiding Mrs. Bennet as much as possible, saying </p>
<p>not much to Elizabeth, and nothing at all to the others. They </p>
<p>were soon gone again, rising from their seats with an activity </p>
<p>which took their brother by surprise, and hurrying off as if </p>
<p>eager to escape from Mrs. Bennet's civilities. </p>
<p>The prospect of the Netherfield ball was extremely </p>
<p>agreeable to every female of the family. Mrs. Bennet chose to </p>
<p>consider it as given in compliment to her eldest daughter, and </p>
<p>was particularly flattered by receiving the invitation from Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley himself, instead of a ceremonious card. Jane pictured </p>
<p>to herself a happy evening in the society of her two friends, </p>
<p>and the attentions of her brother; and Elizabeth thought with </p>
<p>pleasure of dancing a great deal with Mr. Wickham, and of </p>
<p>seeing a confirmation of everything in Mr. Darcy's look and </p>
<p>behavior. The happiness anticipated by Catherine and Lydia </p>
<p>depended less on any single event, or any particular person, </p>
<p>for though they each, like Elizabeth, meant to dance half the </p>
<p>evening with Mr. Wickham, he was by no means the only </p>
<p>partner who could satisfy them, and a ball was, at any rate, a </p>
<p>ball. And even Mary could assure her family that she had no </p>
<p>disinclination for it. </p>
<p>"While I can have my mornings to myself," said she, "it is </p>
<p>enough--I think it is no sacrifice to join occasionally in </p>
<p>evening engagements. Society has claims on us all; and I </p>
<p>profess myself one of those who consider intervals of </p>
<p>recreation and amusement as desirable for everybody." </p>
<p>Elizabeth's spirits were so high on this occasion, that </p>
<p>though she did not often speak unnecessarily to Mr. Collins, </p>
<p>she could not help asking him whether he intended to accept </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley's invitation, and if he did, whether he would think </p>
<p>it proper to join in the evening's amusement; and she was </p>
<p>rather surprised to find that he entertained no scruple </p>
<p>whatever on that head, and was very far from dreading a </p>
<p>rebuke either from the Archbishop, or Lady Catherine de </p>
<p>Bourgh, by venturing to dance. </p>
<p>"I am by no means of the opinion, I assure you," said he, </p>
<p>"that a ball of this kind, given by a young man of character, to </p>
<p>respectable people, can have any evil tendency; and I am so </p>
<p>far from objecting to dancing myself, that I shall hope to be </p>
<p>honoured with the hands of all my fair cousins in the course of </p>
<p>the evening; and I take this opportunity of soliciting yours, </p>
<p>Miss Elizabeth, for the two first dances especially, a </p>
<p>preference which I trust my cousin Jane will attribute to the </p>
<p>right cause, and not to any disrespect for her." </p>
<p>Elizabeth felt herself completely taken in. She had fully </p>
<p>proposed being engaged by Mr. Wickham for those very </p>
<p>dances; and to have Mr. Collins instead! her liveliness had </p>
<p>never been worse timed. There was no help for it, however. </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham's happiness and her own were perforce delayed </p>
<p>a little longer, and Mr. Collins's proposal accepted with as </p>
<p>good a grace as she could. She was not the better pleased with </p>
<p>his gallantry from the idea it suggested of something more. It </p>
<p>now first struck her, that SHE was selected from among her </p>
<p>sisters as worthy of being mistress of Hunsford Parsonage, </p>
<p>and of assisting to form a quadrille table at Rosings, in the </p>
<p>absence of more eligible visitors. The idea soon reached to </p>
<p>conviction, as she observed his increasing civilities toward </p>
<p>herself, and heard his frequent attempt at a compliment on her </p>
<p>wit and vivacity; and though more astonished than gratified </p>
<p>herself by this effect of her charms, it was not long before her </p>
<p>mother gave her to understand that the probability of their </p>
<p>marriage was extremely agreeable to HER. Elizabeth, </p>
<p>however, did not choose to take the hint, being well aware </p>
<p>that a serious dispute must be the consequence of any reply. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins might never make the offer, and till he did, it was </p>
<p>useless to quarrel about him. </p>
<p>If there had not been a Netherfield ball to prepare for and </p>
<p>talk of, the younger Miss Bennets would have been in a very </p>
<p>pitiable state at this time, for from the day of the invitation, to </p>
<p>the day of the ball, there was such a succession of rain as </p>
<p>prevented their walking to Meryton once. No aunt, no </p>
<p>officers, no news could be sought after--the very shoe-roses </p>
<p>for Netherfield were got by proxy. Even Elizabeth might have </p>
<p>found some trial of her patience in weather which totally </p>
<p>suspended the improvement of her acquaintance with Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham; and nothing less than a dance on Tuesday, could </p>
<p>have made such a Friday, Saturday, Sunday, and Monday </p>
<p>endurable to Kitty and Lydia. </p>
<header>Ebd </header>
<header>E-BooksDirectory.com </header>
<heading>Chapter 18. </heading>
<p>Till Elizabeth entered the drawing-room at Netherfield, </p>
<p>and looked in vain for Mr. Wickham among the cluster of red </p>
<p>coats there assembled, a doubt of his being present had never </p>
<p>occurred to her. The certainty of meeting him had not been </p>
<p>checked by any of those recollections that might not </p>
<p>unreasonably have alarmed her. She had dressed with more </p>
<p>than usual care, and prepared in the highest spirits for the </p>
<p>conquest of all that remained unsubdued of his heart, trusting </p>
<p>that it was not more than might be won in the course of the </p>
<p>evening. But in an instant arose the dreadful suspicion of his </p>
<p>being purposely omitted for Mr. Darcy's pleasure in the </p>
<p>Bingleys' invitation to the officers; and though this was not </p>
<p>exactly the case, the absolute fact of his absence was </p>
<p>pronounced by his friend Denny, to whom Lydia eagerly </p>
<p>applied, and who told them that Wickham had been obliged to </p>
<p>go to town on business the day before, and was not yet </p>
<p>returned; adding, with a significant smile, "I do not imagine </p>
<p>his business would have called him away just now, if he had </p>
<p>not wanted to avoid a certain gentleman here." </p>
<p>This part of his intelligence, though unheard by Lydia, was </p>
<p>caught by Elizabeth, and, as it assured her that Darcy was not </p>
<p>less answerable for Wickham's absence than if her first </p>
<p>surmise had been just, every feeling of displeasure against the </p>
<p>former was so sharpened by immediate disappointment, that </p>
<p>she could hardly reply with tolerable civility to the polite in </p>
<p>quiries which he directly afterwards approached to make. </p>
<p>Attendance, forbearance, patience with Darcy, was injury to </p>
<p>Wickham. She was resolved against any sort of conversation </p>
<p>with him, and turned away with a degree of ill-humour which </p>
<p>she could not wholly surmount even in speaking to Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, whose blind partiality provoked her. </p>
<p>But Elizabeth was not formed for ill-humour; and though </p>
<p>every prospect of her own was destroyed for the evening, it </p>
<p>could not dwell long on her spirits; and having told all her </p>
<p>griefs to Charlotte Lucas, whom she had not seen for a week, </p>
<p>she was soon able to make a voluntary transition to the </p>
<p>oddities of her cousin, and to point him out to her particular </p>
<p>notice. The first two dances, however, brought a return of </p>
<p>distress; they were dances of mortification. Mr. Collins, </p>
<p>awkward and solemn, apologising instead of attending, and </p>
<p>often moving wrong without being aware of it, gave her all </p>
<p>the shame and misery which a disagreeable partner for a </p>
<p>couple of dances can give. The moment of her release from </p>
<p>him was ecstasy. </p>
<p>She danced next with an officer, and had the refreshment </p>
<p>of talking of Wickham, and of hearing that he was universally </p>
<p>liked. When those dances were over, she returned to Charlotte </p>
<p>Lucas, and was in conversation with her, when she found </p>
<p>herself suddenly addressed by Mr. Darcy who took her so </p>
<p>much by surprise in his application for her hand, that, without </p>
<p>knowing what she did, she accepted him. He walked away </p>
<p>again immediately, and she was left to fret over her own want </p>
<p>of presence of mind; Charlotte tried to console her: </p>
<p>"I dare say you will find him very agreeable." </p>
<p>"Heaven forbid! THAT would be the greatest misfortune </p>
<p>of all! To find a man agreeable whom one is determined to </p>
<p>hate! Do not wish me such an evil." </p>
<p>When the dancing recommenced, however, and Darcy </p>
<p>approached to claim her hand, Charlotte could not help </p>
<p>cautioning her in a whisper, not to be a simpleton, and allow </p>
<p>her fancy for Wickham to make her appear unpleasant in the </p>
<p>eyes of a man ten times his consequence. Elizabeth made no </p>
<p>answer, and took her place in the set, amazed at the dignity to </p>
<p>which she was arrived in being allowed to stand opposite to </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, and reading in her neighbours' looks, their equal </p>
<p>amazement in beholding it. They stood for some time without </p>
<p>speaking a word; and she began to imagine that their silence </p>
<p>was to last through the two dances, and at first was resolved </p>
<p>not to break it; till suddenly fancying that it would be the </p>
<p>greater punishment to her partner to oblige him to talk, she </p>
<p>made some slight observation on the dance. He replied, and </p>
<p>was again silent. After a pause of some minutes, she </p>
<p>addressed him a second time with:--"It is YOUR turn to say </p>
<p>something now, Mr. Darcy. I talked about the dance, and </p>
<p>YOU ought to make some sort of remark on the size of the </p>
<p>room, or the number of couples." </p>
<p>He smiled, and assured her that whatever she wished him </p>
<p>to say should be said. </p>
<p>"Very well. That reply will do for the present. Perhaps by </p>
<p>and by I may observe that private balls are much pleasanter </p>
<p>than public ones. But NOW we may be silent." </p>
<p>"Do you talk by rule, then, while you are dancing?" </p>
<p>"Sometimes. One must speak a little, you know. It would </p>
<p>look odd to be entirely silent for half an hour together; and yet </p>
<p>for the advantage of SOME, conversation ought to be so </p>
<p>arranged, as that they may have the trouble of saying as little </p>
<p>as possible." </p>
<p>"Are you consulting your own feelings in the present case, </p>
<p>or do you imagine that you are gratifying mine?" </p>
<p>"Both," replied Elizabeth archly; "for I have always seen a </p>
<p>great similarity in the turn of our minds. We are each of an </p>
<p>unsocial, taciturn disposition, unwilling to speak, unless we </p>
<p>expect to say something that will amaze the whole room, and </p>
<p>be handed down to posterity with all the eclat of a proverb." </p>
<p>"This is no very striking resemblance of your own </p>
<p>character, I am sure," said he. "How near it may be to MINE, </p>
<p>I cannot pretend to say. YOU think it a faithful portrait </p>
<p>undoubtedly." </p>
<p>"I must not decide on my own performance." </p>
<p>He made no answer, and they were again silent till they </p>
<p>had gone down the dance, when he asked her if she and her </p>
<p>sisters did not very often walk to Meryton. She answered in </p>
<p>the affirmative, and, unable to resist the temptation, added, </p>
<p>"When you met us there the other day, we had just been </p>
<p>forming a new acquaintance." </p>
<p>The effect was immediate. A deeper shade of hauteur </p>
<p>overspread his features, but he said not a word, and Elizabeth, </p>
<p>though blaming herself for her own weakness, could not go </p>
<p>on. At length Darcy spoke, and in a constrained manner said, </p>
<p>"Mr. Wickham is blessed with such happy manners as may </p>
<p>ensure his MAKING friends--whether he may be equally </p>
<p>capable of RETAINING them, is less certain." </p>
<p>"He has been so unlucky as to lose YOUR friendship," </p>
<p>replied Elizabeth with emphasis, "and in a manner which he is </p>
<p>likely to suffer from all his life." </p>
<p>Darcy made no answer, and seemed desirous of changing </p>
<p>the subject. At that moment, Sir William Lucas appeared </p>
<p>close to them, meaning to pass through the set to the other </p>
<p>side of the room; but on perceiving Mr. Darcy, he stopped </p>
<p>with a bow of superior courtesy to compliment him on his </p>
<p>dancing and his partner. </p>
<p>"I have been most highly gratified indeed, my dear sir. </p>
<p>Such very superior dancing is not often seen. It is evident that </p>
<p>you belong to the first circles. Allow me to say, however, that </p>
<p>your fair partner does not disgrace you, and that I must hope </p>
<p>to have this pleasure often repeated, especially when a certain </p>
<p>desirable event, my dear Eliza (glancing at her sister and </p>
<p>Bingley) shall take place. What congratulations will then flow </p>
<p>in! I appeal to Mr. Darcy:--but let me not interrupt you, sir. </p>
<p>You will not thank me for detaining you from the bewitching </p>
<p>converse of that young lady, whose bright eyes are also up </p>
<p>braiding me." </p>
<p>The latter part of this address was scarcely heard by Darcy; </p>
<p>but Sir William's allusion to his friend seemed to strike him </p>
<p>forcibly, and his eyes were directed with a very serious </p>
<p>expression towards Bingley and Jane, who were dancing </p>
<p>together. Recovering himself, however, shortly, he turned to </p>
<p>his partner, and said, "Sir William's interruption has made me </p>
<p>forget what we were talking of." </p>
<p>"I do not think we were speaking at all. Sir William could </p>
<p>not have interrupted two people in the room who had less to </p>
<p>say for themselves. We have tried two or three subjects </p>
<p>already without success, and what we are to talk of next I </p>
<p>cannot imagine." </p>
<p>"What think you of books?" said he, smiling. </p>
<p>"Books--oh! no. I am sure we never read the same, or not </p>
<p>with the same feelings." </p>
<p>"I am sorry you think so; but if that be the case, there can </p>
<p>at least be no want of subject. We may compare our different </p>
<p>opinions." </p>
<p>"No--I cannot talk of books in a ball-room; my head is </p>
<p>always full of something else." </p>
<p>"The PRESENT always occupies you in such scenes--does </p>
<p>it?" said he, with a look of doubt. </p>
<p>"Yes, always," she replied, without knowing what she said, </p>
<p>for her thoughts had wandered far from the subject, as soon </p>
<p>afterwards appeared by her suddenly exclaiming, "I remember </p>
<p>hearing you once say, Mr. Darcy, that you hardly ever </p>
<p>forgave, that you resentment once created was unappeasable. </p>
<p>You are very cautious, I suppose, as to its BEING </p>
<p>CREATED." </p>
<p>"I am," said he, with a firm voice. </p>
<p>"And never allow yourself to be blinded by prejudice?" </p>
<p>"I hope not." </p>
<p>"It is particularly incumbent on those who never change </p>
<p>their opinion, to be secure of judging properly at first." </p>
<p>"May I ask to what these questions tend?" </p>
<p>"Merely to the illustration of YOUR character," said she, </p>
<p>endeavouring to shake off her gravity. "I am trying to make it </p>
<p>out." </p>
<p>"And what is your success?" </p>
<p>She shook her head. "I do not get on at all. I hear such </p>
<p>different accounts of you as puzzle me exceedingly." </p>
<p>"I can readily believe," answered he gravely, "that reports </p>
<p>may vary greatly with respect to me; and I could wish, Miss </p>
<p>Bennet, that you were not to sketch my character at the </p>
<p>present moment, as there is reason to fear that the </p>
<p>performance would reflect no credit on either." </p>
<p>"But if I do not take your likeness now, I may never have </p>
<p>another opportunity." </p>
<p>"I would by no means suspend any pleasure of yours," he </p>
<p>coldly replied. She said no more, and they went down the </p>
<p>other dance and parted in silence; and on each side </p>
<p>dissatisfied, though not to an equal degree, for in Darcy's </p>
<p>breast there was a tolerable powerful feeling towards her, </p>
<p>which soon procured her pardon, and directed all his anger </p>
<p>against another. </p>
<p>They had not long separated, when Miss Bingley came </p>
<p>towards her, and with an expression of civil disdain accosted </p>
<p>her: </p>
<p>"So, Miss Eliza, I hear you are quite delighted with George </p>
<p>Wickham! Your sister has been talking to me about him, and </p>
<p>asking me a thousand questions; and I find that the young man </p>
<p>quite forgot to tell you, among his other communication, that </p>
<p>he was the son of old Wickham, the late Mr. Darcy's steward. </p>
<p>Let me recommend you, however, as a friend, not to give </p>
<p>implicit confidence to all his assertions; for as to Mr. Darcy's </p>
<p>using him ill, it is perfectly false; for, on the contrary, he has </p>
<p>always been remarkably kind </p>
<p>to </p>
<p>him, though </p>
<p>George </p>
<p>Wickham has treated Mr. Darcy in a most infamous manner. I </p>
<p>do not know the particulars, but I know very well that Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy is not in the least to blame, that he cannot bear to hear </p>
<p>George Wickham mentioned, and that though my brother </p>
<p>thought that he could not well avoid including him in his </p>
<p>invitation to the officers, he was excessively glad to find that </p>
<p>he had taken himself out of the way. His coming into the </p>
<p>country at all is a most insolent thing, indeed, and I wonder </p>
<p>how he could presume to do it. I pity you, Miss Eliza, for this </p>
<p>discovery of your favourite's guilt; but really, considering his </p>
<p>descent, one could not expect much better." </p>
<p>"His guilt and his descent appear by your account to be the </p>
<p>same," said Elizabeth angrily; "for I have heard you accuse </p>
<p>him of nothing worse than of being the son of Mr. Darcy's </p>
<p>steward, and of THAT, I can assure you, he informed me </p>
<p>himself." </p>
<p>"I beg your pardon," replied Miss Bingley, turning away </p>
<p>with a sneer. "Excuse my interference--it was kindly meant." </p>
<p>"Insolent girl!" said Elizabeth to herself. "You are much </p>
<p>mistaken if you expect to influence me by such a paltry attack </p>
<p>as this. I see nothing in it but your own wilful ignorance and </p>
<p>the malice of Mr. Darcy." She then sought her eldest sister, </p>
<p>who has undertaken to make inquiries on the same subject of </p>
<p>Bingley. Jane met her with a smile of such sweet </p>
<p>complacency, a glow of such happy expression, as sufficiently </p>
<p>marked how well she was satisfied with the occurrences of the </p>
<p>evening. Elizabeth instantly read her feelings, and at that </p>
<p>moment solicitude for Wickham, resentment against his </p>
<p>enemies, and everything else, gave way before the hope of </p>
<p>Jane's being in the fairest way for happiness. </p>
<p>"I want to know," said she, with a countenance no less </p>
<p>smiling than her sister's, "what you have learnt about Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham. But perhaps you have been too pleasantly engaged </p>
<p>to think of any third person; in which case you may be sure of </p>
<p>my pardon." </p>
<p>"No," replied Jane, "I have not forgotten him; but I have </p>
<p>nothing satisfactory to tell you. Mr. Bingley does not know </p>
<p>the whole of his history, and is quite ignorant of the circum </p>
<p>stances which have principally offended Mr. Darcy; but he </p>
<p>will vouch for the good conduct, the probity, and honour of </p>
<p>his friend, and is perfectly convinced that Mr. Wickham has </p>
<p>deserved much less attention from Mr. Darcy than he has </p>
<p>received; and I am sorry to say by his account as well as his </p>
<p>sister's, Mr. Wickham is by no means a respectable young </p>
<p>man. I am afraid he has been very imprudent, and has </p>
<p>deserved to lose Mr. Darcy's regard." </p>
<p>"Mr. Bingley does not know Mr. Wickham himself?" </p>
<p>"No; he never saw him till the other morning at Meryton." </p>
<p>"This account then is what he has received from Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy. I am satisfied. But what does he say of the living?" </p>
<p>"He does not exactly recollect the circumstances, though </p>
<p>he has heard them from Mr. Darcy more than once, but he </p>
<p>believes that it was left to him CONDITIONALLY only." </p>
<p>"I have not a doubt of Mr. Bingley's </p>
<p>sincerity," said </p>
<p>Elizabeth warmly; "but you must excuse my not being </p>
<p>convinced by assurances only. Mr. Bingley's defense of his </p>
<p>friend was a very able one, I dare say; but since he is </p>
<p>unacquainted with several parts of the story, and has learnt the </p>
<p>rest from that friend himself, I shall venture to still think of </p>
<p>both gentlemen as I did before." </p>
<p>She then changed the discourse to one more gratifying to </p>
<p>each, and on which there could be no difference of sentiment. </p>
<p>Elizabeth listened with delight to the happy, though modest </p>
<p>hopes which Jane entertained of Mr. Bingley's regard, and </p>
<p>said all in her power to heighten her confidence in it. On their </p>
<p>being joined by Mr. Bingley himself, Elizabeth withdrew to </p>
<p>Miss Lucas; to whose inquiry after the pleasantness of her last </p>
<p>partner she had scarcely replied, before Mr. Collins came up </p>
<p>to them, and told her with great exultation that he had just </p>
<p>been so fortunate as to make a most important discovery. </p>
<p>"I have found out," said he, "by a singular accident, that </p>
<p>there is now in the room a near relation of my patroness. I </p>
<p>happened to overhear the gentleman himself mentioning to the </p>
<p>young lady who does the honours of the house the names of </p>
<p>his cousin Miss de Bourgh, and of her mother Lady Catherine. </p>
<p>How wonderfully these sort of things occur! Who would have </p>
<p>thought of my meeting with, perhaps, a nephew of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine de Bourgh in this assembly! I am most thankful that </p>
<p>the discovery is made in time for me to pay my respects to </p>
<p>him, which I am now going to do, and trust he will excuse my </p>
<p>not having done it before. My total ignorance of the </p>
<p>connection must plead my apology." </p>
<p>"You are not going to introduce yourself to Mr. Darcy!" </p>
<p>"Indeed I am. I shall entreat his pardon for not having done </p>
<p>it earlier. I believe him to be Lady Catherine's NEPHEW. It </p>
<p>will be in my power to assure him that her ladyship was quite </p>
<p>well yesterday se'nnight." </p>
<p>Elizabeth tried hard to dissuade him from such a scheme, </p>
<p>assuring him that Mr. Darcy would consider his addressing </p>
<p>him without introduction as an impertinent freedom, rather </p>
<p>than a compliment to his aunt; that it was not in the least </p>
<p>necessary there should be any notice on either side; and that if </p>
<p>it were, it must belong to Mr. Darcy, the superior in </p>
<p>consequence, to begin the acquaintance. Mr. Collins listened </p>
<p>to her with the determined air of following his own </p>
<p>inclination, and, when she ceased speaking, replied thus: </p>
<p>"My dear Miss Elizabeth, I have the highest opinion in the </p>
<p>world in your excellent judgement in all matters within the </p>
<p>scope of your understanding; but permit me to say, that there </p>
<p>must be a wide difference between the established forms of </p>
<p>ceremony amongst the laity, and those which regulate the </p>
<p>clergy; for, give me leave to observe that I consider the </p>
<p>clerical office as equal in point of dignity with the highest </p>
<p>rank in the kingdom--provided that a proper humility of </p>
<p>behaviour is at the same time maintained. You must therefore </p>
<p>allow me to follow the dictates of my conscience on this </p>
<p>occasion, which leads me to perform what I look on as a point </p>
<p>of duty. Pardon me for neglecting to profit by your advice, </p>
<p>which on every other subject shall be my constant guide, </p>
<p>though in the case before us I consider myself more fitted by </p>
<p>education and habitual study to decide on what is right than a </p>
<p>young lady like yourself." And with a low bow he left her to </p>
<p>attack Mr. Darcy, whose reception of his advances she eagerly </p>
<p>watched, and whose astonishment at being so addressed was </p>
<p>very evident. Her cousin prefaced his speech with a solemn </p>
<p>bow and though she could not hear a word of it, she felt as if </p>
<p>hearing it all, and saw in the motion of his lips the words </p>
<p>"apology," "Hunsford," and "Lady Catherine de Bourgh." It </p>
<p>vexed her to see him expose himself to such a man. Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>was eyeing him with unrestrained wonder, and when at last </p>
<p>Mr. Collins allowed him time to speak, replied with an air of </p>
<p>distant civility. Mr. Collins, however, was not discouraged </p>
<p>from speaking again, and Mr. Darcy's contempt seemed </p>
<p>abundantly increasing with the length of his second speech, </p>
<p>and at the end of it he only made him a slight bow, and moved </p>
<p>another way. Mr. Collins then returned to Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"I have no reason, I assure you," said he, "to be dissatisfied </p>
<p>with my reception. Mr. Darcy seemed much pleased with the </p>
<p>attention. He answered me with the utmost civility, and even </p>
<p>paid me the compliment of saying that he was so well </p>
<p>convinced of Lady Catherine's discernment as to be certain </p>
<p>she could never bestow a favour unworthily. It was really a </p>
<p>very handsome thought. Upon the whole, I am much pleased </p>
<p>with him." </p>
<p>As Elizabeth had no longer any interest of her own to </p>
<p>pursue, she turned her attention almost entirely on her sister </p>
<p>and Mr. Bingley; and the train of agreeable reflections which </p>
<p>her observations gave birth to, made her perhaps almost as </p>
<p>happy as Jane. She saw her in idea settled in that very house, </p>
<p>in all the felicity which a marriage of true affection could </p>
<p>bestow; and she felt capable, under such circumstances, of </p>
<p>endeavouring even to like Bingley's two sisters. Her mother's </p>
<p>thoughts she plainly saw were bent the same way, and she </p>
<p>determined not to venture near her, lest she might hear too </p>
<p>much. When they sat down to supper, therefore, she </p>
<p>considered it a most unlucky perverseness which placed them </p>
<p>within one of each other; and deeply was she vexed to find </p>
<p>that her mother was talking to that one person (Lady Lucas) </p>
<p>freely, openly, and of nothing else but her expectation that </p>
<p>Jane would soon be married to Mr. Bingley. It was an </p>
<p>animating subject, and Mrs. Bennet seemed incapable of </p>
<p>fatigue while enumerating the advantages of the match. His </p>
<p>being such a charming young man, and so rich, and living but </p>
<p>three miles from them, were the first points of self-gratulation; </p>
<p>and then it was such a comfort to think how fond the two </p>
<p>sisters were of Jane, and to be certain that they must desire the </p>
<p>connection as much as she could do. It </p>
<p>was, moreover, such a promising thing for her younger </p>
<p>daughters, as Jane's marrying so greatly must throw them in </p>
<p>the way of other rich men; and lastly, it was so pleasant at her </p>
<p>time of life to be able to consign her single daughters to the </p>
<p>care of their sister, that she might not be obliged to go into </p>
<p>company more than she liked. It was necessary to make this </p>
<p>circumstance a matter of pleasure, because on such occasions </p>
<p>it is the etiquette; but no one was less likely than Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>to find comfort in staying home at any period of her life. She </p>
<p>concluded with many good wishes that Lady Lucas might </p>
<p>soon be equally fortunate, though evidently and triumphantly </p>
<p>believing there was no chance of it. </p>
<p>In vain did Elizabeth endeavour to check the rapidity of </p>
<p>her mother's words, or persuade her to describe her felicity in </p>
<p>a less audible whisper; for, to her inexpressible vexation, she </p>
<p>could perceive that the chief of it was overheard by Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, who sat opposite to them. Her mother only scolded her </p>
<p>for being nonsensical. </p>
<p>"What is Mr. Darcy to me, pray, that I should be afraid of </p>
<p>him? I am sure we owe him no such particular civility as to be </p>
<p>obliged to say nothing HE may not like to hear." </p>
<p>"For heaven's sake, madam, speak lower. What advantage </p>
<p>can it be for you to offend Mr. Darcy? You will never </p>
<p>recommend yourself to his friend by so doing!" </p>
<p>Nothing that she could say, however, had any influence. </p>
<p>Her mother would talk of her views in the same intelligible </p>
<p>tone. Elizabeth blushed and blushed again with shame and </p>
<p>vexation. She could not help frequently glancing her eye at </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, though every glance convinced her of what she </p>
<p>dreaded; for though he was not always looking at her mother, </p>
<p>she was convinced that his attention was invariably fixed by </p>
<p>her. The expression of his face changed gradually from </p>
<p>indignant contempt to a composed and steady gravity. </p>
<p>At length, however, Mrs. Bennet had no more to say; and </p>
<p>Lady Lucas, who had been long yawning at the repetition of </p>
<p>delights which she saw no likelihood of sharing, was left to </p>
<p>the comforts of cold ham and chicken. Elizabeth now began to </p>
<p>revive. But not long was the interval of tranquillity; for, when </p>
<p>supper was over, singing was talked of, and she had the </p>
<p>mortification of seeing Mary, after very little entreaty, </p>
<p>preparing to oblige the company. By many significant looks </p>
<p>and silent entreaties, did she endeavour to prevent such a </p>
<p>proof of complaisance, but in </p>
<p>vain; Mary would not </p>
<p>understand them; such an opportunity of exhibiting was </p>
<p>delightful to her, and she began her song. Elizabeth's eyes </p>
<p>were fixed on her with most painful sensations, and she </p>
<p>watched her progress through the several stanzas with an </p>
<p>impatience which was very ill rewarded at their close; for </p>
<p>Mary, on receiving, amongst the thanks of the table, the hint </p>
<p>of a hope that she might be prevailed on to favour them again, </p>
<p>after the pause of half a minute began another. Mary's powers </p>
<p>were by no means fitted for such a display; her voice was </p>
<p>weak, and her manner affected. Elizabeth was in agonies. She </p>
<p>looked at Jane, to see how she bore it; but Jane was very </p>
<p>composedly talking to Bingley. She looked at his two sisters, </p>
<p>and saw them making signs of derision at each other, and at </p>
<p>Darcy, who continued, however, imperturbably grave. She </p>
<p>looked at her father to entreat his interference, lest Mary </p>
<p>should be singing all night. He took the hint, and when Mary </p>
<p>had finished her second song, said aloud, "That will do </p>
<p>extremely well, child. You have delighted us long enough. Let </p>
<p>the other young ladies have time to exhibit." </p>
<p>Mary, though pretending not to hear, was somewhat </p>
<p>disconcerted; and Elizabeth, sorry for her, and sorry for her </p>
<p>father's speech, was afraid her anxiety had done no good. </p>
<p>Others of the party were now applied to. </p>
<p>"If I," said Mr. Collins, "were so fortunate as to be able to </p>
<p>sing, I should have great pleasure, I am sure, in obliging the </p>
<p>company with an air; for I consider music as a very innocent </p>
<p>diversion, and perfectly compatible with the profession of a </p>
<p>clergyman. I do not mean, however, to assert that we can be </p>
<p>justified in devoting too much of our time to music, for there </p>
<p>are certainly other things to be attended to. The rector of a </p>
<p>parish has much to do. In the first place, he must make such </p>
<p>an agreement for tithes as a may be beneficial to himself and </p>
<p>not offensive to his patron. He must write his own sermons; </p>
<p>and the time that remains will not be too much for his parish </p>
<p>duties, and the care and improvement of his dwelling, which </p>
<p>he cannot be excused from making as a comfortable as </p>
<p>possible. </p>
<p>And I do not think it of light importance that he should have </p>
<p>attentive and conciliatory manner towards everybody, </p>
<p>especially towards those to whom he owes his preferment. I </p>
<p>cannot acquit him of that duty; nor could I think well of the </p>
<p>man who should omit an occasion of testifying his respect </p>
<p>towards anybody connected with the family." And with a bow </p>
<p>to Mr. Darcy, he concluded his speech, which had been </p>
<p>spoken so loud as a to be heard by half the room. Many </p>
<p>stared--many smiled; but no one looked more amused than </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet himself, while his wife seriously commended Mr. </p>
<p>Collins for having spoken so sensibly, and observed in a half- </p>
<p>whisper to Lady Lucas, that he was a remarkably clever, good </p>
<p>kind of young man. </p>
<p>To Elizabeth it appeared that, had her family made an </p>
<p>agreement to expose themselves as a much as a they could </p>
<p>during the evening, it would have been impossible for them to </p>
<p>play their parts with more spirit or finer success; and happy </p>
<p>did she think it for Bingley and her sister that some of the </p>
<p>exhibition had escaped his notice, and that his feelings were </p>
<p>not of a sort to be much distressed by the folly which he must </p>
<p>have witnessed. That his two sisters and Mr. Darcy, however, </p>
<p>should have such an opportunity of ridiculing her relations, </p>
<p>was bad enough, and she could not determine whether the </p>
<p>silent contempt of the gentleman, or the insolent smiles of the </p>
<p>ladies, were more intolerable. </p>
<p>The rest of the evening brought her little amusement. She </p>
<p>was teased by Mr. Collins, who continued most perseveringly </p>
<p>by her side, and though he could not prevail on her to dance </p>
<p>with him again, put it out of her power to dance with others. </p>
<p>In vain did she entreat him to stand up with somebody else, </p>
<p>and offer to introduce him to any young lady in the room. He </p>
<p>assured her, that as to dancing, he was perfectly indifferent to </p>
<p>it; that his chief object was by delicate attentions to </p>
<p>recommend himself to her and that he should therefore make a </p>
<p>point of remaining close to her the whole evening. There was </p>
<p>no arguing upon such a project. She owed her greatest relief to </p>
<p>her friend </p>
<p>Miss Lucas, who often joined them, and </p>
<p>goodnaturedly engaged Mr. Collins's conversation to herself. </p>
<p>She was at least free from the offense of Mr. Darcy's </p>
<p>further notice; though often standing within a very short </p>
<p>distance of her, quite disengaged, he never came near enough </p>
<p>to speak. She felt it to be the probable consequence of her </p>
<p>allusions to Mr. Wickham, and rejoiced in it. </p>
<p>The Longbourn party were the last of all the company to </p>
<p>depart, and, by a manoeuvre of Mrs. Bennet, had to wait for </p>
<p>their carriage a quarter of an hour after everybody else was </p>
<p>gone, which gave them time to see how heartily they were </p>
<p>wished away by some of the family. Mrs. Hurst and her sister </p>
<p>scarcely opened their mouths, except to complain of fatigue, </p>
<p>and were evidently impatient to have the house to themselves. </p>
<p>They repulsed every attempt of Mrs. Bennet at conversation, </p>
<p>and by so doing threw a languor over the whole party, which </p>
<p>was very little relieved by the long speeches of Mr. Collins, </p>
<p>who was complimenting Mr. Bingley and his sisters on the </p>
<p>elegance of their entertainment, and the hospitality and </p>
<p>politeness which had marked their behaviour to their guests. </p>
<p>Darcy said nothing at all. Mr. Bennet, in equal silence, was </p>
<p>enjoying the scene. Mr. Bingley and Jane were standing </p>
<p>together, a little detached from the rest, and talked only to </p>
<p>each other. Elizabeth preserved as steady a silence as either </p>
<p>Mrs. Hurst or Miss Bingley; and even Lydia was too much </p>
<p>fatigued to utter more than the occasional exclamation of </p>
<p>"Lord, how tired I am!" accompanied by a violent yawn. </p>
<p>When at length they arose to take leave, Mrs. Bennet was </p>
<p>most pressingly civil in her hope of seeing the whole family </p>
<p>soon at Longbourn, and addressed herself especially to Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, to assure him how happy he would make them by </p>
<p>eating a family dinner with them at any time, without the </p>
<p>ceremony of a formal invitation. Bingley was all grateful </p>
<p>pleasure, and he readily engaged for taking the earliest </p>
<p>opportunity of waiting on her, after his return from London, </p>
<p>whither he was obliged to go the next day for a short time. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was perfectly satisfied, and quitted the house </p>
<p>under the </p>
<p>delightful </p>
<p>persuasion that, allowing </p>
<p>for the </p>
<p>necessary preparations of settlements, new carriages, and </p>
<p>wedding clothes, she should undoubtedly see her daughter </p>
<p>settled at Netherfield in the course of three or four months. Of </p>
<p>having another daughter married to Mr. Collins, she thought </p>
<p>with equal certainty, and with considerable, though not equal, </p>
<p>pleasure. Elizabeth was the least dear to her of all her </p>
<p>children; and though the man and the match were quite good </p>
<p>enough for HER, the worth of each was eclipsed by Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley and Netherfield. </p>
<heading>Chapter 19. </heading>
<p>The next day opened a new scene at Longbourn. Mr. </p>
<p>Collins made his declaration in form. Having resolved to do it </p>
<p>without loss of time, as his leave of absence extended only to </p>
<p>the following Saturday, and having no feelings of diffidence </p>
<p>to make it distressing to himself even at the moment, he set </p>
<p>about it in a very orderly manner, with all the observances, </p>
<p>which he supposed a regular part of the business. On finding </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, Elizabeth, and one of the younger girls together, </p>
<p>soon after breakfast, he addressed the mother in these words: </p>
<p>"May I hope, madam, for your interest with your fair </p>
<p>daughter Elizabeth, when I solicit for the honour of a private </p>
<p>audience with her in the course of this morning?" </p>
<p>Before Elizabeth had time for anything but a blush of </p>
<p>surprise, Mrs. Bennet answered instantly, "Oh dear!--yes-- </p>
<p>certainly. I am sure Lizzy will be very happy--I am sure she </p>
<p>can have no objection. Come, Kitty, I want you upstairs." </p>
<p>And, gathering her work together, she was hastening away, </p>
<p>when Elizabeth called out: </p>
<p>"Dear madam, do not go. I beg you will not go. Mr. </p>
<p>Collins must excuse me. He can have nothing to say to me </p>
<p>that anybody need not hear. I am going away myself." </p>
<p>"No, no, nonsense, Lizzy. I desire you to stay where you </p>
<p>are." And upon Elizabeth's seeming really, with vexed and </p>
<p>embarrassed looks, about to escape, she added: "Lizzy, I </p>
<p>INSIST upon your staying and hearing Mr. Collins." </p>
<p>Elizabeth would not oppose such an injunction--and a </p>
<p>moment's consideration making her also sensible that it would </p>
<p>be wisest to get it over as soon and as quietly as possible, she </p>
<p>sat down again and tried to conceal, by incessant employment </p>
<p>the feelings which were </p>
<p>divided between distress and </p>
<p>diversion. Mrs. Bennet and Kitty walked off, and as soon as </p>
<p>they were gone, Mr. Collins began. </p>
<p>"Believe me, my dear Miss Elizabeth, that your modesty, </p>
<p>so far from doing you any disservice, rather adds to your other </p>
<p>perfections. You would have been less amiable in my eyes </p>
<p>had there NOT been this little unwillingness; but allow me to </p>
<p>assure you, that I have your respected mother's permission for </p>
<p>this address. You can hardly doubt the purport of my </p>
<p>discourse, however your natural delicacy may lead you to </p>
<p>dissemble; my attentions have been too marked to be </p>
<p>mistaken. Almost as soon as I entered the house, I singled you </p>
<p>out as the companion of my future life. But before I am run </p>
<p>away with by my feelings on this subject, perhaps it would be </p>
<p>advisable for me to state my reasons for marrying--and, </p>
<p>moreover, for coming into Hertfordshire with the design of </p>
<p>selecting a wife, as I certainly did." </p>
<p>The idea of Mr. Collins, with all his solemn composure, </p>
<p>being run away with by his feelings, made Elizabeth so near </p>
<p>laughing, that she could not use the short pause he allowed in </p>
<p>any attempt to stop him further, and he continued: </p>
<p>"My reasons for marrying are, first, that I think it a right </p>
<p>thing for every clergyman in easy circumstances (like myself ) </p>
<p>to set the example of matrimony in his parish; secondly, that I </p>
<p>am convinced that it will add very greatly to my happiness; </p>
<p>and thirdly--which perhaps I ought to have mentioned earlier, </p>
<p>that it is the particular advice and recommendation of the very </p>
<p>noble lady whom I have the honour of calling patroness. </p>
<p>Twice has she condescended to give me her opinion (unasked </p>
<p>too!) on this subject; and it was but the very Saturday night </p>
<p>before I left Hunsford--between our pools at quadrille, while </p>
<p>Mrs. Jenkinson was arranging Miss de Bourgh's footstool, that </p>
<p>she said, 'Mr. Collins, you must marry. A clergyman like you </p>
<p>must marry. Choose properly, choose a gentlewoman for MY </p>
<p>sake; and for your OWN, let her be an active, useful sort of </p>
<p>person, not brought up high, but able to make a small income </p>
<p>go a good way. This is my advice. Find such a woman as soon </p>
<p>as you can, bring her to Hunsford, and I will visit her.' Allow </p>
<p>me, by the way, to observe, my fair cousin, that I do not </p>
<p>reckon the notice and kindness of Lady Catherine de Bourgh </p>
<p>as among the least of the advantages in my power to offer. </p>
<p>You will find her manners beyond anything I can describe; </p>
<p>and your wit and vivacity, I think, must be acceptable to her, </p>
<p>especially when tempered with the silence and respect which </p>
<p>her rank will inevitably excite. Thus much for my general </p>
<p>intention in favour of matrimony; it remains to be told why </p>
<p>my views were directed towards Longbourn instead of my </p>
<p>own neighbourhood, where I can assure you there are many </p>
<p>amiable young women. But the fact is, that being, as I am, to </p>
<p>inherit this estate after the death of your honoured father </p>
<p>(who, however, may live many years longer), I could not </p>
<p>satisfy myself without resolving to choose a wife from among </p>
<p>his daughters, that the loss to them might be as little as </p>
<p>possible, when the melancholy event takes place--which, </p>
<p>however, as I have already said, may not be for several years. </p>
<p>This has been my motive, my fair cousin, and I flatter myself </p>
<p>it will not sink me in your esteem. And now nothing remains </p>
<p>but for me but to assure you in the most animated language of </p>
<p>the violence of my affection. To fortune I am perfectly </p>
<p>indifferent, and shall make no demand of that nature on your </p>
<p>father, since I am well aware that it could not be complied </p>
<p>with; and that one thousand pounds in the four per cents, </p>
<p>which will not be yours till after your mother's decease, is all </p>
<p>that you may ever be entitled to. On that head, therefore, I </p>
<p>shall be uniformly silent; and you may assure yourself that no </p>
<p>ungenerous reproach shall ever pass my lips when we are </p>
<p>married." </p>
<p>It was absolutely necessary to interrupt him now. </p>
<p>"You are too hasty, sir," she cried. "You forget that I have </p>
<p>made no answer. Let me do it without further loss of time. </p>
<p>Accept my thanks for the compliment you are paying me. I </p>
<p>am very sensible of the honour of your proposals, but it is </p>
<p>impossible for me to do otherwise than to decline them." </p>
<p>"I am not now to learn," replied Mr. Collins, with a formal </p>
<p>wave of the hand, "that it is usual with young ladies to reject </p>
<p>the addresses of the man whom they secretly mean to accept, </p>
<p>when he first applies for their favour; and that sometimes the </p>
<p>refusal is repeated a second, or even a third time. I am </p>
<p>therefore by no means discouraged by what you have just </p>
<p>said, and shall hope to lead you to the altar ere long." </p>
<p>"Upon my word, sir," cried Elizabeth, "your hope is a </p>
<p>rather extraordinary one after my declaration. I do assure you </p>
<p>that I am not one of those young ladies (if such young ladies </p>
<p>there are) who are so daring as to risk their happiness on the </p>
<p>chance of being asked a second time. I am perfectly serious in </p>
<p>my refusal. You could not make ME happy, and I am </p>
<p>convinced that I am the last woman in the world who could </p>
<p>make you so. Nay, were your friend Lady Catherine to know </p>
<p>me, I am persuaded she would find me in every respect ill </p>
<p>qualified for the situation." </p>
<p>"Were it certain that Lady Catherine would think so," said </p>
<p>Mr. Collins very gravely--"but I cannot imagine that her </p>
<p>ladyship would at all disapprove of you. And you may be </p>
<p>certain when I have the honour of seeing her again, I shall </p>
<p>speak in the very highest terms of your modesty, economy, </p>
<p>and other amiable qualification." </p>
<p>"Indeed, Mr. Collins, all praise of me will be unnecessary. </p>
<p>You must give me leave to judge for myself, and pay me the </p>
<p>compliment of believing what I say. I wish you very happy </p>
<p>and very rich, and by refusing you hand, do all in my power to </p>
<p>prevent your being otherwise. In making me the offer, you </p>
<p>must have satisfied the delicacy of your feelings with regard </p>
<p>to my family, and may take possession of Longbourn estate </p>
<p>whenever it falls, without any self-reproach. This matter may </p>
<p>be considered, therefore, as finally settled." And rising as she </p>
<p>thus spoke, she would have quitted the room, had Mr. Collins </p>
<p>not thus addressed her: </p>
<p>"When I do myself the honour of speaking to you next on </p>
<p>the subject, I shall hope to receive a more favourable answer </p>
<p>than you have now given me; though I am far from accusing </p>
<p>you of cruelty at present, because I know it to be the </p>
<p>established custom of your sex to reject a man on the first </p>
<p>application, and perhaps you have even now said as much to </p>
<p>encourage my suit as would be consistent with the true </p>
<p>delicacy of the female character." </p>
<p>"Really, Mr. Collins," cried Elizabeth with some warmth, </p>
<p>"you puzzle me exceedingly. If what I have hitherto said can </p>
<p>appear to you in the form of encouragement, I know not how </p>
<p>to express my refusal in such a way as to convince you of its </p>
<p>being one." </p>
<p>"You must give me leave to flatter myself, my dear cousin, </p>
<p>that your refusal of my addresses is merely words of course. </p>
<p>My reasons for believing it are briefly these: It does not </p>
<p>appear to me that my hand is unworthy your acceptance, or </p>
<p>that the establishment I can offer would be any other than </p>
<p>highly desirable. My situation in life, my connections with the </p>
<p>family of de Bourgh, and my relationship to your own, are </p>
<p>circumstances highly in my favour; and you should take it </p>
<p>into further consideration, that in spite of your manifold </p>
<p>attractions, it is by no means certain that another offer of </p>
<p>marriage may ever be made you. Your portion is unhappily so </p>
<p>small that it will in all likelihood undo the effects of your </p>
<p>loveliness and amiable qualifications. As I must therefore </p>
<p>conclude that you are not serious in your rejection of me, I </p>
<p>shall choose to attribute it to your wish of increasing my love </p>
<p>by suspense, according to the usual practice of elegant </p>
<p>females." </p>
<p>"I do assure you, sir, that I have no pretensions whatever to </p>
<p>that kind of elegance which consists in tormenting a </p>
<p>respectable man. I would rather be paid the compliment of </p>
<p>being believed sincere. I thank you again and again for the </p>
<p>honour you have done me in your proposals, but to accept </p>
<p>them is absolutely impossible. My feelings in every respect </p>
<p>forbid it. Can I speak plainer? Do not consider me now as an </p>
<p>elegant female, intending to plague you, but as a rational </p>
<p>creature, speaking the truth from her heart." </p>
<p>"You are uniformly charming!" cried he, with an air of </p>
<p>awkward gallantry; "and I am persuaded that when sanctioned </p>
<p>by the express authority of both your excellent parents, my </p>
<p>proposals will not fail of being acceptable." </p>
<p>To such perseverance in wilful self-deception Elizabeth </p>
<p>would make no reply, and immediately and in silence </p>
<p>withdrew; determined, if he persisted in considering her </p>
<p>repeated refusals as flattering encouragement, to apply to her </p>
<p>father, whose negative might be uttered in such a manner as to </p>
<p>be decisive, and whose behavior at least could not be </p>
<p>mistaken for the affectation and coquetry of an elegant </p>
<p>female. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 20. </heading>
<p>Mr. Collins was not left long to the silent contemplation of </p>
<p>his successful love; for Mrs. Bennet, having dawdled about in </p>
<p>the vestibule to watch for the end of the conference, no sooner </p>
<p>saw Elizabeth open the door and with quick step pass her </p>
<p>towards the staircase, than she entered the breakfast-room, </p>
<p>and congratulated both him and herself in warm terms on the </p>
<p>happy prospect or their nearer connection. Mr. Collins </p>
<p>received and returned these felicitations with equal pleasure, </p>
<p>and then proceeded to relate the particulars of their interview, </p>
<p>with the result of which he trusted he had every reason to be </p>
<p>satisfied, since the refusal which his cousin had steadfastly </p>
<p>given him would naturally flow from her bashful modesty and </p>
<p>the genuine delicacy of her character. </p>
<p>This information, however, startled Mrs. Bennet; she </p>
<p>would have been glad to be equally satisfied that her daughter </p>
<p>had meant to encourage him by protesting against his </p>
<p>proposals, but she dared not believe it, and could not help </p>
<p>saying so. </p>
<p>"But, depend upon it, Mr. Collins," she added, "that Lizzy </p>
<p>shall be brought to reason. I will speak to her about it directly. </p>
<p>She is a very headstrong, foolish girl, and does not know her </p>
<p>own interest but I will MAKE her know it." </p>
<p>"Pardon me for interrupting you, madam," cried Mr. </p>
<p>Collins; "but if she is really headstrong and foolish, I know </p>
<p>not whether she would altogether be a very desirable wife to a </p>
<p>man in my situation, who naturally looks for happiness in the </p>
<p>marriage state. If therefore she actually persists in rejecting </p>
<p>my suit, perhaps it were better not to force her into accepting </p>
<p>me, because if liable to such defects of temper, she could not </p>
<p>contribute much to my felicity." </p>
<p>"Sir, you quite misunderstand me," said Mrs. Bennet, </p>
<p>alarmed. "Lizzy is only headstrong in such matters as these. In </p>
<p>everything else she is as good-natured a girl as ever lived. I </p>
<p>will go directly to Mr. Bennet, and we shall very soon settle it </p>
<p>with her, I am sure." </p>
<p>She would not give him time to reply, but hurrying </p>
<p>instantly to her husband, called out as she entered the library, </p>
<p>"Oh! Mr. Bennet, you are wanted immediately; we are all in </p>
<p>an uproar. You must come and make Lizzy marry Mr. Collins, </p>
<p>for she vows she will not have him, and if you do not make </p>
<p>haste he will change his mind and not have HER." </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet raised his eyes from his book as she entered, </p>
<p>and fixed them on her face with a calm unconcern which was </p>
<p>not in the least altered by her communication. </p>
<p>"I have not the pleasure of understanding you," said he, </p>
<p>when she had finished her speech. "Of what are you talking?" </p>
<p>"Of Mr. Collins and Lizzy. Lizzy declares she will not </p>
<p>have Mr. Collins, and Mr. Collins begins to say that he will </p>
<p>not have Lizzy." </p>
<p>"And what am I to do on the occasion? It seems an </p>
<p>hopeless business." </p>
<p>"Speak to Lizzy about it yourself. Tell her that you insist </p>
<p>upon her marrying him." </p>
<p>"Let her be called down. She shall hear my opinion." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet rang the bell, and Miss Elizabeth was </p>
<p>summoned to the library. </p>
<p>"Come here, child," cried her father as she appeared. "I </p>
<p>have sent for you on an affair of importance. I understand that </p>
<p>Mr. Collins has made you an offer of marriage. Is it true?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth replied that it was. "Very well--and this offer of </p>
<p>marriage you have refused?" </p>
<p>"I have, sir." </p>
<p>"Very well. We now come to the point. Your mother </p>
<p>insists upon your accepting it. Is it not so, Mrs. Bennet?" </p>
<p>"Yes, or I will never see her again." </p>
<p>"An unhappy alternative is before you, Elizabeth. From </p>
<p>this day you must be a stranger to one of your parents. </p>
<p>Your mother will never see you again if you do NOT </p>
<p>marry Mr. Collins, and I will never see you again if you </p>
<p>DO." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not but smile at such a conclusion of such </p>
<p>a beginning, but Mrs. Bennet, who had persuaded herself that </p>
<p>her husband regarded the affair as she wished, was </p>
<p>excessively disappointed. </p>
<p>"What do you mean, Mr. Bennet, in talking this way? You </p>
<p>promised me to INSIST upon her marrying him." </p>
<p>"My dear," replied her husband, "I have two small favours </p>
<p>to request. First, that you will allow me the free use of my </p>
<p>understanding on the present occasion; and secondly, of my </p>
<p>room. I shall be glad to have the library to myself as soon as </p>
<p>may be." </p>
<p>Not yet, however, in spite of her disappointment in her </p>
<p>husband, did Mrs. Bennet give up the point. She talked to </p>
<p>Elizabeth again and again; coaxed and threatened her by turns. </p>
<p>She endeavoured to secure Jane in her interest; but Jane, with </p>
<p>all possible mildness, declined interfering; and Elizabeth, </p>
<p>sometimes with real earnestness, and sometimes with playful </p>
<p>gaiety, replied to her attacks. Though her manner varied, </p>
<p>however, her determination never did. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins, meanwhile, was meditating in solitude on </p>
<p>what had passed. He thought too well of himself to </p>
<p>comprehend on what motives his cousin could refuse him; and </p>
<p>though his pride was hurt, he suffered in no other way. His </p>
<p>regard for her was quite imaginary; and the possibility of her </p>
<p>deserving her mother's reproach prevented his feeling any </p>
<p>regret. </p>
<p>While the family were in this confusion, Charlotte Lucas </p>
<p>came to spend the day with them. She was met in the </p>
<p>vestibule by Lydia, who, flying to her, cried in a half whisper, </p>
<p>"I am glad you are come, for there is such fun here! What do </p>
<p>you think has happened this morning? Mr. Collins has made </p>
<p>an offer to Lizzy, and she will not have him." </p>
<p>Charlotte hardly had time to answer, before they were </p>
<p>joined by Kitty, who came to tell the same news; and no </p>
<p>sooner had they entered the breakfast-room, where Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet was alone, than she likewise began on the subject, </p>
<p>calling on Miss Lucas for her compassion, and entreating her </p>
<p>to persuade her friend Lizzy to comply with the wishes of all </p>
<p>her family. "Pray do, my dear Miss Lucas," she added in a </p>
<p>melancholy tone, "for nobody is on my side, nobody takes </p>
<p>part with me. I am cruelly used, nobody feels for my poor </p>
<p>nerves." </p>
<p>Charlotte's reply was spared by the entrance of Jane and </p>
<p>Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"Aye, there she comes," continued Mrs. Bennet, "looking </p>
<p>as unconcerned as may be, and caring no more for us than if </p>
<p>we were at York, provided she can have her own way. But I </p>
<p>tell you, Miss Lizzy--if you take it into your head to go on </p>
<p>refusing every offer of marriage in this way, you will never </p>
<p>get a husband at all--and I am sure I do not know who is to </p>
<p>maintain you when your father is dead. I shall not be able to </p>
<p>keep you--and so I warn you. I have done with you from this </p>
<p>very day. I told you in the library, you know, that I should </p>
<p>never speak to you again, and you will find me as good as my </p>
<p>word. I have no pleasure in talking to undutiful children. Not </p>
<p>that I have much pleasure, indeed, in talking to anybody. </p>
<p>People who suffer as I do from nervous complaints can have </p>
<p>no great inclination for talking. Nobody can tell what I suffer! </p>
<p>But it is always so. Those who do not complain are never </p>
<p>pitied." </p>
<p>Her daughters listened in silence to this effusion, sensible </p>
<p>that any attempt to reason with her or soothe her would only </p>
<p>increase the irritation. She talked on, therefore, without </p>
<p>interruption from any of them, till they were joined by Mr. </p>
<p>Collins, who entered the room with an air more stately than </p>
<p>usual, and on perceiving whom, she said to the girls, "Now, I </p>
<p>do insist upon it, that you, all of you, hold your tongues, and </p>
<p>let me and Mr. Collins have a little conversation together." </p>
<p>Elizabeth passed quietly out of the room, Jane and Kitty </p>
<p>followed, but Lydia stood her ground, determined to hear all </p>
<p>she could; and Charlotte, detained first by the civility of Mr. </p>
<p>Collins, whose inquiries after herself and all her family were </p>
<p>very minute, and then by a little curiosity, satisfied herself </p>
<p>with walking to the window and pretending not to hear. In a </p>
<p>doleful voice Mrs. Bennet began the projected conversation: </p>
<p>"Oh! Mr. Collins!" </p>
<p>"My dear madam," replied he, "let us be for ever silent on </p>
<p>this point. Far be it from me," he presently continued, in a </p>
<p>voice that marked his displeasure, "to resent the behaviour of </p>
<p>your daughter. Resignation to inevitable evils is the evil duty </p>
<p>of us all; the peculiar duty of a young man who has been so </p>
<p>fortunate as I have been in early preferment; and I trust I am </p>
<p>resigned. Perhaps not the less so from feeling a doubt of my </p>
<p>positive happiness had my fair cousin honoured me with her </p>
<p>hand; for I have often observed that resignation is never so </p>
<p>perfect as when the blessing denied begins to lose somewhat </p>
<p>of its value in our estimation. You will not, I hope, consider </p>
<p>me as showing any disrespect to your family, my dear madam, </p>
<p>by thus withdrawing my pretensions to your daughter's </p>
<p>favour, without having paid yourself and Mr. Bennet the </p>
<p>compliment of requesting you to interpose your authority in </p>
<p>my behalf. My conduct may, I fear, be objectionable in having </p>
<p>accepted my dismission from your daughter's lips instead of </p>
<p>your own. But we are all liable to error. I have certainly meant </p>
<p>well through the whole affair. My object has been to secure an </p>
<p>amiable companion for myself, with due consideration for the </p>
<p>advantage of all your family, and if my MANNER has been at </p>
<p>all reprehensible, I here beg leave to apologise." </p>

<header>E-BooksDirectory.com </header>
<heading>Chapter 21. </heading>
<p>The discussion of Mr. Collins's offer was now nearly at an </p>
<p>end, and Elizabeth had only to suffer from the uncomfortable </p>
<p>feelings necessarily attending it, and occasionally from some </p>
<p>peevish allusions of her mother. As for the gentleman himself, </p>
<p>HIS feelings were chiefly expressed, not by embarrassment or </p>
<p>dejection, or by trying to avoid her, but by stiffness of manner </p>
<p>and resentful silence. He scarcely ever spoke to her, and the </p>
<p>assiduous attentions which he had been so sensible of himself </p>
<p>were transferred for the rest of the day to Miss Lucas, whose </p>
<p>civility in listening to him was a seasonable relief to them all, </p>
<p>and especially to her friend. </p>
<p>The morrow produced no abatement of Mrs. Bennet's ill- </p>
<p>humour or ill health. Mr. Collins was also in the same state of </p>
<p>angry pride. Elizabeth had hoped that his resentment might </p>
<p>shorten his visit, but his plan did not appear in the least </p>
<p>affected by it. He was always to have gone on Saturday, and </p>
<p>to Saturday he meant to stay. </p>
<p>After breakfast, the girls walked to Meryton to inquire if </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham were returned, and to lament over his absence </p>
<p>from the Netherfield ball. He joined them on their entering the </p>
<p>town, and attended them to their aunt's where his regret and </p>
<p>vexation, and the concern of everybody, was well talked over. </p>
<p>To Elizabeth, however, he voluntarily acknowledged that the </p>
<p>necessity of his absence HAD been self-imposed. </p>
<p>"I found," said he, "as the time drew near that I had better </p>
<p>not meet Mr. Darcy; that to be in the same room, the same </p>
<p>party with him for so many hours together, might be more </p>
<p>than I could bear, and that scenes might arise unpleasant to </p>
<p>more than myself." </p>
<p>She highly approved his forbearance, and they had leisure </p>
<p>for a full discussion of it, and for all the commendation which </p>
<p>they civilly bestowed on each other, as Wickham and another </p>
<p>officer walked back with them to Longbourn, and during the </p>
<p>walk he particularly attended to her. His accompanying them </p>
<p>was a double advantage; she felt all the compliment it offered </p>
<p>to herself, and it was most acceptable as an occasion of </p>
<p>introducing him to her father and mother. </p>
<p>Soon after their return, a letter was delivered to Miss </p>
<p>Bennet; it came from Netherfield. The envelope contained a </p>
<p>sheet of elegant, little, hot-pressed paper, well covered with a </p>
<p>lady's fair, flowing hand; and Elizabeth saw her sister's </p>
<p>countenance change as she read it, and saw her dwelling </p>
<p>intently on some particular passages. Jane recollected herself </p>
<p>soon, and putting the letter away, tried to join with her usual </p>
<p>cheerfulness in the general conversation; but Elizabeth felt an </p>
<p>anxiety on the subject which drew off her attention even from </p>
<p>Wickham; and no sooner had he and he companion taken </p>
<p>leave, than a glance from Jane invited her to follow her </p>
<p>upstairs. When they had gained their own room, Jane, taking </p>
<p>out the letter, said: </p>
<p>"This is from Caroline Bingley; what it contains has </p>
<p>surprised me a good deal. The whole party have left </p>
<p>Netherfield by this time, and are on their way to town--and </p>
<p>without any intention of coming back again. You shall hear </p>
<p>what she says." </p>
<p>She then read the first sentence aloud, which comprised </p>
<p>the information of their having just resolved to follow their </p>
<p>brother to town directly, and of their meaning to dine in </p>
<p>Grosvenor Street, where Mr. Hurst had a house. The next was </p>
<p>in these words: "I do not pretend to regret anything I shall </p>
<p>leave in Hertfordshire, except your society, my dearest friend; </p>
<p>but we will hope, at some future period, to enjoy many returns </p>
<p>of that delightful intercourse we have known, and in the </p>
<p>meanwhile may lessen the pain of separation by a very </p>
<p>frequent and most unreserved correspondence. I depend on </p>
<p>you for that." To these highflown expressions Elizabeth </p>
<p>listened with all the insensibility of distrust; and though the </p>
<p>suddenness of their removal surprised her, she saw nothing in </p>
<p>it really to lament; it was not to be supposed that their absence </p>
<p>from Netherfield would prevent Mr. Bingley's being there; </p>
<p>and as to the loss of their society, she was persuaded that Jane </p>
<p>must cease to regard it, in the enjoyment of his. </p>
<p>"It is unlucky," said she, after a short pause, "that you </p>
<p>should not be able to see your friends before they leave the </p>
<p>country. But may we not hope that the period of future </p>
<p>happiness to which Miss Bingley looks forward may arrive </p>
<p>earlier than she is aware, and that the delightful intercourse </p>
<p>you have known as friends will be renewed with yet greater </p>
<p>satisfaction as sisters? Mr. Bingley will not be detained in </p>
<p>London by them." </p>
<p>"Caroline decidedly says that none of the party will return </p>
<p>into Hertfordshire this winter. I will read it to you:" </p>
<p>"When my brother left us yesterday, he imagined that the </p>
<p>business which took him to London might be concluded in </p>
<p>three or four days; but as we are certain it cannot be so, and at </p>
<p>the same time convinced that when Charles gets to town he </p>
<p>will be in no hurry to leave it again, we have determined on </p>
<p>following him thither, that he may not be obliged to spend his </p>
<p>vacant hours in a comfortless hotel. Many of my </p>
<p>acquaintances are already there for the winter; I wish that I </p>
<p>could hear that you, my dearest friend, had any intention of </p>
<p>making one of the crowd--but of that I despair. I sincerely </p>
<p>hope your Christmas in Hertfordshire may abound in the </p>
<p>gaieties which that season generally brings, and that your </p>
<p>beaux will be so numerous as to prevent your feeling the loss </p>
<p>of the three of whom we shall deprive you." </p>
<p>"It is evident by this," added Jane, "that he comes back no </p>
<p>more this winter." </p>
<p>"It is only evident that Miss Bingley does not mean that he </p>
<p>SHOULD." </p>
<p>"Why will you think so? It must be his own doing. He is </p>
<p>his own master. But you do not know ALL. I WILL read you </p>
<p>the passage which particularly hurts me. I will have no </p>
<p>reserves from YOU." </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy is impatient to see his sister; and, to confess </p>
<p>the truth, WE are scarcely less eager to meet her again. I </p>
<p>really do not think Georgiana Darcy has her equal for beauty, </p>
<p>elegance, and accomplishments; and the affection she inspires </p>
<p>in Louisa and myself is heightened into something still more </p>
<p>interesting, from the hope we dare entertain of her being </p>
<p>hereafter our sister. I do not know whether I ever before </p>
<p>mentioned to you my feelings on this subject; but I will not </p>
<p>leave the country without confiding them, and I trust you will </p>
<p>not esteem them unreasonable. My brother admires her </p>
<p>greatly already; he will have frequent opportunity now of </p>
<p>seeing her on the most intimate footing; her relations all wish </p>
<p>the connection as much as his own; and a sister's partiality is </p>
<p>not misleading me, I think, when I call Charles most capable </p>
<p>of engaging any woman's heart. With all these circumstances </p>
<p>to favour an attachment, and nothing to prevent it, am I </p>
<p>wrong, my dearest Jane, in indulging the hope of an event </p>
<p>which will secure the happiness of so many?" </p>
<p>"What do you think of THIS sentence, my dear Lizzy?" </p>
<p>said Jane as she finished it. "Is it not clear enough? Does it not </p>
<p>expressly declare that Caroline neither expects nor wishes me </p>
<p>to be her sister; that she is perfectly convinced of her brother's </p>
<p>indifference; and that if she suspects the nature of my feelings </p>
<p>for him, she means (most kindly!) to put me on my guard? </p>
<p>Can there be any other opinion on the subject?" </p>
<p>"Yes, there can; for mine is totally different. Will you hear </p>
<p>it?" </p>
<p>"Most willingly." </p>
<p>"You shall have it in a few words. Miss Bingley sees that </p>
<p>her brother is in love with you, and wants him to marry Miss </p>
<p>Darcy. She follows him to town in hope of keeping him there, </p>
<p>and tries to persuade you that he does not care about you." </p>
<p>Jane shook her head. </p>
<p>"Indeed, Jane, you ought to believe me. No one who has </p>
<p>ever seen you together can doubt his affection. Miss Bingley, </p>
<p>I am sure, cannot. She is not such a simpleton. Could she have </p>
<p>seen half as much love in Mr. Darcy for herself, she would </p>
<p>have ordered her wedding clothes. But the case is this: We are </p>
<p>not rich enough or grand enough for them; and she is the more </p>
<p>anxious to get Miss Darcy for her brother, from the notion that </p>
<p>when there has been ONE intermarriage, she may have less </p>
<p>trouble in achieving a second; in which there is certainly some </p>
<p>ingenuity, and I dare say it would succeed, if Miss de Bourgh </p>
<p>were out of the way. But, my dearest Jane, you cannot </p>
