<p>seriously imagine that because Miss Bingley tells you her </p>
<p>brother greatly admires Miss Darcy, he is in the smallest </p>
<p>degree less sensible of YOUR merit than when he took leave </p>
<p>of you on Tuesday, or that it will be in her power to persuade </p>
<p>him that, instead of being in love with you, he is very much in </p>
<p>love with her friend." </p>
<p>"If we thought alike of Miss Bingley," replied Jane, "your </p>
<p>representation of all this might make me quite easy. But I </p>
<p>know the foundation is unjust. Caroline is incapable of </p>
<p>wilfully deceiving anyone; and all that I can hope in this case </p>
<p>is that she is deceiving herself." </p>
<p>"That is right. You could not have started a more happy </p>
<p>idea, since you will not take comfort in mine. Believe her to </p>
<p>be deceived, by all means. You have now done your duty by </p>
<p>her, and must fret no longer." </p>
<p>"But, my dear sister, can I be happy, even supposing the </p>
<p>best, in accepting a man whose sisters and friends are all </p>
<p>wishing him to marry elsewhere?" </p>
<p>"You must decide for yourself," said Elizabeth; "and if, </p>
<p>upon mature deliberation, you find that the misery of </p>
<p>disobliging his two sisters is more than equivalent to the </p>
<p>happiness of being his wife, I advise you by all means to </p>
<p>refuse him." </p>
<p>"How can you talk so?" said Jane, faintly smiling. "You </p>
<p>must know that though I should be exceedingly grieved at </p>
<p>their disapprobation, I could not hesitate." </p>
<p>"I did not think you would; and that being the case, I </p>
<p>cannot consider your situation with much compassion." </p>
<p>"But if he returns no more this winter, my choice will </p>
<p>never be required. A thousand things may arise in six </p>
<p>months!" </p>
<p>The idea of his returning no more Elizabeth treated with </p>
<p>the utmost contempt. It appeared to her merely the suggestion </p>
<p>of Caroline's interested wishes, and she could not for a </p>
<p>moment suppose that those wishes, however openly or artfully </p>
<p>spoken, could influence a young man so totally independent </p>
<p>of everyone. </p>
<p>She represented to her sister as forcibly as possible what </p>
<p>she felt on the subject, and had soon the pleasure of seeing its </p>
<p>happy effect. Jane's temper was not desponding, and she was </p>
<p>gradually led to hope, though the diffidence of affection </p>
<p>sometimes overcame the hope, that Bingley would return to </p>
<p>Netherfield and answer every wish of her heart. </p>
<p>They agreed that Mrs. Bennet should only hear of the </p>
<p>departure of the family, without being alarmed on the score of </p>
<p>the gentleman's conduct; but even this partial communication </p>
<p>gave her a great deal of concern, and she bewailed it as </p>
<p>exceedingly unlucky that the ladies should happen to go away </p>
<p>just as they were all getting so intimate together. After </p>
<p>lamenting it, however, at some length, she had the consolation </p>
<p>that Mr. Bingley would be soon down again and soon dining </p>
<p>at Longbourn, and the conclusion of all was the comfortable </p>
<p>declaration, that though he had been invited only to a family </p>
<p>dinner, she would take care to have two full courses. </p>
<header>Ebd </header>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 22. </heading>
<p>The Bennets were engaged to dine with the Lucases and </p>
<p>again during the chief of the day was Miss Lucas so kind as to </p>
<p>listen to Mr. Collins. Elizabeth took an opportunity of </p>
<p>thanking her. "It keeps him in good humour," said she, "and I </p>
<p>am more obliged to you than I can express." Charlotte assured </p>
<p>her friend of her satisfaction in being useful, and that it amply </p>
<p>repaid her for the little sacrifice of her time. This was very </p>
<p>amiable, but Charlotte's kindness extended farther than </p>
<p>Elizabeth had any conception of; its object was nothing else </p>
<p>than to secure her from any return of Mr. Collins's addresses, </p>
<p>by engaging them towards herself. Such was Miss Lucas's </p>
<p>scheme; and appearances were so favourable, that when they </p>
<p>parted at night, she would have felt almost secure of success if </p>
<p>he had </p>
<p>not been to leave Hertfordshire so very soon. But here she did </p>
<p>injustice to the fire and independence of his character, for it </p>
<p>led him to escape out of Longbourn House the next morning </p>
<p>with admirable slyness, and hasten to Lucas Lodge to throw </p>
<p>himself at her feet. He was anxious to avoid the notice of his </p>
<p>cousins, from a conviction that if they saw him depart, they </p>
<p>could not fail to conjecture his design, and he was not willing </p>
<p>to have the attempt known till its success might be known </p>
<p>likewise; for though feeling almost secure, and with reason, </p>
<p>for Charlotte had been tolerably </p>
<p>encouraging, he was </p>
<p>comparatively diffident since the adventure of Wednesday. </p>
<p>His reception, however, was of the most flattering kind. Miss </p>
<p>Lucas perceived him from an upper window as he walked </p>
<p>towards the house, and instantly set out to meet him </p>
<p>accidentally in the lane. But little had she dared to hope that </p>
<p>so much love and eloquence awaited her there. </p>
<p>In as short a time as Mr. Collins's long speeches would </p>
<p>allow, everything was settled between them to the satisfaction </p>
<p>of both; and as they entered the house he earnestly entreated </p>
<p>her to name the day that was to make him the happiest of </p>
<p>men; and though such a solicitation must be waived for the </p>
<p>present, the lady felt no inclination to trifle with his </p>
<p>happiness. The stupidity with which he was favoured by </p>
<p>nature must guard his courtship from any charm that could </p>
<p>make a woman wish for its continuance; and Miss Lucas, who </p>
<p>accepted him solely from the pure and disinterested desire of </p>
<p>an establishment, cared not how soon that establishment were </p>
<p>gained. </p>
<p>Sir William and Lady Lucas were speedily applied to for </p>
<p>their consent; and it was bestowed with a most joyful alacrity. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins's present circumstances made it a most eligible </p>
<p>match for their daughter, to whom they could give little </p>
<p>fortune; and his prospects of future wealth were exceedingly </p>
<p>fair. Lady Lucas began directly to calculate, with more </p>
<p>interest than the matter had ever excited before, how many </p>
<p>years longer Mr. Bennet was likely to live; and Sir William </p>
<p>gave it as his decided opinion, that whenever Mr. Collins </p>
<p>should be in possession of the Longbourn estate, it would be </p>
<p>highly expedient that both he and his wife should make their </p>
<p>appearance at St. James's. The whole family, in short, were </p>
<p>properly overjoyed on the occasion. The younger girls formed </p>
<p>hopes of COM ING OUT a year or two sooner than they </p>
<p>might otherwise have done; and the boys were relieved from </p>
<p>their apprehension of Charlotte's dying an old maid. Charlotte </p>
<p>herself was tolerably composed. She had gained her point, and </p>
<p>had time to consider of it. Her reflections were in general </p>
<p>satisfactory. Mr. Collins, to be sure, was neither sensible nor </p>
<p>agreeable; his society was irksome, and his attachment to her </p>
<p>must be imaginary. But still he would be her husband. </p>
<p>Without thinking highly either of men or matrimony, marriage </p>
<p>had always been her object; it was the only provision for well- </p>
<p>educated young women of small fortune, and however </p>
<p>uncertain of giving happiness, must be their pleasantest </p>
<p>preservative from want. This preservative she had now </p>
<p>obtained; and at the age of twenty-seven, without having ever </p>
<p>been handsome, she felt all the good luck of it. The least </p>
<p>agreeable circumstance in the business was the surprise it </p>
<p>must occasion to Elizabeth Bennet, whose friendship she </p>
<p>valued beyond that of any other person. Elizabeth would </p>
<p>wonder, and probably would blame her; and though her </p>
<p>resolution was not to be shaken, her feelings must be hurt by </p>
<p>such a disapprobation. She resolved to give her the </p>
<p>information herself, and therefore charged Mr. Collins, when </p>
<p>he returned to Longbourn to dinner, to drop no hint of what </p>
<p>had passed before any of the family. A promise of secrecy </p>
<p>was of course very dutifully given, but it could not be kept </p>
<p>without difficulty; for the curiosity excited by his long </p>
<p>absence burst forth in such very direct questions on his return </p>
<p>as required some ingenuity to evade, and he was at the same </p>
<p>time exercising great self-denial, for he was longing to publish </p>
<p>his prosperous love. </p>
<p>As he was to begin his journey too early on the morrow to </p>
<p>see any of the family, the ceremony of leave-taking was </p>
<p>performed when the ladies moved for the night; and Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, with great politeness and cordiality, said how happy </p>
<p>they should be to see him at Longbourn again, whenever his </p>
<p>engagements might allow him to visit them. </p>
<p>"My </p>
<p>dear madam," he replied, "this invitation is </p>
<p>particularly gratifying, because it is what I have been hoping </p>
<p>to receive; and you may be very certain that I shall avail </p>
<p>myself of it as soon as possible." </p>
<p>They were all astonished; and Mr. Bennet, who could by </p>
<p>no means wish for so speedy a return, immediately said: </p>
<p>"But is there not danger of </p>
<p>Lady Catherine's </p>
<p>disapprobation here, my good sir? You had better neglect your </p>
<p>relations than run the risk of offending your patroness." </p>
<p>"My dear sir," replied Mr. Collins," I am particularly </p>
<p>obliged to you for this friendly caution, and you may depend </p>
<p>upon my not taking so material a step without her ladyship's </p>
<p>concurrence." </p>
<p>"You cannot be too much upon your guard. Risk anything </p>
<p>rather than her displeasure; and if you find it likely to be </p>
<p>raised by your coming to us again, which I should think </p>
<p>exceedingly probable, stay quietly at home, and be satisfied </p>
<p>that WE shall take no offence." </p>
<p>"Believe me, my dear sir, my gratitude is warmly excited </p>
<p>by such affectionate attention; and depend upon it, you will </p>
<p>speedily receive from me a letter of thanks for this, and for </p>
<p>every other mark of your regard during my stay in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire. As for my fair cousins, though my absence may </p>
<p>not be long enough to render it necessary, I shall now take the </p>
<p>liberty of wishing them health and happiness, not excepting </p>
<p>my cousin Elizabeth." </p>
<p>With proper civilities the ladies then withdrew; all of them </p>
<p>equally surprised that he meditated a quick return. Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet wished to understand by it that he thought of paying </p>
<p>his addresses to one of her younger girls, and Mary might </p>
<p>have been prevailed on to accept him. She rated his abilities </p>
<p>much higher than any of the others; there was a solidity in his </p>
<p>reflections which often struck her, and though by no means so </p>
<p>clever as herself, she thought that if encouraged to read and </p>
<p>improve himself by such an example as hers, he might </p>
<p>become a very agreeable companion. But on the following </p>
<p>morning, every hope of this kind was done away. Miss Lucas </p>
<p>called soon after breakfast, and in a private conference with </p>
<p>Elizabeth related the event of the day before. </p>
<p>The possibility of Mr. Collins's fancying herself in love </p>
<p>with her friend had once occurred to Elizabeth within the last </p>
<p>day or two; but that Charlotte could encourage him seemed </p>
<p>almost as far from possibility as she could encourage him </p>
<p>herself, and her astonishment was consequently so great as to </p>
<p>overcome at first the bounds of decorum, and she could not </p>
<p>help crying out: </p>
<p>"Engaged to Mr. Collins! My dear Charlotte--impossible!" </p>
<p>The steady countenance which Miss Lucas had </p>
<p>commanded in telling her story, gave way to a momentary </p>
<p>confusion here on receiving so direct a reproach; though, as it </p>
<p>was no more than she expected, she soon regained her </p>
<p>composure, and calmly replied: </p>
<p>"Why should you be surprised, my dear Eliza? Do you </p>
<p>think it incredible that Mr. Collins should be able to procure </p>
<p>any woman's good opinion, because he was not so happy as to </p>
<p>succeed with you?" </p>
<p>But Elizabeth had now recollected herself, and making a </p>
<p>strong effort for it, was able to assure with tolerable firmness </p>
<p>that the prospect of their relationship was highly grateful to </p>
<p>her, and that she wished her all imaginable happiness. </p>
<p>"I see what you are feeling," replied Charlotte. "You must </p>
<p>be surprised, very much surprised--so lately as Mr. Collins </p>
<p>was wishing to marry you. But when you have had time to </p>
<p>think it over, I hope you will be satisfied with what I have </p>
<p>done. I am not romantic, you know; I never was. I ask only a </p>
<p>comfortable home; and considering Mr. Collins's character, </p>
<p>connection, and situation in life, I am convinced that my </p>
<p>chance of happiness with him is as fair as most people can </p>
<p>boast on entering the marriage state." </p>
<p>Elizabeth quietly answered "Undoubtedly;" and after an </p>
<p>awkward pause, they returned to the rest of the family. </p>
<p>Charlotte did not stay much longer, and Elizabeth was then </p>
<p>left to reflect on what she had heard. It was a long time before </p>
<p>she became at all reconciled to the idea of so unsuitable a </p>
<p>match. The strangeness of Mr. Collins's making two offers of </p>
<p>marriage within three days was nothing in comparison of his </p>
<p>being now accepted. She had always felt that Charlotte's </p>
<p>opinion of matrimony was not exactly like her own, but she </p>
<p>had not supposed it to be possible that, when called into </p>
<p>action, she would have sacrificed every better feeling to </p>
<p>worldly advantage. Charlotte the wife of Mr. Collins was a </p>
<p>most humiliating picture! And to the pang of a friend </p>
<p>disgracing herself and sunk in her esteem, was added the </p>
<p>distressing conviction that it was impossible for that friend to </p>
<p>be tolerably happy in the lot she had chosen. </p>
<heading>Chapter 23. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth was sitting with her mother and sisters, reflecting </p>
<p>on what she had heard, and doubting whether she was </p>
<p>authorised to mention it, when Sir William Lucas himself </p>
<p>appeared, sent by his daughter, to announce her engagement </p>
<p>to the family. With many compliments to them, and much </p>
<p>self-gratulation on the prospect of a connection between the </p>
<p>houses, he unfolded the matter--to an audience not merely </p>
<p>wondering, but incredulous; for Mrs. Bennet, with more </p>
<p>perseverance than politeness, protested he must be entirely </p>
<p>mistaken; and Lydia, always unguarded and often uncivil, </p>
<p>boisterously exclaimed: </p>
<p>"Good Lord! Sir William, how can you tell such a story? </p>
<p>Do not you know that Mr. Collins wants to marry Lizzy?" </p>
<p>Nothing less than the complaisance of a courtier could </p>
<p>have borne without anger such treatment; but Sir William's </p>
<p>good breeding carried him through it all; and though he </p>
<p>begged leave to be positive as to the truth of his information, </p>
<p>he listened to all their impertinence with the most forbearing </p>
<p>courtesy. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, feeling it incumbent on her to relieve him from </p>
<p>so unpleasant a situation, now put herself forward to confirm </p>
<p>his account, by mentioning her prior knowledge of it from </p>
<p>Charlotte herself; and endeavoured to put a stop to the </p>
<p>exclamations of her mother and sisters by the earnestness of </p>
<p>her congratulations to Sir William, in which she was readily </p>
<p>joined by Jane, and by making a variety of remarks on the </p>
<p>happiness that might be expected from the match, the </p>
<p>excellent character of Mr. Collins, and the convenient distance </p>
<p>of Hunsford from London. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was in fact too much overpowered to say a </p>
<p>great deal while Sir William remained; but no sooner had he </p>
<p>left them than her feelings found a rapid vent. In the first </p>
<p>place, she persisted in disbelieving the whole of the matter; </p>
<p>secondly, she was very sure that Mr. Collins had been taken </p>
<p>in; thirdly, she trusted that they would never be happy </p>
<p>together; and fourthly, that the match might be broken off. </p>
<p>Two inferences, however, were plainly deduced from the </p>
<p>whole: one, that Elizabeth was the real cause of the mischief; </p>
<p>and the other that she herself had been barbarously misused </p>
<p>by them all; and on these two points she principally dwelt </p>
<p>during the rest of the day. Nothing could console and nothing </p>
<p>could appease her. Nor did that day wear out her resentment. </p>
<p>A week elapsed before she could see Elizabeth without </p>
<p>scolding her, a month passed away before she could speak to </p>
<p>Sir William or Lady Lucas without being rude, and many </p>
<p>months were gone before she could at all forgive their </p>
<p>daughter. </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet's emotions were much more tranquil on the </p>
<p>occasion, and such as he did experience he pronounced to be </p>
<p>of a most agreeable sort; for it gratified him, he said, to </p>
<p>discover that Charlotte Lucas, whom he had been used to </p>
<p>think tolerably sensible, was as foolish as his wife, and more </p>
<p>foolish than his daughter! </p>
<p>Jane confessed herself a little surprised at the match; but </p>
<p>she said less of her astonishment than of her earnest desire for </p>
<p>their happiness; nor could Elizabeth persuade her to consider </p>
<p>it as improbable. Kitty and Lydia were far from envying Miss </p>
<p>Lucas, for Mr. Collins was only a clergyman; and it affected </p>
<p>them in no other way than as a piece of news to spread at </p>
<p>Meryton. </p>
<p>Lady Lucas could not be insensible of triumph on being </p>
<p>able to retort on Mrs. Bennet the comfort of having a daughter </p>
<p>well married; and she called at Longbourn rather oftener than </p>
<p>usual to say how happy she was, though Mrs. Bennet's sour </p>
<p>looks and ill-natured remarks might have been enough to </p>
<p>drive happiness away. </p>
<p>Between Elizabeth and Charlotte there was a restraint </p>
<p>which kept them mutually silent on the subject; and Elizabeth </p>
<p>felt persuaded that no real confidence could ever subsist </p>
<p>between them again. Her disappointment in Charlotte made </p>
<p>her turn with fonder regard to her sister, of whose rectitude </p>
<p>and delicacy she was sure her opinion could never be shaken, </p>
<p>and for whose happiness she grew daily more anxious, as </p>
<p>Bingley had now been gone a week and nothing more was </p>
<p>heard of his return. </p>
<p>Jane had sent Caroline an early answer to her letter, and </p>
<p>was counting the days till she might reasonably hope to hear </p>
<p>again. The promised letter of thanks from Mr. Collins arrived </p>
<p>on Tuesday, addressed to their father, and written with all the </p>
<p>solemnity of gratitude which a twelvemonth's abode in the </p>
<p>family might have prompted. After discharging his conscience </p>
<p>on that head, he proceeded to inform them, with many </p>
<p>rapturous expressions, of his happiness in having obtained the </p>
<p>affection of their amiable neighbour, Miss Lucas, and then </p>
<p>explained that it was merely with the view of enjoying her </p>
<p>society that he had been so ready to close with their kind wish </p>
<p>of seeing him again at Longbourn, whither he hoped to be </p>
<p>able to return on Monday fortnight; for Lady Catherine, he </p>
<p>added, so heartily approved his marriage, that she wished it to </p>
<p>take place as soon as possible, which he trusted would be an </p>
<p>unanswerable argument with his amiable Charlotte to name an </p>
<p>early day for making him the happiest of men. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins's return into Hertfordshire was no longer a </p>
<p>matter of pleasure to Mrs. Bennet. On the contrary, she was as </p>
<p>much disposed to complain of it as her husband. It was very </p>
<p>strange that he should come to Longbourn instead of to Lucas </p>
<p>Lodge; it was also very inconvenient and exceedingly </p>
<p>troublesome. She hated having visitors in the house while her </p>
<p>health was so indifferent, and lovers were of all people the </p>
<p>most disagreeable. Such were the gentle murmurs of Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, and they gave way only to the greater distress of Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley's continued absence. </p>
<p>Neither Jane nor Elizabeth were comfortable on this </p>
<p>subject. Day after day passed away without bringing any other </p>
<p>tidings of him than the report which shortly prevailed in </p>
<p>Meryton of his coming no more to Netherfield the whole w </p>
<p>inter; a report which highly incensed Mrs. Bennet, and which </p>
<p>she never failed to contradict as a most scandalous falsehood. </p>
<p>Even Elizabeth began to fear--not that Bingley was </p>
<p>indifferent--but that his sisters would be successful in keeping </p>
<p>him away. Unwilling as she was to admit an idea so </p>
<p>destructive of Jane's happiness, and so dishonorable to the </p>
<p>stability of her lover, she could not prevent its frequently </p>
<p>occurring. The united efforts of his two unfeeling sisters and </p>
<p>of his overpowering friend, assisted by the attractions of Miss </p>
<p>Darcy and the amusements of London might be too much, she </p>
<p>feared, for the strength of his attachment. </p>
<p>As for Jane, HER anxiety under this suspense was, of </p>
<p>course, more painful than Elizabeth's, but whatever she felt </p>
<p>she was desirous of concealing, and between herself and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, therefore, the subject was never alluded to. But as </p>
<p>no such delicacy restrained her mother, an hour seldom passed </p>
<p>in which she did not talk of Bingley, express her impatience </p>
<p>for his arrival, or even require Jane to confess that if he did </p>
<p>not come back she would think herself very ill used. It needed </p>
<p>all Jane's steady mildness to bear these attacks with tolerable </p>
<p>tranquillity. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins returned most punctually on Monday fortnight, </p>
<p>but his reception at Longbourn was not quite so gracious as it </p>
<p>had been on his first introduction. He was too happy, </p>
<p>however, to need much attention; and luckily for the others, </p>
<p>the business of love-making relieved them from a great deal </p>
<p>of his company. The chief of every day was spent by him at </p>
<p>Lucas Lodge, and he sometimes returned to Longbourn only </p>
<p>in time to make an apology for his absence before the family </p>
<p>went to bed. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet was really in a most pitiable state. The very </p>
<p>mention of anything concerning the match threw her into an </p>
<p>agony of ill-humour, and wherever she went she was sure of </p>
<p>hearing it talked of. The sight of Miss Lucas was odious to </p>
<p>her. As her successor in that house, she regarded her with </p>
<p>jealous abhorrence. Whenever Charlotte came to see them, </p>
<p>she concluded her to be anticipating the hour of possession; </p>
<p>and whenever she spoke in a low voice to Mr. Collins, was </p>
<p>convinced that they were talking of the Longbourn estate, and </p>
<p>resolving to turn herself and her daughters out of the house, as </p>
<p>soon as Mr. Bennet were dead. She complained bitterly of all </p>
<p>this to her husband. </p>
<p>"Indeed, Mr. Bennet," said she, "it is very hard to think </p>
<p>that Charlotte Lucas should ever be mistress of this house, </p>
<p>that I should be forced to make way for HER, and live to see </p>
<p>her take her place in it!" </p>
<p>"My dear, do not give way to such gloomy thoughts. Let </p>
<p>us hope for better things. Let us flatter ourselves that I may be </p>
<p>the survivor." </p>
<p>This was not very consoling to Mrs. Bennet, and therefore, </p>
<p>instead of making any answer, she went on as before. </p>
<p>"I cannot bear to think that they should have all this estate. </p>
<p>If it was not for the entail, I should not mind it." </p>
<p>"What should not you mind?" </p>
<p>"I should not mind anything at all." </p>
<p>"Let us be thankful that you are preserved from a state of </p>
<p>such insensibility." </p>
<p>"I never can be thankful, Mr. Bennet, for anything about </p>
<p>the entail. How anyone could have the conscience to entail </p>
<p>away an estate from one's </p>
<p>own daughters, I cannot </p>
<p>understand; and all for the sake of Mr. Collins too! Why </p>
<p>should HE have it more than anybody else?" </p>
<p>"I leave it to yourself to determine," said Mr. Bennet. </p>
<heading>Chapter 24. </heading>
<p>Miss Bingley's letter arrived, and put an end to doubt. The </p>
<p>very first sentence conveyed the assurance of their being all </p>
<p>settled in London for the winter, and concluded with her </p>
<p>brother's regret at not having had time to pay his respects to </p>
<p>his friends in Hertfordshire before he left the country. </p>
<p>Hope was over, entirely over; and when Jane could attend </p>
<p>to the rest of the letter, she found little, except the professed </p>
<p>affection of the writer, that could give her any comfort. Miss </p>
<p>Darcy's praise occupied the chief of it. Her many attractions </p>
<p>were again dwelt on, and Caroline boasted joyfully of their </p>
<p>increasing intimacy, and ventured to predict the </p>
<p>accomplishment of the wishes which had been unfolded in her </p>
<p>former letter. She wrote also with great pleasure of her </p>
<p>brother's being an inmate of Mr. Darcy's house, and </p>
<p>mentioned with raptures some plans of the latter with regard </p>
<p>to new furniture. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, to whom Jane very soon communicated the </p>
<p>chief of all this, heard it in silent indignation. Her heart was </p>
<p>divided between concern for her sister, and resentment against </p>
<p>all others. To Caroline's assertion of her brother's being partial </p>
<p>to Miss Darcy she paid no credit. That he was really fond of </p>
<p>Jane, she doubted no more than she had ever done; and much </p>
<p>as she had always been disposed to like him, she could not </p>
<p>think without anger, hardly without contempt, on that easiness </p>
<p>of temper, that want of proper resolution, which now made </p>
<p>him the slave of his designing friends, and led him to sacrifice </p>
<p>of his own happiness to the caprice of their inclination. Had </p>
<p>his own happiness, however, been the only sacrifice, he might </p>
<p>have been allowed to sport with it in whatever manner he </p>
<p>thought best, but her sister's was involved in it, as she thought </p>
<p>he must be sensible himself. It was a subject, in short, on </p>
<p>which reflection would be long indulged, and must be </p>
<p>unavailing. She could think of nothing else; and yet whether </p>
<p>Bingley's regard had really died away, or were suppressed by </p>
<p>his friends' interference; whether he had been aware of Jane's </p>
<p>attachment, or whether it had escaped his observation; </p>
<p>whatever were the case, though her opinion of him must be </p>
<p>materially affected by the difference, her sister's situation </p>
<p>remained the same, her peace equally wounded. </p>
<p>A day or two passed before Jane had courage to speak of </p>
<p>her feelings to Elizabeth; but at last, on Mrs. Bennet's leaving </p>
<p>them together, after a longer irritation than usual about </p>
<p>Netherfield and its master, she could not help saying: </p>
<p>"Oh, that my dear mother had more command over herself! </p>
<p>She can have no idea of the pain she gives me by her </p>
<p>continual reflections on him. But I will not repine. It cannot </p>
<p>last long. He will be forgot, and we shall all be as we were </p>
<p>before." </p>
<p>Elizabeth looked at her sister with incredulous solicitude, </p>
<p>but said nothing. </p>
<p>"You doubt me," cried Jane, slightly colouring; "indeed, </p>
<p>you have no reason. He may live in my memory as the most </p>
<p>amiable man of my acquaintance, but that is all. I have </p>
<p>nothing either to hope or fear, and nothing to reproach him </p>
<p>with. Thank God! I have not THAT pain. A little time, </p>
<p>therefore--I shall certainly try to get the better." </p>
<p>With a stronger voice she soon added, "I have this comfort </p>
<p>immediately, that it has not been more than an error of fancy </p>
<p>on my side, and that it has done no harm to anyone but </p>
<p>myself." </p>
<p>"My dear Jane!" exclaimed Elizabeth, "you are too good. </p>
<p>Your sweetness and disinterestedness are really angelic; I do </p>
<p>not know what to say to you. I feel as if I had never done you </p>
<p>justice, or loved you as you deserve." </p>
<p>Miss Bennet eagerly disclaimed all extraordinary merit, </p>
<p>and threw back the praise on her sister's warm affection. </p>
<p>"Nay," said Elizabeth, "this is not fair. YOU wish to think </p>
<p>all the world respectable, and are hurt if I speak ill of </p>
<p>anybody. I only want to think YOU perfect, and you set </p>
<p>yourself against it. Do not be afraid of my running into any </p>
<p>excess, of my encroaching on your privilege of universal </p>
<p>good-will. You need not. There are few people whom I really </p>
<p>love, and still fewer of whom I think well. The more I see of </p>
<p>the world, the more am I dissatisfied with it; and every day </p>
<p>confirms my belief of the inconsistency of all human </p>
<p>characters, and of the little dependence that can be placed on </p>
<p>the appearance of merit or sense. I have met with two </p>
<p>instances lately, one I will not mention; the other is Charlotte's </p>
<p>marriage. It is unaccountable! In every view it is </p>
<p>unaccountable!" </p>
<p>"My dear Lizzy, do not give way to such feelings as these. </p>
<p>They will ruin your happiness. You do not make allowance </p>
<p>enough for difference of situation and temper. Consider Mr. </p>
<p>Collins's respectability, </p>
<p>and Charlotte's steady, prudent </p>
<p>character. Remember that she is one of a large family; that as </p>
<p>to fortune, it is a most eligible match; and be ready to believe, </p>
<p>for everybody's sake, that she may feel something like regard </p>
<p>and esteem for our cousin." </p>
<p>"To oblige you, I would try to believe almost anything, but </p>
<p>no one else could be benefited by such a belief as this; for </p>
<p>were I persuaded that Charlotte had any regard for him, I </p>
<p>should only think worse of her understanding than I now do of </p>
<p>her heart. My dear Jane, Mr. Collins is a conceited, pompous, </p>
<p>narrow-minded, silly man; you know he is, as well as I do; </p>
<p>and you must feel, as well as I do, that the woman who </p>
<p>married him cannot have a proper way of thinking. You shall </p>
<p>not defend her, though it is Charlotte Lucas. You shall not, for </p>
<p>the sake of one individual, change the meaning of principle </p>
<p>and integrity, nor endeavour to persuade yourself or me, that </p>
<p>selfishness is prudence, and insensibility of danger security </p>
<p>for happiness." </p>
<p>"I must think your language too strong in speaking of </p>
<p>both," replied Jane; "and I hope you will be convinced of it by </p>
<p>seeing them happy together. But enough of this. You alluded </p>
<p>to something else. You mentioned TWO instances. I cannot </p>
<p>misunderstand you, but I entreat you, dear Lizzy, not to pain </p>
<p>me by thinking THAT PERSON to blame, and saying your </p>
<p>opinion of him is sunk. We must not be so ready to fancy </p>
<p>ourselves intentionally injured. We must not expect a lively </p>
<p>young man to be always so guarded and circumspect. It is </p>
<p>very often nothing but our own vanity that deceives us. </p>
<p>Women fancy admiration means more than it does." </p>
<p>"And men take care that they should." </p>
<p>"If it is designedly done, they cannot be justified; but I </p>
<p>have no idea of there being so much design in the world as </p>
<p>some persons imagine." </p>
<p>"I am far from attributing any part of Mr. Bingley's </p>
<p>conduct to design," said Elizabeth; "but without scheming to </p>
<p>do wrong, or to make others unhappy, there may be error, and </p>
<p>there may be misery. Thoughtlessness, want of attention to </p>
<p>other people's feelings, and want of resolution, will do the </p>
<p>business." </p>
<p>"And do you impute it to either of those?" </p>
<p>"Yes; to the last. But if I go on, I shall displease you by </p>
<p>saying what I think of persons you esteem. Stop me whilst </p>
<p>you can." </p>
<p>"You persist, then, in supposing his sisters influence him?" </p>
<p>"Yes, in conjunction with his friend." </p>
<p>"I cannot believe it. Why should they try to influence him? </p>
<p>They can only wish his happiness; and if he is attached to me, </p>
<p>no other woman can secure it." </p>
<p>"Your first position is false. They may wish many things </p>
<p>besides his happiness; they may wish his increase of wealth </p>
<p>and consequence; they may wish him to marry a girl who has </p>
<p>all the importance of money, great connections, and pride." </p>
<p>"Beyond a doubt, they DO wish him to choose Miss </p>
<p>Darcy," replied Jane; "but this may be from better feelings </p>
<p>than you are supposing. They have known her much longer </p>
<p>than they have known me; no wonder if they love her better. </p>
<p>But, whatever may be their own wishes, it is very unlikely </p>
<p>they should have opposed their brother's. What sister would </p>
<p>think herself at liberty to do it, unless there were something </p>
<p>very objectionable? If they believed him attached to me, they </p>
<p>would not try to part us; if he were so, they could not succeed. </p>
<p>By supposing such an affection, you make everybody acting </p>
<p>unnaturally and wrong, and me most unhappy. Do not distress </p>
<p>me by the idea. I am not ashamed of having been mistaken-- </p>
<p>or, at least, it is light, it is nothing in comparison of what I </p>
<p>should feel in thinking ill of him or his sisters. Let me take it </p>
<p>in the best light, in the light in which it may be understood." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not oppose such a wish; and from this time </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley's name was scarcely ever mentioned between </p>
<p>them. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet still continued to wonder and repine at his </p>
<p>returning no more, and though a day seldom passed in which </p>
<p>Elizabeth did not account for it clearly, there was little chance </p>
<p>of her ever considering it with less perplexity. Her daughter </p>
<p>endeavoured to convince her of what she did not believe </p>
<p>herself, that his attentions to Jane had been merely the effect </p>
<p>of a common and transient liking, which ceased when he saw </p>
<p>her no more; but though the probability of the statement was </p>
<p>admitted at the time, she had the same story to repeat every </p>
<p>day. Mrs. Bennet's best comfort was that Mr. Bingley must be </p>
<p>down again in the summer. </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet treated the matter differently. "So, Lizzy," said </p>
<p>he one day, </p>
<p>"your sister is crossed in love, I find. I </p>
<p>congratulate her. Next to being married, a girl likes to be </p>
<p>crossed a little in love now and then. It is something to think </p>
<p>of, and it gives her a sort of distinction among her </p>
<p>companions. When is your turn to come? You will hardly bear </p>
<p>to be long outdone by Jane. Now is your time. Here are </p>
<p>officers enough in Meryton to disappoint all the young ladies </p>
<p>in the country. Let Wickham be YOUR man. He is a pleasant </p>
<p>fellow, and would jilt you creditably." </p>
<p>"Thank you, sir, but a less agreeable man would satisfy </p>
<p>me. We must not all expect Jane's good fortune." </p>
<p>"True," said Mr. Bennet, "but it is a comfort to think that </p>
<p>whatever of that kind may befall </p>
<p>you, </p>
<p>you have an </p>
<p>affectionate mother who will make the most of it." </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham's society was of material service in </p>
<p>dispelling the gloom which the late perverse occurrences had </p>
<p>thrown on many of the Longbourn family. They saw him </p>
<p>often, and to his other recommendations was now added that </p>
<p>of general unreserve. The whole of what Elizabeth had </p>
<p>already heard, his claims on Mr. Darcy, and all that he had </p>
<p>suffered from him, was now openly acknowledged and </p>
<p>publicly canvassed; and everybody was pleased to know how </p>
<p>much they had always disliked Mr. Darcy before they had </p>
<p>known anything of the matter. </p>
<p>Miss Bennet was the only creature who could suppose </p>
<p>there might be any extenuating circumstances in the case, </p>
<p>unknown to the society of Hertfordshire; her mild and steady </p>
<p>candour always pleaded for allowances, and urged the </p>
<p>possibility of mistakes--but by everybody else Mr. Darcy was </p>
<p>condemned as the worst of men. </p>
<heading>Chapter 25. </heading>
<p>After a week spent in professions of love and schemes of </p>
<p>felicity, Mr. Collins was called from his amiable Charlotte by </p>
<p>the arrival of Saturday. The pain of separation, however, </p>
<p>might be alleviated on his side, by preparations for the </p>
<p>reception of his bride; as he had reason to hope, that shortly </p>
<p>after his return into Hertfordshire, the day would be fixed that </p>
<p>was to make him the happiest of men. He took leave of his </p>
<p>relations at Longbourn with as much solemnity as before; </p>
<p>wished his fair cousins health and happiness again, and </p>
<p>promised their father another letter of thanks. </p>
<p>On the following Monday, Mrs. Bennet had the pleasure of </p>
<p>receiving her brother and his wife, who came as usual to </p>
<p>spend the Christmas at Longbourn. Mr. Gardiner was a </p>
<p>sensible, gentlemanlike man, greatly superior to his sister, as </p>
<p>well by nature as education. The Netherfield ladies would </p>
<p>have had difficulty in believing that a man who lived by trade, </p>
<p>and within view of his own warehouses, could have been so </p>
<p>well-bred and agreeable. Mrs. Gardiner, who was several </p>
<p>years younger than Mrs. Bennet and Mrs. Phillips, was an </p>
<p>amiable, intelligent, elegant woman, and a great favourite </p>
<p>with all her Longbourn nieces. Between the two eldest and </p>
<p>herself especially, there subsisted a particular regard. They </p>
<p>had frequently been staying with her in town. </p>
<p>The first part of Mrs. Gardiner's business on her arrival </p>
<p>was to distribute her presents and describe the newest fash </p>
<p>ions. When this was done she had a less active part to play. It </p>
<p>became her turn to listen. Mrs. Bennet had many grievances to </p>
<p>relate, and much to complain of. They had all been very ill- </p>
<p>used since she last saw her sister. Two of her girls had been </p>
<p>upon the point of marriage, and after all there was nothing in </p>
<p>it. </p>
<p>"I do not blame Jane," she continued, "for Jane would have </p>
<p>got Mr. Bingley if she could. But Lizzy! Oh, sister! It is very </p>
<p>hard to think that she might have been Mr. Collins's wife by </p>
<p>this time, had it not been for her own perverseness. He made </p>
<p>her an offer in this very room, and she refused him. The </p>
<p>consequence of it is, that Lady Lucas will have a daughter </p>
<p>married before I have, and that the Longbourn estate is just as </p>
<p>much entailed as ever. The Lucases are very artful people </p>
<p>indeed, sister. They are all for what they can get. I am sorry to </p>
<p>say it of them, but so it is. It makes me very nervous and </p>
<p>poorly, to be thwarted so in my own family, and to have </p>
<p>neighbours who think of themselves before anybody else. </p>
<p>However, your coming just at this time is the greatest of </p>
<p>comforts, and I am very glad to hear what you tell us, of long </p>
<p>sleeves." </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner, to whom the chief of this news had been </p>
<p>given before, in the course of Jane and Elizabeth's </p>
<p>correspondence with her, made her sister a slight answer, and, </p>
<p>in compassion to her nieces, turned the conversation. </p>
<p>When alone with Elizabeth afterwards, she spoke more on </p>
<p>the subject. "It seems likely to have been a desirable match for </p>
<p>Jane," said she. "I am sorry it went off. But these things </p>
<p>happen so often! A young man, such as you describe Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, so easily falls in love with a pretty girl for a few </p>
<p>weeks, and when accident separates them, so easily forgets </p>
<p>her, that these sort of inconsistencies are very frequent." </p>
<p>"An excellent consolation in its way," said Elizabeth, "but </p>
<p>it will not do for US. We do not suffer by ACCIDENT. It </p>
<p>does not often happen that the interference of friends will </p>
<p>persuade a young man of independent fortune to think no </p>
<p>more of a girl whom he was violently in love with only a few </p>
<p>days before." </p>
<p>"But that expression of 'violently in love' is so hackneyed, </p>
<p>so doubtful, so indefinite, that it gives me very little idea. It is </p>
<p>as often applied to feelings which arise from a half-hour's </p>
<p>acquaintance, as to a real, strong attachment. Pray, how </p>
<p>VIOLENT WAS Mr. Bingley's love?" </p>
<p>"I never saw a more promising inclination; he was growing </p>
<p>quite inattentive to other people, and wholly engrossed by her. </p>
<p>Every time they met, it was more decided and remarkable. At </p>
<p>his own ball he offended two or three young ladies, by not </p>
<p>asking them to dance; and I spoke to him twice myself, </p>
<p>without receiving an answer. Could there be finer symptoms? </p>
<p>Is not general incivility the very essence of love?" </p>
<p>"Oh, yes!--of that kind of love which I suppose him to </p>
<p>have felt. Poor Jane! I am sorry for her, because, with her </p>
<p>disposition, she may not get over it immediately. It had better </p>
<p>have happened to YOU, Lizzy; you would have laughed </p>
<p>yourself out of it sooner. But do you think she would be </p>
<p>prevailed upon to go back with us? Change of scene might be </p>
<p>of service--and perhaps a little relief from home may be as </p>
<p>useful as anything." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was exceedingly pleased with this proposal, and </p>
<p>felt persuaded of her sister's ready acquiescence. </p>
<p>"I hope," added Mrs. Gardiner, "that no consideration with </p>
<p>regard to this young man will influence her. We live in so </p>
<p>different a part of town, all our connections are so different, </p>
<p>and, as you well know, we go out so little, that it is very </p>
<p>improbable that they should meet at all, unless he really </p>
<p>comes to see her." </p>
<p>"And THAT is quite impossible; for he is now in the </p>
<p>custody of his friend, and Mr. Darcy would no more suffer </p>
<p>him to call on Jane in such a part of London! My dear aunt, </p>
<p>how could you think of it? Mr. Darcy may perhaps have </p>
<p>HEARD of such a place as Gracechurch Street, but he would </p>
<p>hardly think a month's ablution enough to cleanse him from </p>
<p>its impurities, were he once to enter it; and depend upon it, </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley never stirs without him." </p>
<p>"So much the better. I hope they will not meet at all. But </p>
<p>does not Jane correspond with his sister? SHE will not be able </p>
<p>to help calling." </p>
<p>"She will drop the acquaintance entirely." </p>
<p>But in spite of the certainty in which Elizabeth affected to </p>
<p>place this point, as well as the still more interesting one of </p>
<p>Bingley's being withheld from seeing Jane, she felt a </p>
<p>solicitude on the subject which convinced </p>
<p>her, on </p>
<p>examination, that she did not consider it entirely hopeless. It </p>
<p>was possible, and sometimes she thought it probable, that his </p>
<p>affection might be reanimated, and the influence of his friends </p>
<p>successfully combated by the more natural influence of Jane's </p>
<p>attractions. </p>
<p>Miss Bennet accepted her aunt's invitation with pleasure; </p>
<p>and the Bingleys were no otherwise in her thoughts at the </p>
<p>same time, than as she hoped by Caroline's not living in the </p>
<p>same house with her brother, she might occasionally spend a </p>
<p>morning with her, without any danger of seeing him. </p>
<p>The Gardiners stayed a week at Longbourn; and what with </p>
<p>the Phillipses, the Lucases, and the officers, there was not a </p>
<p>day without its engagement. Mrs. Bennet had so carefully </p>
<p>provided for the entertainment of her brother and sister, that </p>
<p>they did not once sit down to a family dinner. When the </p>
<p>engagement was for home, some of the officers always made </p>
<p>part of it--of which officers Mr. Wickham was sure to be one; </p>
<p>and on these occasion, Mrs. Gardiner, rendered suspicious by </p>
<p>Elizabeth's warm commendation, narrowly observed them </p>
<p>both. Without supposing them, from what she saw, to be very </p>
<p>seriously in love, their preference of each other was plain </p>
<p>enough to make her a little uneasy; and she resolved to speak </p>
<p>to Elizabeth on the subject before she left Hertfordshire, and </p>
<p>represent to her the imprudence of encouraging such an </p>
<p>attachment. </p>
<p>To Mrs. Gardiner, Wickham had one means of affording </p>
<p>pleasure, unconnected with his general powers. About ten or a </p>
<p>dozen years ago, before her marriage, she had spent a </p>
<p>considerable time in that very part of Derbyshire to which he </p>
<p>belonged. They had, therefore, many acquaintances in </p>
<p>common; and though Wickham had been little there since the </p>
<p>death of Darcy's father, it was yet in his power to give her </p>
<p>fresher intelligence of her former friends than she had been in </p>
<p>the way of procuring. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner had seen Pemberley, and known the late Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy by character perfectly well. Here consequently was an </p>
<p>inexhaustible subject of discourse. In comparing her </p>
<p>recollection of Pemberley with the minute description which </p>
<p>Wickham could give, and in bestowing her tribute of praise on </p>
<p>the character of its late possessor, she was delighting both him </p>
<p>and herself. On being made acquainted with the present Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy's treatment of him, she tried to remember some of that </p>
<p>gentleman's reputed disposition when quite a lad which might </p>
<p>agree with it, and was confident at last that she recollected </p>
<p>having heard Mr. Fitzwilliam Darcy formerly spoken of as a </p>
<p>very proud, ill-natured boy. </p>
<header>Ebd </header>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 26. </heading>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner's caution to Elizabeth was punctually and </p>
<p>kindly given on the first favourable opportunity of speaking to </p>
<p>her alone; after honestly telling her what she thought, she thus </p>
<p>went on: </p>
<p>"You are too sensible a girl, Lizzy, to fall in love merely </p>
<p>because you are warned against it; and, therefore, I am not </p>
<p>afraid of speaking openly. Seriously, I would have you be on </p>
<p>your guard. Do not involve yourself or endeavour to involve </p>
<p>him in an affection which the want of fortune would make so </p>
<p>very imprudent. I have nothing to say against HIM; he is a </p>
<p>most interesting young man; and if he had the fortune he </p>
<p>ought to have, I should think you could not do better. But as it </p>
<p>is, you must not let your fancy run away with you. You have </p>
<p>sense, and we all expect you to use it. Your father would </p>
<p>depend on YOUR resolution and good conduct, I am sure. </p>
<p>You must not disappoint your father." </p>
<p>"My dear aunt, this is being serious indeed." </p>
<p>"Yes, and I hope to engage you to be serious likewise." </p>
<p>"Well, then, you need not be under any alarm. I will take </p>
<p>care of myself, and of Mr. Wickham too. He shall not be in </p>
<p>love with me, if I can prevent it." </p>
<p>"Elizabeth, you are not serious now." </p>
<p>"I beg your pardon, I will try again. At present I am not in </p>
<p>love with Mr. Wickham; no, I certainly am not. But he is, </p>
<p>beyond all comparison, the most agreeable man I ever saw-- </p>
<p>and if he becomes really attached to me--I believe it will be </p>
<p>better that he should not. I see the imprudence of it. Oh! </p>
<p>THAT abominable Mr. Darcy! My father's opinion of me </p>
<p>does me the greatest honour, and I should be miserable to </p>
<p>forfeit it. My father, however, is partial to Mr. Wickham. In </p>
<p>short, my dear aunt, I should be very sorry to be the means of </p>
<p>making any of you unhappy; but since we see every day that </p>
<p>where there is affection, young people are seldom withheld by </p>
<p>immediate want of fortune from entering into engagements </p>
<p>with each other, how can I promise to be wiser than so many </p>
<p>of my fellow-creatures if I am tempted, or how am I even to </p>
<p>know that it would be wisdom to resist? All that I can promise </p>
<p>you, therefore, is not to be in a hurry. I will not be in a hurry </p>
<p>to believe myself his first object. When I am in company with </p>
<p>him, I will not be wishing. In short, I will do my best." </p>
<p>"Perhaps it will be as well if you discourage his coming </p>
<p>here so very often. At least, you should not REMIND you </p>
<p>mother of inviting him." </p>
<p>"As I did the other day," said Elizabeth with a conscious </p>
<p>smile: "very true, it will be wise in me to refrain from THAT. </p>
<p>But do not imagine that he is always here so often. It is on </p>
<p>your account that he has been so frequently invited this week. </p>
<p>You know my mother's ideas as to the necessity of constant </p>
<p>company for her friends. But really, and upon my honour, I </p>
<p>will try to do what I think to be the wisest; and now I hope </p>
<p>you are satisfied." </p>
<p>Her aunt assured her that she was, and Elizabeth having </p>
<p>thanked her for the kindness of her hints, they parted; a </p>
<p>wonderful instance of advice being given on such a point, </p>
<p>without being resented. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins returned into Hertfordshire soon after it had </p>
<p>been quitted by the Gardiners and Jane; but as he took up his </p>
<p>abode with the Lucases, his arrival was no great </p>
<p>inconvenience to Mrs. Bennet. His marriage was now fast </p>
<p>approaching, and she was at length so far resigned as to think </p>
<p>it inevitable, and even repeatedly to say, in an ill-natured tone, </p>
<p>that she "WISHED they might be happy." Thursday was to be </p>
<p>the wedding day, and on Wednesday Miss Lucas paid her </p>
<p>farewell visit; and when she rose to take leave, Elizabeth, </p>
<p>ashamed of her mother's ungracious and reluctant good </p>
<p>wishes, and sincerely affected herself, accompanied her out of </p>
<p>the room. As they went downstairs together, Charlotte said: </p>
<p>"I shall depend on hearing from you very often, Eliza." </p>
<p>"THAT you certainly shall." </p>
<p>"And I have another favour to ask you. Will you come and </p>
<p>see me?" </p>
<p>"We shall often meet, I hope, in Hertfordshire." </p>
<p>"I am not likely to leave Kent for some time. Promise me, </p>
<p>therefore, to come to Hunsford." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not refuse, though she foresaw little </p>
<p>pleasure in the visit. </p>
<p>"My father and Maria are coming to me in March," added </p>
<p>Charlotte, "and I hope you will consent to be of the party. </p>
<p>Indeed, Eliza, you will be as welcome as either of them." </p>
<p>The wedding took place; the bride and bridegroom set off </p>
<p>for Kent from the church door, and everybody had as much to </p>
<p>say, or to hear, on the subject as usual. Elizabeth soon heard </p>
<p>from her friend; and their correspondence was as regular and </p>
<p>frequent as it had ever been; that it should be equally </p>
<p>unreserved was impossible. Elizabeth could never address her </p>
<p>without feeling that all the comfort of intimacy was over, and </p>
<p>though determined not to slacken as a correspondent, it was </p>
<p>for the sake of what had been, rather than what was. </p>
<p>Charlotte's first letters were received with a good deal of </p>
<p>eagerness; there could not but be curiosity to know how she </p>
<p>would speak of her new home, how she would like Lady </p>
<p>Catherine, and how happy she would dare pronounce herself </p>
<p>to be; though, when the letters were read, Elizabeth felt that </p>
<p>Charlotte expressed herself on every point exactly as she </p>
<p>might have foreseen. She wrote cheerfully, seemed </p>
<p>surrounded with comforts, and mentioned nothing which she </p>
<p>could not praise. The house, furniture, neighbourhood, and </p>
<p>roads, were all to her taste, and Lady Catherine's behaviour </p>
<p>was most friendly and obliging. It was Mr. Collins's picture of </p>
<p>Hunsford and Rosings rationally softened; and Elizabeth </p>
<p>perceived that she must wait for her own visit there to know </p>
<p>the rest. </p>
<p>Jane had already written a few lines to her sister to </p>
<p>announce their safe arrival in London; and when she wrote </p>
<p>again, Elizabeth hoped it would be in her power to say </p>
<p>something of the Bingleys. </p>
<p>Her impatience for this second letter was as well rewarded </p>
<p>as impatience generally is. Jane had been a week in town </p>
<p>without either seeing or hearing from Caroline. She accounted </p>
<p>for it, however, by supposing that her last letter to her friend </p>
<p>from Longbourn had by some accident been lost. </p>
<p>"My aunt," she continued, "is going to-morrow into that </p>
<p>part of the town, and I shall take the opportunity of calling in </p>
<p>Grosvenor Street." </p>
<p>She wrote again when the visit was paid, and she had seen </p>
<p>Miss Bingley. "I did not think Caroline in spirits," were her </p>
<p>words, "but she was very glad to see me, and reproached me </p>
<p>for giving her no notice of my coming to London. I was right, </p>
<p>therefore, my last letter had never reached her. I inquired after </p>
<p>their brother, of course. He was well, but so much engaged </p>
<p>with Mr. Darcy that they scarcely ever saw him. I found that </p>
<p>Miss Darcy was expected to dinner. I wish I could see her. My </p>
<p>visit was not long, as Caroline and Mrs. Hurst were going out. </p>
<p>I dare say I shall see them soon here." </p>
<p>Elizabeth shook her head over this letter. It convinced her </p>
<p>that accident only could discover to Mr. Bingley her sister's </p>
<p>being in town. </p>
<p>Four weeks passed away, and Jane saw nothing of him. </p>
<p>She endeavoured to persuade herself that she did not regret it; </p>
<p>but she could no longer be blind to Miss Bingley's inattention. </p>
<p>After waiting at home every morning for a fortnight, and </p>
<p>inventing every evening a fresh excuse for her, the visitor did </p>
<p>at last appear; but the shortness of her stay, and yet more, the </p>
<p>alteration of her manner would allow Jane to deceive herself </p>
<p>no longer. The letter which she wrote on this occasion to her </p>
<p>sister will prove what she felt. </p>
<p>"My dearest Lizzy will, I am sure, be incapable of </p>
<p>triumphing in her better judgement, at my expense, when I </p>
<p>confess myself to have been entirely deceived in Miss </p>
<p>Bingley's regard for me. But, my dear sister, though the event </p>
<p>has proved you right, do not think me obstinate if I still assert </p>
<p>that, considering what her behaviour was, my confidence was </p>
<p>as natural as your suspicion. I do not at all comprehend her </p>
<p>reason for wishing to be intimate with me; but if the same </p>
<p>circumstances were to happen again, I am sure I should be </p>
<p>deceived again. Caroline did not return my visit till yesterday; </p>
<p>and not a note, not a line, did I receive in the meantime. When </p>
<p>she did come, it was very evident that she had no pleasure in </p>
<p>it; she made a slight, formal apology, for not calling before, </p>
<p>said not a word of wishing to see me again, and was in every </p>
<p>respect so altered a creature, that when she went away I was </p>
<p>perfectly resolved to continue the acquaintance no longer. I </p>
<p>pity, though I cannot help blaming her. She was very wrong in </p>
<p>singling me out as she did; I can safely say that every advance </p>
<p>to intimacy began on her side. But I pity her, because she </p>
<p>must feel that she has been acting wrong, and because I am </p>
<p>very sure that anxiety for her brother is the cause of it. I need </p>
<p>not explain myself farther; and though WE know this anxiety </p>
<p>to be quite needless, yet if she feels it, it will easily account </p>
<p>for her behaviour to me; and so deservedly dear as he is to his </p>
<p>sister, whatever anxiety she must feel on his behalf is natural </p>
<p>and amiable. I cannot but wonder, however, at her having any </p>
<p>such fears now, because, if he had at all cared about me, we </p>
<p>must have met, long ago. He knows of my being in town, I am </p>
<p>certain, from something she said herself; and yet it would </p>
<p>seem, by her manner of talking, as if she wanted to persuade </p>
<p>herself that he is really partial to Miss Darcy. I cannot </p>
<p>understand it. If I were not afraid of judging harshly, I should </p>
<p>be almost tempted to say that there is a strong appearance of </p>
<p>duplicity in all this. But I will endeavour to banish every </p>
<p>painful thought, and think only of what will make me happy-- </p>
<p>your affection, and the invariable kindness of my dear uncle </p>
<p>and aunt. Let me hear from you very soon. Miss Bingley said </p>
<p>something of his never returning to Netherfield again, of </p>
<p>giving up the house, but not with any certainty. We had better </p>
<p>not mention it. I am extremely glad that you have such </p>
<p>pleasant accounts from our friends at Hunsford. Pray go to see </p>
<p>them, with Sir William and Maria. I am sure you will be very </p>
<p>comfortable there.--Yours, etc." </p>
<p>This letter gave Elizabeth some pain; but her spirits </p>
<p>returned as she considered that Jane would no longer be </p>
<p>duped, by the sister at least. All expectation from the brother </p>
<p>was now absolutely over. She would not even wish for a </p>
<p>renewal of his attentions. His character sunk on every review </p>
<p>of it; and as a punishment for him, as well as a possible </p>
<p>advantage to Jane, she seriously hoped he might really soon </p>
<p>marry Mr. Darcy's sister, as by Wickham's account, she would </p>
<p>make him abundantly regret what he had thrown away. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner about this time reminded Elizabeth of her </p>
<p>promise concerning that gentleman, and required information; </p>
<p>and Elizabeth had such to send as might rather give </p>
<p>contentment to her aunt than to herself. His apparent partiality </p>
<p>had subsided, his attentions were over, he was the admirer of </p>
<p>some one else. Elizabeth was watchful enough to see it all, but </p>
<p>she could see it and write of it without material pain. Her </p>
<p>heart had been but slightly touched, and her vanity was </p>
<p>satisfied with believing that SHE would have been his only </p>
<p>choice, had fortune permitted it. The sudden acquisition of ten </p>
<p>thousand pounds was the most remarkable charm of the young </p>
<p>lady to whom he was now rendering himself agreeable; but </p>
<p>Elizabeth, less clear-sighted perhaps in this case than in </p>
<p>Charlotte's, did not quarrel with him for his wish of </p>
<p>independence. Nothing, on the contrary, could be more </p>
<p>natural; and while able to suppose that it cost him a few </p>
<p>struggle to relinquish her, she was ready to allow it a wise and </p>
<p>desirable measure for both, and could very sincerely wish him </p>
<p>happy. </p>
<p>All this was acknowledged to Mrs. Gardiner; and after </p>
<p>relating the circumstances, she thus went on: "I am now </p>
<p>convinced, my dear aunt, that I have never been much in love; </p>
<p>for had I really experienced that pure and elevating passion, I </p>
<p>should at present detest his very name, and wish him all </p>
<p>manner of evil. But my feelings are not only cordial towards </p>
<p>HIM; they are even impartial towards Miss King. I cannot </p>
<p>find out that I hate her at all, or that I am in the least unwilling </p>
<p>to think her a very good sort of girl. There can be no love in </p>
<p>all this. My watchfulness has been effectual; and though I </p>
<p>certainly should be a more interesting object to all my </p>
<p>acquaintances were I distractedly in love with him, I cannot </p>
<p>say that I regret my comparative insignificance. Importance </p>
<p>may sometimes be purchased too dearly. Kitty and Lydia take </p>
<p>his defection much more to heart than I do. They are young in </p>
<p>the ways of the world, and not yet open to the mortifying </p>
<p>conviction that handsome young men must have something to </p>
<p>live on as well as the plain." </p>
<heading>Chapter 27. </heading>
<p>With no greater events than these in the Longbourn family, </p>
<p>and otherwise diversified by little beyond the walks to </p>
<p>Meryton, sometimes dirty and sometimes cold, did January </p>
<p>and February pass away. March was to take Elizabeth to </p>
<p>Hunsford. She had not at first thought very seriously of going </p>
<p>thither; but Charlotte, she soon found, was depending on the </p>
<p>plan and she gradually learned to consider it herself with </p>
<p>greater pleasure as well as greater certainty. Absence had </p>
<p>increased her desire of seeing Charlotte again, and weakened </p>
<p>her disgust of Mr. Collins. There was novelty in the scheme, </p>
<p>and as, with such a mother and such uncompanionable sisters, </p>
<p>home could not be faultless, a little change was not </p>
<p>unwelcome for its own sake. The journey would moreover </p>
<p>give her a peep at Jane; and, in short, as the time drew near, </p>
<p>she would have been very sorry for any delay. Everything, </p>
<p>however, went on smoothly, and was finally settled according </p>
<p>to Charlotte's first sketch. She was to accompany Sir William </p>
<p>and his second daughter. The improvement of spending a </p>
<p>night in London was added in time, and the plan became </p>
<p>perfect as plan could be. </p>
<p>The only pain was in leaving her father, who would </p>
<p>certainly miss her, and who, when it came to the point, so </p>
<p>little liked her going, that he told her to write to him, and </p>
<p>almost promised to answer her letter. </p>
<p>The farewell between herself and Mr. Wickham was </p>
<p>perfectly friendly; on his side even more. His present pursuit </p>
<p>could not make him forget that Elizabeth had been the first to </p>
<p>excite and to deserve his attention, the first to listen and to </p>
<p>pity, the first to be admired; and in his manner of bidding her </p>
<p>adieu, wishing her every enjoyment, reminding her of what </p>
<p>she was to expect in Lady Catherine de Bourgh, and trusting </p>
<p>their opinion of her--their opinion of everybody--would </p>
<p>always coincide, there was a solicitude, an interest which she </p>
<p>felt must ever attach her to him with a most sincere regard; </p>
<p>and she parted from him convinced that, whether married or </p>
<p>single, he must always be her model of the amiable and </p>
<p>pleasing. </p>
<p>Her fellow-travellers the next day were not of a kind to </p>
<p>make her think him less agreeable. Sir William Lucas, and his </p>
<p>daughter Maria, a good-humoured girl, but as empty-headed </p>
<p>as himself, had nothing to say that could be worth hearing, </p>
<p>and were listened to with about as much delight as the rattle of </p>
<p>the chaise. Elizabeth loved absurdities, but she had known Sir </p>
<p>William's too long. He could tell her nothing new of the </p>
<p>wonders of his presentation and knighthood; and his civilities </p>
<p>were worn out, like his information. </p>
<p>It was a journey of only twenty-four miles, and they began </p>
<p>it so early as to be in Gracechurch Street by noon. As they </p>
<p>drove to Mr. Gardiner's door, Jane was at a drawing-room </p>
<p>window watching their arrival; when they entered the passage </p>
<p>she was there to welcome them, and Elizabeth, looking </p>
<p>earnestly in her face, was pleased to see it healthful and lovely </p>
<p>as ever. On the stairs were a troop of little boys and girls, </p>
<p>whose eagerness for their cousin's appearance would not </p>
<p>allow them to wait in the drawing-room, and whose shyness, </p>
<p>as they had not seen her for a twelvemonth, prevented their </p>
<p>coming lower. All was joy and kindness. The day passed most </p>
<p>pleasantly away; the morning in bustle and shopping, and the </p>
<p>evening at one of the theatres. </p>
<p>Elizabeth then contrived to sit by her aunt. Their first </p>
<p>object was her sister; and she was more grieved than </p>
<p>astonished to hear, in reply to her minute inquiries, that </p>
<p>though Jane always struggled to support her spirits, there were </p>
<p>periods of dejection. It was reasonable, however, to hope that </p>
<p>they would not continue long. Mrs. Gardiner gave her the </p>
<p>particulars also of Miss Bingley's visit in Gracechurch Street, </p>
<p>and repeated conversations occurring at different times </p>
<p>between Jane and herself, which proved that the former had, </p>
<p>from her heart, given up the acquaintance. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner then rallied her niece on Wickham's </p>
<p>desertion, and complimented her on bearing it so well. </p>
<p>"But my dear Elizabeth," she added, "what sort of girl is </p>
<p>Miss King? I should be sorry to think our friend mercenary." </p>
<p>"Pray, my dear aunt, what is the difference in matrimonial </p>
<p>affairs, between the mercenary and the prudent motive? </p>
<p>Where does discretion end, and avarice begin? Last Christmas </p>
<p>you were afraid of his marrying me, because it would be </p>
<p>imprudent; and now, because he is trying to get a girl with </p>
<p>only ten thousand pounds, you want to find out that he is </p>
<p>mercenary." </p>
<p>"If you will only tell me what sort of girl Miss King is, I </p>
<p>shall know what to think." </p>
<p>"She is a very good kind of girl, I believe. I know no harm </p>
<p>of her." </p>
<p>"But he paid her not the smallest attention till her </p>
<p>grandfather's death made her mistress of this fortune." </p>
<p>"No--what should he? If it were not allowable for him to </p>
<p>gain MY affections because I had no money, what occasion </p>
<p>could there be for making love to a girl whom he did not care </p>
<p>about, and who was equally poor?" </p>
<p>"But there seems an indelicacy in directing his attentions </p>
<p>towards her so soon after this event." </p>
<p>"A man in distressed circumstances has not time for all </p>
<p>those elegant decorums which other people may observe. If </p>
<p>SHE does not object to it, why should WE?" </p>
<p>"HER not objecting does not justify HIM. It only shows </p>
<p>her being deficient in something herself--sense or feeling." </p>
<p>"Well," cried Elizabeth, "have it as you choose. HE shall </p>
<p>be mercenary, and SHE shall be foolish." </p>
<p>"No, Lizzy, that is what I do NOT choose. I should be </p>
<p>sorry, you know, to think ill of a young man who has lived so </p>
<p>long in Derbyshire." </p>
<p>"Oh! if that is all, I have a very poor opinion of young men </p>
<p>who live in Derbyshire; and their intimate friends who live in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire are not much better. I am sick of them all. </p>
<p>Thank Heaven! I am going to-morrow where I shall find a </p>
<p>man who has not one agreeable quality, who has neither </p>
<p>manner nor sense to recommend him. Stupid men are the only </p>
<p>ones worth knowing, after all." </p>
<p>"Take care, Lizzy; that speech savours strongly of </p>
<p>disappointment." </p>
<p>Before they were separated by the conclusion of the play, </p>
<p>she had the unexpected happiness of an invitation to </p>
<p>accompany her uncle and aunt in a tour of pleasure which </p>
<p>they proposed taking in the summer. </p>
<p>"We have not determined how far it shall carry us," said </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner, "but, perhaps, to the Lakes." </p>
<p>No scheme could have been more agreeable to Elizabeth, </p>
<p>and her acceptance of the invitation was most ready and </p>
<p>grateful. </p>
<p>"Oh, my dear, dear aunt," she rapturously cried, "what </p>
<p>delight! what felicity! You give me fresh life and vigour. </p>
<p>Adieu to disappointment and spleen. What are young men to </p>
<p>rocks and mountains? Oh! what hours of transport we shall </p>
<p>spend! And when we DO return, it shall not be like other </p>
<p>travellers, without being able to give one accurate idea of </p>
<p>anything. We WILL know where we have gone--we WILL </p>
<p>recollect what we have seen. Lakes, mountains, and rivers </p>
<p>shall not be jumbled together in our imaginations; nor when </p>
<p>we attempt to describe any particular scene, will we begin </p>
<p>quarreling about its relative situation. Let OUR first effusions </p>
<p>be less insupportable than those of the generality </p>
<p>of </p>
<p>travellers." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 28. </heading>
<p>Every object in the next day's journey was new and </p>
<p>interesting to Elizabeth; and her spirits were in a state of </p>
<p>enjoyment; for she had seen her sister looking so well as to </p>
<p>banish all fear for her health, and the prospect of her northern </p>
<p>tour was a constant source of delight. </p>
<p>When they left the high road for the lane to Hunsford, </p>
<p>every eye was in search of the Parsonage, and every turning </p>
<p>expected to bring it in view. The palings of Rosings Park was </p>
<p>their boundary </p>
<p>on </p>
<p>one </p>
<p>side. Elizabeth smiled at the </p>
<p>recollection of all that she had heard of its inhabitants. </p>
<p>At length the Parsonage was discernible. The garden </p>
<p>sloping to the road, the house standing in it, the green pales, </p>
<p>and the laurel hedge, everything declared they were arriving. </p>
<p>Mr. Collins and Charlotte appeared at the door, and the </p>
<p>carriage stopped at the small gate which led by a short gravel </p>
<p>walk to the house, amidst the nods and smiles of the whole </p>
<p>party. In a moment they were all out of the chaise, rejoicing at </p>
<p>the sight of each other. Mrs. Collins welcomed her friend with </p>
<p>the liveliest pleasure, and Elizabeth was more and more </p>
<p>satisfied with coming when she found herself so affectionately </p>
<p>received. She saw instantly that her cousin's manners were not </p>
<p>altered by his marriage; his formal civility was just what it </p>
<p>had been, and he detained her some minutes at the gate to hear </p>
<p>and satisfy his inquiries after all her family. They were then, </p>
<p>with no other delay than his pointing out the neatness of the </p>
<p>entrance, taken into the house; and as soon as they were in the </p>
<p>parlour, he welcomed them a second time, with ostentatious </p>
<p>formality to his humble abode, and punctually repeated all his </p>
<p>wife's offers of refreshment. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was prepared to see him in his glory; and she </p>
<p>could not help in fancying that in displaying the good </p>
<p>proportion of the room, its aspect and its furniture, he </p>
<p>addressed himself particularly to her, as if wishing to make </p>
<p>her feel what she had lost in refusing him. But though </p>
<p>everything seemed neat and comfortable, she was not able to </p>
<p>gratify him by any sigh of repentance, and rather looked with </p>
<p>wonder at her friend that she could have so cheerful an air </p>
<p>with such a companion. When Mr. Collins said anything of </p>
<p>which his wife might reasonably be ashamed, which certainly </p>
<p>was not unseldom, she involuntarily turned her eye on </p>
<p>Charlotte. Once or twice she could discern a faint blush; but in </p>
<p>general Charlotte wisely did not hear. After sitting long </p>
<p>enough to admire every article of furniture in the room, from </p>
<p>the sideboard to the fender, to give an account of their </p>
<p>journey, and of all that had happened in London, Mr. Collins </p>
<p>invited them to take a stroll in the garden, which was large </p>
<p>and well laid out, and to the cultivation of which he attended </p>
<p>himself. To work in this garden was one of his most </p>
<p>respectable pleasures; and Elizabeth admired the command of </p>
<p>countenance with which Charlotte talked of the healthfulness </p>
<p>of the exercise, and owned she encouraged it as much as </p>
<p>possible. Here, leading the way through every walk and cross </p>
<p>walk, and scarcely allowing them an interval to utter the </p>
<p>praises he asked for, every view was pointed out with a </p>
<p>minuteness which left beauty entirely behind. He could </p>
<p>number the fields in every direction, and could tell how many </p>
<p>tress there were in the most distant clump. But of all the views </p>
<p>which his garden, or which the country or kingdom could </p>
<p>boast, none were to be compared with the prospect of </p>
<p>Rosings, afforded by an opening in the trees that bordered the </p>
<p>park nearly opposite the front of his house. It was a handsome </p>
<p>modern building, well situated on rising ground. </p>
<p>From his garden, Mr. Collins would have led them round </p>
<p>his two meadows; but the ladies, not having shoes to </p>
<p>encounter the remains of a white frost, turned back; and while </p>
<p>Sir William accompanied him, Charlotte took her sister and </p>
<p>friend over the house, extremely well pleased, probably, to </p>
<p>have the opportunity of showing it without her husband's help. </p>
<p>It was rather small, but well built and convenient; and </p>
<p>everything was fitted up and arranged with a neatness and </p>
<p>consistency of which Elizabeth gave Charlotte all the credit. </p>
<p>When Mr. Collins could be forgotten, there was really an air </p>
<p>of great comfort throughout, and by Charlotte's </p>
<p>evident </p>
<p>enjoyment of it, Elizabeth supposed he must be often </p>
<p>forgotten. </p>
<p>She had already learnt that Lady Catherine was still in the </p>
<p>country. It was spoken of again while they were at dinner, </p>
<p>when Mr. Collins joining in, observed: </p>
<p>"Yes, Miss Elizabeth, you will have the honour of seeing </p>
<p>Lady Catherine de Bourgh on the ensuing Sunday at church, </p>
<p>and I need not say you will be delighted with her. She is all </p>
<p>affability and condescension, and I doubt not but you will be </p>
<p>honoured with some portion of her notice when service is </p>
<p>over. I have scarcely any hesitation in saying she will include </p>
<p>you and my sister Maria in every invitation with which she </p>
<p>honours us during your stay here. Her behaviour to my dear </p>
<p>Charlotte is charming. We dine at Rosings twice every week, </p>
<p>and are never allowed to walk home. Her ladyship's carriage </p>
<p>is regularly ordered for us. I SHOULD say, one of her </p>
<p>ladyship's carriages, for she has several." </p>
<p>"Lady Catherine is a very respectable, sensible woman </p>
<p>indeed," added Charlotte, "and a most attentive neighbour." </p>
<p>"Very true, my dear, that is exactly what I say. She is the </p>
<p>sort of woman whom one cannot regard with too much </p>
<p>deference." </p>
<p>The evening was spent chiefly in talking over </p>
<p>Hertfordshire news, and telling again what had already been </p>
<p>written; and when it closed, Elizabeth, in the solitude of her </p>
<p>chamber, had to meditate upon Charlotte's </p>
<p>degree of </p>
<p>contentment, to understand her address in guiding, and </p>
<p>composure in bearing with, her husband, and to acknowledge </p>
<p>that it was all done very well. She had also to anticipate how </p>
<p>her visit would pass, the quiet tenor of their usual </p>
<p>employments, the vexatious interruptions of Mr. Collins, and </p>
<p>the gaieties of their intercourse with Rosings. A lively </p>
<p>imagination soon settled it all. </p>
<p>About the middle of the next day, as she was in her room </p>
<p>getting ready for a walk, a sudden noise below seemed to </p>
<p>speak the whole house in confusion; and, after listening a </p>
<p>moment, she heard somebody running upstairs in a violent </p>
<p>hurry, and calling loudly after her. She opened the door and </p>
<p>met Maria in the landing place, who, breathless with agitation, </p>
<p>cried out- </p>
<p>"Oh, my dear Eliza! pray make haste and come into the </p>
<p>dining-room, for there is such a sight to be seen! I will not tell </p>
<p>you what it is. Make haste, and come down this moment." </p>
<p>Elizabeth asked questions in vain; Maria would tell her </p>
<p>nothing more, and down they ran into the dining-room, which </p>
<p>fronted the lane, in quest of this wonder; It was two ladies </p>
<p>stopping in a low phaeton at the garden gate. </p>
<p>"And is this all?" cried Elizabeth. "I expected at least that </p>
<p>the pigs were got into the garden, and here is nothing but </p>
<p>Lady Catherine and her daughter." </p>
<p>"La! my dear," said Maria, quite shocked at the mistake, "it </p>
<p>is not Lady Catherine. The old lady is Mrs. Jenkinson, who </p>
<p>lives with them; the other is Miss de Bourgh. Only look at </p>
<p>her. She is quite a little creature. Who would have thought </p>
<p>that she could be so thin and small?" </p>
<p>"She is abominably rude to keep Charlotte out of doors in </p>
<p>all this wind. Why does she not come in?" </p>
<p>"Oh, Charlotte says she hardly ever does. It is the greatest </p>
<p>of favours when Miss de Bourgh comes in." </p>
<p>"I like her appearance," said Elizabeth, struck with other </p>
<p>ideas. "She looks sickly and cross. Yes, she will do for him </p>
<p>very well. She will make him a very proper wife." </p>
<p>Mr. Collins and Charlotte were both standing at the gate in </p>
<p>conversation with the ladies; and Sir William, to Elizabeth's </p>
<p>high diversion, was stationed in the doorway, in earnest </p>
<p>contemplation of the greatness before him, and constantly </p>
<p>bowing whenever Miss de Bourgh looked that way. </p>
<p>At length there was nothing more to be said; the ladies </p>
<p>drove on, and the others returned into the house. Mr. Collins </p>
<p>no sooner saw the two girls than he began to congratulate </p>
<p>them on their good fortune, which Charlotte explained by </p>
<p>letting them know that the whole party was asked to dine at </p>
