<p>Rosings the next day. </p>
<heading>Chapter 29. </heading>
<p>Mr. Collins's triumph, in consequence of this invitation, </p>
<p>was complete. The power of displaying the grandeur of his </p>
<p>patroness to his wondering visitors, and of letting them see </p>
<p>her civility towards himself and his wife, was exactly what he </p>
<p>had wished for; and that an opportunity of doing it should be </p>
<p>given so soon, was such an instance of Lady Catherine's </p>
<p>condescension, as he knew not how to admire enough. </p>
<p>"I confess," said he, "that I should not have been at all </p>
<p>surprised by her ladyship's asking us on Sunday to drink tea </p>
<p>and spend the evening at Rosings. I rather expected, from my </p>
<p>knowledge of her affability, that it would happen. But who </p>
<p>could have foreseen such an attention as this? Who could have </p>
<p>imagined that we should receive an invitation to dine there (an </p>
<p>invitation, moreover, including the whole party) </p>
<p>so </p>
<p>immediately after your arrival!" </p>
<p>"I am the less surprised at what has happened," replied Sir </p>
<p>William, "from that knowledge of what the manners of the </p>
<p>great really are, which my situation in life has allowed me to </p>
<p>acquire. About the court, such instances of elegant breeding </p>
<p>are not uncommon." </p>
<p>Scarcely anything was talked of the whole day or next </p>
<p>morning but their visit to Rosings. Mr. Collins was carefully </p>
<p>instructing them in what they were to expect, that the sight of </p>
<p>such rooms, so many servants, and so splendid a dinner, might </p>
<p>not wholly overpower them. </p>
<p>When the ladies were separating for the toilette, he said to </p>
<p>Elizabeth- </p>
<p>"Do not make yourself uneasy, my dear cousin, about your </p>
<p>apparel. Lady Catherine is far from requiring that elegance of </p>
<p>dress in us which becomes herself and her daughter. I would </p>
<p>advise you merely to put on whatever of your clothes is </p>
<p>superior to the rest--there is no occasion for anything more. </p>
<p>Lady Catherine will not think the worse of you for being </p>
<p>simply dressed. She likes to have the distinction of rank </p>
<p>preserved." </p>
<p>While they were dressing, he came two or three times to </p>
<p>their different doors, to recommend their being quick, as Lady </p>
<p>Catherine very much objected to be kept waiting for her </p>
<p>dinner. Such formidable accounts of her ladyship, and her </p>
<p>manner of living, quite frightened Maria Lucas who had been </p>
<p>little used to company, and she looked forward to her </p>
<p>introduction at Rosings with as much apprehension as her </p>
<p>father had done to his presentation at St. James's. </p>
<p>As the weather was fine, they had a pleasant walk of about </p>
<p>half a mile across the park. Every park has its beauty and its </p>
<p>prospects; and Elizabeth saw much to be pleased with, though </p>
<p>she could not be in such raptures as Mr. Collins expected the </p>
<p>scene to inspire, and was but slightly affected by his </p>
<p>enumeration of the windows in front of the house, and his </p>
<p>relation of what the glazing altogether had originally cost Sir </p>
<p>Lewis de Bourgh. </p>
<p>When they ascended the steps to the hall, Maria's alarm </p>
<p>was every moment increasing, and even Sir William did not </p>
<p>look perfectly calm. Elizabeth's courage did not fail her. She </p>
<p>had heard nothing of Lady Catherine that spoke her awful </p>
<p>from any extraordinary talents or miraculous virtue, and the </p>
<p>mere stateliness of money or rank she thought she could </p>
<p>witness without trepidation. </p>
<p>From the entrance-hall, of which Mr. Collins pointed out, </p>
<p>with a rapturous air, the fine proportion and the finished </p>
<p>ornaments, they followed the servants through an ante- </p>
<p>chamber, to the room where Lady Catherine, her daughter, </p>
<p>and Mrs. Jenkinson were sitting. Her ladyship, with great </p>
<p>condescension, arose to receive them; and as Mrs. Collins had </p>
<p>settled it with her husband that the office of introduction </p>
<p>should be hers, it was performed in a proper manner, without </p>
<p>any of those apologies and thanks which he would have </p>
<p>thought necessary. </p>
<p>In spite of having been at St. James's Sir William was so </p>
<p>completely awed by the grandeur surrounding him, that he </p>
<p>had but just courage enough to make a very low bow, and take </p>
<p>his seat without saying a word; and his daughter, frightened </p>
<p>almost out of her senses, sat on the edge of her chair, not </p>
<p>knowing which way to look. Elizabeth found herself quite </p>
<p>equal to the scene, and could observe the three ladies before </p>
<p>her composedly. Lady Catherine was a tall, large woman, with </p>
<p>strongly-marked features, which might once have been </p>
<p>handsome. Her air was not conciliating, nor was her manner </p>
<p>of receiving them such as to make her visitors forget their </p>
<p>inferior rank. She was not rendered formidable by silence; but </p>
<p>whatever she said was spoken in so authoritative a tone, as </p>
<p>marked her self-importance, and brought Mr. Wickham </p>
<p>immediately to Elizabeth's mind; and from the observation of </p>
<p>the day altogether, she believed Lady Catherine to be exactly </p>
<p>what he represented. </p>
<p>When, after examining the mother, in whose countenance </p>
<p>and deportment she soon found some resemblance of Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, she turned her eyes on the daughter, she could almost </p>
<p>have joined in Maria's astonishment at her being so thin and </p>
<p>so small. There was neither in figure nor face any likeness </p>
<p>between the ladies. Miss de Bourgh was pale and sickly; her </p>
<p>features, though not plain, were insignificant; and she spoke </p>
<p>very little, except in a low voice, to Mrs. Jenkinson, in whose </p>
<p>appearance there was nothing remarkable, and who was </p>
<p>entirely engaged in listening to what she said, and placing a </p>
<p>screen in the proper direction before her eyes. </p>
<p>After sitting a few minutes, they were all sent to one of the </p>
<p>windows to admire the view, Mr. Collins attending them to </p>
<p>point out its beauties, and Lady Catherine kindly informing </p>
<p>them that it was much better worth looking at in the summer. </p>
<p>The dinner was exceedingly handsome, and there were all </p>
<p>the servants and all the articles of plate which Mr. Collins had </p>
<p>promised; and, as he had likewise foretold, he took his seat at </p>
<p>the bottom of the table, by her ladyship's desire, and looked as </p>
<p>if he felt that life could furnish nothing greater. He carved, </p>
<p>and ate, and praised with delighted alacrity; and every dish </p>
<p>was commended, first by him and then by Sir William, who </p>
<p>was now enough recovered to echo whatever his son-in-law </p>
<p>said, in a manner which Elizabeth wondered Lady Catherine </p>
<p>could bear. But Lady Catherine seemed gratified by their </p>
<p>excessive admiration, and gave most gracious smiles, </p>
<p>especially when any dish on the table proved a novelty to </p>
<p>them. The party did not supply much conversation. Elizabeth </p>
<p>was ready to speak whenever there was an opening, but she </p>
<p>was seated between Charlotte and Miss de Bourgh--the former </p>
<p>of whom was engaged in listening to Lady Catherine, and the </p>
<p>latter said not a word to her all dinner-time. Mrs. Jenkinson </p>
<p>was chiefly employed in watching how little Miss de Bourgh </p>
<p>ate, pressing her to try some other dish, and fearing she was </p>
<p>indisposed. Maria thought speaking out of the question, and </p>
<p>the gentlemen did nothing but eat and admire. </p>
<p>When the ladies returned to the drawing-room, there was </p>
<p>little to be done but to hear Lady Catherine talk, which she did </p>
<p>without any intermission till coffee came in, delivering her </p>
<p>opinion on every subject in so decisive a manner, as proved </p>
<p>that she was not used to have her judgement controverted. She </p>
<p>inquired into Charlotte's domestic concerns familiarly and </p>
<p>minutely, gave her a great deal of advice as to the </p>
<p>management of them all; told her how everything ought to be </p>
<p>regulated in so small a family as hers, and instructed her as to </p>
<p>the care of her cows and her poultry. Elizabeth found that </p>
<p>nothing was beneath this great lady's attention, which could </p>
<p>furnish her with an occasion of dictating to others. In the </p>
<p>intervals of her discourse with Mrs. Collins, she addressed a </p>
<p>variety of questions to Maria and Elizabeth, but especially to </p>
<p>the latter, of whose connections she knew the least, and who </p>
<p>she observed to Mrs. Collins was a very genteel, pretty kind </p>
<p>of girl. She asked her, at different times, how many sisters she </p>
<p>had, whether they were older or younger than herself, whether </p>
<p>any of them were likely to be married, whether they were </p>
<p>handsome, where they had been educated, what carriage her </p>
<p>father kept, and what had been her mother's maiden name? </p>
<p>Elizabeth felt all the impertinence of her questions but </p>
<p>answered them very composedly. Lady Catherine then </p>
<p>observed, </p>
<p>"Your father's estate is entailed on Mr. Collins, I think. For </p>
<p>your sake," turning to Charlotte, "I am glad of it; but </p>
<p>otherwise I see no occasion for entailing estates from the </p>
<p>female line. It was not thought necessary in Sir Lewis de </p>
<p>Bourgh's family. Do you play and sing, Miss Bennet?" </p>
<p>"A little." </p>
<p>"Oh! then--some time or other we shall be happy to hear </p>
<p>you. Our instrument is a capital one, probably superior to--- </p>
<p>You shall try it some day. Do your sisters play and sing?" </p>
<p>"One of them does." </p>
<p>"Why did not you all learn? You ought all to have learned. </p>
<p>The Miss Webbs all play, and their father has not so good an </p>
<p>income as yours. Do you draw?" </p>
<p>"No, not at all." </p>
<p>"What, none of you?" </p>
<p>"Not one." </p>
<p>"That is very strange. But I suppose you had no </p>
<p>opportunity. Your mother should have taken you to town </p>
<p>every spring for the benefit of masters." </p>
<p>"My mother would have had no objection, but my father </p>
<p>hates London." </p>
<p>"Has your governess left you?" </p>
<p>"We never had any governess." </p>
<p>"No governess! How was that possible? Five daughters </p>
<p>brought up at home without a governess! I never heard of </p>
<p>such a thing. Your mother must have been quite a slave to </p>
<p>your education." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could hardly help smiling as she assured her that </p>
<p>had not been the case. </p>
<p>"Then, who taught you? who attended to you? Without a </p>
<p>governess, you must have been neglected." </p>
<p>"Compared with some families, I believe we were; but </p>
<p>such of us as wished to learn never wanted the means. We </p>
<p>were always encouraged to read, and had all the masters that </p>
<p>were necessary. Those who chose to be idle, certainly might." </p>
<p>"Aye, no doubt; but that is what a governess will prevent, </p>
<p>and if I had known your mother, I should have advised her </p>
<p>most strenuously to engage one. I always say that nothing is </p>
<p>to be done in education without steady and regular instruction, </p>
<p>and nobody but a governess can give it. It is wonderful how </p>
<p>many families I have been the means of supplying in that way. </p>
<p>I am always glad to get a young person well placed out. Four </p>
<p>nieces of Mrs. Jenkinson are most delightfully situated </p>
<p>through my means; and it was but the other day that I </p>
<p>recommended another young person, who was merely </p>
<p>accidentally mentioned to me, and the family are quite </p>
<p>delighted with her. Mrs. Collins, did I tell you of Lady </p>
<p>Metcalf's calling yesterday to thank me? She finds Miss Pope </p>
<p>a treasure. 'Lady Catherine,' said she, 'you have given me a </p>
<p>treasure.' Are any of your younger sisters out, Miss Bennet?" </p>
<p>"Yes, ma'am, all." </p>
<p>"All! What, all five out at once? Very odd! And you only </p>
<p>the second. The younger ones out before the elder ones are </p>
<p>married! Your younger sisters must be very young?" </p>
<p>"Yes, my youngest is not sixteen. Perhaps SHE is full </p>
<p>young to be much in company. But really, ma'am, I think it </p>
<p>would be very hard upon younger sisters, that they should not </p>
<p>have their share of society and amusement, because the elder </p>
<p>may not have the means or inclination to marry early. The </p>
<p>last-born has as good a right to the pleasures of youth at the </p>
<p>first. And to be kept back on SUCH a motive! I think it would </p>
<p>not be very likely to promote sisterly affection or delicacy of </p>
<p>mind." </p>
<p>"Upon my word," said her ladyship, "you give your </p>
<p>opinion very decidedly for so young a person. Pray, what is </p>
<p>your age?" </p>
<p>"With three younger sisters grown up," replied Elizabeth, </p>
<p>smiling, "your ladyship can hardly expect me to own it." </p>
<p>Lady Catherine seemed quite astonished at not receiving a </p>
<p>direct answer; and Elizabeth suspected herself to be the first </p>
<p>creature who had ever dared to trifle with so much dignified </p>
<p>impertinence. </p>
<p>"You cannot be more than twenty, I am sure, therefore you </p>
<p>need not conceal your age." </p>
<p>"I am not one-and-twenty." </p>
<p>When the gentlemen had joined them, and tea was over, </p>
<p>the card-tables were placed. Lady Catherine, Sir William, and </p>
<p>Mr. and Mrs. Collins sat down to quadrille; and as Miss de </p>
<p>Bourgh chose to play at cassino, the two girls had the honour </p>
<p>of assisting Mrs. Jenkinson to make up her party. Their table </p>
<p>was superlatively stupid. Scarcely a syllable was uttered that </p>
<p>did not relate to the game, except when Mrs. Jenkinson </p>
<p>expressed her fears of Miss de Bourgh's being too hot or too </p>
<p>cold, or having too much or too little light. A great deal more </p>
<p>passed at the other table. Lady Catherine was generally </p>
<p>speaking--stating the mistakes of the three others, or relating </p>
<p>some anecdote of herself. Mr. Collins was employed in </p>
<p>agreeing to everything her ladyship said, thanking her for </p>
<p>every fish he won, and apologising if he thought he won too </p>
<p>many. Sir William did not say much. He was storing his </p>
<p>memory with anecdotes and noble names. </p>
<p>When Lady Catherine and her daughter had played as long </p>
<p>as they chose, the tables were broken up, the carriage was </p>
<p>offered to Mrs. Collins, gratefully accepted and immediately </p>
<p>ordered. The party then gathered round the fire to hear Lady </p>
<p>Catherine determine what weather they were to have on the </p>
<p>morrow. From these instructions they were summoned by the </p>
<p>arrival of the coach; and with many speeches of thankfulness </p>
<p>on Mr. Collins's side and as many bows on Sir William's they </p>
<p>departed. As soon as they had driven from the door, Elizabeth </p>
<p>was called on by her cousin to give her opinion of all that she </p>
<p>had seen at Rosings, which, for Charlotte's sake, she made </p>
<p>more favourable than it really was. But her commendation, </p>
<p>though costing her some trouble, could by no means satisfy </p>
<p>Mr. Collins, and he was very soon obliged to take her </p>
<p>ladyship's praise into his own hands. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 30. </heading>
<p>Sir William stayed only a week at Hunsford, but his visit </p>
<p>was long enough to convince him of his daughter's being most </p>
<p>comfortably settled, and of her possessing such a husband and </p>
<p>such a neighbour as were not often met with. While Sir </p>
<p>William was with them, Mr. Collins devoted his morning to </p>
<p>driving him out in his gig, and showing him the country; but </p>
<p>when he went away, the whole family returned to their usual </p>
<p>employments, and Elizabeth was thankful to find that they did </p>
<p>not see more of her cousin by the alteration, for the chief of </p>
<p>the time between breakfast and dinner was now passed by him </p>
<p>either at work in the garden or in reading and writing, and </p>
<p>looking out of the window in his own book-room, which </p>
<p>fronted the road. The room in which the ladies sat was </p>
<p>backwards. Elizabeth had at first rather wondered that </p>
<p>Charlotte should not prefer the dining-parlour for common </p>
<p>use; it was a better sized room, and had a more pleasant </p>
<p>aspect; but she soon saw that her friend had an excellent </p>
<p>reason for what she did, for Mr. Collins would undoubtedly </p>
<p>have been much less in his own apartment, had they sat in one </p>
<p>equally lively; and she gave Charlotte credit for the </p>
<p>arrangement. </p>
<p>From the drawing-room they could distinguish nothing in </p>
<p>the lane, and were indebted to Mr. Collins for the knowledge </p>
<p>of what carriages went along, and how often especially Miss </p>
<p>de Bourgh drove by in her phaeton, which he never failed </p>
<p>coming to inform them of, though it happened almost every </p>
<p>day. She not unfrequently stopped at the Parsonage, and had a </p>
<p>few minutes' conversation with Charlotte, but was scarcely </p>
<p>ever prevailed upon to get out. </p>
<p>Very few days passed in which Mr. Collins did not walk to </p>
<p>Rosings, and not many in which his wife did not think it </p>
<p>necessary to go likewise; and till Elizabeth recollected that </p>
<p>there might be other family livings to be disposed of, she </p>
<p>could not understand the sacrifice of so many hours. Now and </p>
<p>then they were honoured with a call from her ladyship, and </p>
<p>nothing escaped her observation that was passing in the room </p>
<p>during these visits. She examined into their employments, </p>
<p>looked at their work, and advised them to do it differently; </p>
<p>found fault with the arrangement of the furniture; or detected </p>
<p>the housemaid in negligence; and if she accepted any </p>
<p>refreshment, seemed to do it only for the sake of finding out </p>
<p>that Mrs. Collins's joints of meat were too large for her </p>
<p>family. </p>
<p>Elizabeth soon perceived, that though this great lady was </p>
<p>not in commission of the peace of the county, she was a most </p>
<p>active magistrate in her own parish, the minutest concerns of </p>
<p>which were carried to her by Mr. Collins; and whenever any </p>
<p>of the cottagers were disposed to be quarrelsome, </p>
<p>discontented, or too poor, she sallied forth into the village to </p>
<p>settle their differences, silence their complaints, and scold </p>
<p>them into harmony and plenty. </p>
<p>The entertainment of dining at Rosings was repeated about </p>
<p>twice a week; and, allowing for the loss of Sir William, and </p>
<p>there being only one card-table in the evening, every such </p>
<p>entertainment was the counterpart of the first. Their other </p>
<p>engagements were few, as the style of living in the </p>
<p>neighbourhood in general was beyond Mr. Collins's reach. </p>
<p>This, however, was no evil to Elizabeth, and upon the whole </p>
<p>she spent her time comfortably enough; there were half-hours </p>
<p>of pleasant conversation with Charlotte, and the weather was </p>
<p>so fine for the time of year that she had often great enjoyment </p>
<p>out of doors. Her favourite walk, and where she frequently </p>
<p>went while the others were calling on Lady Catherine, was </p>
<p>along the open grove which edged that side of the park, where </p>
<p>there was a nice sheltered path, which no one seemed to value </p>
<p>but herself, and where she felt beyond the reach of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine's curiosity. </p>
<p>In this quiet way, the first fortnight of her visit soon passed </p>
<p>away. Easter was approaching, and the week preceding it was </p>
<p>to bring an addition to the family at Rosings, which in so </p>
<p>small a circle must be important. Elizabeth had heard soon </p>
<p>after her arrival that Mr. Darcy was expected there in the </p>
<p>course of a few weeks, and though there were not many of her </p>
<p>acquaintances whom she did not prefer, his coming would </p>
<p>furnish one comparatively new to look at in their Rosings </p>
<p>parties, and she might be amused in seeing how hopeless Miss </p>
<p>Bingley's designs on him were, by his behaviour to his cousin, </p>
<p>for whom he was evidently destined by Lady Catherine, who </p>
<p>talked of his coming with the greatest satisfaction, spoke of </p>
<p>him in terms of the highest admiration, and seemed almost </p>
<p>angry to find that he had already been frequently seen by Miss </p>
<p>Lucas and herself. </p>
<p>His arrival was soon known at the Parsonage; for Mr. </p>
<p>Collins was walking the whole morning within view of the </p>
<p>lodges opening into Hunsford Lane, in order to have the </p>
<p>earliest assurance of it, and after making his bow as the </p>
<p>carriage turned into the Park, hurried home with the great </p>
<p>intelligence. On the following morning he hastened to </p>
<p>Rosings to pay his respects. There were two nephews of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine to require them, for Mr. Darcy had brought with </p>
<p>him a Colonel Fitzwilliam, the younger son of his uncle Lord </p>
<p>----, and, to the great surprise of all the party, when Mr. </p>
<p>Collins returned, the gentleman accompanied him. Charlotte </p>
<p>had seen them from her husband's room, crossing the road, </p>
<p>and immediately running into the other, told the girls what an </p>
<p>honour they might expect, adding: </p>
<p>"I may thank you, Eliza, for this piece of civility. Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy would never have come so soon to wait upon me." </p>
<p>Elizabeth had scarcely time to disclaim all right to the </p>
<p>compliment, before their approach was announced by the </p>
<p>doorbell, and shortly afterwards the three gentlemen entered </p>
<p>the room. Colonel Fitzwilliam, who led the way, was about </p>
<p>thirty, not handsome, but in person and address most truly the </p>
<p>gentleman. Mr. Darcy looked just as he had been used to look </p>
<p>in Hertfordshire--paid his compliments, with his usual </p>
<p>reserve, to Mrs. Collins, and whatever might be his feelings </p>
<p>toward her friend, met her with every appearance </p>
<p>of </p>
<p>composure. Elizabeth merely curtseyed to him without saying </p>
<p>a word. </p>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam entered into conversation directly with </p>
<p>the readiness and ease of a well-bred man, and talked very </p>
<p>pleasantly; but his cousin, after having addressed a slight </p>
<p>observation on the house and garden to Mrs. Collins, sat for </p>
<p>some time without speaking to anybody. At length, however, </p>
<p>his civility was so far awakened as to inquire of Elizabeth </p>
<p>after the health of her family. She answered him in the usual </p>
<p>way, and after a moment's pause, added: </p>
<p>"My eldest sister has been in town these three months. </p>
<p>Have you never happened to see her there?" </p>
<p>She was perfectly sensible that he never had; but she </p>
<p>wished to see whether he would betray any consciousness of </p>
<p>what had passed between the Bingleys and Jane, and she </p>
<p>thought he looked a little confused as he answered that he had </p>
<p>never been so fortunate as to meet Miss Bennet. The subject </p>
<p>was pursued no farther, and the gentlemen soon afterwards </p>
<p>went away. </p>
<heading>Chapter 31. </heading>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam's manners were very much admired at </p>
<p>the Parsonage, and the ladies all felt that he must add </p>
<p>considerably to the pleasures of their engagements at Rosings. </p>
<p>It was some days, however, before they received any </p>
<p>invitation thither-for while there were visitors in the house, </p>
<p>they could not be necessary; and it was not till Easter-day, </p>
<p>almost a week after the gentlemen's arrival, that they were </p>
<p>honoured by such an attention, and then they were merely </p>
<p>asked on leaving church to come there in the evening. For the </p>
<p>last week they had seen very little of Lady Catherine or her </p>
<p>daughter. Colonel Fitzwilliam had called at the Parsonage </p>
<p>more than once during the time, but Mr. Darcy they had seen </p>
<p>only at church. </p>
<p>The invitation was accepted of course, and at a proper hour </p>
<p>they joined the party in Lady Catherine's drawing-room. Her </p>
<p>ladyship received them civilly, but it was plain that their </p>
<p>company was by no means so acceptable as when she could </p>
<p>get nobody else; and she was, in fact, almost engrossed by her </p>
<p>nephews, speaking to them, especially to Darcy, much more </p>
<p>than to any other person in the room. </p>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam seemed really glad to see them; </p>
<p>anything was a welcome relief to him at Rosings; and Mrs. </p>
<p>Collins's pretty friend had moreover caught his fancy very </p>
<p>much. He now seated himself by her, and talked so agreeably </p>
<p>of Kent and Hertfordshire, of travelling and staying at home, </p>
<p>of new books and music, that Elizabeth had never been half so </p>
<p>well enter tained in that room before; and they conversed with </p>
<p>so much spirit and flow, as to draw the attention of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine herself, as well as of Mr. Darcy. HIS eyes had been </p>
<p>soon and repeatedly turned towards them with a look of </p>
<p>curiosity; and that her ladyship, after a while, shared the </p>
<p>feeling, was more openly acknowledged, for she did not </p>
<p>scruple to call out: </p>
<p>"What is that you are saying, Fitzwilliam? What is it you </p>
<p>are talking of? What are you telling Miss Bennet? Let me hear </p>
<p>what it is." </p>
<p>"We are speaking of music, madam," said he, when no </p>
<p>longer able to avoid a reply. </p>
<p>"Of music! Then pray speak aloud. It is of all subjects my </p>
<p>delight. I must have my share in the conversation if you are </p>
<p>speaking of music. There are few people in England, I </p>
<p>suppose, who have more true enjoyment of music than </p>
<p>myself, or a better natural taste. If I had ever learnt, I should </p>
<p>have been a great proficient. And so would Anne, if her health </p>
<p>had allowed her to apply. I am confident that she would have </p>
<p>performed delightfully. How does Georgiana get on, Darcy?" </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy spoke with affectionate praise of his sister's </p>
<p>proficiency. </p>
<p>"I am very glad to hear such a good account of her," said </p>
<p>Lady Catherine; "and pray tell her from me, that she cannot </p>
<p>expect to excel if she does not practice a good deal." </p>
<p>"I assure you, madam," he replied, "that she does not need </p>
<p>such advice. She practises very constantly." </p>
<p>"So much the better. It cannot be done too much; and when </p>
<p>I next write to her, I shall charge her not to neglect it on any </p>
<p>account. I often tell young ladies that no excellence in music </p>
<p>is to be acquired without constant practice. I have told Miss </p>
<p>Bennet several times, that she will never play really well </p>
<p>unless she practises more; and though Mrs. Collins has no </p>
<p>instrument, she is very welcome, as I have often told her, to </p>
<p>come to Rosings every day, and play on the pianoforte in Mrs. </p>
<p>Jenkinson's room. She would be in nobody's way, you know, </p>
<p>in that part of the house." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy looked a little ashamed of his aunt's </p>
<p>breeding, and made no answer. </p>
<p>ill- </p>
<p>When coffee was over, Colonel Fitzwilliam reminded </p>
<p>Elizabeth of having promised to play to him; and she sat down </p>
<p>directly to the instrument. He drew a chair near her. Lady </p>
<p>Catherine listened to half a song, and then talked, as before, to </p>
<p>her other nephew; till the latter walked away from her, and </p>
<p>making with his usual deliberation towards the pianoforte </p>
<p>stationed himself so as to command a full view of the fair </p>
<p>performer's countenance. Elizabeth saw what he was doing, </p>
<p>and at the first convenient pause, turned to him with an arch </p>
<p>smile, and said: </p>
<p>"You mean to frighten me, Mr. Darcy, by coming in all </p>
<p>this state to hear me? I will not be alarmed though your sister </p>
<p>DOES play so well. There is a stubbornness about me that </p>
<p>never can bear to be frightened at the will of others. My </p>
<p>courage always rises at every attempt to intimidate me." </p>
<p>"I shall not say you are mistaken," he replied, "because </p>
<p>you could not really believe me to entertain any design of </p>
<p>alarming you; and I have had the pleasure of your </p>
<p>acquaintance long enough to know that you find great </p>
<p>enjoyment in occasionally professing opinions which in fact </p>
<p>are not your own." </p>
<p>Elizabeth laughed heartily at this picture of herself, and </p>
<p>said to Colonel Fitzwilliam, "Your cousin will give you a very </p>
<p>pretty notion of me, and teach you not to believe a word I say. </p>
<p>I am particularly unlucky in meeting with a person so able to </p>
<p>expose my real character, in a part of the world where I had </p>
<p>hoped to pass myself off with some degree of credit. Indeed, </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, it is very ungenerous in you to mention all that </p>
<p>you knew to my disadvantage in Hertfordshire--and, give me </p>
<p>leave to say, very impolitic too--for it is provoking me to </p>
<p>retaliate, and such things may come out as will shock your </p>
<p>relations to hear." </p>
<p>"I am not afraid of you," said he, smilingly. </p>
<p>"Pray let me hear what you have to accuse him of," cried </p>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam. "I should like to know how he behaves </p>
<p>among strangers." </p>
<p>"You shall hear then--but prepare yourself for something </p>
<p>very dreadful. The first time of my ever seeing him in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire, you must know, was at a ball--and at this ball, </p>
<p>what do you think he did? He danced only four dances, </p>
<p>though gentlemen were scarce; and, to my certain knowledge, </p>
<p>more than one young lady was sitting down in want of a </p>
<p>partner. Mr. Darcy, you cannot deny the fact." </p>
<p>"I had not at that time the honour of knowing any lady in </p>
<p>the assembly beyond my own party." </p>
<p>"True; and nobody can ever be introduced in a ball-room. </p>
<p>Well, Colonel Fitzwilliam, what do I play next? My fingers </p>
<p>wait your orders." </p>
<p>"Perhaps," said Darcy, "I should have judged better, had I </p>
<p>sought an introduction; but I am ill-qualified to recommend </p>
<p>myself to strangers." </p>
<p>"Shall we ask your cousin the reason of this?" said </p>
<p>Elizabeth, still addressing Colonel Fitzwilliam. "Shall we ask </p>
<p>him why a man of sense and education, and who has lived in </p>
<p>the world, is ill qualified to recommend himself to strangers?" </p>
<p>"I can answer your question," said Fitzwilliam, "without </p>
<p>applying to him. It is because he will not give himself the </p>
<p>trouble." </p>
<p>"I certainly have not the talent which some people </p>
<p>possess," said Darcy, "of conversing easily with those I have </p>
<p>never seen before. I cannot catch their tone of conversation, or </p>
<p>appear interested in their concerns, as I often see done." </p>
<p>"My fingers," said Elizabeth, "do not move over this </p>
<p>instrument in the masterly manner which I see so many </p>
<p>women's do. They have not the same force or rapidity, and do </p>
<p>not produce the same expression. But then I have always </p>
<p>supposed it to be my own fault--because I will not take the </p>
<p>trouble of practising. It is not that I do not believe MY fingers </p>
<p>as capable as any other woman's of superior execution." </p>
<p>Darcy smiled and said, "You are perfectly right. You have </p>
<p>employed your time much better. No one admitted to the </p>
<p>privilege of hearing you can think anything wanting. We </p>
<p>neither of us perform to strangers." </p>
<p>Here they were interrupted by Lady Catherine, who called </p>
<p>out to know what they were talking of. Elizabeth immediately </p>
<p>began playing again. Lady Catherine approached, and, after </p>
<p>listening for a few minutes, said to Darcy: </p>
<p>"Miss Bennet would not play at all amiss if she practised </p>
<p>more, and could have the advantage of a London master. She </p>
<p>has a very good notion of fingering, though her taste is not </p>
<p>equal to Anne's. Anne would have been a delightful </p>
<p>performer, had her health allowed her to learn." </p>
<p>Elizabeth looked at Darcy to see how cordially he assented </p>
<p>to his cousin's praise; but neither at that moment nor at any </p>
<p>other could she discern any symptom of love; and from the </p>
<p>whole of his behaviour to Miss de Bourgh she derived this </p>
<p>comfort for Miss Bingley, that he might have been just as </p>
<p>likely to marry HER, had she been his relation. </p>
<p>Lady Catherine continued her remarks on Elizabeth's </p>
<p>performance, mixing with them many instructions on </p>
<p>execution and taste. Elizabeth received them with all the </p>
<p>forbearance of civility, and, at the request of the gentlemen, </p>
<p>remained at the instrument till her ladyship's carriage was </p>
<p>ready to take them all home. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 32. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth was sitting by herself the next morning, and </p>
<p>writing to Jane while Mrs. Collins and Maria were gone on </p>
<p>business into the village, when she was startled by a ring at </p>
<p>the door, the certain signal of a visitor. As she had heard no </p>
<p>carriage, she thought it not unlikely to be Lady Catherine, and </p>
<p>under that apprehension was putting away her half-finished </p>
<p>letter that she might escape all impertinent questions, when </p>
<p>the door opened, and, to her very great surprise, Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>and Mr. Darcy only, entered the room. </p>
<p>He seemed astonished too on finding her alone, and </p>
<p>apologised for his intrusion by letting her know that he had </p>
<p>understood all the ladies were to be within. </p>
<p>They then sat down, and when her inquiries after Rosings </p>
<p>were made, seemed in danger of sinking into total silence. It </p>
<p>was absolutely necessary, therefore, to think of something, </p>
<p>and in this emergence recollecting WHEN she had seen him </p>
<p>last in Hertfordshire, and feeling curious to know what he </p>
<p>would say on the subject of their hasty departure, she </p>
<p>observed: </p>
<p>"How very suddenly you all quitted Netherfield last </p>
<p>November, Mr. Darcy! It must have been a most agreeable </p>
<p>surprise to Mr. Bingley to see you all after him so soon; for, if </p>
<p>I recollect right, he went but the day before. He and his sisters </p>
<p>were well, I hope, when you left London?" </p>
<p>"Perfectly so, I thank you." </p>
<p>She found that she was to receive no other answer, and, </p>
<p>after a short pause added: </p>
<p>"I think I have understood that Mr. Bingley has not much </p>
<p>idea of ever returning to Netherfield again?" </p>
<p>"I have never heard him say so; but it is probable that he </p>
<p>may spend very little of his time there in the future. He has </p>
<p>many friends, and is at a time of life when friends and </p>
<p>engagements are continually increasing." </p>
<p>"If he means to be but little at Netherfield, it would be </p>
<p>better for the neighbourhood that he should give up the place </p>
<p>entirely, for then we might possibly get a settled family there. </p>
<p>But, perhaps, Mr. Bingley did not take the house so much for </p>
<p>the convenience of the neighbourhood as for his own, and we </p>
<p>must expect him to keep it or quit it on the same principle." </p>
<p>"I should not be surprised," said Darcy, "if he were to give </p>
<p>it up as soon as any eligible purchase offers." </p>
<p>Elizabeth made no answer. She was afraid of talking </p>
<p>longer of his friend; and, having nothing else to say, was now </p>
<p>determined to leave the trouble of finding a subject to him. </p>
<p>He took the hint, and soon began with, "This seems a very </p>
<p>comfortable house. Lady Catherine, I believe, did a great deal </p>
<p>to it when Mr. Collins first came to Hunsford." </p>
<p>"I believe she did--and I am sure she could not have </p>
<p>bestowed her kindness on a more grateful object." </p>
<p>"Mr. Collins appears to be very fortunate in his choice of a </p>
<p>wife." </p>
<p>"Yes, indeed, his friends may well rejoice in his having </p>
<p>met with one of the very few sensible women who would </p>
<p>have accepted him, or have made him happy if they had. My </p>
<p>friend has an excellent understanding--though I am not certain </p>
<p>that I consider her marrying Mr. Collins as the wisest thing </p>
<p>she ever did. She seems perfectly happy, however, and in a </p>
<p>prudential light it is certainly a very good match for her." </p>
<p>"It must be very agreeable for her to be settled within so </p>
<p>easy a distance of her own family and friends." </p>
<p>"An easy distance, do you call it? It is nearly fifty miles." </p>
<p>"And what is fifty miles of good road? Little more than </p>
<p>half a day's journey. Yes, I call it a VERY easy distance." </p>
<p>"I should never have considered the distance as one of the </p>
<p>ADVANTAGES of the match," cried Elizabeth. "I should </p>
<p>never have said Mrs. Collins was settled NEAR her family." </p>
<p>"It is a proof of your own attachment to Hertfordshire. </p>
<p>Anything beyond the very neighbourhood of Longbourn, I </p>
<p>suppose, would appear far." </p>
<p>As he spoke there was a sort of smile which Elizabeth </p>
<p>fancied she understood; he must be supposing her to be </p>
<p>thinking of Jane and Netherfield, and she blushed as she </p>
<p>answered: </p>
<p>"I do not mean to say that a woman may not be settled too </p>
<p>near her family. The far and the near must be relative, and </p>
<p>depend on many varying circumstances. Where there is </p>
<p>fortune to make the expenses of travelling unimportant, </p>
<p>distance becomes no evil. But that is not the case HERE. Mr. </p>
<p>and Mrs. Collins have a comfortable income, but not such a </p>
<p>one as will allow of frequent journeys--and I am persuaded </p>
<p>my friend would not call herself NEAR her family under less </p>
<p>than HALF the present distance." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy drew his chair a little towards her, and said, </p>
<p>"YOU cannot have a right to such very strong local </p>
<p>attachment. YOU cannot have been always at Longbourn." </p>
<p>Elizabeth looked surprised. The gentleman experienced </p>
<p>some change of feeling; he drew back his chair, took a </p>
<p>newspaper from the table, and glancing over it, said, in a </p>
<p>colder voice: </p>
<p>"Are you pleased with Kent?" </p>
<p>A short dialogue on the subject of the country ensued, on </p>
<p>either side calm and concise--and soon put an end to by the </p>
<p>entrance of Charlotte and her sister, just returned from her </p>
<p>walk. The tete-a-tete surprised them. Mr. Darcy related the </p>
<p>mistake which had occasioned his intruding on Miss Bennet, </p>
<p>and after sitting a few minutes longer without saying much to </p>
<p>anybody, went away. </p>
<p>"What can be the meaning of this?" said Charlotte, as soon </p>
<p>as he was gone. "My dear, Eliza, he must be in love with you, </p>
<p>or he would never have called us in this familiar way." </p>
<p>But when Elizabeth told of his silence; it did not seem very </p>
<p>likely, even to Charlotte's wishes, to be the case; and after </p>
<p>various conjectures, they could at last only suppose his visit to </p>
<p>proceed from the difficulty of finding anything to do, which </p>
<p>was the more probable from the time of year. All field sports </p>
<p>were over. Within doors there was Lady Catherine, books, and </p>
<p>a billiard-table, but gentlemen cannot always be within doors; </p>
<p>and in the nearness of the Parsonage, or the pleasantness of </p>
<p>the walk to it, or of the people who lived in it, the two cousins </p>
<p>found a temptation from this period of walking thither almost </p>
<p>every day. They called at various times of the morning, </p>
<p>sometimes separately, sometimes together, and now and then </p>
<p>accompanied by their aunt. It was plain to them all that </p>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam came because he had pleasure in their </p>
<p>society, a persuasion which of course recommended him still </p>
<p>more; and Elizabeth was reminded by her own satisfaction in </p>
<p>being with him, as well as by his evident admiration of her, of </p>
<p>her former favourite George Wickham; and though, in </p>
<p>comparing them, she saw there was less captivating softness </p>
<p>in Colonel Fitzwilliam's manners, she believed he might have </p>
<p>the best informed mind. </p>
<p>But why Mr. Darcy came so often to the Parsonage, it was </p>
<p>more difficult to understand. It could not be for society, as he </p>
<p>frequently sat there ten minutes together without opening his </p>
<p>lips; and when he did speak, it seemed the effect of necessity </p>
<p>rather than of choice--a sacrifice to propriety, not a pleasure to </p>
<p>himself. He seldom appeared really animated. Mrs. Collins </p>
<p>knew not what to make of him. Colonel Fitzwilliam's </p>
<p>occasionally laughing at his stupidity, proved that he was </p>
<p>generally different, which her own knowledge of him could </p>
<p>not have told her; and as she would liked to have believed this </p>
<p>change the effect of love, and the object of that love her friend </p>
<p>Eliza, she set herself seriously to work to find it out. She </p>
<p>watched him whenever they were at Rosings, and whenever </p>
<p>he came to Hunsford; but without much success. He certainly </p>
<p>looked at her friend a great deal, but the expression of that </p>
<p>look was disputable. It was an earnest, steadfast gaze, but she </p>
<p>often doubted whether there were much admiration in it, and </p>
<p>sometimes it seemed nothing but absence of mind. She had </p>
<p>once or twice suggested to Elizabeth the possibility of his </p>
<p>being partial to her, but Elizabeth always laughed at the idea; </p>
<p>and Mrs. Collins did not think it right to press the subject, </p>
<p>from the danger of raising expectations which might only end </p>
<p>in disappointment; for in her opinion it admitted not of a </p>
<p>doubt, that all her friend's dislike would vanish, if she could </p>
<p>suppose him to be in her power. </p>
<p>In her kind schemes for Elizabeth, she sometimes planned </p>
<p>her marrying </p>
<p>Colonel </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam. He was beyond </p>
<p>comparison the most pleasant man; he certainly admired her, </p>
<p>and his situation in life was most eligible; but, to </p>
<p>counterbalance these advantages, Mr. Darcy had considerable </p>
<p>patronage in the church, and his cousin could have none at all. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 33 </heading>
<heading>More than once did Elizabeth, in her ramble within the park, </heading>
<heading>unexpectedly meet Mr. Darcy. She felt all the perverseness of </heading>
<heading>the mischance that should bring him where no one else was </heading>
<heading>brought, and, to prevent its ever happening again, took care to </heading>
<heading>inform him at first that it was a favourite haunt of hers. How it </heading>
<heading>could occur a second time, therefore, was very odd! Yet it did, </heading>
<heading>and even a third. It seemed like wilful ill-nature, or a </heading>
<heading>voluntary penance, for on these occasions it was not merely a </heading>
<heading>few formal inquiries and an awkward pause and then away, </heading>
<heading>but he actually thought it necessary to turn back and walk </heading>
<heading>with her. He never said a great deal, nor did she give herself </heading>
<heading>the trouble of talking or of listening much; but it struck her in </heading>
<heading>the course of their third rencontre that he was asking some </heading>
<p>odd unconnected questions--about her pleasure in being at </p>
<p>Hunsford, her love of solitary walks, and her opinion of Mr. </p>
<p>and Mrs. Collins's happiness; and that in speaking of Rosings </p>
<p>and her not perfectly understanding the house, he seemed to </p>
<p>expect that whenever she came into Kent again she would be </p>
<p>staying THERE too. His words seemed to imply it. Could he </p>
<p>have Colonel Fitzwilliam in his thoughts? She supposed, if he </p>
<p>meant anything, he must mean and allusion to what might </p>
<p>arise in that quarter. It distressed her a little, and she was quite </p>
<p>glad to find herself at the gate in the pales opposite the </p>
<p>Parsonage. </p>
<p>She was engaged one day as she walked, in perusing Jane's </p>
<p>last letter, and dwelling on some passages which proved that </p>
<p>Jane had not written in spirits, when, instead of being again </p>
<p>surprised by Mr. Darcy, she saw on looking up that Colonel </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam was meeting her. Putting away the letter </p>
<p>immediately and forcing a smile, she said: </p>
<p>"I did not know before that you ever walked this way." </p>
<p>"I have been making the tour of the park," he replied, "as I </p>
<p>generally do every year, and intend to close it with a call at </p>
<p>the Parsonage. Are you going much farther?" </p>
<p>"No, I should have turned in a moment." </p>
<p>And accordingly she did turn, and they walked towards the </p>
<p>Parsonage together. </p>
<p>"Do you certainly leave Kent on Saturday?" said she. </p>
<p>"Yes--if Darcy does not put it off again. But I am at his </p>
<p>disposal. He arranges the business just as he pleases." </p>
<p>"And if not able to please himself in the arrangement, he </p>
<p>has at least pleasure in the great power of choice. I do not </p>
<p>know anybody who seems more to enjoy the power of doing </p>
<p>what he likes than Mr. Darcy." </p>
<p>"He likes to have his own way very well," replied Colonel </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam. "But so we all do. It is only that he has better </p>
<p>means of having it than many others, because he is rich, and </p>
<p>many others are poor. I speak feelingly. A younger son, you </p>
<p>know, must be inured to self-denial and dependence." </p>
<p>"In my opinion, the younger son of an earl can know very </p>
<p>little of either. Now seriously, what have you ever known of </p>
<p>self-denial and dependence? When have you been prevented </p>
<p>by want of money from going wherever you chose, or </p>
<p>procuring anything you had a fancy for?" </p>
<p>"These are home questions--and perhaps I cannot say that I </p>
<p>have experienced many hardships of that nature. But in </p>
<p>matters of greater weight, I may suffer from want of money. </p>
<p>Younger sons cannot marry where they like." </p>
<p>"Unless where they like women of fortune, which I think </p>
<p>they very often do." </p>
<p>"Our habits of expense make us too dependent, and there </p>
<p>are too many in my rank of life who can afford to marry </p>
<p>without some attention to money." </p>
<p>"Is this," thought Elizabeth, "meant for me?" and she </p>
<p>coloured at the idea; but, recovering herself, said in a lively </p>
<p>tone, "And pray, what is the usual price of an earl's younger </p>
<p>son? Unless the elder brother is very sickly, I suppose you </p>
<p>would not ask above fifty thousand pounds." </p>
<p>He answered her in the same style, and the subject </p>
<p>dropped. To interrupt a silence which might make him fancy </p>
<p>her affected with what had passed, she soon afterwards said: </p>
<p>"I imagine your cousin brought you down with him chiefly </p>
<p>for the sake of having someone at his disposal. I wonder he </p>
<p>does not marry, to secure a lasting convenience of that kind. </p>
<p>But, perhaps, his sister does as well for the present, and, as </p>
<p>she is under his sole care, he may do what he likes with her." </p>
<p>"No," said Colonel Fitzwilliam, "that is an advantage </p>
<p>which he must divide with me. I am joined with him in the </p>
<p>guardian ship of Miss Darcy." </p>
<p>"Are you indeed? And pray what sort of guardians do you </p>
<p>make? Does your charge give you much trouble? Young </p>
<p>ladies of her age are sometimes a little difficult to manage, </p>
<p>and if she has the true Darcy spirit, she may like to have her </p>
<p>own way." </p>
<p>As she spoke she observed him looking at her earnestly; </p>
<p>and the manner in which he immediately asked her why she </p>
<p>supposed Miss Darcy likely to give them any uneasiness, </p>
<p>convinced her that she had somehow or other got pretty near </p>
<p>the truth. She directly replied: </p>
<p>"You need not be frightened. I never heard any harm of </p>
<p>her; and I dare say she is one of the most tractable creatures in </p>
<p>the world. She is a very great favourite with some ladies of </p>
<p>my acquaintance, Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley. I think I have </p>
<p>heard you say that you know them." </p>
<p>"I know them a little. Their brother is a pleasant </p>
<p>gentlemanlike man--he is a great friend of Darcy's." </p>
<p>"Oh! yes," said Elizabeth drily; </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy is </p>
<p>uncommonly kind to Mr. Bingley, and takes a prodigious deal </p>
<p>of care of him." </p>
<p>"Care of him! Yes, I really believe Darcy DOES take care </p>
<p>of him in those points where he most wants care. From </p>
<p>something that he told me in our journey hither, I have reason </p>
<p>to think Bingley very much indebted to him. But I ought to </p>
<p>beg his pardon, for I have no right to suppose that Bingley </p>
<p>was the person meant. It was all conjecture." </p>
<p>"What is it you mean?" </p>
<p>"It is a circumstance which Darcy could not wish to be </p>
<p>generally known, because if it were to get round to the lady's </p>
<p>family, it would be an unpleasant thing." </p>
<p>"You may depend upon my not mentioning it." </p>
<p>"And remember that I have not much reason for supposing </p>
<p>it to be Bingley. What he told me was merely this: that he </p>
<p>congratulated himself on having lately saved a friend from the </p>
<p>inconveniences of a most imprudent marriage, but without </p>
<p>mentioning names or any other particulars, and I only </p>
<p>suspected it to be Bingley from believing him the kind of </p>
<p>young man to get into a scrape of that sort, and from knowing </p>
<p>them to have been together the whole of last summer." </p>
<p>"Did Mr. Darcy give you reasons for this interference?" </p>
<p>"I understood that there were some very strong objections </p>
<p>against the lady." </p>
<p>"And what arts did he use to separate them?" </p>
<p>"He did not talk to me of his own arts," said Fitzwilliam, </p>
<p>smiling. "He only told me what I have now told you." </p>
<p>Elizabeth made no answer, and walked on, her heart </p>
<p>swelling with indignation. After watching her a little, </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam asked her why she was so thoughtful. </p>
<p>"I am thinking of what you have been telling me," said she. </p>
<p>"Your cousin's conduct does not suit my feelings. Why was he </p>
<p>to be the judge?" </p>
<p>"You are rather disposed to call his interference officious?" </p>
<p>"I do not see what right Mr. Darcy had to decide on the </p>
<p>propriety of his friend's inclination, or why, upon his own </p>
<p>judgement alone, he was to determine and direct in what </p>
<p>manner his friend was to be happy. But," she continued, </p>
<p>recollecting herself, "as we know none of the particulars, it is </p>
<p>not fair to condemn him. It is not to be supposed that there </p>
<p>was much affection in the case." </p>
<p>"That is not an unnatural surmise," said Fitzwilliam, "but it </p>
<p>is a lessening of the honour of my cousin's triumph very </p>
<p>sadly." </p>
<p>This was spoken jestingly; but it appeared to her so just a </p>
<p>picture of Mr. Darcy, that she would not trust herself with an </p>
<p>answer, and therefore, abruptly changing the conversation </p>
<p>talked on indifferent matters until they reached the Parson- </p>
<p>age. There, shut into her own room, as soon as their visitor </p>
<p>left them, she could think without interruption of all that she </p>
<p>had heard. It was not to be supposed that any other people </p>
<p>could be meant than those with whom she was connected. </p>
<p>There could not exist in the world TWO men over whom Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy could have such boundless influence. That he had been </p>
<p>concerned in the measures taken to separate Bingley and Jane </p>
<p>she had never doubted; but she had always attributed to Miss </p>
<p>Bingley the principal design and arrangement of them. If his </p>
<p>own vanity, however, did not mislead him, HE was the cause, </p>
<p>his pride and caprice were the cause, of all that Jane had </p>
<p>suffered, and still continued to suffer. He had ruined for a </p>
<p>while every hope of happiness for the most affectionate, </p>
<p>generous heart in the world; and no one could say how lasting </p>
<p>an evil he might have inflicted. </p>
<p>"There were some very strong objections against the lady," </p>
<p>were Colonel Fitzwilliam's words; and those strong objections </p>
<p>probably were, her having one uncle who was a country </p>
<p>attorney, and another who was in business in London. </p>
<p>"To Jane herself," she exclaimed, "there could be no </p>
<p>possibility of objection; all loveliness and goodness as she is!- </p>
<p>-her understanding excellent, her mind improved, and her </p>
<p>manners captivating. Neither could anything be urged against </p>
<p>my father, who, though with some peculiarities, has abilities </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy himself need not disdain, and respectability which </p>
<p>he will probably never each." When she thought of her </p>
<p>mother, her confidence gave way a little; but she would not </p>
<p>allow that any objections THERE had material weight with </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, whose pride, she was convinced, would receive a </p>
<p>deeper wound from the want of importance in his friend's </p>
<p>connections, than from their want of sense; and she was quite </p>
<p>decided, at last, that he had been partly governed by this worst </p>
<p>kind of pride, and partly by the wish of retaining Mr. Bingley </p>
<p>for his sister. </p>
<p>The agitation and tears which the subject occasioned, </p>
<p>brought on a headache; and it grew so much worse towards </p>
<p>the evening, that, added to her unwillingness to see Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, it determined her not to attend her cousins to Rosings, </p>
<p>where they were engaged to drink tea. Mrs. Collins, seeing </p>
<p>that she was really unwell, did not press her to go and as much </p>
<p>as possible prevented her husband from pressing her; but Mr. </p>
<p>Collins could not conceal his apprehension of Lady </p>
<p>Catherine's being rather displeased by her staying at home. </p>
<header>Ebd </header>
<header>E-BooksDirectory.com </header>
<heading>Chapter 34. </heading>
<p>When they were gone, Elizabeth, as if intending to </p>
<p>exasperate herself as much as possible against Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>chose for her employment the examination of all the letters </p>
<p>which Jane had written to her since her being in Kent. They </p>
<p>contained no actual complaint, nor was there any revival of </p>
<p>past occurrences, or any communication of present suffering. </p>
<p>But in all, and in almost every line of each, there was a want </p>
<p>of that cheerfulness which had been used to characterise her </p>
<p>style, and which, proceeding from the serenity of a mind at </p>
<p>ease with itself and kindly disposed towards everyone, had </p>
<p>been scarcely ever clouded. Elizabeth noticed every sentence </p>
<p>conveying the idea of uneasiness, with an attention which it </p>
<p>had hardly received on the first perusal. Mr. Darcy's shameful </p>
<p>boast of what misery he had been able to inflict, gave her a </p>
<p>keener sense of her sister's sufferings. It was some consolation </p>
<p>to think that his visit to Rosings was to end on the day after </p>
<p>the next--and, a still greater, that in less than a fortnight she </p>
<p>should herself be with Jane again, and enabled to contribute to </p>
<p>the recovery of her spirits, by all that affection could do. </p>
<p>She could not think of Darcy's leaving Kent without </p>
<p>remembering that his cousin was to go with him; but Colonel </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam had made it clear that he had no intentions at all, </p>
<p>and agreeable as he was, she did not mean to be unhappy </p>
<p>about him. </p>
<p>While settling this point, she was suddenly roused by the </p>
<p>sound of the door-bell, and her spirits were a little fluttered by </p>
<p>the idea of its being Colonel Fitzwilliam himself, who had </p>
<p>once before called late in the evening, and might now come to </p>
<p>inquire particularly after her. But this idea was soon banished, </p>
<p>and her spirits were very differently affected, when, to her </p>
<p>utter amazement, she saw Mr. Darcy walk into the room. In an </p>
<p>hurried manner he immediately began an inquiry after her </p>
<p>health, imputing his visit to a wish of hearing that she were </p>
<p>better. She answered him with cold civility. He sat down for a </p>
<p>few moments, and then getting up, walked about the room. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was surprised, but said not a word. After a silence </p>
<p>of several minutes, he came towards her in an agitated </p>
<p>manner, and thus began: </p>
<p>"In vain I have struggled. It will not do. My feelings will </p>
<p>not be repressed. You must allow me to tell you how ardently </p>
<p>I admire and love you." </p>
<p>Elizabeth's astonishment was beyond expression. She </p>
<p>stared, coloured, doubted, and was silent. This he considered </p>
<p>sufficient encouragement; and the avowal of all that he felt, </p>
<p>and had long felt for her, immediately followed. He spoke </p>
<p>well; but there were feelings besides those of the heart to be </p>
<p>detailed; and he was not more eloquent on the subject of </p>
<p>tenderness than of pride. His sense of her inferiority--of its </p>
<p>being a degradation--of the family obstacles which had always </p>
<p>opposed to inclination, were dwelt on with a warmth which </p>
<p>seemed due to the consequence he was wounding, but was </p>
<p>very unlikely to recommend his suit. </p>
<p>In spite of her deeply-rooted dislike, she could not be </p>
<p>insensible to the compliment of such a man's affection, and </p>
<p>though her intentions did not vary for an instant, she was at </p>
<p>first sorry for the pain he was to receive; till, roused to </p>
<p>resentment by his subsequent language, she lost all </p>
<p>compassion in anger. She tried, however, to compose herself </p>
<p>to answer him with patience, when he should have done. He </p>
<p>concluded with representing to her the strength of that </p>
<p>attachment which, in spite of all his endeavours, he had found </p>
<p>impossible to conquer; and with expressing his hope that it </p>
<p>would now be rewarded by her acceptance of his hand. As he </p>
<p>said this, she could easily see that he had no doubt of a </p>
<p>favourable answer. He SPOKE of apprehension and anxiety, </p>
<p>but his countenance expressed real security. Such a </p>
<p>circumstance could only exasperate farther, and, when he </p>
<p>ceased, the colour rose into her cheeks, and she said: </p>
<p>"In such cases as this, it is, I believe, the established mode </p>
<p>to express a sense of obligation for the sentiments avowed, </p>
<p>however unequally they may be returned. It is natural that </p>
<p>obligation should be felt, and if I could FEEL gratitude, I </p>
<p>would now thank you. But I cannot--I have never desired your </p>
<p>good opinion, and you have certainly bestowed it most </p>
<p>unwillingly. I am sorry to have occasioned pain to anyone. It </p>
<p>has been most unconsciously done, however, and I hope will </p>
<p>be of short duration. The feelings which, you tell me, have </p>
<p>long prevented the acknowledgment of your regard, can have </p>
<p>little difficulty in overcoming it after this explanation." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy, who was leaning against the mantelpiece with </p>
<p>his eyes fixed on her face, seemed to catch her words with no </p>
<p>less resentment than surprise. His complexion became pale </p>
<p>with anger, and the disturbance of his mind was visible in </p>
<p>every feature. He was struggling for the appearance of </p>
<p>composure, and would not open his lips till he believed </p>
<p>himself to have attained it. The pause was to Elizabeth's </p>
<p>feelings dreadful. At length, with a voice of forced calmness, </p>
<p>he said: </p>
<p>"And this is all the reply which I am to have the honour of </p>
<p>expecting! I might, perhaps, wish to be informed why, with so </p>
<p>little ENDEAVOUR at civility, I am thus rejected. But it is of </p>
<p>small importance." </p>
<p>"I might as well inquire," replied she, "why with so evident </p>
<p>a desire of offending and insulting me, you chose to tell me </p>
<p>that you liked me against your will, against your reason, and </p>
<p>even against your character? Was not this some excuse for </p>
<p>incivility, if I WAS uncivil? But I have other provocations. </p>
<p>You know I have. Had not my feelings decided against you- </p>
<p>had they been indifferent, or had they even been favourable, </p>
<p>do you think that any consideration would tempt me to accept </p>
<p>the man who has been the means of ruining, perhaps for ever, </p>
<p>the happiness of a most beloved sister?" </p>
<p>As she pronounced these words, Mr. Darcy changed </p>
<p>colour; but the emotion was short, and he listened without </p>
<p>attempting to interrupt her while she continued: </p>
<p>"I have every reason in the world to think ill of you. No </p>
<p>motive can excuse the unjust and ungenerous part you acted </p>
<p>THERE. You dare not, you cannot deny, that you have been </p>
<p>the principal, if not the only means of dividing them from </p>
<p>each other--of exposing one to the censure of the world for </p>
<p>caprice and instability, and the other to its derision for </p>
<p>disappointed hopes, and involving them both in misery of the </p>
<p>acutest kind." </p>
<p>She paused, and saw with no slight indignation that he was </p>
<p>listening with an air which proved him wholly unmoved by </p>
<p>any feeling of remorse. He even looked at her with a smile of </p>
<p>affected incredulity. </p>
<p>"Can you deny that you have done it?" she repeated. </p>
<p>With assumed tranquillity he then replied: "I have no wish </p>
<p>of denying that I did everything in my power to separate my </p>
<p>friend from your sister, or that I rejoice in my success. </p>
<p>Towards HIM I have been kinder than towards myself." </p>
<p>Elizabeth disdained the appearance of noticing this civil </p>
<p>reflection, but its meaning did not escape, nor was it likely to </p>
<p>conciliate her. </p>
<p>"But it is not merely this affair," she continued, "on which </p>
<p>my dislike is founded. Long before it had taken place my </p>
<p>opinion of you was decided. Your character was unfolded in </p>
<p>the recital which I received many months ago from Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham. On this subject, what can you have to say? In what </p>
<p>imaginary act of friendship can you here defend yourself? or </p>
<p>under what misrepresentation can you here impose upon </p>
<p>others?" </p>
<p>"You take an eager interest in that gentleman's concerns," </p>
<p>said Darcy, in a less tranquil tone, and with a heightened </p>
<p>colour. </p>
<p>"Who that knows what his misfortunes have been, can help </p>
<p>feeling an interest in him?" </p>
<p>"His misfortunes!" repeated Darcy contemptuously; "yes, </p>
<p>his misfortunes have been great indeed." </p>
<p>"And of your infliction," cried Elizabeth with energy. </p>
<p>"You have reduced him to his present state of poverty-- </p>
<p>comparative poverty. You have withheld the advantages </p>
<p>which you must know to have been designed for him. You </p>
<p>have deprived the best years of his life of that independence </p>
<p>which was no less his due than his desert. You have done all </p>
<p>this! and yet you can treat the mention of his misfortune with </p>
<p>contempt and ridicule." </p>
<p>"And this," cried Darcy, as he walked with quick steps </p>
<p>across the room, "is your opinion of me! This is the estimation </p>
<p>in which you hold me! I thank you for explaining it so fully. </p>
<p>My faults, according to this calculation, are heavy indeed! But </p>
<p>perhaps," added he, stopping in his walk, and turning towards </p>
<p>her, "these offenses might have been overlooked, had not your </p>
<p>pride been hurt by my honest confession of the scruples that </p>
<p>had long prevented my forming any serious design. These </p>
<p>bitter accusations might have been suppressed, had I, with </p>
<p>greater policy, concealed my struggles, and flattered you into </p>
<p>the belief of my being impelled by unqualified, unalloyed </p>
<p>inclination; by reason, by reflection, by everything. But </p>
<p>disguise of every sort is my abhorrence. Nor am I ashamed of </p>
<p>the feelings I related. They were natural and just. Could you </p>
<p>expect me to rejoice in the inferiority of your connections?--to </p>
<p>congratulate myself on the hope of relations, whose condition </p>
<p>in life is so decidedly beneath my own?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth felt herself growing more angry every moment; </p>
<p>yet she tried to the utmost to speak with composure when she </p>
<p>said: </p>
<p>"You are mistaken, Mr. Darcy, if you suppose that the </p>
<p>mode of your declaration affected me in any other way, than </p>
<p>as it spared the concern which I might have felt in refusing </p>
<p>you, had you behaved in a more gentlemanlike manner." </p>
<p>She saw him start at this, but he said nothing, and she </p>
<p>continued: </p>
<p>"You could not have made the offer of your hand in any </p>
<p>possible way that would have tempted me to accept it." </p>
<p>Again his astonishment was obvious; and he looked at her </p>
<p>with an expression of mingled incredulity and mortification. </p>
<p>She went on: </p>
<p>"From the very beginning--from the first moment, I may </p>
<p>almost say--of my acquaintance with you, your manners, </p>
<p>impressing me with the fullest belief of your arrogance, your </p>
<p>conceit, and your selfish disdain of the feelings of others, </p>
<p>were such as to form the groundwork of disapprobation on </p>
<p>which succeeding events have built so immovable a dislike; </p>
<p>and I had not known you a month before I felt that you were </p>
<p>the last man in the world whom I could ever be prevailed on </p>
<p>to marry." </p>
<p>"You have said quite enough, madam. I perfectly </p>
<p>comprehend your feelings, and have now only to be ashamed </p>
<p>of what my own have been. Forgive me for having taken up </p>
<p>so much of your time, and accept my best wishes for your </p>
<p>health and happiness." </p>
<p>And with these words he hastily left the room, and </p>
<p>Elizabeth heard him the next moment open the front door and </p>
<p>quit the house. </p>
<p>The tumult of her mind, was now painfully great. She </p>
<p>knew not how to support herself, and from actual weakness </p>
<p>sat down and cried for half-an-hour. Her astonishment, as she </p>
<p>reflected on what had passed, was increased by every review </p>
<p>of it. That she should receive an offer of marriage from Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy! That he should have been in love with her for so many </p>
<p>months! So much in love as to wish to marry her in spite of all </p>
<p>the objections which had made him prevent his friend's </p>
<p>marrying her sister, and which must appear at least with equal </p>
<p>force in his own case--was almost incredible! It was gratifying </p>
<p>to have inspired unconsciously so strong an affection. But his </p>
<p>pride, his abominable pride--his shameless avowal of what he </p>
<p>had done with respect to Jane--his unpardonable assurance in </p>
<p>acknowledging, though he could not justify it, and the </p>
<p>unfeeling manner in which he had mentioned Mr. Wickham, </p>
<p>his cruelty towards whom he had not attempted to deny, soon </p>
<p>overcame the pity which the consideration of his attachment </p>
<p>had for a moment excited. She continued in very agitated </p>
<p>reflections till the sound of Lady Catherine's carriage made </p>
<p>her feel how unequal she was to encounter </p>
<p>Charlotte's </p>
<p>observation, and hurried her away to her room. </p>
<heading>Chapter 35. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth awoke the next morning to the same thoughts </p>
<p>and meditations which had at length closed her eyes. She </p>
<p>could not yet recover from the surprise of what had happened; </p>
<p>it was impossible to think of anything else; and, totally </p>
<p>indisposed for employment, she resolved, soon after breakfast, </p>
<p>to indulge herself in air and exercise. She was proceeding </p>
<p>directly to her favourite walk, when the recollection of Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy's sometimes coming there stopped her, and instead of </p>
<p>entering the park, she turned up the lane, which led farther </p>
<p>from the turn-pike-road. The park paling was still the </p>
<p>boundary on one side, and she soon passed one of the gates </p>
<p>into the ground. </p>
<p>After walking two or three times along that part of the </p>
<p>lane, she was tempted, by the pleasantness of the morning, to </p>
<p>stop at the gates and look into the park. The five weeks which </p>
<p>she had now passed in Kent had made a great difference in the </p>
<p>country, and every day was adding to the verdure of the early </p>
<p>trees. She was on the point of continuing her walk, when she </p>
<p>caught a glimpse of a gentleman within the sort of grove </p>
<p>which edged the park; he was moving that way; and, fearful of </p>
<p>its being Mr. Darcy, she was directly retreating. But the </p>
<p>person who advanced was now near enough to see her, and </p>
<p>stepping forward with eagerness, pronounced her name. She </p>
<p>had turned away; but on hearing herself called, though in a </p>
<p>voice which proved it to be Mr. Darcy, she moved again </p>
<p>towards the gate. He had by that time reached it also, and, </p>
<p>holding out a letter, which she instinctively took, said, with a </p>
<p>look of haughty composure, "I have been walking in the grove </p>
<p>some time in the hope of meeting you. Will you do me the </p>
<p>honour of reading that letter?" And then, with a slight bow, </p>
<p>turned again into the plantation, and was soon out of sight. </p>
<p>With no expectation of pleasure, but with the strongest </p>
<p>curiosity, Elizabeth opened the letter, and, to her still </p>
<p>increasing wonder, perceived an envelope containing two </p>
<p>sheets of letter-paper, written quite through, in a very close </p>
<p>hand. The envelope itself was likewise full. Pursuing her way </p>
<p>along the lane, she then began it. It was dated from Rosings, </p>
<p>at eight o'clock in the morning, and was as follows:- </p>
<p>"Be not alarmed, madam, on receiving this letter, by the </p>
<p>apprehension of its containing any repetition of those </p>
<p>sentiments or renewal of those offers which were last night so </p>
<p>disgusting to you. I write without any intention of paining </p>
<p>you, or humbling myself, by dwelling on wishes which, for </p>
<p>the happiness of both, cannot be too soon forgotten; and the </p>
<p>effort which the formation and the perusal of this letter must </p>
<p>occasion, should have been spared, had not my character </p>
<p>required it to be written and read. You must, therefore, pardon </p>
<p>the freedom with which I demand your attention; your </p>
<p>feelings, I know, will bestow it unwillingly, but I demand it of </p>
<p>your justice. </p>
<p>"Two offenses of a very different nature, and by no means </p>
<p>of equal magnitude, you last night laid to my charge. The first </p>
<p>mentioned was, that, regardless of the sentiments of either, I </p>
<p>had detached Mr. Bingley from your sister, and the other, that </p>
<p>I had, in defiance of various claims, in defiance of honour and </p>
<p>humanity, ruined the immediate prosperity and blasted the </p>
<p>prospects of Mr. Wickham. Wilfully and wantonly to have </p>
<p>thrown off the companion of my youth, the acknowledged </p>
<p>favourite of my father, a young man who had scarcely any </p>
<p>other dependence than on our patronage, and who had been </p>
<p>brought up to expect its exertion, would be a depravity, to </p>
<p>which the separation of two young persons, whose affection </p>
<p>could be the growth of only a few weeks, could bear no </p>
<p>comparison. But from the severity of that blame which was </p>
<p>last night so liberally bestowed, respecting each circumstance, </p>
<p>I shall hope to be in the future secured, when the following </p>
<p>account of my actions and their motives has been read. If, in </p>
<p>the explanation of them, which is due to myself, I am under </p>
<p>the necessity of relating feelings which may be offensive to </p>
<p>yours, I can only say that I am sorry. The necessity must be </p>
<p>obeyed, and further apology would be absurd. </p>
<p>"I had not been long in Hertfordshire, before I saw, in </p>
<p>common with others, that Bingley preferred your elder sister </p>
<p>to any other young woman in the country. But it was not till </p>
<p>the evening of the dance at Netherfield that I had any </p>
<p>apprehension of his feeling a serious attachment. I had often </p>
<p>seen him in love before. At that ball, while I had the honour of </p>
<p>dancing with you, I was first made acquainted, by Sir William </p>
<p>Lucas's accidental information, that Bingley's attentions to </p>
<p>your sister had given rise to a general expectation of their </p>
<p>marriage. He spoke of it as a certain event, of which the time </p>
<p>alone could be undecided. From that moment I observed my </p>
<p>friend's behaviour attentively; and I could then perceive that </p>
<p>his partiality for Miss Bennet was beyond what I had ever </p>
<p>witnessed in him. Your sister I also watched. Her look and </p>
<p>manners were open, cheerful, and engaging as ever, but </p>
<p>without any symptom of peculiar regard, and I remained </p>
<p>convinced from the evening's scrutiny, that though she </p>
<p>received his attentions with pleasure, she did not invite them </p>
<p>by any participation of sentiment. If YOU have not been </p>
<p>mistaken here, _I_ must have been in error. Your superior </p>
<p>knowledge of your sister must make the latter probable. If it </p>
<p>be so, if I have been misled by such error to inflict pain on </p>
<p>her, your resentment has not been unreasonable. But I shall </p>
<p>not scruple to assert, that the serenity </p>
<p>of your sister's </p>
<p>countenance and air was such as might have given the most </p>
<p>acute observer a conviction that, however amiable her temper, </p>
<p>her heart was not likely to be easily touched. That I was </p>
<p>desirous of believing her indifferent is certain--but I will </p>
<p>venture to say that my investigation and decisions are not </p>
<p>usually influenced by my hopes or fears. I did not believe her </p>
<p>to be indifferent because I wished it; I believed it on impartial </p>
<p>conviction, as truly as I wished it in reason. My objections to </p>
<p>the marriage were not merely those which I last night </p>
<p>acknowledged to have the utmost force of passion to put </p>
<p>aside, in my own case; the want of connection could not be so </p>
<p>great an evil to my friend as to me. But there were other </p>
<p>causes of repugnance; causes which, though still existing, and </p>
<p>existing to an equal degree in both instances, I had myself </p>
<p>endeavoured to forget, because they were not immediately </p>
<p>before me. These causes must be stated, though briefly. The </p>
<p>situation of your mother's family, though objectionable, was </p>
<p>nothing in comparison to that total want of propriety so </p>
<p>frequently, so almost uniformly betrayed by herself, by your </p>
<p>three younger sisters, and occasionally even by your father. </p>
<p>Pardon me. It pains me to offend you. But amidst your </p>
<p>concern for the defects of your nearest relations, and your </p>
<p>displeasure at this representation of them, let it give you </p>
<p>consolation to consider that, to have conducted yourselves so </p>
<p>as to avoid any share of the like censure, is praise no less </p>
<p>generally bestowed on you and your elder sister, than it is </p>
<p>honourable to the sense and disposition of both. I will only </p>
<p>say farther that from what passed that evening, my opinion of </p>
<p>all parties was confirmed, and every inducement heightened </p>
<p>which could have led me before, to preserve my friend from </p>
<p>what I esteemed a most unhappy connection. He left </p>
<p>Netherfield for London, on the day following, as you, I am </p>
<p>certain, remember, with the design of soon returning. </p>
<p>"The part which I acted is now to be explained. His sisters' </p>
<p>uneasiness had been equally excited with my own; our </p>
<p>coincidence of feeling was soon discovered, and, alike </p>
<p>sensible that no time was to be lost in detaching their brother, </p>
<p>we shortly resolved on joining him directly in London. We </p>
<p>accordingly went--and there I readily engaged in the office of </p>
<p>pointing out to my friend the certain evils of such a choice. I </p>
<p>described, and enforced them earnestly. But, however this </p>
<p>remonstrance might have staggered or </p>
<p>delayed </p>
<p>his </p>
<p>determination, I do not suppose that it would ultimately have </p>
<p>prevented the marriage, had it not been seconded by the </p>
<p>assurance that I hesitated not in giving, of your sister's </p>
<p>indifference. He had before believed her to return his affection </p>
<p>with sincere, if not with equal regard. But Bingley has great </p>
<p>natural modesty, with a stronger dependence on my </p>
<p>judgement than on his own. To convince him, therefore, that </p>
<p>he had deceived himself, was no very difficult point. To </p>
<p>persuade him against returning into Hertfordshire, when that </p>
<p>conviction had been given, was scarcely the work of a </p>
<p>moment. I cannot blame myself for having done thus much. </p>
<p>There is but one part of my conduct in the whole affair on </p>
<p>which I do not reflect with satisfaction; it is that I </p>
<p>condescended to adopt the measures of art so far as to conceal </p>
<p>from him your sister's being in town. I knew it myself, as it </p>
<p>was known to Miss Bingley; but her brother is even yet </p>
<p>ignorant of it. That they might </p>
<p>have met without ill </p>
<p>consequence is perhaps probable; but his regard did not </p>
<p>appear to me enough extinguished for him to see her without </p>
<p>some danger. Perhaps this concealment, this disguise was </p>
<p>beneath me; it is done, however, and it was done for the best. </p>
