<p>On this subject I have nothing more to say, no other apology </p>
<p>to offer. If I have wounded your sister's feelings, it was </p>
<p>unknowingly done and though the motives which governed </p>
<p>me may to you very naturally appear insufficient, I have not </p>
<p>yet learnt to condemn them. </p>
<p>"With respect to that other, more weighty accusation, of </p>
<p>having injured Mr. Wickham, I can only refute it by laying </p>
<p>before you the whole of his connection with my family. Of </p>
<p>what he has PARTICULARLY accused me I am ignorant; but </p>
<p>of the truth of what I shall relate, I can summon more than one </p>
<p>witness of undoubted veracity. </p>
<p>"Mr. Wickham is the son of a very respectable man, who </p>
<p>had for many years the management of all the Pemberley </p>
<p>estates, and whose good conduct in the discharge of his trust </p>
<p>naturally inclined my father to be of service to him; and on </p>
<p>George Wickham, who was his godson, his kindness was </p>
<p>therefore liberally bestowed. My father supported him at </p>
<p>school, and </p>
<p>afterwards at Cambridge--most important </p>
<p>assistance, as his own father, always </p>
<p>poor </p>
<p>from </p>
<p>the </p>
<p>extravagance of his wife, would have been unable to give him </p>
<p>a gentleman's education. My father was not only fond of this </p>
<p>young man's society, whose manner were always engaging; he </p>
<p>had also the highest opinion of him, and hoping the church </p>
<p>would be his profession, intended to provide for him in it. As </p>
<p>for myself, it is many, many years since I first began to think </p>
<p>of him in a very different manner. The vicious propensities-- </p>
<p>the want of principle, which he was careful to guard from the </p>
<p>knowledge of his best friend, could not escape the observation </p>
<p>of a young man of nearly the same age with himself, and who </p>
<p>had opportunities of seeing him in unguarded moments, which </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy could not have. Here again shall give you pain--to </p>
<p>what degree you only can tell. But whatever may be the </p>
<p>sentiments which Mr. Wickham has created, a suspicion of </p>
<p>their nature shall not prevent me from unfolding his real </p>
<p>character--it adds even another motive. </p>
<p>"My excellent father died about five years ago; and his </p>
<p>attachment to Mr. Wickham was to the last so steady, that in </p>
<p>his will he particularly recommended it to me, to promote his </p>
<p>advancement in the best manner that his profession might al- </p>
<p>low--and if he took orders, desired that a valuable family </p>
<p>living might be his as soon as it became vacant. There was </p>
<p>also a legacy of one thousand pounds. His own father did not </p>
<p>long survive mine, and within half a year from these events, </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham wrote to inform me that, having finally </p>
<p>resolved against taking orders, he hoped I should not think it </p>
<p>unreasonable for him to expect some more immediate </p>
<p>pecuniary advantage, in lieu of the preferment, by which he </p>
<p>could not be benefited. He had some intention, he added, of </p>
<p>studying law, and I must be aware that the interest of one </p>
<p>thousand pounds would be a very insufficient support therein. </p>
<p>I rather wished, than believed him to be sincere; but, at any </p>
<p>rate, was perfectly ready to accede to his proposal. I knew that </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham ought not to be a clergyman; the business was </p>
<p>therefore soon settled--he resigned all claim to assistance in </p>
<p>the church, were it possible that he could ever be in a situation </p>
<p>to receive it, and accepted in return three thousand pounds. </p>
<p>All connection between us seemed now dissolved. I thought </p>
<p>too ill of him to invite him to Pemberley, or admit his society </p>
<p>in town. In town I believe he chiefly lived, but his studying </p>
<p>the law was a mere pretence, and being now free from all </p>
<p>restraint, his life was a life of idleness and dissipation. For </p>
<p>about three years I heard little of him; but on the decease of </p>
<p>the incumbent of the living which had been designed for him, </p>
<p>he applied to me again by letter for the presentation. His </p>
<p>circumstances, he assured me, and I had no difficulty in </p>
<p>believing it, were exceedingly bad. He had found the law a </p>
<p>most unprofitable study, and was now absolutely resolved on </p>
<p>being ordained, if I would present him to the living in </p>
<p>question--of which he trusted there could be little doubt, as he </p>
<p>was well assured that I had no other person to provide for, and </p>
<p>I could not have forgotten my revered father's intentions. You </p>
<p>will hardly blame me for refusing to comply with this </p>
<p>entreaty, or for resisting every repetition to it. His resentment </p>
<p>was in proportion to the distress of his circumstances--and he </p>
<p>was doubtless as violent in his abuse of me to others as in his </p>
<p>reproaches to myself. After this period every appearance of </p>
<p>acquaintance was dropped. How he lived I know not. But last </p>
<p>summer he was again most painfully obtruded on my notice. </p>
<p>"I must now mention a circumstance which I would wish </p>
<p>to forget myself, and which no obligation less than the present </p>
<p>should induce me to unfold to any human being. Having said </p>
<p>thus much, I feel no doubt of your secrecy. My sister, who is </p>
<p>more than ten years my junior, was left to the guardianship of </p>
<p>my mother's nephew, Colonel Fitzwilliam, and myself. About </p>
<p>a year ago, she was taken from school, and an establishment </p>
<p>formed for her in London; and last summer she went with the </p>
<p>lady who presided over it, to Ramsgate; and thither also went </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham, undoubtedly by design; for there proved to </p>
<p>have been a prior acquaintance between him and Mrs. </p>
<p>Younge, </p>
<p>in </p>
<p>whose character we were most unhappily </p>
<p>deceived; and by her connivance and aid, he so far </p>
<p>recommended himself to Georgiana, whose affectionate heart </p>
<p>retained a strong impression of his kindness to her as a child, </p>
<p>that she was persuaded to believe herself in love, and to </p>
<p>consent to an elopement. She was then but fifteen, which must </p>
<p>be her excuse; and after stating her imprudence, I am happy to </p>
<p>add, that I owed the knowledge of it to herself. I joined them </p>
<p>unexpectedly a day or two before the intended elopement, and </p>
<p>then Georgiana, unable to support the idea of grieving and </p>
<p>offending a brother whom she almost looked up to as a father, </p>
<p>acknowledged the whole to me. You may imagine what I felt </p>
<p>and how I acted. Regard for my sister's credit and feelings </p>
<p>prevented any public exposure; but I wrote to Mr. Wickham, </p>
<p>who left the place immediately, and Mrs. Younge was of </p>
<p>course removed from her charge. Mr. Wickham's chief object </p>
<p>was unquestionably my sister's fortune, which is thirty </p>
<p>thousand pounds; but I cannot help supposing that the hope of </p>
<p>revenging himself on me was a strong inducement. His </p>
<p>revenge would have been complete indeed. </p>
<p>"This, madam, is a faithful narrative of every event in </p>
<p>which we have been concerned together; and if you do not </p>
<p>absolutely reject it as false, you will, I hope, acquit me </p>
<p>henceforth of cruelty towards Mr. Wickham. I know not in </p>
<p>what manner, under what form of falsehood he had imposed </p>
<p>on you; but his success is not perhaps to be wondered at. </p>
<p>Ignorant as you previously were of everything concerning </p>
<p>either, detection could not be in your power, and suspicion </p>
<p>certainly not in your inclination. </p>
<p>"You may possibly wonder why all this was not told you </p>
<p>last night; but I was not then master enough of myself to </p>
<p>know what could or ought to be revealed. For the truth of </p>
<p>everything here related, I can appeal more particularly to the </p>
<p>testimony of Colonel Fitzwilliam, who, from our near </p>
<p>relationship and constant intimacy, and, still more, as one of </p>
<p>the executors of my father's will, has been unavoidably </p>
<p>acquainted with every particular of these transactions. If your </p>
<p>abhorrence of ME should make MY assertions valueless, you </p>
<p>cannot be prevented by the same cause from confiding in my </p>
<p>cousin; and that there may be the possibility of consulting </p>
<p>him, I shall endeavour to find some opportunity of putting this </p>
<p>letter in your hands in the course of the morning. I will only </p>
<p>add, God bless you. </p>
<p>"FITZWILLIAM DARCY" </p>
<heading>Chapter 36. </heading>
<p>If Elizabeth, when Mr. Darcy gave her the letter, did not </p>
<p>expect it to contain a renewal of his offers, she had formed no </p>
<p>expectation at all of its contents. But such as they were, it may </p>
<p>well be supposed how eagerly she went through them, and </p>
<p>what a contrariety of emotion they excited. Her feelings as she </p>
<p>read were scarcely to be defined. With amazement did she </p>
<p>first understand that he believed any apology to be in his </p>
<p>power; and steadfastly was she persuaded, that he could have </p>
<p>no explanation to give, which a just sense of shame would not </p>
<p>conceal. With a strong prejudice against everything he might </p>
<p>say, she began his account of what had happened at </p>
<p>Netherfield. She read with an eagerness which hardly left her </p>
<p>power of comprehension, and from impatience of knowing </p>
<p>what the next sentence might bring, was incapable of </p>
<p>attending to the sense of the one before her eyes. His belief of </p>
<p>her sister's insensibility she instantly resolved to be false; and </p>
<p>his account of the real, the worst objections to the match, </p>
<p>made her too angry to have any wish of doing him justice. He </p>
<p>expressed no regret for what he had done which satisfied her; </p>
<p>his style was not penitent, but haughty. It was all pride and </p>
<p>insolence. </p>
<p>But when this subject was succeeded by his account of Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham--when she read with somewhat clearer attention a </p>
<p>relation of events which, if true, must overthrow every </p>
<p>cherished opinion of his worth, and which bore so alarming an </p>
<p>affinity to his own history of himself--her feelings were yet </p>
<p>more acutely painful and more difficult of definition. </p>
<p>Astonishment, apprehension, and even horror, oppressed her. </p>
<p>She wished to discredit it entirely, repeatedly exclaiming, </p>
<p>"This must be false! This cannot be! This must be the grossest </p>
<p>falsehood!"-and when she had gone through the whole letter, </p>
<p>though scarcely knowing anything of the last page or two, put </p>
<p>it hastily away, protesting that she would not regard it, that </p>
<p>she would never look in it again. </p>
<p>In this perturbed state of mind, with thoughts that could </p>
<p>rest on nothing, she walked on; but it would not do; in half a </p>
<p>minute the letter was unfolded again, and collecting herself as </p>
<p>well as she could, she again began the mortifying perusal of </p>
<p>all that related to Wickham, and commanded herself so far as </p>
<p>to examine the meaning of every sentence. The account of his </p>
<p>connection with the Pemberley family was exactly what he </p>
<p>had related himself; and the kindness of the late Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>though she had not before known its extent, agreed equally </p>
<p>well with his own words. So far each recital confirmed the </p>
<p>other; but when she came to the will, the difference was great. </p>
<p>What Wickham had said of the living was fresh in her </p>
<p>memory, and as she recalled his very words, it was impossible </p>
<p>not to feel that there was gross duplicity on one side or the </p>
<p>other; and, for a few moments, she flattered herself that her </p>
<p>wishes did not err. But when she read and re-read with the </p>
<p>closest attention, the particulars immediately following of </p>
<p>Wickham's resigning all pretensions to the living, of his </p>
<p>receiving in lieu so consider able a sum as three thousand </p>
<p>pounds, again was she forced to hesitate. She put down the </p>
<p>letter, weighed every circumstance with what she meant to be </p>
<p>impartiality--deliberated on the probability of each statement-- </p>
<p>but with little success. On both sides it was only assertion. </p>
<p>Again she read on; but every line proved more clearly that the </p>
<p>affair, which she had believed it impossible that any </p>
<p>contrivance could so represent as to render Mr. Darcy's </p>
<p>conduct in it less than infamous, was capable of a turn which </p>
<p>must make him entirely blameless throughout the whole. </p>
<p>The extravagance and general profligacy which he </p>
<p>scrupled not to lay at Mr. Wickham's charge, exceedingly </p>
<p>shocked her; the more so, as she could bring no proof of its </p>
<p>injustice. She had never heard of him before his entrance into </p>
<p>the ----shire Militia, in which he had engaged at the </p>
<p>persuasion of the </p>
<p>young man who, on meeting him </p>
<p>accidentally in town, had there renewed a slight acquaintance. </p>
<p>Of his former way of life nothing had been known in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire but what he told himself. As to his real </p>
<p>character, had information been in her power, she had never </p>
<p>felt a wish of inquiring. His countenance, voice, and manner </p>
<p>had established him at once in the possession of every virtue. </p>
<p>She tried to recollect some instance of goodness, some </p>
<p>distinguished trait of integrity or benevolence, that might </p>
<p>rescue him from the attacks of Mr. Darcy; or at least, by the </p>
<p>predominance of virtue, atone for those casual errors under </p>
<p>which she would endeavour to class what Mr. Darcy had </p>
<p>described as the idleness and vice of many years' continuance. </p>
<p>But no such recollection befriended her. She could see him </p>
<p>instantly before her, in every charm of air and address; but she </p>
<p>could remember no more substantial good than the general </p>
<p>approbation of the neighbourhood, and the regard which his </p>
<p>social powers had gained him in the mess. After pausing on </p>
<p>this point a considerable while, she once more continued to </p>
<p>read. But, alas! the story which followed, of his designs on </p>
<p>Miss Darcy, received some confirmation from what had </p>
<p>passed between Colonel Fitzwilliam and herself only the </p>
<p>morning before; and at last she was referred for the truth of </p>
<p>every particular to Colonel Fitzwilliam himself--from whom </p>
<p>she had previously received the information of his near </p>
<p>concern in all his cousin's affairs, and whose character she had </p>
<p>no reason to question. At one time she had almost resolved on </p>
<p>applying </p>
<p>to </p>
<p>him, but the idea was checked by the </p>
<p>awkwardness of the application, and at length wholly </p>
<p>banished by the conviction that Mr. Darcy would never have </p>
<p>hazarded such a proposal, if he had not been well assured of </p>
<p>his cousin's corroboration. </p>
<p>She perfectly remembered everything that had passed in </p>
<p>conversation between Wickham and herself, in their first </p>
<p>evening at Mr. Phillips's. Many of his expressions were still </p>
<p>fresh in her memory. She was NOW struck with the </p>
<p>impropriety of such communications to a stranger, and </p>
<p>wondered it had escaped her before. She saw the indelicacy of </p>
<p>putting himself forward as he had done, and the inconsistency </p>
<p>of his professions with his conduct. She remembered that he </p>
<p>had boasted of having no fear of seeing Mr. Darcy--that Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy might leave the country, but that HE should stand his </p>
<p>ground; yet he had avoided the Netherfield ball the very next </p>
<p>week. She remembered also that, till the Netherfield family </p>
<p>had quitted the country, he had told his story to no one but </p>
<p>herself; but that after their removal it had been everywhere </p>
<p>discussed; that he had then no reserves, no scruples in sinking </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's character, though he had assured her that respect </p>
<p>for the father would always prevent his exposing the son. </p>
<p>How differently did everything now appear in which he </p>
<p>was concerned! His attentions to Miss King were now the </p>
<p>consequence of views solely and hatefully mercenary; and the </p>
<p>mediocrity of her fortune proved no longer the moderation of </p>
<p>his wishes, but his eagerness to grasp at anything. His </p>
<p>behaviour to herself could now have had no tolerable motive; </p>
<p>he had either been deceived with regard to her fortune, or had </p>
<p>been gratifying his vanity by encouraging the preference </p>
<p>which she believed she had most incautiously shown. Every </p>
<p>lingering struggle in his favour grew fainter and fainter; and in </p>
<p>farther justification of Mr. Darcy, she could not but allow Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, when questioned by Jane, had long ago asserted his </p>
<p>blamelessness in the affair; that proud and repulsive as were </p>
<p>his manners, she had never, in the whole course of their </p>
<p>acquaintance-an acquaintance which had latterly brought them </p>
<p>much together, and given her a sort of intimacy with his ways- </p>
<p>-seen anything that betrayed him to be unprincipled or unjust- </p>
<p>-anything that spoke him of irreligious or immoral habits; that </p>
<p>among his own connections he was esteemed and valued--that </p>
<p>even Wickham had allowed him merit as a brother, and that </p>
<p>she had often heard him speak so affectionately of his sister as </p>
<p>to prove him capable of SOME amiable feeling; that had his </p>
<p>actions been what Mr. Wickham represented them, so gross a </p>
<p>violation </p>
<p>of </p>
<p>everything </p>
<p>right could hardly have been </p>
<p>concealed from the world; and that friendship between a </p>
<p>person capable of it, and such an amiable man as Mr. Bingley, </p>
<p>was incomprehensible. </p>
<p>She grew absolutely ashamed of herself. Of neither Darcy </p>
<p>nor Wickham could she think without feeling she had been </p>
<p>blind, partial, prejudiced, absurd. </p>
<p>"How despicably I have acted!" she cried; "I, who have </p>
<p>prided myself on my discernment! I, who have valued myself </p>
<p>on my abilities! who have often disdained the generous </p>
<p>candour of my sister, and gratified my vanity in useless or </p>
<p>blameable mistrust! How humiliating is this discovery! Yet, </p>
<p>how just a humiliation! Had I been in love, I could not have </p>
<p>been more wretchedly blind! But vanity, not love, has been </p>
<p>my folly. Pleased with the preference of one, and offended by </p>
<p>the neglect of the other, on the very beginning of our </p>
<p>acquaintance, I have courted prepossession and ignorance, </p>
<p>and driven reason away, where either were concerned. Till </p>
<p>this moment I never knew myself." </p>
<p>From herself to Jane--from Jane to Bingley, her thoughts </p>
<p>were in a line which soon brought to her recollection that Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy's explanation THERE had appeared very insufficient, </p>
<p>and she read it again. Widely different was the effect of a </p>
<p>second perusal. How could she deny that credit to his </p>
<p>assertions in one instance, which she had been obliged to give </p>
<p>in the other? He declared himself to be totally unsuspicious of </p>
<p>her sister's attachment; and she could not help remembering </p>
<p>what Charlotte's opinion had always been. Neither could she </p>
<p>deny the justice of his description of Jane. She felt that Jane's </p>
<p>feelings, though fervent, were little displayed, and that there </p>
<p>was a constant complacency in her air and manner not often </p>
<p>united with great sensibility. </p>
<p>When she came to that part of the letter in which her </p>
<p>family were mentioned in terms of such mortifying, yet </p>
<p>merited reproach, her sense of shame was severe. The justice </p>
<p>of the charge struck her too forcibly for denial, and the </p>
<p>circumstances to which he particularly alluded as having </p>
<p>passed at the Netherfield ball, and as confirming all his first </p>
<p>disapprobation, could not have made a stronger impression on </p>
<p>his mind than on hers. </p>
<p>The compliment to herself and her sister was not unfelt. It </p>
<p>soothed, but it could not console her for the contempt which </p>
<p>had thus been self-attracted by the rest of her family; and as </p>
<p>she considered that Jane's disappointment had in fact been the </p>
<p>work of her nearest relations, and reflected how materially the </p>
<p>credit of both must be hurt by such impropriety of conduct, </p>
<p>she felt depressed beyond anything she had ever known </p>
<p>before. </p>
<p>to every </p>
<p>After wandering along the lane for two hours, giving way </p>
<p>variety of </p>
<p>thought--re-considering events, </p>
<p>determining probabilities, and reconciling herself, as well as </p>
<p>she could, to a change so sudden and so important, fatigue, </p>
<p>and a recollection of her long absence, made her at length </p>
<p>return home; and she entered the house with the wish of </p>
<p>appearing cheerful as usual, and the resolution of repressing </p>
<p>such reflections as must make her unfit for conversation. </p>
<p>She was immediately told that the two gentlemen from </p>
<p>Rosings had each called during her absence; Mr. Darcy, only </p>
<p>for a few minutes, to take leave--but that Colonel Fitzwilliam </p>
<p>had been sitting with them at least an hour, hoping for her </p>
<p>return, and almost resolving to walk after her till she could be </p>
<p>found. Elizabeth could but just AFFECT concern in missing </p>
<p>him; she really rejoiced at it. Colonel Fitzwilliam was no </p>
<p>longer an object; she could think only of her letter. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 37. </heading>
<p>The two gentlemen left Rosings the next morning, and Mr. </p>
<p>Collins having been in waiting near the lodges, to make them </p>
<p>his parting obeisance, was able to bring home the pleasing </p>
<p>intelligence, of their appearing in very good health, and in as </p>
<p>tolerable spirits as could be expected, after the melancholy </p>
<p>scene so lately gone through at Rosings. To Rosings he then </p>
<p>hastened, to console Lady Catherine and her daughter; and on </p>
<p>his return brought back, with great satisfaction, a message </p>
<p>from her ladyship, importing that she felt herself so dull as to </p>
<p>make her very desirous of having them all to dine with her. </p>
<p>Elizabeth could </p>
<p>not </p>
<p>see Lady </p>
<p>Catherine without </p>
<p>recollecting that, had she chosen it, she might by this time </p>
<p>have been presented to her as her future niece; nor could she </p>
<p>think, without a smile, of what her ladyship's indignation </p>
<p>would have been. "What would she have said? how would she </p>
<p>have behaved?" were questions with which she amused </p>
<p>herself. </p>
<p>Their first subject was the diminution of the Rosings party. </p>
<p>"I assure you, I feel it exceedingly," said Lady Catherine; "I </p>
<p>believe no one feels the loss of friends so much as I do. But I </p>
<p>am particularly attached to these young men, and know them </p>
<p>to be so much attached to me! They were excessively sorry to </p>
<p>go! But so they always are. The dear Colonel rallied his spirits </p>
<p>tolerably till just at last; but Darcy seemed to feel it most </p>
<p>acutely, more, I think, than last year. His attachment to </p>
<p>Rosings certainly increases." </p>
<p>Mr. Collins had a compliment, and an allusion to throw in </p>
<p>here, which were kindly smiled on by the mother and </p>
<p>daughter. </p>
<p>Lady Catherine observed, after dinner, that Miss Bennet </p>
<p>seemed out of spirits, and immediately accounting for it by </p>
<p>herself, by supposing that she did not like to go home again so </p>
<p>soon, she added: </p>
<p>"But if that is the case, you must write to your mother and </p>
<p>beg that you may stay a little longer. Mrs. Collins will be very </p>
<p>glad of your company, I am sure." </p>
<p>"I am much obliged to your ladyship for your kind </p>
<p>invitation," replied Elizabeth, "but it is not in my power to </p>
<p>accept it. I must be in town next Saturday." </p>
<p>"Why, at that rate, you will have been here only six weeks. </p>
<p>I expected you to stay two months. I told Mrs. Collins so </p>
<p>before you came. There can be no occasion for your going so </p>
<p>soon. Mrs. Bennet could certainly spare you for another </p>
<p>fortnight." </p>
<p>"But my father cannot. He wrote last week to hurry my </p>
<p>return." </p>
<p>"Oh! your father of course may spare you, if your mother </p>
<p>can. Daughters are never of so much consequence to a father. </p>
<p>And if you will stay another MONTH complete, it will be in </p>
<p>my power to take one of you as far as London, for I am going </p>
<p>there early in June, for a week; and as Dawson does not object </p>
<p>to the barouche-box, there will be very good room for one of </p>
<p>you--and indeed, if the weather should happen to be cool, I </p>
<p>should not object to taking you both, as you are neither of you </p>
<p>large." </p>
<p>"You are all kindness, madam; but I believe we must abide </p>
<p>by our original plan." </p>
<p>Lady Catherine seemed resigned. "Mrs. Collins, you must </p>
<p>send a servant with them. You know I always speak my mind, </p>
<p>and I cannot bear the idea of two young women travelling </p>
<p>post by themselves. It is highly improper. You must contrive </p>
<p>to send somebody. I have the greatest dislike in the world to </p>
<p>that sort of thing. Young women should always be properly </p>
<p>guarded and attended, according to their situation in life. </p>
<p>When my niece Georgiana went to Ramsgate last summer, I </p>
<p>made a point of her having two men-servants go with her. </p>
<p>Miss Darcy, the daughter of Mr. Darcy, of Pemberley, and </p>
<p>Lady Anne, could not have appeared with propriety in a </p>
<p>different manner. I am excessively attentive to all those </p>
<p>things. You must send John with the young ladies, Mrs. </p>
<p>Collins. I am glad it occurred to me to mention it; for it would </p>
<p>really be discreditable to YOU to let them go alone." </p>
<p>"My uncle is to send a servant for us." </p>
<p>"Oh! Your uncle! He keeps a man-servant, does he? I am </p>
<p>very glad you have somebody who thinks of these things. </p>
<p>Where shall you change horses? Oh! Bromley, of course. If </p>
<p>you mention my name at the Bell, you will be attended to." </p>
<p>Lady Catherine had many other questions to ask respecting </p>
<p>their journey, and as she did not answer them all herself, </p>
<p>attention was necessary, which Elizabeth believed to be lucky </p>
<p>for her; or, with a mind so occupied, she might have forgotten </p>
<p>where she was. Reflection must be reserved for solitary hours; </p>
<p>whenever she was alone, she gave way to it as the greatest </p>
<p>relief; and not a day went by without a solitary walk, in which </p>
<p>she might indulge in </p>
<p>all the delight of unpleasant </p>
<p>recollections. </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's letter she was in a fair way of soon knowing </p>
<p>by heart. She studied every sentence; and her feelings towards </p>
<p>its writer were at times widely different. When she </p>
<p>remembered the style of his address, she was still full of </p>
<p>indignation; but when she considered how unjustly she had </p>
<p>condemned and upbraided him, her anger was turned against </p>
<p>herself; and his disappointed feelings became the object of </p>
<p>compassion. His attachment excited gratitude, his general </p>
<p>character respect; but she could not approve him; nor could </p>
<p>she for a moment repent her refusal, or feel the slightest </p>
<p>inclination ever to see him again. In her own past behaviour, </p>
<p>there was a constant source of vexation and regret; and in the </p>
<p>unhappy defects of her family, a subject of yet heavier </p>
<p>chagrin. They were hopeless of remedy. Her father, contented </p>
<p>with laughing at them, would never exert himself to restrain </p>
<p>the wild giddiness of his youngest daughters; and her mother, </p>
<p>with manners so far from right herself, was entirely insensible </p>
<p>of the evil. Elizabeth had frequently united with Jane in an </p>
<p>endeavour to check the imprudence of Catherine and Lydia; </p>
<p>but while they were supported by their mother's indulgence, </p>
<p>what chance could there be of improvement? Catherine, </p>
<p>weak-spirited, irritable, and completely </p>
<p>under Lydia's </p>
<p>guidance, had been always affronted by their advice; and </p>
<p>Lydia, self-willed and careless, would scarcely give them a </p>
<p>hearing. They were ignorant, idle, and vain. While there was </p>
<p>an officer in Meryton, they would flirt with him; and while </p>
<p>Meryton was within a walk of Longbourn, they would be </p>
<p>going there forever. </p>
<p>Anxiety on Jane's behalf was another prevailing concern; </p>
<p>and Mr. Darcy's explanation, by restoring Bingley to all her </p>
<p>former good opinion, heightened the sense of what Jane had </p>
<p>lost. His affection was proved to have been sincere, and his </p>
<p>conduct cleared of all blame, unless any could attach to the </p>
<p>implicitness of his confidence in his friend. How grievous </p>
<p>then was the thought that, of a situation so desirable in every </p>
<p>respect, so replete with advantage, so promising for </p>
<p>happiness, Jane had been deprived, by the folly and </p>
<p>indecorum of her own family! </p>
<p>When to these recollections was added the development of </p>
<p>Wickham's character, it may be easily believed that the happy </p>
<p>spirits which had seldom been depressed before, were now so </p>
<p>much affected as to make it almost impossible for her to </p>
<p>appear tolerably cheerful. </p>
<p>Their engagements at Rosings were as frequent during the </p>
<p>last week of her stay as they had been at first. The very last </p>
<p>evening was spent there; and her ladyship again inquired </p>
<p>minutely into the particulars of their journey, gave them </p>
<p>directions as to the best method of packing, and was so urgent </p>
<p>on the necessity of placing gowns in the only right way, that </p>
<p>Maria thought herself obliged, on her return, to undo all the </p>
<p>work of the morning, and pack her trunk afresh. </p>
<p>When they parted, </p>
<p>Lady Catherine, with great </p>
<p>condescension, wished them a good journey, and invited them </p>
<p>to come to Hunsford again next year; and Miss de Bourgh </p>
<p>exerted herself so far as to curtsey and hold out her hand to </p>
<p>both. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 38. </heading>
<p>On Saturday morning Elizabeth and Mr. Collins met for </p>
<p>breakfast a few minutes before the others appeared; and he </p>
<p>took the opportunity of paying the parting civilities which he </p>
<p>deemed indispensably necessary. </p>
<p>"I know not, Miss Elizabeth," said he, "whether Mrs. </p>
<p>Collins has yet expressed her sense of your kindness in </p>
<p>coming to us; but I am very certain you will not leave the </p>
<p>house without receiving her thanks for it. The favor of your </p>
<p>company has been much felt, I assure you. We know how </p>
<p>little there is to tempt anyone to our humble abode. Our plain </p>
<p>manner of living, our small rooms and few domestics, and the </p>
<p>little we see of the world, must make Hunsford extremely dull </p>
<p>to a young lady like yourself; but I hope you will believe us </p>
<p>grateful for the condescension, and that we have done </p>
<p>everything in our power to prevent your spending your time </p>
<p>unpleasantly." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was eager with her thanks and assurances of </p>
<p>happiness. She had spent six weeks with great enjoyment; and </p>
<p>the pleasure of being with Charlotte, and the kind attentions </p>
<p>she had received, must make HER feel the obliged. Mr. </p>
<p>Collins was gratified, and with a more smiling solemnity </p>
<p>replied: </p>
<p>"It gives me great pleasure to hear that you have passed </p>
<p>your time not disagreeably. We have certainly done our best; </p>
<p>and most fortunately having it in our power to introduce you </p>
<p>to very superior society, and, from our connection with </p>
<p>Rosings, the frequent means of varying the humble home </p>
<p>scene, I think we may flatter ourselves that your Hunsford </p>
<p>visit cannot have been entirely irksome. Our situation with </p>
<p>regard to Lady Catherine's family is indeed the sort of </p>
<p>extraordinary advantage and blessing which few can boast. </p>
<p>You see on what a footing we are. You see how continually </p>
<p>we are engaged there. In truth I must acknowledge that, with </p>
<p>all the disadvantages of this humble parsonage, I should not </p>
<p>think anyone abiding in it an object of compassion, while they </p>
<p>are sharers of our intimacy at Rosings." </p>
<p>Words were insufficient for the elevation of his feelings; </p>
<p>and he was obliged to walk about the room, while Elizabeth </p>
<p>tried to unite civility and truth in a few short sentences. </p>
<p>"You may, in fact, carry a very favourable report of us into </p>
<p>Hertfordshire, my dear cousin. I flatter myself at least that you </p>
<p>will be able to do so. Lady Catherine's great attentions to Mrs. </p>
<p>Collins you have been a daily witness of; and altogether I trust </p>
<p>it does not appear that your friend has drawn an unfortunate- </p>
<p>but on this point it will be as well to be silent. Only let me </p>
<p>assure you, my dear Miss Elizabeth, that I can from my heart </p>
<p>most cordially wish you equal felicity in marriage. My dear </p>
<p>Charlotte and I have but one mind and one way of thinking. </p>
<p>There is in everything a most remarkable resemblance of </p>
<p>character and ideas between us. We seem to have been </p>
<p>designed for each other." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could safely say that it was a great happiness </p>
<p>where that was the case, and with equal sincerity could add, </p>
<p>that she firmly believed and rejoiced in his domestic comforts. </p>
<p>She was not sorry, however, to have the recital of them </p>
<p>interrupted by the lady from whom they sprang. Poor </p>
<p>Charlotte! it was melancholy to leave her to such society! But </p>
<p>she had chosen it with her eyes open; and though evidently </p>
<p>regretting that her visitors were to go, she did not seem to ask </p>
<p>for compassion. Her home and her housekeeping, her parish </p>
<p>and her poultry, and all their dependent concerns, had not yet </p>
<p>lost their charms. </p>
<p>At length the chaise arrived, the trunks were fastened on, </p>
<p>the parcels placed within, and it was pronounced to be ready. </p>
<p>After an affectionate parting between the friends, Elizabeth </p>
<p>was attended to the carriage by Mr. Collins, and as they </p>
<p>walked down the garden he was commissioning her with his </p>
<p>best respects to all her family, not forgetting his thanks for the </p>
<p>kindness he had received at Longbourn in the winter, and his </p>
<p>compliments to Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, though unknown. He </p>
<p>then handed her in, Maria followed, and the door was on the </p>
<p>point of being closed, when he suddenly reminded them, with </p>
<p>some consternation, that they had hitherto forgotten to leave </p>
<p>any message for the ladies at Rosings. </p>
<p>"But," he added, "you will of course wish to have your </p>
<p>humble respects delivered to them, with your grateful thanks </p>
<p>for their kindness to you while you have been here." </p>
<p>Elizabeth made no objection; the door was then allowed to </p>
<p>be shut, and the carriage drove off. </p>
<p>"Good gracious!" cried Maria, after a few minutes' silence, </p>
<p>"it seems but a day or two since we first came! and yet h ow </p>
<p>many things have happened!" </p>
<p>"A great many indeed," said her companion with a sigh. </p>
<p>"We have dined nine times at Rosings, besides drinking tea </p>
<p>there twice! How much I shall have to tell!" Elizabeth added </p>
<p>privately, "And how much I shall have to conceal!" </p>
<p>Their journey was performed without much conversation, </p>
<p>or any alarm; and within four hours of their leaving Hunsford </p>
<p>they reached Mr. Gardiner's house, where they were to remain </p>
<p>a few days. </p>
<p>Jane looked well, and Elizabeth had little opportunity of </p>
<p>studying her spirits, amidst the various engagements which </p>
<p>the kindness of her aunt had reserved for them. But Jane was </p>
<p>to go home with her, and at Longbourn there would be leisure </p>
<p>enough for observation. </p>
<p>It was not without an effort, meanwhile, that she could </p>
<p>wait even for Longbourn, before she told her sister of Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy's proposals. To know that she had the power of </p>
<p>revealing what would so exceedingly astonish Jane, and must, </p>
<p>at the same time, so highly gratify whatever of her own vanity </p>
<p>she had not yet been able to reason away, was such a </p>
<p>temptation to openness as nothing could have conquered but </p>
<p>the state of indecision in which she remained as to the extent </p>
<p>of what she should communicate; and her fear, if she once </p>
<p>entered on the subject, of being hurried into repeating </p>
<p>something of Bingley which might only grieve her sister </p>
<p>further. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 39. </heading>
<p>It was the second week in May, in which the three young </p>
<p>ladies set out together from Gracechurch Street for the town </p>
<p>of ----, in Hertfordshire; and, as they drew near the appointed </p>
<p>inn where Mr. Bennet's carriage was to meet them, they </p>
<p>quickly perceived, in token of the coachman's punctuality, </p>
<p>both Kitty and Lydia looking out of a dining-room upstairs. </p>
<p>These two girls had been above an hour in the place, happily </p>
<p>employed in visiting an opposite milliner, watching the </p>
<p>sentinel on guard, and dressing a salad and cucumber. </p>
<p>After welcoming their sisters, they triumphantly displayed </p>
<p>a table set out with such cold meat as an inn larder usually </p>
<p>affords, exclaiming, "Is not this nice? Is not this an agreeable </p>
<p>surprise?" </p>
<p>"And we mean to treat you all," added Lydia, "but you </p>
<p>must lend us the money, for we have just spent ours at the </p>
<p>shop out there." Then, showing her purchases--"Look here, I </p>
<p>have bought this bonnet. I do not think it is very pretty; but I </p>
<p>thought I might as well buy it as not. I shall pull it to pieces as </p>
<p>soon as I get home, and see if I can make it up any better." </p>
<p>And when her sisters abused it as ugly, she added, with </p>
<p>perfect unconcern, "Oh! but there were two or three much </p>
<p>uglier in the shop; and when I have bought some prettier- </p>
<p>coloured satin to trim it with fresh, I think it will be very </p>
<p>tolerable. Besides, it will not much signify what one wears </p>
<p>this summer, after the ----shire have left Meryton, and they are </p>
<p>going in a fortnight." </p>
<p>"Are they indeed!" cried Elizabeth, with the greatest </p>
<p>satisfaction. </p>
<p>"They are going to be encamped near Brighton; and I do so </p>
<p>want papa to take us all there for the summer! It would be </p>
<p>such a delicious scheme; and I dare say would hardly cost </p>
<p>anything at all. Mamma would like to go too of all things! </p>
<p>Only think what a miserable summer else we shall have!" </p>
<p>"Yes," thought Elizabeth, "THAT would be a delightful </p>
<p>scheme indeed, and completely do for us at once. Good </p>
<p>Heaven! Brighton, and a whole campful of soldiers, to us, </p>
<p>who have been overset already by one poor regiment of </p>
<p>militia, and the monthly balls of Meryton!" </p>
<p>"Now I have got some news for you," said Lydia, as they </p>
<p>sat down at table. "What do you think? It is excellent news- </p>
<p>capital news--and about a certain person we all like!" </p>
<p>Jane and Elizabeth looked at each other, and the waiter </p>
<p>was told he need not stay. Lydia laughed, and said: </p>
<p>"Aye, that is just like your formality and discretion. You </p>
<p>thought the waiter must not hear, as if he cared! I dare say he </p>
<p>often hears worse things said than I am going to say. But he is </p>
<p>an ugly fellow! I am glad he is gone. I never saw such a long </p>
<p>chin in my life. Well, but now for my news; it is about dear </p>
<p>Wickham; too good for the waiter, is it not? There is no </p>
<p>danger of Wickham's marrying Mary King. There's for you! </p>
<p>She is gone down to her uncle at Liverpool: gone to stay. </p>
<p>Wickham is safe." </p>
<p>"And Mary King is safe!" added Elizabeth; "safe from a </p>
<p>connection imprudent as to fortune." </p>
<p>"She is a great fool for going away, if she liked him." </p>
<p>"But I hope there is no strong attachment on either side," </p>
<p>said Jane. </p>
<p>"I am sure there is not on HIS. I will answer for it, he </p>
<p>never cared three straws about her--who could about such a </p>
<p>nasty little freckled thing?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth was shocked to think that, however incapable of </p>
<p>such coarseness of EXPRESSION herself, the coarseness of </p>
<p>the SENTIMENT was little other than her own breast had </p>
<p>harboured and fancied liberal! </p>
<p>As soon as all had ate, and the elder ones paid, the carriage </p>
<p>was ordered; and after some contrivance, the whole party, </p>
<p>with all their boxes, work-bags, and parcels, and the </p>
<p>unwelcome addition of Kitty's and Lydia's purchases, were </p>
<p>seated in it. </p>
<p>"How nicely we are all crammed in," cried Lydia. "I am </p>
<p>glad I bought my bonnet, if it is only for the fun of having </p>
<p>another bandbox! Well, now let us be quite comfortable and </p>
<p>snug, and talk and laugh all the way home. And in the first </p>
<p>place, let us hear what has happened to you all since you went </p>
<p>away. Have you seen any pleasant men? Have you had any </p>
<p>flirting? I was in great hopes that one of you would have got a </p>
<p>husband before you came back. Jane will be quite an old maid </p>
<p>soon, I declare. She is almost three-and-twenty! Lord, how </p>
<p>ashamed I should be of not being married before three- </p>
<p>andtwenty! My aunt Phillips wants you so to get husbands, </p>
<p>you can't think. She says Lizzy had better have taken Mr. </p>
<p>Collins; but _I_ do not think there would have been any fun in </p>
<p>it. Lord! how I should like to be married before any of you; </p>
<p>and then I would chaperon you about to all the balls. Dear me! </p>
<p>we had such a good piece of fun the other day at Colonel </p>
<p>Forster's. Kitty and me were to spend the day there, and Mrs. </p>
<p>Forster promised to have a little dance in the evening; (by the </p>
<p>bye, Mrs. Forster and me are SUCH friends!) and so she </p>
<p>asked the two Harringtons to come, but Harriet was ill, and so </p>
<p>Pen was forced to come by herself; and then, what do you </p>
<p>think we did? We dressed up Chamberlayne in woman's </p>
<p>clothes on purpose to pass for a lady, only think what fun! Not </p>
<p>a soul knew of it, but Colonel and Mrs. Forster, and Kitty and </p>
<p>me, except my aunt, for we were forced to borrow one of her </p>
<p>gowns; and you cannot imagine how well he looked! When </p>
<p>Denny, and Wickham, and Pratt, and two or three more of the </p>
<p>men came in, they did not know him in the least. Lord! how I </p>
<p>laughed! and so did Mrs. Forster. I thought I should have </p>
<p>died. And THAT made the men suspect something, and then </p>
<p>they soon found out what was the matter." </p>
<p>With such kinds of histories of their parties and good </p>
<p>jokes, did Lydia, assisted by Kitty's hints and additions, </p>
<p>endeavour to amuse her companions all the way to </p>
<p>Longbourn. Elizabeth listened as little as she could, but there </p>
<p>was no escaping the frequent mention of Wickham's name. </p>
<p>Their reception at home was most kind. Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>rejoiced to see Jane in undiminished beauty; and more than </p>
<p>once during dinner did Mr. Bennet say voluntarily to </p>
<p>Elizabeth: </p>
<p>"I am glad you are come back, Lizzy." </p>
<p>Their party in the dining-room was large, for almost all the </p>
<p>Lucases came to meet Maria and hear the news; and various </p>
<p>were the subjects that occupied them: Lady Lucas was </p>
<p>inquiring of Maria, after the welfare and poultry of her eldest </p>
<p>daughter; Mrs. Bennet was doubly engaged, on one hand </p>
<p>collecting an account of the present fashions from Jane, who </p>
<p>sat some way below her, and, on the other, retailing them all </p>
<p>to the younger Lucases; and Lydia, in a voice rather louder </p>
<p>than any other person's, was enumerating the various </p>
<p>pleasures of the morning to anybody who would hear her. </p>
<p>"Oh! Mary," said she, "I wish you had gone with us, for we </p>
<p>had such fun! As we went along, Kitty and I drew up the </p>
<p>blinds, and pretended there was nobody in the coach; and I </p>
<p>should have gone so all the way, if Kitty had not been sick; </p>
<p>and when we got to the George, I do think we behaved very </p>
<p>handsomely, for we treated the other three with the nicest cold </p>
<p>luncheon in the world, and if you would have gone, we would </p>
<p>have treated you too. And then when we came away it was </p>
<p>such fun! I thought we never should have got into the coach. I </p>
<p>was ready to die of laughter. And then we were so merry all </p>
<p>the way home! we talked and laughed so loud, that anybody </p>
<p>might have heard us ten miles off!" </p>
<p>To this Mary very gravely replied, "Far be it from me, my </p>
<p>dear sister, to depreciate such pleasures! They would </p>
<p>doubtless be congenial with the generality of female minds. </p>
<p>But I confess they would have no charms for ME--I should </p>
<p>infinitely prefer a book." </p>
<p>But of this answer Lydia heard not a word. She seldom </p>
<p>listened to anybody for more than half a minute, and never </p>
<p>attended to Mary at all. </p>
<p>In the afternoon Lydia was urgent with the rest of the girls </p>
<p>to walk to Meryton, and to see how everybody went on; but </p>
<p>Elizabeth steadily opposed the scheme. It should not be said </p>
<p>that the Miss Bennets could not be at home half a day before </p>
<p>they were in pursuit of the officers. There was another reason </p>
<p>too for her opposition. She dreaded seeing Mr. Wickham </p>
<p>again, and was resolved to avoid it as long as possible. The </p>
<p>comfort to HER of the regiment's approaching removal was </p>
<p>indeed beyond expression. In a fortnight they were to go--and </p>
<p>once gone, she hoped there could be nothing more to plague </p>
<p>her on his account. </p>
<p>She had not been many hours at home before she found </p>
<p>that the Brighton scheme, of which Lydia had given them a </p>
<p>hint at the inn, was under frequent discussion between her </p>
<p>parents. Elizabeth saw directly that her father had not the </p>
<p>smallest intention of yielding; but his answers were at the </p>
<p>same time so vague and equivocal, that her mother, though </p>
<p>often disheartened, had never yet despaired of succeeding at </p>
<p>last. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 40. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth's impatience to acquaint Jane with what had </p>
<p>happened could no longer be overcome; and at length, </p>
<p>resolving to suppress every particular in which her sister was </p>
<p>concerned, and preparing her to be surprised, she related to </p>
<p>her the next morning the chief of the scene between Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy and herself. </p>
<p>Miss Bennet's astonishment was soon lessened by the </p>
<p>strong sisterly partiality which made any admiration of </p>
<p>Elizabeth appear perfectly natural; and all surprise was shortly </p>
<p>lost in other feelings. She was sorry that Mr. Darcy should </p>
<p>have delivered his sentiments in a manner so little suited to </p>
<p>recommend them; but still more was she grieved for the </p>
<p>unhappiness which her sister's refusal must have given him. </p>
<p>"His being so sure of succeeding was wrong," said she, </p>
<p>"and certainly ought not to have appeared; but consider how </p>
<p>much it must increase his disappointment!" </p>
<p>"Indeed," replied Elizabeth, "I am heartily sorry for him; </p>
<p>but he has other feelings, which will probably soon drive </p>
<p>away his regard for me. You do not blame me, however, for </p>
<p>refusing him?" </p>
<p>"Blame you! Oh, no." </p>
<p>"But you blame me for having spoken so warmly of </p>
<p>Wickham?" </p>
<p>"No--I do not know that you were wrong in saying what </p>
<p>you did." </p>
<p>"But you WILL know it, when I tell you what happened </p>
<p>the very next day." </p>
<p>She then spoke of the letter, repeating the whole of its con </p>
<p>tents as far as they concerned George Wickham. What a </p>
<p>stroke was this for poor Jane! who would willingly have gone </p>
<p>through the world without believing that so much wickedness </p>
<p>existed in the whole race of mankind, as was here collected in </p>
<p>one individual. Nor was Darcy's vindication, though grateful </p>
<p>to her feelings, capable of consoling her for such discovery. </p>
<p>Most earnestly did she labour to prove the probability of error, </p>
<p>and seek to clear the one without involving the other. </p>
<p>"This will not do," said Elizabeth; "you never will be able </p>
<p>to make both of them good for anything. Take your choice, </p>
<p>but you must be satisfied with only one. There is but such a </p>
<p>quantity of merit between them; just enough to make one </p>
<p>good sort of man; and of late it has been shifting about pretty </p>
<p>much. For my part, I am inclined to believe it all Darcy's; but </p>
<p>you shall do as you choose." </p>
<p>It was some time, however, before a smile could be </p>
<p>extorted from Jane. </p>
<p>"I do not know when I have been more shocked," said she. </p>
<p>"Wickham so very bad! It is almost past belief. And poor Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy! Dear Lizzy, only consider what he must have suffered. </p>
<p>Such a disappointment! and with the knowledge of your ill </p>
<p>opinion, too! and having to relate such a thing of his sister! It </p>
<p>is really too distressing. I am sure you must feel it so." </p>
<p>"Oh! no, my regret and compassion are all done away by </p>
<p>seeing you so full of both. I know you will do him such ample </p>
<p>justice, that I am growing every moment more unconcerned </p>
<p>and indifferent. Your profusion makes me saving; and if you </p>
<p>lament over him much longer, my heart will be as light as a </p>
<p>feather." </p>
<p>"Poor Wickham! there is such an expression of goodness </p>
<p>in his countenance! such an openness and gentleness in his </p>
<p>manner!" </p>
<p>"There certainly was some great mismanagement in the </p>
<p>education of those two young men. One has got all the </p>
<p>goodness, and the other all the appearance of it." </p>
<p>"I never thought Mr. Darcy so deficient in the </p>
<p>APPEARANCE of it as you used to do." </p>
<p>"And yet I meant to be uncommonly clever in taking so </p>
<p>decided a dislike to him, without any reason. It is such a spur </p>
<p>to one's genius, such an opening for wit, to have a dislike of </p>
<p>that kind. One may be continually abusive without saying </p>
<p>anything just; but one cannot always be laughing at a man </p>
<p>without now and then stumbling on something witty." </p>
<p>"Lizzy, when you first read that letter, I am sure you could </p>
<p>not treat the matter as you do now." </p>
<p>"Indeed, I could not. I was uncomfortable enough, I may </p>
<p>say unhappy. And with no one to speak to about what I felt, </p>
<p>no Jane to comfort me and say that I had not been so very </p>
<p>weak and vain and nonsensical as I knew I had! Oh! how I </p>
<p>wanted you!" </p>
<p>"How unfortunate that you should have used such v ery </p>
<p>strong expressions in speaking of Wickham to Mr. Darcy, for </p>
<p>now they DO appear wholly undeserved." </p>
<p>"Certainly. But the misfortune of speaking with bitterness </p>
<p>is a most natural consequence of the prejudices I had been </p>
<p>encouraging. There is one point on which I want your advice. </p>
<p>I want to be told whether I ought, or ought not, to make our </p>
<p>acquaintances in general understand Wickham's character." </p>
<p>Miss Bennet paused a little, and then replied, "Surely there </p>
<p>can be no occasion for exposing him so dreadfully. What is </p>
<p>your opinion?" </p>
<p>"That it ought not to be attempted. Mr. Darcy has not </p>
<p>authorised me to make his communication public. On the </p>
<p>contrary, every particular relative to his sister was meant to be </p>
<p>kept as much as possible to myself; and if I endeavour to </p>
<p>undeceive people as to the rest of his conduct, who will </p>
<p>believe me? The general prejudice against Mr. Darcy is so </p>
<p>violent, that it would be the death of half the good people in </p>
<p>Meryton to attempt to place him in an amiable light. I am not </p>
<p>equal to it. Wickham will soon be gone; and therefore it will </p>
<p>not signify to anyone here what he really is. Some time hence </p>
<p>it will be all found out, and then we may laugh at their </p>
<p>stupidity in not knowing it before. At present I will say </p>
<p>nothing about it." </p>
<p>"You are quite right. To have his errors made public might </p>
<p>ruin him for ever. He is now, perhaps, sorry for what he has </p>
<p>done, and anxious to re-establish a character. We must not </p>
<p>make him desperate." </p>
<p>The tumult of Elizabeth's mind was allayed by this </p>
<p>conversation. She had got rid of two of the secrets which had </p>
<p>weighed on her for a fortnight, and was certain of a willing </p>
<p>listener in Jane, whenever she might wish to talk again of </p>
<p>either. But there was still something lurking behind, of which </p>
<p>prudence forbade the disclosure. She dared not relate the other </p>
<p>half of Mr. Darcy's letter, nor explain to her sister how </p>
<p>sincerely she had been valued by her friend. Here was </p>
<p>knowledge in which no one could partake; and she was </p>
<p>sensible that nothing less than a perfect understanding </p>
<p>between the parties could justify her in throwing off this last </p>
<p>encumbrance of mystery. "And then," said she, "if that very </p>
<p>improbable event should ever take place, I shall merely be </p>
<p>able to tell what Bingley may tell in a much more agreeable </p>
<p>manner himself. The liberty of communication cannot be </p>
<p>mine till it has lost all its value!" </p>
<p>She was now, on being settled at home, at leisure to </p>
<p>observe the real state of her sister's spirits. Jane was not </p>
<p>happy. She still cherished a very tender affection for Bingley. </p>
<p>Having never even fancied herself in love before, her regard </p>
<p>had all the warmth of first attachment, and, from her age and </p>
<p>disposition, greater steadiness than most first attachments </p>
<p>often boast; and so fervently did she value his remembrance, </p>
<p>and prefer him to every other man, that all her good sense, and </p>
<p>all her attention to the feelings of her friends, were requisite to </p>
<p>check the indulgence of those regrets which must have been </p>
<p>injurious to her own health and their tranquillity. </p>
<p>"Well, Lizzy," said Mrs. Bennet one day, "what is your </p>
<p>opinion NOW of this sad business of Jane's? For my part, I </p>
<p>am determined never to speak of it again to anybody. I told </p>
<p>my sister Phillips so the other day. But I cannot find out that </p>
<p>Jane saw anything of him in London. Well, he is a very </p>
<p>undeserving young man--and I do not suppose there's the least </p>
<p>chance in the world of her ever getting him now. There is no </p>
<p>talk of his coming to Netherfield again in the summer; and I </p>
<p>have inquired of everybody, too, who is likely to know." </p>
<p>"I do not believe he will ever live at Netherfield any </p>
<p>more." </p>
<p>"Oh well! it is just as he chooses. Nobody wants him to </p>
<p>come. Though I shall always say he used my daughter </p>
<p>extremely ill; and if I was her, I would not have put up with it. </p>
<p>Well, my comfort is, I am sure Jane will die of a broken heart; </p>
<p>and then he will be sorry for what he has done." </p>
<p>But as Elizabeth could not receive comfort from any such </p>
<p>expectation, she made no answer. </p>
<p>"Well, Lizzy," continued her mother, soon afterwards, </p>
<p>"and so the Collinses live very comfortable, do they? Well, </p>
<p>well, I only hope it will last. And what sort of table do they </p>
<p>keep? Charlotte is an excellent manager, I dare say. If she is </p>
<p>half as sharp as her mother, she is saving enough. There is </p>
<p>nothing extravagant in THEIR housekeeping, I dare say." </p>
<p>"No, nothing at all." </p>
<p>"A great deal of good management, depend upon it. Yes, </p>
<p>yes. THEY will take care not to outrun their income. THEY </p>
<p>will never be distressed for money. Well, much good may it </p>
<p>do them! And so, I suppose, they often talk of having </p>
<p>Longbourn when your father is dead. They look upon it as </p>
<p>quite their own, I dare say, whenever that happens." </p>
<p>"It was a subject which they could not mention before me." </p>
<p>"No; it would have been strange if they had; but I make no </p>
<p>doubt they often talk of it between themselves. Well, if they </p>
<p>can be easy with an estate that is not lawfully their own, so </p>
<p>much the better. I should be ashamed of having one that was </p>
<p>only entailed on me." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 41. </heading>
<p>The first week of their return was soon gone. The second </p>
<p>began. It was the last of the regiment's stay in Meryton, and </p>
<p>all the young ladies in the neighbourhood were drooping </p>
<p>apace. The dejection was almost universal. The elder Miss </p>
<p>Bennets alone were still able to eat, drink, and sleep, and </p>
<p>pursue the usual course of their employments. Very frequently </p>
<p>were they reproached for this insensibility by Kitty and Lydia, </p>
<p>whose own misery was extreme, and who could </p>
<p>not </p>
<p>comprehend such hardheartedness in any of the family. </p>
<p>"Good Heaven! what is to become of us? What are we to </p>
<p>do?" would they often exclaiming the bitterness of woe. "How </p>
<p>can you be smiling so, Lizzy?" </p>
<p>Their affectionate mother shared all their grief; she </p>
<p>remembered what she had herself endured on a similar </p>
<p>occasion, five and-twenty years ago. </p>
<p>"I am sure," said she, "I cried for two days together when </p>
<p>Colonel Miller's regiment went away. I thought I should have </p>
<p>broken my heart." </p>
<p>"I am sure I shall break MINE," said Lydia. </p>
<p>"If one could but go to Brighton!" observed Mrs. Bennet. </p>
<p>"Oh, yes!--if one could but go to Brighton! But papa is so </p>
<p>disagreeable." </p>
<p>"A little sea-bathing would set me up forever." </p>
<p>"And my aunt Phillips is sure it would do ME a great deal </p>
<p>of good," added Kitty. </p>
<p>Such were the kind of lamentations resounding perpetually </p>
<p>through Longbourn House. Elizabeth tried to be diverted by </p>
<p>them; but all sense of pleasure was lost in shame. She felt </p>
<p>anew the justice of Mr. Darcy's objections; and never had she </p>
<p>been so much disposed to pardon his interference in the views </p>
<p>of his friend. </p>
<p>But the gloom of Lydia's prospect was shortly cleared </p>
<p>away; for she received an invitation from Mrs. Forster, the </p>
<p>wife of the colonel of the regiment, to accompany her to </p>
<p>Brighton. This invaluable friend was a very young woman, </p>
<p>and very lately married. A resemblance in good humour and </p>
<p>good spirits had recommended her and Lydia to each other, </p>
<p>and out of their THREE months' acquaintance they had been </p>
<p>intimate TWO. </p>
<p>The rapture of Lydia on this occasion, her adoration of </p>
<p>Mrs. Forster, the delight of Mrs. Bennet, and the mortification </p>
<p>of Kitty, are scarcely to be described. Wholly inattentive to </p>
<p>her sister's feelings, Lydia flew about the house in restless </p>
<p>ecstasy, calling for everyone's congratulations, and laughing </p>
<p>and talking with more violence than ever; whilst the luckless </p>
<p>Kitty continued in the parlour repined at her fate in terms as </p>
<p>unreasonable as her accent was peevish. </p>
<p>"I cannot see why Mrs. Forster should not ask ME as well </p>
<p>as Lydia," said she, "Though I am NOT her particular friend. I </p>
<p>have just as much right to be asked as she has, and more too, </p>
<p>for I am two years older." </p>
<p>In vain did Elizabeth attempt to make her reasonable, and </p>
<p>Jane to make her resigned. As for Elizabeth herself, this </p>
<p>invitation was so far from exciting in her the same feelings as </p>
<p>in her mother and Lydia, that she considered it as the death </p>
<p>warrant of all possibility of common sense for the latter; and </p>
<p>detestable as such a step must make her were it known, she </p>
<p>could not help secretly advising her father not to let her go. </p>
<p>She represented to him all the improprieties of Lydia's general </p>
<p>behaviour, the little advantage she could derive from the </p>
<p>friendship of such a woman as Mrs. Forster, and the </p>
<p>probability of her being yet more imprudent with such a </p>
<p>companion at Brighton, where the temptations must be greater </p>
<p>than at home. He heard her attentively, and then said: </p>
<p>"Lydia will never be easy until she has exposed herself in </p>
<p>some public place or other, and we can never expect her to do </p>
<p>it with so little expense or inconvenience to her family as </p>
<p>under the present circumstances." </p>
<p>"If you were aware," said Elizabeth, "of the very great </p>
<p>disadvantage to us all which must arise from the public notice </p>
<p>of Lydia's unguarded and imprudent manner--nay, which has </p>
<p>already arisen from it, I am sure you would judge differently </p>
<p>in the affair." </p>
<p>"Already arisen?" repeated Mr. Bennet. "What, has she </p>
<p>frightened away some of your lovers? Poor little Lizzy! But </p>
<p>do not be cast down. Such squeamish youths as cannot bear to </p>
<p>be connected with a little absurdity are not worth a regret. </p>
<p>Come, let me see the list of pitiful fellows who have been kept </p>
<p>aloof by Lydia's folly." </p>
<p>"Indeed you are mistaken. I have no such injuries to resent. </p>
<p>It is not of particular, but of general evils, which I am now </p>
<p>complaining. Our importance, our respectability in the world </p>
<p>must be affected by the wild volatility, the assurance and </p>
<p>disdain of all restraint which mark Lydia's character. Excuse </p>
<p>me, for I must speak plainly. If you, my dear father, will not </p>
<p>take the trouble of checking her exuberant spirits, and of </p>
<p>teaching her that her present pursuits are not to be the </p>
<p>business of her life, she will soon be beyond the reach of </p>
<p>amendment. Her character will be fixed, and she will, at </p>
<p>sixteen, be the most determined flirt that ever made herself or </p>
<p>her family ridiculous; a flirt, too, in the worst and meanest </p>
<p>degree of flirtation; without any attraction beyond youth and a </p>
<p>tolerable person; and, from the ignorance and emptiness of her </p>
<p>mind, wholly unable to ward off any portion of that universal </p>
<p>contempt which her rage for admiration will excite. In this </p>
<p>danger Kitty also is comprehended. She will follow wherever </p>
<p>Lydia leads. Vain, ignorant, idle, and absolutely uncontrolled! </p>
<p>Oh! my dear father, can you suppose it possible that they will </p>
<p>not be censured and despised wherever they are known, and </p>
<p>that their sisters will not be often involved in the disgrace?" </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet saw that her whole heart was in the subject, </p>
<p>and affectionately taking her hand said in reply: </p>
<p>"Do not make yourself uneasy, my love. Wherever you </p>
<p>and Jane are known you must be respected and valued; and </p>
<p>you will not appear to less advantage for having a couple of-- </p>
<p>or I may say, three--very silly sisters. We shall have no peace </p>
<p>at Longbourn if Lydia does not go to Brighton. Let her go, </p>
<p>then. Colonel Forster is a sensible man, and will keep her out </p>
<p>of any real mischief; and she is luckily too poor to be an </p>
<p>object of prey to anybody. At Brighton she will be of less </p>
<p>importance even as a common flirt than she has been here. </p>
<p>The officers will find women better worth their notice. Let us </p>
<p>hope, therefore, that her being there may teach her her own </p>
<p>insignificance. At any rate, she cannot grow many degrees </p>
<p>worse, without authorising us to lock her up for the rest of her </p>
<p>life." </p>
<p>With this answer Elizabeth was forced to be content; but </p>
<p>her own opinion continued the same, and she left him </p>
<p>disappointed and sorry. It was not in her nature, however, to </p>
<p>increase her vexations by dwelling on them. She was </p>
<p>confident of having performed her duty, and to fret over </p>
<p>unavoidable evils, or augment them by anxiety, was no part of </p>
<p>her disposition. </p>
<p>Had Lydia and her mother known the substance of her </p>
<p>conference with her father, their indignation would hardly </p>
<p>have found expression in their united volubility. In Lydia's </p>
<p>imagination, a visit to Brighton comprised every possibility of </p>
<p>earthly happiness. She saw, with the creative eye of fancy, the </p>
<p>streets of that gay bathing-place covered with officers. She </p>
<p>saw herself the object of attention, to tens and to scores of </p>
<p>them at present unknown. She saw all the glories of the camp- </p>
<p>-its tents stretched forth in beauteous uniformity of lines, </p>
<p>crowded with the young and the gay, and dazzling with </p>
<p>scarlet; and, to complete the view, she saw herself seated </p>
<p>beneath a tent, tenderly flirting with at least six officers at </p>
<p>once. </p>
<p>Had she known her sister sought to tear her from such </p>
<p>prospects and such realities as these, what would have been </p>
<p>her sensations? They could have been understood only by her </p>
<p>mother, who might have felt nearly the same. Lydia's going to </p>
<p>Brighton was all that consoled her for her melancholy </p>
<p>conviction of her husband's never intending to go there </p>
<p>himself. </p>
<p>But they were entirely ignorant of what had passed; and </p>
<p>their raptures continued, with little intermission, to the very </p>
<p>day of Lydia's leaving home. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was now to see Mr. Wickham for the last time. </p>
<p>Having been frequently in company with him since her return, </p>
<p>agitation was pretty well over; the agitations of formal </p>
<p>partiality entirely so. She had even learnt to detect, in the very </p>
<p>gentleness which had first delighted her, an affectation and a </p>
<p>sameness to disgust and weary. In his present behaviour to </p>
<p>herself, moreover, she had a fresh source of displeasure, for </p>
<p>the inclination he soon testified of renewing those intentions </p>
<p>which had marked the early part of their acquaintance could </p>
<p>only serve, after what had since passed, to provoke her. She </p>
<p>lost all concern for him in finding herself thus selected as the </p>
<p>object of such idle and frivolous gallantry; and while she </p>
<p>steadily repressed it, could not but feel the reproof contained </p>
<p>in his believing, that however long, and for whatever cause, </p>
<p>his attentions had been withdrawn, her vanity would be </p>
<p>gratified, and her preference secured at any time by their </p>
<p>renewal. </p>
<p>On the very last day of the regiment's remaining at </p>
<p>Meryton, he dined, with other of the officers, at Longbourn; </p>
<p>and so little was Elizabeth disposed to part from him in good </p>
<p>humour, that on his making some inquiry as to the manner in </p>
<p>which her time had passed at Hunsford, she mentioned </p>
<p>Colonel Fitzwilliam's and Mr. Darcy's having both spent three </p>
<p>weeks at Rosings, and asked him, if he was acquainted with </p>
<p>the former. </p>
<p>He looked surprised, displeased, alarmed; but with a </p>
<p>moment's recollection and a returning smile, replied, that he </p>
<p>had formerly seen him often; and, after observing that he was </p>
<p>a very gentlemanlike man, asked her how she had liked him. </p>
<p>Her answer was warmly in his favour. With an air of </p>
<p>indifference he soon afterwards added: </p>
<p>"How long did you say he was at Rosings?" </p>
<p>"Nearly three weeks." </p>
<p>"And you saw him frequently?" </p>
<p>"Yes, almost every day." </p>
<p>"His manners are very different from his cousin's." </p>
<p>"Yes, very different. But I think Mr. Darcy improves upon </p>
<p>acquaintance." </p>
<p>"Indeed!" cried Mr. Wickham with a look which did not </p>
<p>escape her. "And pray, may I ask?--" But checking himself, he </p>
<p>added, in a gayer tone, "Is it in address that he improves? Has </p>
<p>he deigned to add aught of civility to his ordinary style?--for I </p>
<p>dare not hope," he continued in a lower and more serious tone, </p>
<p>"that he is improved in essentials." </p>
<p>"Oh, no!" said Elizabeth. "In essentials, I believe, he is </p>
<p>very much what he ever was." </p>
<p>While she spoke, Wickham looked as if scarcely knowing </p>
<p>whether to rejoice over her words, or to distrust their meaning. </p>
<p>There was a something in her countenance which made him </p>
<p>listen with an apprehensive and anxious attention, while she </p>
<p>added: </p>
<p>"When I said that he improved on acquaintance, I did not </p>
<p>mean that his mind or his manners were in a state of </p>
<p>improvement, but </p>
<p>that, </p>
<p>from knowing him better, his </p>
<p>disposition was better understood." </p>
<p>Wickham's alarm now appeared in a heightened </p>
<p>complexion and agitated look; for a few minute he was silent, </p>
<p>till, shaking off his embarrassment, he turned to her again, and </p>
<p>said in the gentlest of accents: </p>
<p>"You, who so well know my feeling towards Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>will readily comprehend how sincerely I must rejoice that he </p>
<p>is wise enough to assume even the APPEARANCE of what is </p>
<p>right. His pride, in that direction, may be of service, if not to </p>
<p>himself, to many others, for it must only deter him from such </p>
<p>foul misconduct as I have suffered by. I only fear that the sort </p>
<p>of cautiousness to which you, I imagine, have been alluding, </p>
<p>is merely adopted on his visits to his aunt, of whose good </p>
<p>opinion and judgement he stands much in awe. His fear of her </p>
<p>has always operated, I know, when they were together; and a </p>
<p>good deal is to be imputed to his wish of forwarding the </p>
<p>match with Miss de Bourgh, which I am certain he has very </p>
<p>much at heart." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not repress a smile at this, but she </p>
<p>answered only by a slight inclination of the head. She saw that </p>
<p>he wanted to engage her on the old subject of his grievances, </p>
<p>and she was in no humour to indulge him. The rest of the </p>
<p>evening passed with the APPEARANCE, on his side, of usual </p>
<p>cheerfulness, but with no further attempt to distinguish </p>
<p>Elizabeth; and they parted at last with mutual civility, and </p>
<p>possibly a mutual desire of never meeting again. </p>
<p>When the party broke up, Lydia returned with Mrs. Forster </p>
<p>to Meryton, from whence they were to set out early the next </p>
<p>morning. The separation between her and her family was </p>
<p>rather noisy than pathetic. Kitty was the only one who shed </p>
<p>tears; but she did weep from vexation and envy. Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>was diffuse in her good wishes for the felicity of her daughter, </p>
<p>and impressive in her injunctions that she should not miss the </p>
<p>opportunity of enjoying herself as much as possible--advice </p>
<p>which there was every reason to believe would be well </p>
<p>attended to; and in the clamorous happiness of Lydia herself </p>
<p>in bidding farewell, the more gentle adieus of her sisters were </p>
<p>uttered without being heard. </p>
<heading>Chapter 42. </heading>
<p>Had Elizabeth's opinion been all drawn from her own </p>
<p>family, she could not have formed a very pleasing opinion of </p>
<p>conjugal felicity or domestic comfort. Her father, captivated </p>
<p>by youth and beauty, and that appearance of good humour </p>
<p>which youth and beauty generally give, had married a woman </p>
<p>whose weak understanding and illiberal mind had very early </p>
<p>in their marriage put and end to all real affection for her. </p>
<p>Respect, esteem, and confidence had vanished for ever; and </p>
<p>all his views of domestic happiness were overthrown. But Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet was not of a disposition to seek comfort for the </p>
<p>disappointment which his own imprudence had brought on, in </p>
<p>any of those pleasures which too often console the unfortunate </p>
<p>for their folly of their vice. He was fond of the country and of </p>
<p>books; and from these tastes had arisen his principal </p>
<p>enjoyments. To his wife he was very little otherwise indebted, </p>
<p>than as her ignorance and folly had contributed to his </p>
<p>amusement. This is not the sort of happiness which a man </p>
<p>would in general wish to owe to his wife; but where other </p>
<p>powers of entertainment are wanting, the true philosopher will </p>
<p>derive benefit from such as are given. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, however, had never been blind to </p>
<p>the </p>
<p>impropriety of her father's behaviour as a husband. She had </p>
<p>always seen it with pain; but respecting his abilities, and </p>
<p>grateful for his affectionate treatment of herself, she </p>
<p>endeavoured to forget what she could not overlook, and to </p>
<p>banish from her thoughts that continual breach of conjugal </p>
<p>obligation and decorum which, in exposing his wife to the </p>
<p>contempt of her own children, was so highly reprehensible. </p>
<p>But she had never felt so strongly as now the disadvantages </p>
<p>which must attend the children of so unsuitable a marriage, </p>
<p>nor ever been so fully aware of the evils arising from so ill- </p>
<p>judged a direction of talents; talents, which, rightly used, </p>
<p>might at least have preserved the respectability of his </p>
<p>daughters, even if incapable of enlarging the mind of his wife. </p>
<p>When Elizabeth had rejoiced over Wickham's departure </p>
<p>she found little other cause for satisfaction in the loss of the </p>
<p>regiment. Their parties abroad were less varied than before, </p>
<p>and at home she had a mother and sister whose constant </p>
<p>repinings at the dullness of everything around them threw a </p>
<p>real gloom over their domestic circle; and, though Kitty might </p>
<p>in time regain her natural degree of sense, since the disturbers </p>
<p>of her brain were removed, her other sister, from whose </p>
<p>disposition greater evil might be apprehended, was likely to </p>
<p>be hardened in all her folly and assurance by a situation of </p>
<p>such double danger as a watering-place and a camp. Upon the </p>
<p>whole, therefore, she found, what has been sometimes been </p>
<p>found before, that an event to which she had been looking </p>
<p>with impatient desire did not, in taking place, bring all the </p>
<p>satisfaction she had promised herself. It was consequently </p>
<p>necessary to name some other period for the commencement </p>
<p>of actual felicity-to have some other point on which her </p>
<p>wishes and hopes might be fixed, and by again enjoying the </p>
<p>pleasure of anticipation, console herself for the present, and </p>
<p>prepare for another disappointment. Her tour to the Lakes was </p>
<p>now the object of her happiest thoughts; it was her best </p>
<p>consolation for all the uncomfortable hours which the </p>
<p>discontentedness of her mother and Kitty made inevitable; and </p>
<p>could she have included Jane in the scheme, every part of it </p>
<p>would have been perfect. </p>
<p>"But it is fortunate," thought she, "that I have something to </p>
<p>wish for. Were the whole arrangement complete, my </p>
<p>disappointment would be certain. But here, by carrying with </p>
<p>me one ceaseless source of regret in my sister's absence, I </p>
<p>may reasonably hope to have all my expectations of pleasure </p>
<p>realised. A scheme of which every part promises delight can </p>
<p>never be successful; and general disappointment is only </p>
<p>warded off by the defence of some little peculiar vexation." </p>
<p>When Lydia went away she promised to write very often </p>
<p>and very minutely to her mother and Kitty; but her letters </p>
<p>were always long expected, and always very short. Those to </p>
<p>her mother contained little else than that they were just </p>
<p>returned from the library, where such and such officers had </p>
<p>attended them, and where she had seen such beautiful </p>
<p>ornaments as made her quite wild; that she had a new gown, </p>
<p>or a new parasol, which she would have described more fully, </p>
<p>but was obliged to leave off in a violent hurry, as Mrs. Forster </p>
<p>called her, and they were going off to the camp; and from her </p>
<p>correspondence with her sister, there was still less to be </p>
<p>learnt--for her letters to Kitty, though rather longer, were </p>
<p>much too full of lines under the words to be made public. </p>
<p>After the first fortnight or three weeks of her absence, </p>
<p>health, good humour, and cheerfulness began to reappear at </p>
<p>Longbourn. Everything wore a happier aspect. The families </p>
<p>who had been in town for the winter came back again, and </p>
<p>summer finery and summer engagements arose. Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>was restored to her usual querulous serenity; and, by the </p>
<p>middle of June, Kitty was so much recovered as to be able to </p>
<p>enter Meryton without tears; an event of such happy promise </p>
<p>as to make Elizabeth hope that by the following Christmas she </p>
<p>might be so tolerably reasonable as not to mention an officer </p>
<p>above once a day, unless, by some cruel and malicious </p>
<p>arrangement at the War Office, another regiment should be </p>
<p>quartered in Meryton. </p>
