<p>The time fixed for the beginning of their northern tour was </p>
<p>now fast approaching, and a fortnight only was wanting of it, </p>
<p>when a letter arrived from Mrs. Gardiner, which at once </p>
<p>delayed its commencement and curtailed its extent. Mr. </p>
<p>Gardiner would be prevented by business from setting out till </p>
<p>a fortnight later in July, and must be in London again within a </p>
<p>month, and as that left too short a period for them to go so far, </p>
<p>and see so much as they had proposed, or at least to see it with </p>
<p>the leisure and comfort they had built on, they were obliged to </p>
<p>give up the Lakes, and substitute a more contracted tour, and, </p>
<p>according to the present plan, were to go no farther north </p>
<p>wards than Derbyshire. In that county there was enough to be </p>
<p>seen to occupy the chief of their three weeks; and to Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner it had a peculiarly strong attraction. The town where </p>
<p>she had formerly passed some years of her life, and where </p>
<p>they were now to spend a few days, was probably as great an </p>
<p>object of her curiosity as all the celebrated beauties of </p>
<p>Matlock, Chatsworth, Dovedale, or the Peak. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was excessively disappointed; she had set her </p>
<p>heart on seeing the Lakes, and still thought there might have </p>
<p>been time enough. But it was her business to be satisfied--and </p>
<p>certainly her temper to be happy; and all was soon right again. </p>
<p>With the mention of Derbyshire there were many ideas </p>
<p>connected. It was impossible for her to see the word without </p>
<p>thinking of Pemberley and its owner. "But surely," said she, "I </p>
<p>may enter his county without impunity, and rob it of a few </p>
<p>petrified spars without his perceiving me." </p>
<p>The period of expectation was now doubled. Four weeks </p>
<p>were to pass away before her uncle and aunt's arrival. But </p>
<p>they did pass away, and Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner, with their </p>
<p>four children, did at length appear at Longbourn. The </p>
<p>children, two girls of six and eight years old, and two younger </p>
<p>boys, were to be left under the particular care of their cousin </p>
<p>Jane, who was the general favourite, and whose steady sense </p>
<p>and sweetness of temper exactly adapted her for attending to </p>
<p>them in every way-teaching them, playing with them, and </p>
<p>loving them. </p>
<p>The Gardiners stayed only one night at Longbourn, and set </p>
<p>off the next morning with Elizabeth in pursuit of novelty and </p>
<p>amusement. One enjoyment was certain--that of suitableness </p>
<p>of companions; a suitableness which comprehended health </p>
<p>and temper to bear inconveniences--cheerfulness to enhance </p>
<p>every pleasure--and affection and intelligence, which might </p>
<p>supply it among themselves if there were disappointments </p>
<p>abroad. </p>
<p>It is not the object of this work to give a description of </p>
<p>Derbyshire, nor of any of the remarkable places through </p>
<p>which their route thither lay; Oxford, Blenheim, Warwick, </p>
<p>Kenilworth, Birmingham, etc. are sufficiently known. A small </p>
<p>part of Derbyshire is all the present concern. To the little town </p>
<p>of Lambton, the scene of Mrs. Gardiner's former residence, </p>
<p>and where she had lately learned some acquaintance still </p>
<p>remained, they bent their steps, after having seen all the </p>
<p>principal wonders of the country; and within five miles of </p>
<p>Lambton, Elizabeth found from her aunt that Pemberley was </p>
<p>situated. It was not in their direct road, nor more than a mile </p>
<p>or two out of it. In talking over their route the evening before, </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner expressed an inclination to see the place again. </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner declared his willingness, and Elizabeth was </p>
<p>applied to for her approbation. </p>
<p>"My love, should not you like to see a place of which you </p>
<p>have heard so much?" said her aunt; "a place, too, with which </p>
<p>so many of your acquaintances are connected. Wickham </p>
<p>passed all his youth there, you know." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was distressed. She felt that she had no business </p>
<p>at Pemberley, and was obliged to assume a disinclination for </p>
<p>seeing it. She must own that she was tired of seeing great </p>
<p>houses; after going over so many, she really had no pleasure </p>
<p>in fine carpets or satin curtains. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner abused her stupidity. "If it were merely a </p>
<p>fine house richly furnished," said she, "I should not care about </p>
<p>it myself; but the grounds are delightful. They have some of </p>
<p>the finest woods in the country." </p>
<p>Elizabeth said no more--but her mind could not acquiesce. </p>
<p>The possibility of meeting Mr. Darcy, while viewing the </p>
<p>place, instantly occurred. It would be dreadful! She blushed at </p>
<p>the very idea, and thought it would be better to speak openly </p>
<p>to her aunt than to run such a risk. But against this there were </p>
<p>objections; and she finally resolved that it could be the last </p>
<p>resource, if her private inquiries to the absence of the family </p>
<p>were unfavourably answered. </p>
<p>Accordingly, when she retired at night, she asked the </p>
<p>chambermaid whether Pemberley were not a very fine place? </p>
<p>what was the name of its proprietor? and, with no little alarm, </p>
<p>whether the family were down for the summer? A most </p>
<p>welcome negative followed the last question--and her alarms </p>
<p>now being removed, she was at leisure to feel a great deal of </p>
<p>curiosity to see the house herself; and when the subject was </p>
<p>revived the next morning, and she was again applied to, could </p>
<p>readily answer, and with a proper air of indifference, that she </p>
<p>had not really any dislike to the scheme. To Pemberley, </p>
<p>therefore, they were to go. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 43. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth, as they drove along, watched for the first </p>
<p>appearance of Pemberley Woods with some perturbation; and </p>
<p>when at length they turned in at the lodge, her spirits were in a </p>
<p>high flutter. </p>
<p>The park was very large, and contained great variety of </p>
<p>ground. They entered it in one of its lowest points, and drove </p>
<p>for some time through a beautiful wood stretching over a wide </p>
<p>extent. </p>
<p>Elizabeth's mind was too full for conversation, but she saw </p>
<p>and admired every remarkable spot and point of view. They </p>
<p>gradually ascended for half-a-mile, and then found themselves </p>
<p>at the top of a considerable eminence, where the wood ceased, </p>
<p>and the eye was instantly caught by Pemberley House, </p>
<p>situated on the opposite side of a valley, into which the road </p>
<p>with some abruptness wound. It was a large, handsome stone </p>
<p>building, standing well on rising ground, and backed by a </p>
<p>ridge of high woody hills; and in front, a stream of some </p>
<p>natural importance was swelled into greater, but without any </p>
<p>artificial appearance. Its banks were neither formal nor falsely </p>
<p>adorned. Elizabeth was delighted. She had never seen a place </p>
<p>for which nature had done more, or where natural beauty had </p>
<p>been so little counteracted by an awkward taste. They were all </p>
<p>of them warm in their admiration; and at that moment she felt </p>
<p>that to be mistress of Pemberley might be something! </p>
<p>They descended the hill, crossed the bridge, and drove to </p>
<p>the door; and, while examining the nearer aspect of the house, </p>
<p>all her apprehension of meeting its owner returned. She </p>
<p>dreaded lest the chambermaid had been mistaken. On </p>
<p>applying to see the place, they were admitted into the hall; and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, as they waited for the housekeeper, had leisure to </p>
<p>wonder at her being where she was. </p>
<p>The housekeeper came; a respectable-looking elderly </p>
<p>woman, much less fine, and more civil, than she had any </p>
<p>notion of finding her. They followed her into the dining- </p>
<p>parlour. It was a large, well proportioned room, handsomely </p>
<p>fitted up. Elizabeth, after slightly surveying it, went to a </p>
<p>window to enjoy its prospect. The hill, crowned with wood, </p>
<p>which they had descended, receiving increased abruptness </p>
<p>from the distance, was a beautiful object. Every disposition of </p>
<p>the ground was good; and she looked on the whole scene, the </p>
<p>river, the trees scattered on its banks and the winding of the </p>
<p>valley, as far as she could trace it, with delight. As they </p>
<p>passed into other rooms these objects were taking different </p>
<p>positions; but from every window there were beauties to be </p>
<p>seen. The rooms were lofty and handsome, and their furniture </p>
<p>suitable to the fortune of its proprietor; but Elizabeth saw, </p>
<p>with admiration of his taste, that it was neither gaudy nor </p>
<p>uselessly fine; with less of splendour, and more real elegance, </p>
<p>than the furniture of Rosings. </p>
<p>"And of this place," thought she, "I might have been </p>
<p>mistress! With these rooms I might now have been familiarly </p>
<p>acquainted! Instead of viewing them as a stranger, I might </p>
<p>have rejoiced in them as my own, and welcomed to them as </p>
<p>visitors my uncle and aunt. But no,"--recollecting herself-- </p>
<p>"that could never be; my uncle and aunt would have been lost </p>
<p>to me; I should not have been allowed to invite them." </p>
<p>This was a lucky recollection--it saved her from something </p>
<p>very like regret. </p>
<p>She longed to inquire of the housekeeper whether her </p>
<p>master was really absent, but had not the courage for it. At </p>
<p>length however, the question was asked by her uncle; and she </p>
<p>turned away with alarm, while Mrs. Reynolds replied that he </p>
<p>was, adding, "But we expect him to-morrow, with a large </p>
<p>party of friends." How rejoiced was Elizabeth that their own </p>
<p>journey had not by any circumstance been delayed a day! </p>
<p>Her aunt now called her to look at a picture. She </p>
<p>approached and saw the likeness of Mr. Wickham, suspended, </p>
<p>amongst several other miniatures, over the mantelpiece. Her </p>
<p>aunt asked her, smilingly, how she liked it. The housekeeper </p>
<p>came forward, and told them it was a picture of a young </p>
<p>gentleman, the son of her late master's steward, who had been </p>
<p>brought up by him at his own expense. "He is now gone into </p>
<p>the army," she added; "but I am afraid he has turned out very </p>
<p>wild." </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece with a smile, but </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not return it. </p>
<p>"And that," said Mrs. Reynolds, pointing to another of the </p>
<p>miniatures, "is my master--and very like him. It was drawn at </p>
<p>the same time as the other--about eight years ago." </p>
<p>"I have heard much of your master's fine person," said </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner, looking at the picture; "it is a handsome face. </p>
<p>But, Lizzy, you can tell us whether it is like or not." </p>
<p>Mrs. Reynolds respect for Elizabeth seemed to increase on </p>
<p>this intimation of her knowing her master. </p>
<p>"Does that young lady know Mr. Darcy?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth coloured, and said: "A little." </p>
<p>"And do not you think him a very handsome gentleman, </p>
<p>ma'am?" </p>
<p>"Yes, very handsome." </p>
<p>"I am sure I know none so handsome; but in the gallery </p>
<p>upstairs you will see a finer, larger picture of him than this. </p>
<p>This room was my late master's favourite room, and these </p>
<p>miniatures are just as they used to be then. He was very fond </p>
<p>of them." </p>
<p>This accounted to Elizabeth for Mr. Wickham's being </p>
<p>among them. </p>
<p>Mrs. Reynolds then directed their attention to one of Miss </p>
<p>Darcy, drawn when she was only eight years old. </p>
<p>"And is Miss Darcy as handsome as her brother?" said </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner. </p>
<p>"Oh! yes--the handsomest young lady that ever was seen; </p>
<p>and so accomplished!--She plays and sings all day long. In the </p>
<p>next room is a new instrument just come down for her--a </p>
<p>present from my master; she comes here to-morrow with </p>
<p>him." </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner, whose manners were very easy and pleasant, </p>
<p>encouraged her communicativeness by his questions and </p>
<p>remarks; Mrs. Reynolds, either by pride or attachment, had </p>
<p>evidently great pleasure in talking of her master and his sister. </p>
<p>"Is your master much at Pemberley in the course of the </p>
<p>year?" </p>
<p>"Not so much as I could wish, sir; but I dare say he may </p>
<p>spend half his time here; and Miss Darcy is always down for </p>
<p>the summer months." </p>
<p>"Except," thought Elizabeth, "when she goes to </p>
<p>Ramsgate." </p>
<p>"If your master would marry, you might see more of him." </p>
<p>"Yes, sir; but I do not know when THAT will be. I do not </p>
<p>know who is good enough for him." </p>
<p>Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner smiled. Elizabeth could not help </p>
<p>saying, "It is very much to his credit, I am sure, that you </p>
<p>should think so." </p>
<p>"I say no more than the truth, and everybody will say that </p>
<p>knows him," replied the other. Elizabeth thought this was </p>
<p>going pretty far; and she listened with increasing astonishment </p>
<p>as the housekeeper added, "I have never known a cross word </p>
<p>from him in my life, and I have known him ever since he was </p>
<p>four years old." </p>
<p>This was praise, of all others most extraordinary, most </p>
<p>opposite to her ideas. That he was not a good-tempered man </p>
<p>had been her firmest opinion. Her keenest attention was </p>
<p>awakened; she longed to hear more, and was grateful to her </p>
<p>uncle for saying: </p>
<p>"There are very few people of whom so much can be said. </p>
<p>You are lucky in having such a master." </p>
<p>"Yes, sir, I know I am. If I were to go through the world, I </p>
<p>could not meet with a better. But I have always observed, that </p>
<p>they who are good-natured when children, are good-natured </p>
<p>when they grow up; and he was always </p>
<p>the </p>
<p>sweetest- </p>
<p>tempered, most generous-hearted boy in the world." </p>
<p>Elizabeth almost stared at her. "Can this be Mr. Darcy?" </p>
<p>thought she. </p>
<p>"His father was an excellent man," said Mrs. Gardiner. </p>
<p>"Yes, ma'am, that he was indeed; and his son will be just </p>
<p>like him--just as affable to the poor." </p>
<p>Elizabeth listened, wondered, doubted, and was impatient </p>
<p>for more. Mrs. Reynolds could interest her on no other </p>
<p>point. She related the subjects of the pictures, the </p>
<p>dimensions of the rooms, and the price of the furniture, in </p>
<p>vain, Mr. Gardiner, highly amused by the kind of family </p>
<p>prejudice to which </p>
<p>he attributed her excessive </p>
<p>commendation of her master, soon led again to the subject; </p>
<p>and she dwelt with energy on his many merits as they </p>
<p>proceeded together up the great staircase. </p>
<p>"He is the best landlord, and the best master," said she, </p>
<p>"that ever lived; not like the wild young men nowadays, who </p>
<p>think of nothing but themselves. There is not one of his </p>
<p>tenants or servants but will give him a good name. Some </p>
<p>people call him proud; but I am sure I never saw anything of </p>
<p>it. To my fancy, it is only because he does not rattle away like </p>
<p>other young men." </p>
<p>"In what an amiable light does this place him!" thought </p>
<p>Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"This fine account of him," whispered her aunt as they </p>
<p>walked, "is not quite consistent with his behaviour to our poor </p>
<p>friend." </p>
<p>"Perhaps we might be deceived." </p>
<p>"That is not very likely; our authority was too good." </p>
<p>On reaching the spacious lobby above they were shown </p>
<p>into a very pretty sitting-room, lately fitted up with greater </p>
<p>elegance and lightness than the apartments below; and were </p>
<p>informed that it was but just done to give pleasure to Miss </p>
<p>Darcy, who had taken a liking to the room when last at </p>
<p>Pemberley. </p>
<p>"He is certainly a good brother," said Elizabeth, as she </p>
<p>walked towards one of the windows. </p>
<p>Mrs. Reynolds anticipated Miss Darcy's delight, when she </p>
<p>should enter the room. "And this is always the way with him," </p>
<p>she added. "Whatever can give his sister any pleasure is sure </p>
<p>to be done in a moment. There is nothing he would not do for </p>
<p>her." </p>
<p>The picture-gallery, and two or three of the principal </p>
<p>bedrooms, were all that remained to be shown. In the former </p>
<p>were many good paintings; but Elizabeth knew nothing of the </p>
<p>art; and from such as had been already visible below, she had </p>
<p>willingly turned to look at some drawings of Miss Darcy's, in </p>
<p>crayons, whose subjects were usually more interesting, and </p>
<p>also more intelligible. </p>
<p>In the gallery there were many family portraits, but they </p>
<p>could have little to fix the attention of a stranger. Elizabeth </p>
<p>walked in quest of the only face whose features would be </p>
<p>known to her. At last it arrested her--and she beheld a striking </p>
<p>resemblance to Mr. Darcy, with such a smile over the face as </p>
<p>she remembered to have sometimes seen when he looked at </p>
<p>her. She stood several minutes before the picture, in earnest </p>
<p>contemplation, and returned to it again before they quitted the </p>
<p>gallery. Mrs. Reynolds informed them that it had been taken </p>
<p>in his father's lifetime. </p>
<p>There was certainly at this moment, in Elizabeth's mind, a </p>
<p>more gentle sensation towards the original than she had ever </p>
<p>felt at the height of their acquaintance. The commendation </p>
<p>bestowed on him by Mrs. Reynolds was of no trifling nature. </p>
<p>What praise is more valuable than the praise of an intelligent </p>
<p>servant? As a brother, a landlord, a master, she considered </p>
<p>how many people's happiness were in his guardianship!--how </p>
<p>much of pleasure or pain was it in his power to bestow!--how </p>
<p>much of good or evil must be done by him! Every idea that </p>
<p>had been brought forward by the housekeeper was favourable </p>
<p>to his character, and as she stood before the canvas on which </p>
<p>he was represented, and fixed his eyes upon herself, she </p>
<p>thought of his regard with a deeper sentiment of gratitude than </p>
<p>it had ever raised before; she remembered its warmth, and </p>
<p>softened its impropriety of expression. </p>
<p>When all of the house that was open to general inspection </p>
<p>had been seen, they returned downstairs, and, taking leave of </p>
<p>the housekeeper, were consigned over to the gardener, who </p>
<p>met them at the hall-door. </p>
<p>As they walked across the hall towards the river, Elizabeth </p>
<p>turned back to look again; her uncle and aunt stopped also, </p>
<p>and while the former was conjecturing as to the date of the </p>
<p>building, the owner of it himself suddenly came forward from </p>
<p>the road, which led behind it to the stables. </p>
<p>They were within twenty yards of each other, and so </p>
<p>abrupt was his appearance, that it was impossible to avoid his </p>
<p>sight. Their eyes instantly met, and the cheeks of both were </p>
<p>overspread with the deepest blush. He absolutely started, and </p>
<p>for a moment seemed immovable from surprise; but shortly </p>
<p>recovering himself, advanced towards the party, and spoke to </p>
<p>Elizabeth, if not in terms of perfect composure, at least of </p>
<p>perfect civility. </p>
<p>She had instinctively turned away; but stopping on his </p>
<p>approach, received his compliments with an embarrassment </p>
<p>impossible to be overcome. Had his first appearance, or his </p>
<p>resemblance to the picture they had just been examining, been </p>
<p>insufficient to assure the other two that they now saw Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy, the gardener's expression of surprise, on beholding his </p>
<p>master, must immediately have told it. They stood a little </p>
<p>aloof while he was talking to their niece, who, astonished and </p>
<p>confused, scarcely dared lift her eyes to his face, and knew </p>
<p>not what answer she returned to his civil inquiries after her </p>
<p>family. Amazed at the alteration of his manner since they last </p>
<p>parted, every sentence that he uttered was increasing her </p>
<p>embarrassment; and every idea of the impropriety of her being </p>
<p>found there recurring to her mind, the few minutes in which </p>
<p>they continued were some of the most uncomfortable in her </p>
<p>life. Nor did he seem much more at ease; when he spoke, his </p>
<p>accent had none of its usual sedateness; and he repeated his </p>
<p>inquiries as to the time of her having left Longbourn, and of </p>
<p>her having stayed in Derbyshire, so often, and in so hurried a </p>
<p>way, as plainly spoke the distraction of his thoughts. </p>
<p>At length every idea seemed to fail him; and, after standing </p>
<p>a few moments without saying a word, he suddenly </p>
<p>recollected himself, and took leave. </p>
<p>The others then joined her, and expressed admiration of his </p>
<p>figure; but Elizabeth heard not a word, and wholly engrossed </p>
<p>by her own feelings, followed them in silence. She was </p>
<p>overpowered by shame and vexation. Her coming there was </p>
<p>the most unfortunate, the most ill-judged thing in the world! </p>
<p>How strange it must appear to him! In what a disgraceful light </p>
<p>might it not strike so vain a man! It might seem as if she had </p>
<p>purposely thrown herself in his way again! Oh! why did she </p>
<p>come? Or, why did he thus come a day before he was </p>
<p>expected? Had they been only ten minutes sooner, they should </p>
<p>have been beyond the reach of his discrimination; for it was </p>
<p>plain that he was that moment arrived--that moment alighted </p>
<p>from his horse or his carriage. She blushed again and again </p>
<p>over the perverseness of the meeting. And his behaviour, so </p>
<p>strikingly altered--what could it mean? That he should even </p>
<p>speak to her was amazing!--but to speak with such civility, to </p>
<p>inquire after her family! Never in her life had she seen his </p>
<p>manners so little dignified, never had he spoken with such </p>
<p>gentleness as on this unexpected meeting. What a contrast did </p>
<p>it offer to his last address in Rosings Park, when he put his </p>
<p>letter into her hand! She knew not what to think, or how to </p>
<p>account for it. </p>
<p>They had now entered a beautiful walk by the side of the </p>
<p>water, and every step was bringing forward a nobler fall of </p>
<p>ground, or a finer reach of the woods to which they were </p>
<p>approaching; but it was some time before Elizabeth was </p>
<p>sensible of any of it; and, though she answered mechanically </p>
<p>to the repeated appeals of her uncle and aunt, and seemed to </p>
<p>direct her eyes to such objects as they pointed out, she </p>
<p>distinguished no part of the scene. Her thoughts were all fixed </p>
<p>on that one spot of Pemberley House, whichever it might be, </p>
<p>where Mr. Darcy then was. She longed to know what at the </p>
<p>moment was passing in his mind--in what manner he thought </p>
<p>of her, and whether, in defiance of everything, she was still </p>
<p>dear to him. Perhaps he had been civil only because he felt </p>
<p>himself at ease; yet there had been THAT in his voice which </p>
<p>was not like ease. Whether he had felt more of pain or of </p>
<p>pleasure in seeing her she could not tell, but he certainly had </p>
<p>not seen her with composure. </p>
<p>At length, however, the remarks of her companions on her </p>
<p>absence of mind aroused her, and she felt the necessity of </p>
<p>appearing more like herself. </p>
<p>They entered the woods, and bidding adieu to the river for </p>
<p>a while, ascended some of the higher grounds; when, in spots </p>
<p>where the opening of the trees gave the eye power to wander, </p>
<p>were many charming views of the valley, the opposite hills, </p>
<p>with the long range of woods overspreading many, and </p>
<p>occasionally part of the stream. Mr. Gardiner expressed a </p>
<p>wish of going round the whole park, but feared it might be </p>
<p>beyond a walk. With a triumphant smile they were told that it </p>
<p>was ten miles round. It settled the matter; and they pursued </p>
<p>the accustomed circuit; which brought them again, after some </p>
<p>time, in a descent among hanging woods, to the edge of the </p>
<p>water, and one of its narrowest parts. They crossed it by a </p>
<p>simple bridge, in character with the general air of the scene; it </p>
<p>was a spot less adorned than any they had yet visited; and the </p>
<p>valley, here contracted into a glen, allowed room only for the </p>
<p>stream, and a narrow walk amidst the rough coppice-wood </p>
<p>which bordered it. Elizabeth longed to explore its windings; </p>
<p>but when they had crossed the bridge, and perceived their </p>
<p>distance from the house, Mrs. Gardiner, who was not a great </p>
<p>walker, could go no farther, and thought only of returning to </p>
<p>the carriage as quickly as possible. Her niece was, therefore, </p>
<p>obliged to submit, and they took their way towards the house </p>
<p>on the opposite side of the river, in the nearest direction; but </p>
<p>their progress was slow, for Mr. Gardiner, though seldom able </p>
<p>to indulge the taste, was very fond of fishing, and was so </p>
<p>much engaged in watching the occasional appearance of some </p>
<p>trout in the water, and talking to the man about them, that he </p>
<p>advanced but little. Whilst wandering on in this slow manner, </p>
<p>they were again surprised, and Elizabeth's astonishment was </p>
<p>quite equal to what it had been at first, by the sight of Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy approaching them, and at no great distance. The walk </p>
<p>here being here less sheltered than on the other side, allowed </p>
<p>them to see him before </p>
<p>they met. Elizabeth, however </p>
<p>astonished, was at least more prepared for an interview than </p>
<p>before, and resolved to appear and to speak with calmness, if </p>
<p>he really intended to meet them. For a few moments, indeed, </p>
<p>she felt that he would probably strike into some other path. </p>
<p>The idea lasted while a turning in the walk concealed him </p>
<p>from their view; the turning past, he was immediately before </p>
<p>them. With a glance, she saw that he had lost none of his </p>
<p>recent civility; and, to imitate his politeness, she began, as </p>
<p>they met, to admire the beauty of the place; but she had not </p>
<p>got beyond the words "delightful," and "charming," when </p>
<p>some unlucky recollections obtruded, and she fancied that </p>
<p>praise of Pemberley from her might be mischievously </p>
<p>construed. Her colour changed, and she said no more. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner was standing a little behind; and on her </p>
<p>pausing, he asked her if she would do him the honour of </p>
<p>introducing him to her friends. This was a stroke of civility </p>
<p>for which she was quite unprepared; and she could hardly </p>
<p>suppress a smile at his being now seeking the acquaintance of </p>
<p>some of those very people against whom his pride had </p>
<p>revolted in his offer to herself. "What will be his surprise," </p>
<p>thought she, "when he knows who they are? He takes them </p>
<p>now for people of fashion." </p>
<p>The introduction, however, was immediately made; and as </p>
<p>she named their relationship to herself, she stole a sly look at </p>
<p>him, to see how he bore it, and was not without the </p>
<p>expectation of his decamping as fast as he could from such </p>
<p>disgraceful companions. That he was SURPRISED by the </p>
<p>connection was evident; he sustained it, however, with </p>
<p>fortitude, and so far from going away, turned his back with </p>
<p>them, and entered into conversation with Mr. Gardiner. </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not but be pleased, could not but triumph. It </p>
<p>was consoling that he should know she had some relations for </p>
<p>whom there was no need to blush. She listened most </p>
<p>attentively to all that passed between them, and gloried in </p>
<p>every expression, every sentence of her uncle, which marked </p>
<p>his intelligence, his taste, or his good manners. </p>
<p>The conversation soon turned upon fishing; and she heard </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy invite him, with the greatest civility, to fish there as </p>
<p>often as he chose while he continued in the neighbourhood, </p>
<p>offering at the same time to supply him with fishing tackle, </p>
<p>and pointing out those parts of the stream where there was </p>
<p>usually most sport. Mrs. Gardiner, who was walking arm- </p>
<p>inarm with Elizabeth, gave her a look expressive of wonder. </p>
<p>Elizabeth said nothing, but it gratified her exceedingly; the </p>
<p>compliment must be all for herself. Her astonishment, </p>
<p>however, was extreme, and continually was she repeating, </p>
<p>"Why is he so altered? From what can it proceed? It cannot be </p>
<p>for ME--it cannot be for MY sake that his manners are thus </p>
<p>softened. My reproofs at Hunsford could not work such a </p>
<p>change as this. It is impossible that he should still love me." </p>
<p>After walking some time in this way, the two ladies in </p>
<p>front, the two gentlemen behind, on resuming their places, </p>
<p>after descending to the brink of the river for the better </p>
<p>inspection of some curious water-plant, there chanced to be a </p>
<p>little alteration. It originated in Mrs. Gardiner, who, fatigued </p>
<p>by the exercise of the morning, found Elizabeth's arm </p>
<p>inadequate to her support, and consequently preferred her </p>
<p>husband's. Mr. Darcy took her place by her niece, and they </p>
<p>walked on together. After a short silence, the lady first spoke. </p>
<p>She wished him to know that she had been assured of his </p>
<p>absence before she came to the place, and accordingly began </p>
<p>by observing, that his arrival had been very unexpected--"for </p>
<p>your housekeeper," she added, "informed us that you would </p>
<p>certainly not be here till to-morrow; and indeed, before we left </p>
<p>Bakewell, we understood that you were not immediately </p>
<p>expected in the country." He acknowledged the truth of it all, </p>
<p>and said that business with his steward had occasioned his </p>
<p>coming forward a few hours before the rest of the party with </p>
<p>whom he had been travelling. "They will join me early to- </p>
<p>morrow," he continued, "and among them are some who will </p>
<p>claim an acquaintance with you--Mr. Bingley and his sisters." </p>
<p>Elizabeth answered only by a slight bow. Her thoughts </p>
<p>were instantly driven back to the time when Mr. Bingley's </p>
<p>name had been the last mentioned between them; and, if she </p>
<p>might judge by his complexion, HIS mind was not very </p>
<p>differently engaged. </p>
<p>"There is also one other person in the party," he continued </p>
<p>after a pause, "who more particularly wishes to be known to </p>
<p>you. Will you allow me, or do I ask too much, to introduce </p>
<p>my sister to your acquaintance during your stay at Lambton?" </p>
<p>The surprise of such an application was great indeed; it </p>
<p>was too great for her to know in what manner she acceded to </p>
<p>it. She immediately felt that whatever desire Miss Darcy </p>
<p>might have of being acquainted with her must be the work of </p>
<p>her brother, and, without looking farther, it was satisfactory; it </p>
<p>was gratifying to know that his resentment had not made him </p>
<p>think really ill of her. </p>
<p>They now walked on in silence, each of them deep in </p>
<p>thought. Elizabeth was not comfortable; that was impossible; </p>
<p>but she was flattered and pleased. His wish of introducing his </p>
<p>sister to her was a compliment of the highest kind. They soon </p>
<p>outstripped the others, and when they had reached the </p>
<p>carriage, Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner were half a quarter of a mile </p>
<p>behind. </p>
<p>He then asked her to walk into the house--but she declared </p>
<p>herself not tired, and they stood together on the lawn. At such </p>
<p>a time much might have been said, and silence was very </p>
<p>awkward. She wanted to talk, but there seemed to be an </p>
<p>embargo on every subject. At last she recollected that she had </p>
<p>been travelling, and they talked of Matlock and Dove Dale </p>
<p>with great perseverance. Yet time and her aunt moved slowly- </p>
<p>and her patience and her ideas were nearly worn our before </p>
<p>the tete-a-tete was over. On Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner's coming </p>
<p>up they were all pressed to go into the house and take some </p>
<p>refreshment; but this was declined, and they parted on each </p>
<p>side with utmost politeness. Mr. Darcy handed the ladies into </p>
<p>the carriage; and when it drove off, Elizabeth saw him </p>
<p>walking slowly towards the house. </p>
<p>The observations of her uncle and aunt now began; and </p>
<p>each of them pronounced him to be infinitely superior to </p>
<p>anything they had expected. "He is perfectly well behaved, </p>
<p>polite, and unassuming," said her uncle. </p>
<p>"There IS something a little stately in him, to be sure," </p>
<p>replied her aunt, "but it is confined to his air, and is not </p>
<p>unbecoming. I can now say with the housekeeper, that though </p>
<p>some people may call him proud, I have seen nothing of it." </p>
<p>"I was never more surprised than by his behaviour to us. It </p>
<p>was more than civil; it was really attentive; and there was no </p>
<p>necessity for such attention. His acquaintance with Elizabeth </p>
<p>was very trifling." </p>
<p>"To be sure, Lizzy," said her aunt, "he is not so handsome </p>
<p>as Wickham; or, rather, he has not Wickham's countenance, </p>
<p>for his features are perfectly good. But how came you to tell </p>
<p>me that he was so disagreeable?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth excused herself as well as she could; said that </p>
<p>she had liked him better when they had met in Kent than </p>
<p>before, and that she had never seen him so pleasant as this </p>
<p>morning. </p>
<p>"But perhaps he may be a little whimsical in his civilities," </p>
<p>replied her uncle. "Your great men often are; and therefore I </p>
<p>shall not take him at his word, as he might change his mind </p>
<p>another day, and warn me off his grounds." </p>
<p>Elizabeth felt that they had entirely misunderstood his </p>
<p>character, but said nothing. </p>
<p>"From what we have seen of him," continued Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner, "I really should not have thought that he could have </p>
<p>behaved in so cruel a way by anybody as he has done by poor </p>
<p>Wickham. He has not an ill-natured look. On the contrary, </p>
<p>there is something pleasing about his mouth when he speaks. </p>
<p>And there is something of dignity in his countenance that </p>
<p>would not give one an unfavourable idea of his heart. But, to </p>
<p>be sure, the good lady who showed us his house did give him </p>
<p>a most flaming character! I could hardly help laughing aloud </p>
<p>sometimes. But he is a liberal master, I suppose, and THAT in </p>
<p>the eye of a servant comprehends every virtue." </p>
<p>Elizabeth here felt herself called on to say something in </p>
<p>vindication of his behaviour to Wickham; and therefore gave </p>
<p>them to understand, in as guarded a manner as she could, that </p>
<p>by what she had heard from his relations in Kent, his actions </p>
<p>were capable of a very different construction; and that his </p>
<p>character was by no means so faulty, nor Wickham's so </p>
<p>amiable, as they had been considered in Hertfordshire. In </p>
<p>confirmation of this, she related the particulars of all the </p>
<p>pecuniary transactions in which they had been connected, </p>
<p>without actually naming her authority, but stating it to be such </p>
<p>as such as might be relied on. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner was surprised and concerned; but as they </p>
<p>were now approaching the scene of her former pleasures, </p>
<p>every idea gave way to the charm of recollection; and she was </p>
<p>too much engaged in pointing out to her husband all the </p>
<p>interesting spots in its environs to think of anything else. </p>
<p>Fatigued as she had been by the morning's walk they had no </p>
<p>sooner dined than she set off again in quest of her former </p>
<p>acquaintance, and the evening was spent in the satisfactions of </p>
<p>a intercourse renewed after many years' discontinuance. </p>
<p>The occurrences of the day were too full of interest to </p>
<p>leave Elizabeth much attention for any of these new friends; </p>
<p>and she could do nothing but think, and think with wonder, of </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's civility, and, above all, of his wishing her to be </p>
<p>acquainted with his sister. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 44. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth had settled it that Mr. Darcy would bring his </p>
<p>sister to visit her the very day after her reaching Pemberley; </p>
<p>and was consequently resolved not to be out of sight of the </p>
<p>inn the whole of that morning. But her conclusion was false; </p>
<p>for on the very morning after their arrival at Lambton, these </p>
<p>visitors came. They had been walking about the place with </p>
<p>some of their new friends, and were just returning to the inn to </p>
<p>dress themselves for dining with the same family, when the </p>
<p>sound of a carriage drew them to a window, and they saw a </p>
<p>gentleman and a lady in a curricle driving up the street. </p>
<p>Elizabeth immediately recognizing the livery, guessed what it </p>
<p>meant, and imparted no small degree of her surprise to her </p>
<p>relations by acquainting them with the honour which she </p>
<p>expected. Her uncle and aunt were all amazement; and the </p>
<p>embarrassment of her manner as she spoke, joined to the </p>
<p>circumstance itself, and many of the circumstances of the </p>
<p>preceding day, opened to them a new idea on the business. </p>
<p>Nothing had ever suggested it before, but they felt that there </p>
<p>was no other way of accounting for such attentions from such </p>
<p>a quarter than by supposing a partiality for their niece. While </p>
<p>these newly-born notions were passing in their heads, the </p>
<p>perturbation of Elizabeth's feelings was at every moment </p>
<p>increasing. She was quite amazed at her own discomposure; </p>
<p>but amongst other causes of disquiet, she dreaded lest the </p>
<p>partiality of the brother should have said too much in her </p>
<p>favour; and, more than commonly anxious to please, she </p>
<p>naturally suspected that every power of pleasing would fail </p>
<p>her. </p>
<p>She retreated from the window, fearful of being seen; and </p>
<p>as she walked up and down the room, endeavouring to </p>
<p>compose herself, saw such looks of inquiring surprise in her </p>
<p>uncle and aunt as made everything worse. </p>
<p>Miss Darcy and her brother appeared, and this formidable </p>
<p>introduction took place. With astonishment did Elizabeth see </p>
<p>that her new acquaintance was at least as much embarrassed </p>
<p>as herself. Since her being at Lambton, she had heard that </p>
<p>Miss Darcy was exceedingly proud; but the observation of a </p>
<p>very few minutes convinced her that she was only </p>
<p>exceedingly shy. She found it difficult to obtain even a word </p>
<p>from her beyond a monosyllable. </p>
<p>Miss Darcy was tall, and on a larger scale than Elizabeth; </p>
<p>and, though little more than sixteen, her figure was formed, </p>
<p>and her appearance womanly and graceful. She was less </p>
<p>handsome than her brother; but there was sense and good </p>
<p>humour in her face, and her manners were perfectly </p>
<p>unassuming and gentle. Elizabeth, who had expected to find </p>
<p>in her as acute and unembarrassed an observer as ever Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy had been, was much relieved by discerning such </p>
<p>different feelings. </p>
<p>They had not long been together before Mr. Darcy told her </p>
<p>that Bingley was also coming to wait on her; and she had </p>
<p>barely time to express her satisfaction, and prepare for such a </p>
<p>visitor, when Bingley's quick step was heard on the stairs, and </p>
<p>in a moment he entered the room. All Elizabeth's anger </p>
<p>against him had been long done away; but had she still felt </p>
<p>any, it could hardly have stood its ground against the </p>
<p>unaffected cordiality with which he expressed himself on </p>
<p>seeing her again. He inquired in a friendly, though general </p>
<p>way, after her family, and looked and spoke with the same </p>
<p>good-humoured ease that he had ever done. </p>
<p>To Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner he was scarcely a less </p>
<p>interesting personage than to herself. They had long wished to </p>
<p>see him. The whole party before them, indeed, excited a lively </p>
<p>attention. The suspicions which had just arisen of Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>and their niece directed their observation towards each with </p>
<p>an earnest though guarded inquiry; and they soon drew from </p>
<p>those inquiries the full conviction that one of them at least </p>
<p>knew what it was to love. Of the lady's sensations they </p>
<p>remained a little in doubt; but that the gentleman was </p>
<p>overflowing with admiration was evident enough. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, on her side, had much to do. She wanted to </p>
<p>ascertain the feelings of each of her visitors; she wanted to </p>
<p>compose her own, and to make herself agreeable to all; and in </p>
<p>the latter object, where she feared most to fail, she was most </p>
<p>sure of success, for those to whom she endeavoured to give </p>
<p>pleasure were prepossessed in her favour. Bingley was ready, </p>
<p>Georgiana was eager, and Darcy determined, to be pleased. </p>
<p>In seeing Bingley, her thoughts naturally flew to her sister; </p>
<p>and, oh! how ardently did she long to know whether any of </p>
<p>his were directed in a like manner. Sometimes she could fancy </p>
<p>that he talked less than on former occasions, and once or twice </p>
<p>pleased herself with the notion that, as he looked at her, he </p>
<p>was trying to trace a resemblance. But, though this might be </p>
<p>imaginary, she could not be deceived as to his behaviour to </p>
<p>Miss Darcy, who had been set up as a rival to Jane. No look </p>
<p>appeared on either side that spoke particular regard. Nothing </p>
<p>occurred between them that could justify the hopes of his </p>
<p>sister. On this point she was soon satisfied; and two or three </p>
<p>little circumstances occurred ere they parted, which, in her </p>
<p>anxious interpretation, denoted a recollection of Jane not </p>
<p>untinctured by tenderness, and a wish of saying more that </p>
<p>might lead to the mention of her, had he dared. He observed to </p>
<p>her, at a moment when the others were talking together, and in </p>
<p>a tone which had something of real regret, that it "was a very </p>
<p>long time since he had had the pleasure of seeing her;" and, </p>
<p>before she could reply, he added, "It is above eight months. </p>
<p>We have not met since the 26th of November, when we were </p>
<p>all dancing together at Netherfield." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was pleased to find his memory so exact; and he </p>
<p>afterwards took occasion to ask her, when unattended to by </p>
<p>any of the rest, whether ALL her sisters were at Longbourn. </p>
<p>There was not much in the question, nor in the preceding </p>
<p>remark; but there was a look and a manner which gave them </p>
<p>meaning. </p>
<p>It was not often that she could turn her eyes on Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>himself; but, whenever she did catch a glimpse, she saw an </p>
<p>expression of general complaisance, and in all that he said she </p>
<p>heard an accent so removed from hauteur or disdain of his </p>
<p>companions, as convinced her that the improvement of </p>
<p>manners which she had yesterday witnessed however </p>
<p>temporary its existence might prove, had at least outlived one </p>
<p>day. When she saw him thus seeking the acquaintance and </p>
<p>courting </p>
<p>the </p>
<p>good </p>
<p>opinion of people with whom any </p>
<p>intercourse a few months ago would have been a disgrace-- </p>
<p>when she saw him thus civil, not only to herself, but to the </p>
<p>very relations whom he had openly disdained, and recollected </p>
<p>their last lively scene in Hunsford Parsonage--the difference, </p>
<p>the change was so great, and struck so forcibly on her mind, </p>
<p>that she could hardly restrain her astonishment from being </p>
<p>visible. Never, even in the company of his dear friends at </p>
<p>Netherfield, or his dignified relations at Rosings, had she seen </p>
<p>him so desirous to please, so free from self-consequence or </p>
<p>unbending reserve, as now, when no importance could result </p>
<p>from the success of his endeavours, and when even the </p>
<p>acquaintance of those to whom his attentions were addressed </p>
<p>would draw down the ridicule and censure of the ladies both </p>
<p>of Netherfield as Rosings. </p>
<p>Their visitors stayed with them above half-an-hour; and </p>
<p>when they arose to depart, Mr. Darcy called on his sister to </p>
<p>join him in expressing their wish of seeing Mr. and Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner, and Miss Bennet, to dinner at Pemberley, before </p>
<p>they left the country. Miss Darcy, though with a diffidence </p>
<p>which marked her little in the habit of giving invitations, </p>
<p>readily obeyed. Mrs. Gardiner looked at her niece, desirous of </p>
<p>knowing how SHE, whom the invitation most concerned, felt </p>
<p>disposed as to its acceptance, but Elizabeth had turned away </p>
<p>her head. Presuming however, that this studied avoidance </p>
<p>spoke rather a momentary embarrassment than any dislike of </p>
<p>the proposal, and seeing in her husband, who was fond of </p>
<p>society, a perfect willingness to accept it, she ventured to </p>
<p>engage for her attendance, and the day after the next was fixed </p>
<p>on. </p>
<p>Bingley expressed great pleasure in the certainty of seeing </p>
<p>Elizabeth again, having still a great deal to say to her, and </p>
<p>many inquiries to make after all their Hertfordshire friends. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, construing all this into a wish of hearing her speak </p>
<p>of her sister, was pleased, and on this account, as well as some </p>
<p>others, found herself, when their visitors left them, capable of </p>
<p>considering the last half-hour with some satisfaction, though </p>
<p>while it was passing, the enjoyment of it had been little. Eager </p>
<p>to be alone, and fearful of inquiries or hints from her uncle </p>
<p>and aunt, she stayed with them only long enough to hear their </p>
<p>favourable opinion of Bingley, and then hurried away to </p>
<p>dress. </p>
<p>But she had no reason to fear Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner's </p>
<p>curiosity; it was not their wish to force her communication. It </p>
<p>was evident that she was much better acquainted with Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy than they had before any idea of; it was evident that he </p>
<p>was very much in love with her. They saw much to interest, </p>
<p>but nothing to justify inquiry. </p>
<p>Of Mr. Darcy it was now a matter of anxiety to think well; </p>
<p>and, as far as their acquaintance reached, there was no fault to </p>
<p>find. They could not be untouched by his politeness; and had </p>
<p>they drawn his character from their own feelings and his </p>
<p>servant's report, without any reference to any other account, </p>
<p>the circle in Hertfordshire to which he was known would not </p>
<p>have recognized it for Mr. Darcy. There was now an interest, </p>
<p>however, in believing the housekeeper; and they soon became </p>
<p>sensible that the authority of a servant who had known him </p>
<p>since he was four years old, and whose own manners </p>
<p>indicated respectability, was not to be hastily rejected. Neither </p>
<p>had anything occurred in the intelligence of their Lambton </p>
<p>friends that could materially lessen its weight. They had </p>
<p>nothing to accuse him of but pride; pride he probably had, and </p>
<p>if not, it would certainly be imputed by the inhabitants of a </p>
<p>small market-town where the family did not visit. It was </p>
<p>acknowledged, however, that he was a liberal man, and did </p>
<p>much good among the poor. </p>
<p>With respect to Wickham, the travellers soon found that he </p>
<p>was not held there in much estimation; for though the chief of </p>
<p>his concerns with the son of his patron were imperfectly </p>
<p>understood, it was yet a well-known fact that, on his quitting </p>
<p>Derbyshire, he had left many debts behind him, which Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy afterwards discharged. </p>
<p>As for Elizabeth, her thoughts were at Pemberley this </p>
<p>evening more than the last; and the evening, though as it </p>
<p>passed it seemed long, was not long enough to determine her </p>
<p>feelings towards ONE in that mansion; and she lay awake two </p>
<p>whole hours endeavouring to make them out. She certainly </p>
<p>did not hate him. No; hatred had vanished long ago, and she </p>
<p>had almost as long been ashamed of ever feeling a dislike </p>
<p>against him, that could be so called. The respect created by </p>
<p>the conviction of his valuable qualities, though at first </p>
<p>unwillingly admitted, had for some time ceased to be </p>
<p>repugnant to her feeling; and it was now heightened into </p>
<p>somewhat of a friendlier nature, by the testimony so highly in </p>
<p>his favour, and bringing forward his disposition in so amiable </p>
<p>a light, which yesterday had produced. But above all, above </p>
<p>respect and esteem, there was a motive within her of goodwill </p>
<p>which could not be overlooked. It was gratitude; gratitude, not </p>
<p>merely for having once loved her, but for loving her still well </p>
<p>enough to forgive all the petulance and acrimony of her </p>
<p>manner in rejecting him, and all the unjust accusations </p>
<p>accompanying her rejection. He who, she had been persuaded, </p>
<p>would avoid her as his greatest enemy, seemed, on this </p>
<p>accidental meeting, most eager to preserve the acquaintance, </p>
<p>and without </p>
<p>any indelicate display of regard, or any </p>
<p>peculiarity of manner, where their two selves only were </p>
<p>concerned, was soliciting the good opinion of her friends, and </p>
<p>bent on making her known to his sister. Such a change in a </p>
<p>man of so much pride exciting not only astonishment but </p>
<p>gratitude--for to love, ardent love, it must be attributed; and as </p>
<p>such its impression on her was of a sort to be encouraged, as </p>
<p>by no means unpleasing, though it could not be exactly </p>
<p>defined. She respected, she esteemed, she was grateful to him, </p>
<p>she felt a real interest in his welfare; and she only wanted to </p>
<p>know how far she wished that welfare to depend upon herself, </p>
<p>and how far it would be for the happiness of both that she </p>
<p>should employ the power, which her fancy told her she still </p>
<p>possessed, of bringing on her the renewal of his addresses. </p>
<p>It had been settled in the evening between the aunt and the </p>
<p>niece, that such a striking civility as Miss Darcy's in coming </p>
<p>to see them on the very day of her arrival at Pemberley, for </p>
<p>she had reached it only to a late breakfast, ought to be </p>
<p>imitated, though it could not be equalled, by some exertion of </p>
<p>politeness on their side; and, consequently, that it would be </p>
<p>highly expedient to wait on her at Pemberley the following </p>
<p>morning. They were, therefore, to go. Elizabeth was pleased; </p>
<p>though when she asked herself the reason, she had very little </p>
<p>to say in reply. </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner left them soon after breakfast. The fishing </p>
<p>scheme had been renewed the day before, and a positive </p>
<p>engagement made of his meeting some of the gentlemen at </p>
<p>Pemberley before noon. </p>
<heading>Chapter 45. </heading>
<p>Convinced as Elizabeth now was that Miss Bingley's </p>
<p>dislike of her had originated in jealousy, she could not help </p>
<p>feeling how unwelcome her appearance at Pemberley must be </p>
<p>to her, and was curious to know with how much civility on </p>
<p>that lady's side the acquaintance would now be renewed. </p>
<p>On reaching the house, they were shown through the hall </p>
<p>into the saloon, whose northern aspect rendered it delightful </p>
<p>for summer. Its windows opening to the ground, admitted a </p>
<p>most refreshing view of the high woody hills behind the </p>
<p>house, and of the beautiful oaks and Spanish chestnuts which </p>
<p>were scattered over the intermediate lawn. </p>
<p>In this house they were received by Miss Darcy, who was </p>
<p>sitting there with Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley, and the lady </p>
<p>with whom she lived in London. Georgiana's reception of </p>
<p>them was very civil, but attended with all the embarrassment </p>
<p>which, though proceeding from shyness and the fear of doing </p>
<p>wrong, would easily give to those who felt themselves inferior </p>
<p>the belief of her being proud and reserved. Mrs. Gardiner and </p>
<p>her niece, however, did her justice, and pitied her. </p>
<p>By Mrs. Hurst and Miss Bingley they were noticed only by </p>
<p>a curtsey; and, on their being seated, a pause, awkward as </p>
<p>such pauses must always be, succeeded for a few moments. It </p>
<p>was first broken by Mrs. Annesley, a genteel, agreeable- </p>
<p>looking woman, whose endeavour to introduce some kind of </p>
<p>discourse proved her to be more truly well-bred than either of </p>
<p>the others; and between her and Mrs. Gardiner, with </p>
<p>occasional help from Elizabeth, the conversation was carried </p>
<p>on. Miss Darcy looked as if she wished for courage enough to </p>
<p>join in it; and sometimes did venture a short sentence when </p>
<p>there was least danger of its being heard. </p>
<p>Elizabeth soon saw that she was herself closely watched by </p>
<p>Miss Bingley, and that she could not speak a word, especially </p>
<p>to Miss Darcy, without calling her attention. This observation </p>
<p>would not have prevented her from trying to talk to the latter, </p>
<p>had they not been seated at an inconvenient distance; but she </p>
<p>was not sorry to be spared the necessity of saying much. Her </p>
<p>own thoughts were employing her. She expected every </p>
<p>moment that some of the gentlemen would enter the room. </p>
<p>She wished, she feared that the master of the house might be </p>
<p>amongst them; and whether she wished or feared it most, she </p>
<p>could scarcely determine. After sitting in this manner a </p>
<p>quarter of an hour without hearing Miss Bingley's voice, </p>
<p>Elizabeth was roused by receiving from her a cold inquiry </p>
<p>after the health of her family. She answered with equal </p>
<p>indifference and brevity, and the others said no more. </p>
<p>The next variation which their visit afforded was produced </p>
<p>by the entrance of servants with cold meat, cake, and a variety </p>
<p>of all the finest fruits in season; but this did not take place till </p>
<p>after many a significant look and smile from Mrs. Annesley to </p>
<p>Miss Darcy had been given, to remind her of her post. There </p>
<p>was now employment for the whole party--for though they </p>
<p>could not all talk, they could all eat; and the beautiful </p>
<p>pyramids of grapes, nectarines, and peaches soon collected </p>
<p>them round the table. </p>
<p>While thus engaged, Elizabeth had a fair opportunity of </p>
<p>deciding whether she most feared or wished for the </p>
<p>appearance of Mr. Darcy, by the feelings which prevailed on </p>
<p>his entering the room; and then, though but a moment before </p>
<p>she had believed her wishes to predominate, she began to </p>
<p>regret that he came. </p>
<p>He had been some time with Mr. Gardiner, who, with two </p>
<p>or three other gentlemen from the house, was engaged by the </p>
<p>river, and had left him only on learning that the ladies of the </p>
<p>family intended a visit to Georgiana that morning. No sooner </p>
<p>did he appear than Elizabeth wisely resolved to be perfectly </p>
<p>easy and unembarrassed; a resolution the more necessary to </p>
<p>be made, but perhaps not the more easily kept, because she </p>
<p>saw that the suspicions of the whole party were awakened </p>
<p>against them, and that there was scarcely an eye which did not </p>
<p>watch his behaviour when he first came into the room. In no </p>
<p>countenance was attentive curiosity so strongly marked as in </p>
<p>Miss Bingley's, in spite of the smiles which overspread her </p>
<p>face whenever she spoke to one of its objects; for jealousy had </p>
<p>not yet made her desperate, and her attentions to Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>were by no means over. Miss Darcy, on her brother's entrance, </p>
<p>exerted herself much more to talk, and Elizabeth saw that he </p>
<p>was anxious for his sister and herself to get acquainted, and </p>
<p>forwarded as much as possible, every attempt at conversation </p>
<p>on either side. Miss Bingley saw all this likewise; and, in the </p>
<p>imprudence of anger, took the first opportunity of saying, with </p>
<p>sneering civility: </p>
<p>"Pray, Miss Eliza, are not the ----shire Militia removed </p>
<p>from Meryton? They must be a great loss to YOUR family." </p>
<p>In Darcy's presence she dared not mention Wickham's </p>
<p>name; but Elizabeth instantly comprehended that he was </p>
<p>uppermost in her thoughts; and the various recollections </p>
<p>connected with him gave her a moment's distress; but exerting </p>
<p>herself vigorously to repel the ill-natured attack, she presently </p>
<p>answered the question in a tolerably detached tone. While she </p>
<p>spoke, an involuntary glance showed her Darcy, with a </p>
<p>heightened complexion, earnestly looking at her, and his sister </p>
<p>overcome with confusion, and unable to lift up her eyes. Had </p>
<p>Miss Bingley known what pain she was then giving her </p>
<p>beloved friend, she undoubtedly would have refrained from </p>
<p>the hint; but she had merely intended to discompose Elizabeth </p>
<p>by bringing forward the idea of a man to whom she believed </p>
<p>her partial, to make her betray a sensibility which might injure </p>
<p>her in Darcy's opinion, and, perhaps, to remind the latter of all </p>
<p>the follies and absurdities by which some part of her family </p>
<p>were connected with that corps. Not a syllable had ever </p>
<p>reached her of Miss Darcy's meditated elopement. To no </p>
<p>creature had it been revealed, where secrecy was possible, </p>
<p>except to Elizabeth; and from all Bingley's connections her </p>
<p>brother was particularly anxious to conceal it, from the very </p>
<p>wish which Elizabeth had long ago attributed to him, of their </p>
<p>becoming hereafter her own. He had certainly formed such a </p>
<p>plan, and without meaning that it should effect his endeavour </p>
<p>to separate him from Miss Bennet, it is probable that it might </p>
<p>add something to his lively concern for the welfare of his </p>
<p>friend. </p>
<p>Elizabeth's collected behaviour, however, soon quieted his </p>
<p>emotion; and as Miss Bingley, vexed and disappointed, dared </p>
<p>not approach nearer to Wickham, Georgiana also recovered in </p>
<p>time, though not enough to be able to speak any more. Her </p>
<p>brother, whose eye she feared to meet, scarcely recollected </p>
<p>her interest in the affair, and the very circumstance which had </p>
<p>been designed to turn his thoughts from Elizabeth seemed to </p>
<p>have fixed them on her more and more cheerfully. </p>
<p>Their visit did not continue long after the question and </p>
<p>answer above mentioned; and while Mr. Darcy was attending </p>
<p>them to their carriage Miss Bingley was venting her feelings </p>
<p>in criticisms on Elizabeth's person, behaviour, and dress. But </p>
<p>Georgiana would not join her. Her brother's recommendation </p>
<p>was enough to ensure her favour; his judgement could not err. </p>
<p>And he had spoken in such terms of Elizabeth as to leave </p>
<p>Georgiana without the power of finding her otherwise than </p>
<p>lovely and amiable. When Darcy returned to the saloon, Miss </p>
<p>Bingley could not help repeating to him some part of what she </p>
<p>had been saying to his sister. </p>
<p>"How very ill Miss Eliza Bennet looks this morning, Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy," she cried; "I never in my life saw anyone so much </p>
<p>altered as she is since the winter. She is grown so brown and </p>
<p>coarse! Louisa and I were agreeing that we should not have </p>
<p>known her again." </p>
<p>However little Mr. Darcy might have liked such an </p>
<p>address, he contented himself with coolly replying that he </p>
<p>perceived no other alteration than her being rather tanned, no </p>
<p>miraculous consequence of travelling in the summer. </p>
<p>"For my own part," she rejoined, "I must confess that I </p>
<p>never could see any beauty in her. Her face is too thin; her </p>
<p>complexion has no brilliancy; and her features are not at all </p>
<p>handsome. Her nose wants character--there is nothing marked </p>
<p>in its lines. Her teeth are tolerable, but not out of the common </p>
<p>way; and as for her eyes, which have sometimes been called </p>
<p>so fine, I could never see anything extraordinary in them. </p>
<p>They have a sharp, shrewish look, which I do not like at all; </p>
<p>and in her air altogether there is a self-sufficiency without </p>
<p>fashion, which is intolerable." </p>
<p>Persuaded as Miss Bingley was that Darcy admired </p>
<p>Elizabeth, this was not the best method of recommending </p>
<p>herself; but angry people are not always wise; and in seeing </p>
<p>him at last look somewhat nettled, she had all the success she </p>
<p>expected. He was resolutely silent, however, and, from a </p>
<p>determination of making him speak, she continued: </p>
<p>"I remember, when we first knew her in Hertfordshire, </p>
<p>how amazed we all were to find that she was a reputed beauty; </p>
<p>and I particularly recollect your saying one night, after they </p>
<p>had been dining at Netherfield, 'SHE a beauty!--I should as </p>
<p>soon call her mother a wit.' But afterwards she seemed to </p>
<p>improve on you, and I believe you thought her rather pretty at </p>
<p>one time." </p>
<p>"Yes," replied Darcy, who could contain himself no </p>
<p>longer, "but THAT was only when I first saw her, for it is </p>
<p>many months since I have considered her as one of the </p>
<p>handsomest women of my acquaintance." </p>
<p>He then went away, and Miss Bingley was left to all the </p>
<p>satisfaction of having forced him to say what gave no one any </p>
<p>pain but herself. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner and Elizabeth talked of all that had occurred </p>
<p>during their visit, as they returned, except what had </p>
<p>particularly interested them both. The look and behaviour of </p>
<p>everybody they had seen were discussed, except of the person </p>
<p>who had mostly engaged their attention. They talked of his </p>
<p>sister, his friends, his house, his fruit--of everything but </p>
<p>himself; yet Elizabeth was longing to know what Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner thought of him, and Mrs. Gardiner would have been </p>
<p>highly gratified by her niece's beginning the subject. </p>
<heading>Chapter 46. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth had been a good deal disappointed in not finding </p>
<p>a letter from Jane on their first arrival at Lambton; and this </p>
<p>disappointment had been renewed on each of the mornings </p>
<p>that had now been spent there; but on the third her repining </p>
<p>was over, and her sister justified, by the receipt of two letters </p>
<p>from her at once, on one of which was marked that it had been </p>
<p>missent elsewhere. Elizabeth was not surprised at it, as Jane </p>
<p>had written the direction remarkably ill. </p>
<p>They had just been preparing to walk as the letters came </p>
<p>in; and her uncle and aunt, leaving her to enjoy them in quiet, </p>
<p>set off by themselves. The one missent must first be attended </p>
<p>to; it had been written five days ago. The beginning contained </p>
<p>an account of all their little parties and engagements, with </p>
<p>such news as the country afforded; but the latter half, which </p>
<p>was dated a day later, and written in evident agitation, gave </p>
<p>more important intelligence. It was to this effect: </p>
<p>"Since writing the above, dearest Lizzy, something has </p>
<p>occurred of a most unexpected and serious nature; but I am </p>
<p>afraid of alarming you--be assured that we are all well. What I </p>
<p>have to say relates to poor Lydia. An express came at twelve </p>
<p>last night, just as we were all gone to bed, from Colonel </p>
<p>Forster, to inform us that she was gone off to Scotland with </p>
<p>one of his officers; to own the truth, with Wickham! Imagine </p>
<p>our surprise. To Kitty, however, it does not seem so wholly </p>
<p>unexpected. I am very, very sorry. So imprudent a match on </p>
<p>both sides! But I am willing to hope the best, and that his </p>
<p>character has been misunderstood. Thoughtless and indiscreet </p>
<p>I can easily believe him, but this step (and let us rejoice over </p>
<p>it) marks nothing bad at heart. His choice is disinterested at </p>
<p>least, for he must know my father can give her nothing. Our </p>
<p>poor mother is sadly grieved. My father bears it better. How </p>
<p>thankful am I that we never let them know what has been said </p>
<p>against him; we must forget it ourselves. They were off </p>
<p>Saturday night about twelve, as is conjectured, but were not </p>
<p>missed till yesterday morning at eight. The express was sent </p>
<p>off directly. My dear Lizzy, they must have passed within ten </p>
<p>miles of us. Colonel Forster gives us reason to expect him </p>
<p>here soon. Lydia left a few lines for his wife, informing her of </p>
<p>their intention. I must conclude, for I cannot be long from my </p>
<p>poor mother. I am afraid you will not be able to make it out, </p>
<p>but I hardly know what I have written." </p>
<p>Without allowing herself time for consideration, and </p>
<p>scarcely knowing what she felt, Elizabeth on finishing this </p>
<p>letter instantly seized the other, and opening it with the utmost </p>
<p>impatience, read as follows: it had been written a day later </p>
<p>than the conclusion of the first. </p>
<p>"By this time, my dearest sister, you have received my </p>
<p>hurried letter; I wish this may be more intelligible, but though </p>
<p>not confined for time, my head is so bewildered that I cannot </p>
<p>answer for being coherent. Dearest Lizzy, I hardly know what </p>
<p>I would write, but I have bad news for you, and it cannot be </p>
<p>delayed. Imprudent as the marriage between Mr. Wickham </p>
<p>and our poor Lydia would be, we are now anxious to be </p>
<p>assured it has taken place, for there is but too much reason to </p>
<p>fear they are not gone to Scotland. Colonel Forster came </p>
<p>yesterday, having left Brighton the day before, not many </p>
<p>hours after the express. Though Lydia's short letter to Mrs. F. </p>
<p>gave them to understand that they were going to Gretna </p>
<p>Green, something was dropped by Denny expressing his </p>
<p>belief that W. never intended to go there, or to marry Lydia at </p>
<p>all, which was repeated to Colonel F., who, instantly taking </p>
<p>the alarm, set off from B. intending to trace their route. He did </p>
<p>trace them easily to Clapham, but no further; for on entering </p>
<p>that place, they removed into a hackney coach, and dismissed </p>
<p>the chaise that brought them from Epsom. All that is known </p>
<p>after this is, that they were seen to continue the London road. I </p>
<p>know not what to think. After making every possible inquiry </p>
<p>on that side London, Colonel F. came on into Hertfordshire, </p>
<p>anxiously renewing them at all the turnpikes, and at the inns </p>
<p>in Barnet and Hatfield, but without any success--no such </p>
<p>people had been seen to pass through. With the kindest </p>
<p>concern he came on to Longbourn, and </p>
<p>broke his </p>
<p>apprehensions to us in a manner most creditable to his heart. I </p>
<p>am sincerely grieved for him and Mrs. F., but no one can </p>
<p>throw any blame on them. Our distress, my dear Lizzy, is very </p>
<p>great. My father and mother believe the worst, but I cannot </p>
<p>think so ill of him. Many circumstances might make it more </p>
<p>eligible for them to be married privately in town than to </p>
<p>pursue their first plan; and even if HE could form such a </p>
<p>design against a young woman of Lydia's connections, which </p>
<p>is not likely, can I suppose her so lost to everything? </p>
<p>Impossible! I grieve to find, however, that Colonel F. is not </p>
<p>disposed to depend upon their marriage; he shook his head </p>
<p>when I expressed my hopes, and said he fear W. was not a </p>
<p>man to be trusted. My poor mother is really ill, and keeps her </p>
<p>room. Could she exert herself, it would be better; but this is </p>
<p>not to be expected. And as to my father, I never in my life saw </p>
<p>him so affected. Poor Kitty has anger for having concealed </p>
<p>their attachment; but as it was a matter of confidence, one </p>
<p>cannot wonder. I am truly glad, dearest Lizzy, that you have </p>
<p>been spared something of these distressing scenes; but now, </p>
<p>as the first shock is over, shall I own that I long for your </p>
<p>return? I am not so selfish, however, as to press for it, if </p>
<p>inconvenient. Adieu! I take up my pen again to do what I have </p>
<p>just told you I would not; but circumstances are such that I </p>
<p>cannot help earnestly begging you all to come here as soon as </p>
<p>possible. I know my dear uncle and aunt so well, that I am not </p>
<p>afraid of requesting it, though I have still something more to </p>
<p>ask of the former. My father is going to London with Colonel </p>
<p>Forster instantly, to try to discover her. What he means to do I </p>
<p>am sure I know not; but his excessive distress will not allow </p>
<p>him to pursue any measure in the best and safest way, and </p>
<p>Colonel Forster is obliged to be at Brighton again tomorrow </p>
<p>evening. In such and exigence, my uncle's advice and </p>
<p>assistance would be everything in the world; he will </p>
<p>immediately comprehend what I must feel, and I rely upon his </p>
<p>goodness." </p>
<p>"Oh! where, where is my uncle?" cried Elizabeth, darting </p>
<p>from her seat as she finished the letter, in eagerness to follow </p>
<p>him, without losing a moment of the time so precious; but as </p>
<p>she reached the door it was opened by a servant, and Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy appeared. Her pale face and impetuous manner made </p>
<p>him start, and before he could recover himself to speak, she, </p>
<p>in whose mind every idea was superseded by Lydia's </p>
<p>situation, hastily exclaimed, "I beg your pardon, but I must </p>
<p>leave you. I must find Mr. Gardiner this moment, on business </p>
<p>that cannot be delayed; I have not an instant to lose." </p>
<p>"Good God! what is the matter?" cried he, with more </p>
<p>feeling than politeness; then recollecting himself, "I will not </p>
<p>detain you a minute; but let me, or let the servant go after Mr. </p>
<p>and Mrs. Gardiner. You are not well enough; you cannot go </p>
<p>yourself." </p>
<p>Elizabeth hesitated, but her knees trembled under her and </p>
<p>she felt how little would be gained by her attempting to </p>
<p>pursue them. Calling back the servant, therefore, she </p>
<p>commissioned him, though in so breathless an accent as made </p>
<p>her almost unintelligible, to fetch his master and mistress </p>
<p>home instantly. </p>
<p>On his quitting the room she sat down, unable to support </p>
<p>herself, and looking so miserably ill, that it was impossible for </p>
<p>Darcy to leave her, or to refrain from saying, in a tone of </p>
<p>gentleness and commiseration, "Let me call your maid. Is </p>
<p>there nothing you could take to give you present relief? A </p>
<p>glass of wine; shall I get you one? You are very ill." </p>
<p>"No, I thank you," she replied, endeavouring to recover </p>
<p>herself. "There is nothing the matter with me. I am quite well; </p>
<p>I am only distressed by some dreadful news which I have just </p>
<p>received from Longbourn." </p>
<p>She burst into tears as she alluded to it, and for a few </p>
<p>minutes could not speak another word. Darcy, in wretched </p>
<p>suspense, could only say something indistinctly of his </p>
<p>concern, and observe her in compassionate silence. At length </p>
<p>she spoke again. "I have just had a letter from Jane, with such </p>
<p>dreadful news. It cannot be concealed from anyone. My </p>
<p>younger sister has left all her friends--has eloped; has thrown </p>
<p>herself into the power of--of Mr. Wickham. They are gone off </p>
<p>together from Brighton. YOU know him too well to doubt the </p>
<p>rest. She has no money, no connections, nothing that can </p>
<p>tempt him to-she is lost for ever." </p>
<p>Darcy was fixed in astonishment. "When I consider," she </p>
<p>added in a yet more agitated voice, "that I might have </p>
<p>prevented it! I, who knew what he was. Had I but explained </p>
<p>some part of it only--some part of what I learnt, to my own </p>
<p>family! Had his character been known, this could not have </p>
<p>happened. But it is all--all too late now." </p>
<p>"I am grieved indeed," cried Darcy; "grieved--shocked. </p>
<p>But is it certain--absolutely certain?" </p>
<p>"Oh, yes! They left Brighton together on Sunday nig ht, </p>
<p>and were traced almost to London, but not beyond; they are </p>
<p>certainly not gone to Scotland." </p>
<p>"And what has been done, what has been attempted, to </p>
<p>recover her?" </p>
<p>"My father is gone to London, and Jane has written to beg </p>
<p>my uncle's immediate assistance; and we shall be off, I hope, </p>
<p>in half-an-hour. But nothing can be done--I know very well </p>
<p>that nothing can be done. How is such a man to be worked </p>
<p>on? How are they even to be discovered? I have not the </p>
<p>smallest hope. It is every way horrible!" </p>
<p>Darcy shook his head in silent acquiescence. </p>
<p>"When MY eyes were opened to his real character--Oh! </p>
<p>had I known what I ought, what I dared to do! But I knew not- </p>
<p>-I was afraid of doing too much. Wretched, wretched </p>
<p>mistake!" </p>
<p>Darcy made no answer. He seemed scarcely to hear her, </p>
<p>and was walking up and down the room in earnest meditation, </p>
<p>his brow contracted, his air gloomy. Elizabeth soon observed, </p>
<p>and instantly understood it. Her </p>
<p>power was sinking; </p>
<p>everything MUST sink under such a proof of family </p>
<p>weakness, such an assurance of the deepest disgrace. She </p>
<p>could neither wonder nor condemn, but the belief of his self- </p>
<p>conquest brought nothing to her consolatory to her bosom, </p>
<p>afforded no palliation of her distress. It was, on the contrary, </p>
<p>exactly calculated to make her understand her own wishes; </p>
<p>and never had she so honestly felt that she could have loved </p>
<p>him, as now, when all love must be vain. </p>
<p>But self, though it would intrude, could not engross her. </p>
<p>Lydia--the humiliation, the misery she was bringing on them </p>
<p>all, soon swallowed up every private care; and covering her </p>
<p>face with her handkerchief, Elizabeth was soon lost to </p>
<p>everything else; and, after a pause of several minutes, was </p>
<p>only recalled to a sense of her situation by the voice of her </p>
<p>companion, who, in a manner which, though it spoke </p>
<p>compassion, spoke likewise restraint, said, "I am afraid you </p>
<p>have been long desiring my absence, nor have I anything to </p>
<p>plead in excuse of my stay, but real, though unavailing </p>
<p>concern. Would to Heaven that anything could be either said </p>
<p>or done on my part that might offer consolation to such </p>
<p>distress! But I will not torment you with vain wishes, which </p>
<p>may seem purposely to ask for your thanks. This unfortunate </p>
<p>affair will, I fear, prevent my sister's having the pleasure of </p>
<p>seeing you at Pemberley to-day." </p>
<p>"Oh, yes. Be so kind as to apologise for us to Miss Darcy. </p>
<p>Say that urgent business calls us home immediately. Conceal </p>
<p>the unhappy truth as long as it is possible, I know it cannot be </p>
<p>long." </p>
<p>He readily assured her of his secrecy; again expressed his </p>
<p>sorrow for her distress, wished it a happier conclusion than </p>
<p>there was at present reason to hope, and leaving his </p>
<p>compliments for her relations, with only one serious, parting </p>
<p>look, went away. </p>
<p>As he quitted the room, Elizabeth felt how improbable it </p>
<p>was that they should ever see each other again on such terms </p>
<p>of cordiality as had marked their several meetings in </p>
<p>Derbyshire; and as she threw a retrospective glance over the </p>
<p>whole of their acquaintance, so full of contradictions and </p>
<p>varieties, sighed at the perverseness of those feelings which </p>
<p>would now have promoted its continuance, and would </p>
<p>formerly have rejoiced in its termination. </p>
<p>If gratitude and esteem are good foundations of affection, </p>
<p>Elizabeth's change of sentiment will be neither improbable </p>
<p>nor faulty. But if otherwise--if regard springing from such </p>
<p>sources is unreasonable or unnatural, in comparison of what is </p>
<p>so often described as arising on a first interview with its </p>
<p>object, and even before two words have been exchanged, </p>
<p>nothing can be said in her defence, except that she had given </p>
<p>somewhat of a trial to the latter method in her partiality for </p>
<p>Wickham, and that its ill success might, perhaps, authorise her </p>
<p>to seek the other less interesting mode of attachment. Be that </p>
<p>as it may, she saw him go with regret; and in this early </p>
<p>example of what Lydia's infamy must produce, found </p>
<p>additional anguish as she reflected on that wretched business. </p>
<p>Never, since reading Jane's second letter, had she entertained </p>
<p>a hope of Wickham's meaning to marry her. No one but Jane, </p>
<p>she thought, could flatter herself with such an expectation. </p>
<p>Surprise was the least of her feelings on this development. </p>
<p>While the contents of the first letter remained in her mind, she </p>
<p>was all surprise--all astonishment that Wickham should marry </p>
<p>a girl whom it was impossible he could marry for money; and </p>
<p>how Lydia could ever have attached him had appeared </p>
<p>incomprehensible. But now it was all too natural. For such an </p>
