<p>attachment as this she might have sufficient charms; and </p>
<p>though she did not suppose Lydia to be deliberately engaging </p>
<p>in an elopement without the intention of marriage, she had no </p>
<p>difficulty in </p>
<p>believing that neither her virtue nor her </p>
<p>understanding would preserve her from falling an easy prey. </p>
<p>She had never perceived, while the regiment was in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire, that Lydia had any partiality for him; but she </p>
<p>was convinced that Lydia wanted only encouragement to </p>
<p>attach herself to anybody. Sometimes one officer, sometimes </p>
<p>another, had been her favourite, as their attentions raised them </p>
<p>in her opinion. Her affections had continually been fluctuating </p>
<p>but never without an object. The mischief of neglect and </p>
<p>mistaken indulgence towards such a girl--oh! how acutely did </p>
<p>she now feel it! </p>
<p>She was wild to be at home--to hear, to see, to be upon the </p>
<p>spot to share with Jane in the cares that must now fall wholly </p>
<p>upon her, in a family so deranged, a father absent, a mother </p>
<p>incapable of exertion, and requiring constant attendance; and </p>
<p>though almost persuaded that nothing could be done for </p>
<p>Lydia, her </p>
<p>uncle's interference seemed of the utmost </p>
<p>importance, and till he entered the room her impatience was </p>
<p>severe. Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner had hurried back in alarm, </p>
<p>supposing by the servant's account that their niece was taken </p>
<p>suddenly ill; but satisfying them instantly on that head, she </p>
<p>eagerly communicated the cause of their summons, reading </p>
<p>the two letters aloud, and dwelling on the postscript of the last </p>
<p>with trembling energy, </p>
<p>though Lydia had never been a </p>
<p>favourite with them, Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner could not but be </p>
<p>deeply afflicted. Not Lydia only, but all were concerned in it; </p>
<p>and after the first exclamations of surprise and horror, Mr. </p>
<p>Gardiner promised every assistance in his power. Elizabeth, </p>
<p>though expecting no less, thanked him with tears of gratitude; </p>
<p>and all three being actuated by one spirit, everything relating </p>
<p>to their journey was speedily settled. They were to be off as </p>
<p>soon as possible. </p>
<p>"But what is to be done about Pemberley?" cried Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner. "John told us Mr. Darcy was here when you sent for </p>
<p>us; was it so?" </p>
<p>"Yes; and I told him we should not be able to keep our </p>
<p>engagement. THAT is all settled." </p>
<p>"What is all settled?" repeated the other, as she ran into her </p>
<p>room to prepare. "And are they upon such terms as for her to </p>
<p>disclose the real truth? Oh, that I knew how it was!" </p>
<p>But wishes were vain, or at least could only serve to amuse </p>
<p>her in the hurry and confusion of the following hour. Had </p>
<p>Elizabeth been at leisure to be idle, she would have remained </p>
<p>certain that all employment was impossible to one so </p>
<p>wretched as herself; but she had her share of business as well </p>
<p>as her aunt, and amongst the rest there were notes to be </p>
<p>written to all their friends at Lambton, with false excuses for </p>
<p>their sudden departure. An hour, however, saw the whole </p>
<p>completed; and Mr. Gardiner meanwhile having settled his </p>
<p>account at the inn, nothing remained to be done but to go; and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, after all the misery of the morning, found herself, </p>
<p>in a shorter space of time than she could have supposed, </p>
<p>seated in the carriage, and on the road to Longbourn. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 47. </heading>
<p>"I have been thinking it over again, Elizabeth," said her </p>
<p>uncle, as they drove from the town; "and really, upon serious </p>
<p>consideration, I am much more inclined than I was to judge as </p>
<p>your eldest sister does on the matter. It appears to me so very </p>
<p>unlikely that any young man should form such a design </p>
<p>against a girl who is by no means unprotected or friendless, </p>
<p>and who was actually staying in his colonel's family, that I am </p>
<p>strongly inclined to hope the best. Could he expect that her </p>
<p>friends would not step forward? Could he expect to be noticed </p>
<p>again by the regiment, after such an affront to Colonel </p>
<p>Forster? His temptation is not adequate to the risk!" </p>
<p>"Do you really think so?" cried Elizabeth, brightening up </p>
<p>for a moment. </p>
<p>"Upon my word," said Mrs. Gardiner, "I begin to be of </p>
<p>your uncle's opinion. It is really too great a violation of </p>
<p>decency, honour, and interest, for him to be guilty of. I cannot </p>
<p>think so very ill of Wickham. Can you yourself, Lizzy, so </p>
<p>wholly give him up, as to believe him capable of it?" </p>
<p>"Not, perhaps, of neglecting his own interest; but of every </p>
<p>other neglect I can believe him capable. If, indeed, it should </p>
<p>be so! But I dare not hope it. Why should they not go on to </p>
<p>Scotland if that had been the case?" </p>
<p>"In the first place," replied Mr. Gardiner, "there is no </p>
<p>absolute proof that they are not gone to Scotland." </p>
<p>"Oh! but their removing from the chaise into a hackney </p>
<p>coach is such a presumption! And, besides, no traces of them </p>
<p>were to be found on the Barnet road." </p>
<p>"Well, then--supposing them to be in London. They may </p>
<p>be there, though for the purpose of concealment, for no more </p>
<p>exceptional purpose. It is not likely that money should be very </p>
<p>abundant on either side; and it might strike them that they </p>
<p>could be more economically, though less expeditiously, </p>
<p>married in London than in Scotland." </p>
<p>"But why all this secrecy? Why any fear of detection? </p>
<p>Why must their marriage be private? Oh, no, no--this is not </p>
<p>likely. His most particular friend, you see by Jane's account, </p>
<p>was persuaded of his never intending to marry her. Wickham </p>
<p>will never marry a woman without some money. He cannot </p>
<p>afford it. And what claims has Lydia--what attraction has she </p>
<p>beyond youth, health, and good humour that could make him, </p>
<p>for her sake, forego every chance of benefiting himself by </p>
<p>marrying well? As to what restraint the apprehensions of </p>
<p>disgrace in the corps might throw on a dishonourable </p>
<p>elopement with her, I am not able to judge; for I know nothing </p>
<p>of the effects that such a step might produce. But as to your </p>
<p>other objection, I am afraid it will hardly hold good. Lydia has </p>
<p>no brothers to step forward; and he might imagine, from my </p>
<p>father's behaviour, from his indolence and the little attention </p>
<p>he has ever seemed to give to what was going forward in his </p>
<p>family, that HE would do as little, and think as little about it, </p>
<p>as any father could do, in such a matter." </p>
<p>"But can you think that Lydia is so lost to everything but </p>
<p>love of him as to consent to live with him on any terms ot her </p>
<p>than marriage?" </p>
<p>"It does seem, and it is most shocking indeed," replied </p>
<p>Elizabeth, with tears in her eyes, "that a sister's sense of </p>
<p>decency and virtue in such a point should admit of doubt. But, </p>
<p>really, I know not what to say. Perhaps I am not doing her </p>
<p>justice. But she is very young; she has never been taught to </p>
<p>think on serious subjects; and for the last half-year, nay, for a </p>
<p>twelvemonth-she has been given up to nothing but amusement </p>
<p>and vanity. She has been allowed to dispose of her time in the </p>
<p>most idle and frivolous manner, and to adopt any opinions </p>
<p>that came in her way. Since the ----shire were first quartered </p>
<p>in Meryton, nothing but love, flirtation, and officers have been </p>
<p>in her head. She has been doing everything in her power by </p>
<p>thinking and talking on the subject, to give greater--what shall </p>
<p>I call it? susceptibility to her feelings; which are naturally </p>
<p>lively enough. And we all know that Wickham has every </p>
<p>charm of person and address that can captivate a woman." </p>
<p>"But you see that Jane," said her aunt, "does not think so </p>
<p>very ill of Wickham as to believe him capable of the attempt." </p>
<p>"Of whom does Jane ever think ill? And who is there, </p>
<p>whatever might be their former conduct, that she would think </p>
<p>capable of such an attempt, till it were proved against them? </p>
<p>But Jane knows, as well as I do, what Wickham really is. We </p>
<p>both know that he has been profligate in every sense of the </p>
<p>word; that he has neither integrity nor honour; that he is as </p>
<p>false and deceitful as he is insinuating." </p>
<p>"And do you really know all this?" cried Mrs. Gardiner, </p>
<p>whose curiosity as to the mode of her intelligence was all </p>
<p>alive. </p>
<p>"I do indeed," replied Elizabeth, colouring. "I told you, the </p>
<p>other day, of his infamous behaviour to Mr. Darcy; and you </p>
<p>yourself, when last at Longbourn, heard in what manner he </p>
<p>spoke of the man who had behaved with such forbearance and </p>
<p>liberality towards him. And there are other circumstances </p>
<p>which I am not at liberty--which it is not worth while to relate; </p>
<p>but his lies about the whole Pemberley family are endless. </p>
<p>From what he said of Miss Darcy I was thoroughly prepared </p>
<p>to see a proud, reserved, disagreeable girl. Yet he knew to the </p>
<p>contrary himself. He must know that she was as amiable and </p>
<p>unpretending as we have found her." </p>
<p>"But does Lydia know nothing of this? can she be ignorant </p>
<p>of what you and Jane seem so well to understand?" </p>
<p>"Oh, yes!--that, that is the worst of all. Till I was in Kent, </p>
<p>and saw so much both of Mr. Darcy and his relation Colonel </p>
<p>Fitzwilliam, I was ignorant of the truth myself. And when I </p>
<p>returned home, the ----shire was to leave Meryton in a week </p>
<p>or fortnight's time. As that was the case, neither Jane, to </p>
<p>whom I related the whole, nor I, thought it necessary to make </p>
<p>our knowledge public; for of what use could it apparently be </p>
<p>to any one, that the good opinion which all the neighbourhood </p>
<p>had of him should then be overthrown? And even when it was </p>
<p>settled that Lydia should go with Mrs. Forster, the necessity </p>
<p>of opening her eyes to his character never occurred to me. </p>
<p>That SHE could be in any danger from the deception never </p>
<p>entered my head. That such a consequence as THIS could </p>
<p>ensue, you may easily believe, was far enough from my </p>
<p>thoughts." </p>
<p>"When they all removed to Brighton, therefore, you had no </p>
<p>reason, I suppose, to believe them fond of each other?" </p>
<p>"Not the slightest. I can remember no symptom of </p>
<p>affection on either side; and had anything of the kind been </p>
<p>perceptible, you must be aware that ours is not a family on </p>
<p>which it could be thrown away. When first he entered the </p>
<p>corps, she was ready enough to admire him; but so we all </p>
<p>were. Every girl in or near Meryton was out of her senses </p>
<p>about him for the first two months; but he never distinguished </p>
<p>HER by any particular attention; and, consequently, after a </p>
<p>moderate period of extravagant and wild admiration, her </p>
<p>fancy for him gave way, and others of the regiment, who </p>
<p>treated her with more </p>
<p>favourites." </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>It may be easily believed, that however little of novelty </p>
<p>could be added to their fears, hopes, and conjectures, on this </p>
<p>interesting subject, by its repeated discussion, no other could </p>
<p>detain them from it long, during the whole of the journey. </p>
<p>From Elizabeth's thoughts it was never absent. Fixed there by </p>
<p>the keenest of all anguish, self-reproach, she could find no </p>
<p>interval of ease or forgetfulness. </p>
<p>They travelled as expeditiously as possible, and, sleeping </p>
<p>one night on the road, reached Longbourn by dinner time the </p>
<p>next day. It was a comfort to Elizabeth to consider that Jane </p>
<p>could not have been wearied by long expectations. </p>
<p>The little Gardiners, attracted by the sight of a chaise, were </p>
<p>standing on the steps of the house as they entered the </p>
<p>paddock; and, when the carriage drove up to the door, the </p>
<p>joyful surprise that lighted up their faces, and displayed itself </p>
<p>over their whole bodies, in a variety of capers and frisks, was </p>
<p>the first pleasing earnest of their welcome. </p>
<p>Elizabeth jumped out; and, after giving each of them a </p>
<p>hasty kiss, hurried into the vestibule, where Jane, who came </p>
<p>running down from her mother's apartment, immediately met </p>
<p>her. </p>
<p>distinction, again became her </p>
<p>Elizabeth, as she affectionately embraced her, whilst tears </p>
<p>filled the eyes of both, lost not a moment in asking whether </p>
<p>anything had been heard of the fugitives. </p>
<p>"Not yet," replied Jane. "But now that my dear uncle is </p>
<p>come, I hope everything will be well." </p>
<p>"Is my father in town?" </p>
<p>"Yes, he went on Tuesday, as I wrote you word." </p>
<p>"And have you heard from him often?" </p>
<p>"We have heard only twice. He wrote me a few lines on </p>
<p>Wednesday to say that he had arrived in safety, and to give </p>
<p>me his directions, which I particularly begged him to do. He </p>
<p>merely added that he should not write again till he had </p>
<p>something of importance to mention." </p>
<p>"And my mother--how is she? How are you all?" </p>
<p>"My mother is tolerably well, I trust; though her spirits are </p>
<p>greatly shaken. She is upstairs and will have great satisfaction </p>
<p>in seeing you all. She does not yet leave her dressing-room. </p>
<p>Mary and Kitty are, thank Heaven, are quite well." </p>
<p>"But you--how are you?" cried Elizabeth. "You look pale. </p>
<p>How much you must have gone through!" </p>
<p>Her sister, however, assured her of her being perfectly </p>
<p>well; and their conversation, which had been passing while </p>
<p>Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner were engaged with their children, was </p>
<p>now put an end to by the approach of the whole party. Jane </p>
<p>ran to her uncle and aunt, and welcomed and thanked them </p>
<p>both, with alternate smiles and tears. </p>
<p>When they were all in the drawing-room, the questions </p>
<p>which Elizabeth had already asked were of course repeated by </p>
<p>the others, and they soon found that Jane had no intelligence </p>
<p>to give. The sanguine hope of good, however, which the </p>
<p>benevolence of her heart suggested had not yet deserted her; </p>
<p>she still expected that it would all end well, and that every </p>
<p>morning would bring some letter, either from Lydia or her </p>
<p>father, to explain their proceedings, and, perhaps, announce </p>
<p>their marriage. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, to whose apartment they all repaired, after a </p>
<p>few minutes' conversation together, received them exactly as </p>
<p>might be expected; with tears and lamentations of regret, </p>
<p>invectives against the villainous conduct of Wickham, and </p>
<p>complaints of her own sufferings and ill-usage; blaming </p>
<p>everybody but the person to whose ill-judging indulgence the </p>
<p>errors of her daughter must principally be owing. </p>
<p>"If I had been able," said she, "to carry my point in going </p>
<p>to Brighton, with all my family, THIS would not have </p>
<p>happened; but poor dear Lydia had nobody to take care of her. </p>
<p>Why did the Forsters ever let her go out of their sight? I am </p>
<p>sure there was some great neglect or other on their side, for </p>
<p>she is not the kind of girl to do such a thing if she had been </p>
<p>well looked after. I always thought they were very unfit to </p>
<p>have the charge of her; but I was overruled, as I always am. </p>
<p>Poor dear child! And now here's Mr. Bennet gone away, and I </p>
<p>know he will fight Wickham, wherever he meets him and then </p>
<p>he will be killed, and what is to become of us all? The </p>
<p>Collinses will turn us out before he is cold in his grave, and if </p>
<p>you are not kind to us, brother, I do not know what we shall </p>
<p>do." </p>
<p>They all exclaimed against such terrific ideas; and Mr. </p>
<p>Gardiner, after general assurances of his affection for her and </p>
<p>all her family, told her that he meant to be in London the very </p>
<p>next day, and would assist Mr. Bennet in every endeavour for </p>
<p>recovering Lydia. </p>
<p>"Do not give way to useless alarm," added he; "though it is </p>
<p>right to be prepared for the worst, there is no occasion to look </p>
<p>on it as certain. It is not quite a week since they left Brighton. </p>
<p>In a few days more we may gain some news of them; and till </p>
<p>we know that they are not married, and have no design of </p>
<p>marrying, do not let us give the matter over as lost. As soon as </p>
<p>I get to town I shall go to my brother, and make him come </p>
<p>home with me to Gracechurch Street; and then we may </p>
<p>consult together as to what is to be done." </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear brother," replied Mrs. Bennet, "that is </p>
<p>exactly what I could most wish for. And now do, when you </p>
<p>get to town, find them out, wherever they may be; and if they </p>
<p>are not married already, MAKE them marry. And as for </p>
<p>wedding clothes, do not let them wait for that, but tell Lydia </p>
<p>she shall have as much money as she chooses to buy them, </p>
<p>after they are married. And, above all, keep Mr. Bennet from </p>
<p>fighting. Tell him what a dreadful state I am in, that I am </p>
<p>frightened out of my wits--and have such tremblings, such </p>
<p>flutterings, all over me-such spasms in my side and pains in </p>
<p>my head, and such beatings at heart, that I can get no rest by </p>
<p>night nor by day. And tell my dear Lydia not to give any </p>
<p>directions about her clothes till she has seen me, for she does </p>
<p>not know which are the best warehouses. Oh, brother, how </p>
<p>kind you are! I know you will contrive it all." </p>
<p>But Mr. Gardiner, though he assured her again of his </p>
<p>earnest endeavours in </p>
<p>the cause, could </p>
<p>not avoid </p>
<p>recommending moderation to her, as well in her hopes as her </p>
<p>fear; and after talking with her in this manner till dinner was </p>
<p>on the table, they all left her to vent all her feelings on the </p>
<p>housekeeper, who attended in the absence of her daughters. </p>
<p>Though her brother and sister were persuaded that there </p>
<p>was no real occasion for such a seclusion from the family, </p>
<p>they did not attempt to oppose it, for they knew that she had </p>
<p>not prudence enough to hold her tongue before the servants, </p>
<p>while they waited at table, and judged it better that ONE only </p>
<p>of the household, and the one whom they could most trust </p>
<p>should comprehend all her fears and solicitude on the subject. </p>
<p>In the dining-room they were soon joined by Mary and </p>
<p>Kitty, who had been too busily engaged in their separate </p>
<p>apartments to make their appearance before. One came from </p>
<p>her books, and the other from her toilette. The faces of both, </p>
<p>however, were tolerably calm; and no change was visible in </p>
<p>either, except that the loss of her favourite sister, or the anger </p>
<p>which she had herself incurred in this business, had given </p>
<p>more of fretfulness than usual to the accents of Kitty. As for </p>
<p>Mary, she was mistress enough of herself to whisper to </p>
<p>Elizabeth, with a countenance of grave reflection, soon after </p>
<p>they were seated at table: </p>
<p>"This is a most unfortunate affair, and will probably be </p>
<p>much talked of. But we must stem the tide of malice, and pour </p>
<p>into the wounded bosoms of each other the balm of sisterly </p>
<p>consolation." </p>
<p>Then, perceiving in Elizabeth no inclination of replying, </p>
<p>she added, "Unhappy as the event must be for Lydia, we may </p>
<p>draw from it this useful lesson: that loss of virtue in a female </p>
<p>is irretrievable; that one false step involves her in endless ruin; </p>
<p>that her reputation is no less brittle than it is beautiful; and </p>
<p>that she cannot be too much guarded in her behaviour towards </p>
<p>the undeserving of the other sex." </p>
<p>Elizabeth lifted up her eyes in amazement, but was too </p>
<p>much oppressed to make any reply. Mary, however, continued </p>
<p>to console herself with such kind of moral extractions from </p>
<p>the evil before them. </p>
<p>In the afternoon, the two elder Miss Bennets were able to </p>
<p>be for half-an-hour by themselves; and Elizabeth instantly </p>
<p>availed herself of the opportunity of making any inquiries, </p>
<p>which Jane was equally eager to satisfy. After joining in </p>
<p>general lamentations over the dreadful sequel of this event, </p>
<p>which Elizabeth considered as all but certain, and Miss </p>
<p>Bennet could not assert to be wholly impossible, the former </p>
<p>continued the subject, by saying, "But tell me all and </p>
<p>everything about it which I have not already heard. Give me </p>
<p>further particulars. What did Colonel Forster say? Had they no </p>
<p>apprehension of anything before the elopement took place? </p>
<p>They must have seen them together for ever." </p>
<p>"Colonel Forster did own that he had often suspected some </p>
<p>partiality, especially on Lydia's side, but nothing to give him </p>
<p>any alarm. I am so grieved for him! His behaviour was </p>
<p>attentive and kind to the utmost. He WAS coming to us, in </p>
<p>order to assure us of his concern, before he had any idea of </p>
<p>their not being gone to Scotland: when that apprehension first </p>
<p>got abroad, it hastened his journey." </p>
<p>"And was Denny convinced that Wickham would not </p>
<p>marry? Did he know of their intending to go off? Had Colonel </p>
<p>Forster seen Denny himself?" </p>
<p>"Yes; but, when questioned by HIM, Denny denied </p>
<p>knowing anything of their plans, and would not give his real </p>
<p>opinion about it. He did not repeat his persuasion of their not </p>
<p>mar-rying--and from THAT, I am inclined to hope, he might </p>
<p>have been misunderstood before." </p>
<p>"And till Colonel Forster came himself, not one of you </p>
<p>entertained a doubt, I suppose, of their being really married?" </p>
<p>"How was it possible that such an idea should enter our </p>
<p>brains? I felt a little uneasy--a little fearful of my sister's </p>
<p>happiness with him in marriage, because I knew that his </p>
<p>conduct had not been always quite right. My father and </p>
<p>mother knew nothing of that; they only felt how imprudent a </p>
<p>match it must be. Kitty then owned, with a very natural </p>
<p>triumph on knowing more than the rest of us, that in Lydia's </p>
<p>last letter she had prepared her for such a step. She had </p>
<p>known, it seems, of their being in love with each other, many </p>
<p>weeks." </p>
<p>"But not before they went to Brighton?" </p>
<p>"No, I believe not." </p>
<p>"And did Colonel Forster appear to think well of Wickham </p>
<p>himself? Does he know his real character?" </p>
<p>"I must confess that he did not speak so well of Wickham </p>
<p>as he formerly did. He believed him to be imprudent and </p>
<p>extravagant. And since this sad affair has taken place, it is </p>
<p>said that he left Meryton greatly in debt; but I hope this may </p>
<p>be false." </p>
<p>"Oh, Jane, had we been less secret, had we told what we </p>
<p>knew of him, this could not have happened!" </p>
<p>"Perhaps it would have been better," replied her sister. </p>
<p>"But to expose the former faults of any person without </p>
<p>knowing what their present feelings were, seemed </p>
<p>unjustifiable. We acted with the best intentions." </p>
<p>"Could Colonel Forster repeat the particulars of Lydia's </p>
<p>note to his wife?" </p>
<p>"He brought it with him for us to see." </p>
<p>Jane then took it from her pocket-book, and gave it to </p>
<p>Elizabeth. These were the contents: </p>
<p>"MY DEAR HARRIET, </p>
<p>"You will laugh when you know where I am gone, and I </p>
<p>cannot help laughing myself at your surprise to-morrow </p>
<p>morning, as soon as I am missed. I am going to Gretna </p>
<p>Green, and if you cannot guess with who, I shall think you </p>
<p>a simpleton, for there is but one man in the world I love, </p>
<p>and he is an angel. I should never be happy without him, </p>
<p>so think it no harm to be off. You need not send them word </p>
<p>at Longbourn of my going, if you do not like it, for it will </p>
<p>make the surprise the greater, when I write to them and </p>
<p>sign my name 'Lydia Wickham.' What a good joke it will </p>
<p>be! I can hardly write for laughing. Pray make my excuses </p>
<p>to Pratt for not keeping my engagement, and dancing with </p>
<p>him to-night. Tell him I hope he will excuse me when he </p>
<p>knows all; and tell him I will dance with him at the next </p>
<p>ball we meet, with great pleasure. I shall send for my </p>
<p>clothes when I get to Longbourn; but I wish you would tell </p>
<p>Sally to mend a great slit in my worked muslin gown </p>
<p>before they are packed up. Good-bye. Give my love to </p>
<p>Colonel Forster. I hope you will drink to our good journey. </p>
<p>"Your affectionate friend, </p>
<p>"LYDIA BENNET." </p>
<p>"Oh! thoughtless, thoughtless Lydia!" cried Elizabeth </p>
<p>when she had finished it. "What a letter is this, to be written at </p>
<p>such a moment! But at least it shows that SHE was serious on </p>
<p>the subject of their journey. Whatever he might afterwards </p>
<p>persuade her to, it was not on her side a SCHEME of infamy. </p>
<p>My poor father! how he must have felt it!" </p>
<p>"I never saw anyone so shocked. He could not speak a </p>
<p>word for full ten minutes. My mother was taken ill </p>
<p>immediately, and the whole house in such confusion!" </p>
<p>"Oh! Jane," cried Elizabeth, "was there a servant belonging </p>
<p>to it who did not know the whole story before the end of the </p>
<p>day?" </p>
<p>"I do not know. I hope there was. But to be guarded at </p>
<p>such a time is very difficult. My mother was in hysterics, and </p>
<p>though I endeavoured to give her every assistance in my </p>
<p>power, I am afraid I did not do so much as I might have done! </p>
<p>But the horror of what might possibly happen almost took </p>
<p>from me my faculties." </p>
<p>"Your attendance upon her has been too much for you. </p>
<p>You do not look well. Oh that I had been with you! you have </p>
<p>had every care and anxiety upon yourself alone." </p>
<p>"Mary and Kitty have been very kind, and would have </p>
<p>shared in every fatigue, I am sure; but I did not think it right </p>
<p>for either of them. Kitty is slight and delicate; and Mary </p>
<p>studies so much, that her hours of repose should not be broken </p>
<p>in on. My aunt Phillips came to Longbourn on Tuesday, after </p>
<p>my father went away; and was so good as to stay till Thursday </p>
<p>with me. She was of great use and comfort to us all. And Lady </p>
<p>Lucas has been very kind; she walked here on Wednesday </p>
<p>morning to condole with us, and offered her services, or any </p>
<p>of her daughters', if they should be of use to us." </p>
<p>"She had better have stayed at home," cried Elizabeth; </p>
<p>"perhaps she MEANT well, but, under such a misfortune as </p>
<p>this, one cannot see too little of one's neighbours. Assistance </p>
<p>is impossible; condolence insufferable. Let them triumph over </p>
<p>us at a distance, and be satisfied." </p>
<p>She then proceeded to inquire into the measures which her </p>
<p>father had intended to pursue, while in town, for the recovery </p>
<p>of his daughter. </p>
<p>"He meant I believe," replied Jane, "to go to Epsom, the </p>
<p>place where they last changed horses, see the postilions and </p>
<p>try if anything could be made out from them. His principal </p>
<p>object must be to discover the number of the hackney coach </p>
<p>which took them from Clapham. It had come with a fare from </p>
<p>London; and as he thought that the circumstance of a </p>
<p>gentleman and lady's removing from one carriage into another </p>
<p>might be remarked he meant to make inquiries at Clapham. If </p>
<p>he could anyhow discover at what house the coachman had </p>
<p>before set down his fare, he determined to make inquiries </p>
<p>there, and hoped it might not be impossible to find out the </p>
<p>stand and number of the coach. I do not know of any other </p>
<p>designs that he had formed; but he was in such a hurry to be </p>
<p>gone, and his spirits so greatly discomposed, that I had </p>
<p>difficulty in finding out even so much as this." </p>
<heading>Chapter 48. </heading>
<p>The whole party were in hopes of a letter from Mr. Bennet </p>
<p>the next morning, but the post came in without bringing a </p>
<p>single line from him. His family knew him to be, on all </p>
<p>common occasions, a most negligent and dilatory </p>
<p>correspondent; but at such a time they had hoped for exertion. </p>
<p>They were forced to conclude that he had no pleasing </p>
<p>intelligence to send; but even of THAT they would have been </p>
<p>glad to be certain. Mr. Gardiner had waited only for the letters </p>
<p>before he set off. </p>
<p>When he was gone, they were certain at least of receiving </p>
<p>constant information of what was going on, and their uncle </p>
<p>promised, at parting, to prevail on Mr. Bennet to return to </p>
<p>Longbourn, as soon as he could, to the great consolation of his </p>
<p>sister, who considered it as the only security for her husband's </p>
<p>not being killed in a duel. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner and the children were to remain in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire a few days longer, as the former thought her </p>
<p>presence might be serviceable to her nieces. She shared in </p>
<p>their attendance on Mrs. Bennet, and was a great comfort to </p>
<p>them in their hours of freedom. Their other aunt also visited </p>
<p>them frequently, and always, as she said, with the design of </p>
<p>cheering and heartening them up--though, as she never came </p>
<p>without reporting some fresh instance of Wickham's </p>
<p>extravagance or irregularity, she seldom went away without </p>
<p>leaving them more dispirited than she found them. </p>
<p>All Meryton seemed striving to blacken the man who, but </p>
<p>three months before, had been almost an angel of light. He </p>
<p>was declared to be in debt to every tradesman in the place, </p>
<p>and his intrigues, all honoured with the title of seduction, had </p>
<p>been extended into every tradesman's family. Everybody </p>
<p>declared that he was the wickedest young man in the world; </p>
<p>and everybody began to find out that they had always </p>
<p>distrusted the appearance of his goodness. Elizabeth, though </p>
<p>she did not credit above half of what was said, believed </p>
<p>enough to make her former assurance of her sister's ruin more </p>
<p>certain; and even Jane, who believed still less of it, became </p>
<p>almost hopeless, more especially as the time was now come </p>
<p>when, if they had gone to Scotland, which she had never </p>
<p>before entirely despaired of, they must in all probability have </p>
<p>gained some news of them. </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner left Longbourn on Sunday; on Tuesday his </p>
<p>wife received a letter from him; it told them that, on his </p>
<p>arrival, he had immediately found out his brother, and </p>
<p>persuaded him to come to Gracechurch Street; that Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet had been to Epsom and Clapham, before his arrival, </p>
<p>but without gaining any satisfactory information; and that he </p>
<p>was now determined to inquire at all the principal hotels in </p>
<p>town, as Mr. Bennet thought it possible they might have gone </p>
<p>to one of them, on their first coming to London, before they </p>
<p>procured lodgings. Mr. Gardiner himself did not expect any </p>
<p>success from this measure, but as his brother was eager in it, </p>
<p>he meant to assist him in pursuing it. He added that Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet seemed wholly disinclined at present to leave London </p>
<p>and promised to write again very soon. There was also a </p>
<p>postscript to this effect: </p>
<p>"I have written to Colonel Forster to desire him to find out, </p>
<p>if possible, from some of the young man's intimates in the </p>
<p>regiment, whether Wickham has any relations or connections </p>
<p>who would be likely to know in what part of town he has now </p>
<p>concealed himself. If there were anyone that one could apply </p>
<p>to with a probability of gaining such a clue as that, it might be </p>
<p>of essential consequence. At present we have nothing to guide </p>
<p>us. Colonel Forster will, I dare say, do everything in his </p>
<p>power to satisfy us on this head. But, on second thoughts, </p>
<p>perhaps, Lizzy could tell us what relations he has now living, </p>
<p>better than any other person." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was at no loss to understand from whence this </p>
<p>deference to her authority proceeded; but it was not in her </p>
<p>power to give any information of so satisfactory a nature as </p>
<p>the compliment deserved. She had never heard of his having </p>
<p>had any relations, except a father and mother, both of whom </p>
<p>had been dead many years. It was possible, however, that </p>
<p>some of his companions in the ----shire might be able to give </p>
<p>more information; and though she was not very sanguine in </p>
<p>expecting it, the application was a something to look forward </p>
<p>to. </p>
<p>Every day at Longbourn was now a day of anxiety; but the </p>
<p>most anxious part of each was when the post was expected. </p>
<p>The arrival of letters was the grand object of every morning's </p>
<p>impatience. Through letters, whatever of good or bad was to </p>
<p>be told would be communicated, and every succeeding day </p>
<p>was expected to bring some news of importance. </p>
<p>But before they heard again from Mr. Gardiner, a letter </p>
<p>arrived for their father, from a different quarter, from Mr. </p>
<p>Collins; which, as Jane had received directions to open all that </p>
<p>came for him in his absence, she accordingly read; and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, who knew what curiosities his letters always were, </p>
<p>looked over her, and read it likewise. It was as follows: </p>
<p>"MY DEAR SIR, </p>
<p>"I feel myself called upon, by our relationship, and my </p>
<p>situation in life, to condole with you on the grievous affliction </p>
<p>you are now suffering under, of which we were yesterday </p>
<p>informed by a letter from Hertfordshire. Be assured, my dear </p>
<p>sir, that Mrs. Collins and myself sincerely sympathise with </p>
<p>you and all your respectable family, in your present distress, </p>
<p>which must be of the bitterest kind, because proceeding from </p>
<p>a cause which no time can remove. No arguments shall be </p>
<p>wanting on my part that can alleviate so severe a misfortune-- </p>
<p>or that may comfort you, under a circumstance that must be of </p>
<p>all others the most afflicting to a parent's mind. The death of </p>
<p>your daughter would have been a blessing in comparison of </p>
<p>this. And it is the more to be lamented, because there is reason </p>
<p>to suppose as my dear Charlotte informs me, that this </p>
<p>licentiousness of behaviour in your daughter has proceeded </p>
<p>from a faulty degree of indulgence; though, at the same time, </p>
<p>for the consolation of yourself and Mrs. Bennet, I am inclined </p>
<p>to think that her own disposition must be naturally bad, or she </p>
<p>could not be guilty of such an enormity, at so early an age. </p>
<p>Howsoever that may be, you are grievously to be pitied; in </p>
<p>which opinion I am not only joined by Mrs. Collins, but </p>
<p>likewise by Lady Catherine and her daughter, to whom I have </p>
<p>related the affair. They agree with me in apprehending that </p>
<p>this false step in one daughter will be injurious to the fortunes </p>
<p>of all the others; for who, as Lady Catherine herself </p>
<p>condescendingly says, will connect themselves with such a </p>
<p>family? And this consideration leads me moreover to reflect, </p>
<p>with augmented satisfaction, on a certain event of last </p>
<p>November; for had it been otherwise, I must have been </p>
<p>involved in all your sorrow and disgrace. Let me then advise </p>
<p>you, dear sir, to console yourself as much as possible, to </p>
<p>throw off your unworthy child from your affection for ever, </p>
<p>and leave her to reap the fruits of her own heinous offense. </p>
<p>"I am, dear sir, etc., etc." </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner did not write again till he had received an </p>
<p>answer from Colonel Forster; and then he had nothing of a </p>
<p>pleasant nature to send. It was not known that Wickham had a </p>
<p>single relationship with whom he kept up any connection, and </p>
<p>it was certain that he had no near one living. His former </p>
<p>acquaintances had been numerous; but since he had been in </p>
<p>the militia, it did not appear that he was on terms of particular </p>
<p>friendship with any of them. There was no one, therefore, who </p>
<p>could be pointed out as likely to give any news of him. And in </p>
<p>the wretched state of his own finances, there was a very </p>
<p>powerful motive for secrecy, in addition to his fear of </p>
<p>discovery by Lydia's relations, for it had just transpired that he </p>
<p>had left gaming debts behind him to a very considerable </p>
<p>amount. Colonel Forster believed that more than a thousand </p>
<p>pounds would be necessary to clear his expenses at Brighton. </p>
<p>He owed a good deal in town, but his debts of honour were </p>
<p>still more formidable. Mr. Gardiner did not attempt to conceal </p>
<p>these particulars from the Longbourn family. Jane heard them </p>
<p>with horror. "A gamester!" she cried. "This is wholly </p>
<p>unexpected. I had not an idea of it." </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner added in his letter, that they might expect to </p>
<p>see their father at home on the following day, which was </p>
<p>Saturday. Rendered spiritless by the ill-success of all their </p>
<p>endeavours, he had yielded to his brother-in-law's entreaty </p>
<p>that he would return to his family, and leave it to him to do </p>
<p>whatever occasion might suggest to be advisable for </p>
<p>continuing their pursuit. When Mrs. Bennet was told of this, </p>
<p>she did not express so much satisfaction as her children </p>
<p>expected, considering what her anxiety for his life had been </p>
<p>before. </p>
<p>"What, is he coming home, and without poor Lydia?" she </p>
<p>cried. "Sure he will not leave London before he has found </p>
<p>them. Who is to fight Wickham, and make him marry her, if </p>
<p>he comes away?" </p>
<p>As Mrs. Gardiner began to wish to be at home, it was </p>
<p>settled that she and the children should go to London, at the </p>
<p>same time that Mr. Bennet came from it. The coach, therefore, </p>
<p>took them the first stage of their journey, and brought its </p>
<p>master back to Longbourn. </p>
<p>Mrs. Gardiner went away in all the perplexity about </p>
<p>Elizabeth and her Derbyshire friend that had attended her </p>
<p>from that part of the world. His name had never been </p>
<p>voluntarily mentioned before them by her niece; and the kind </p>
<p>of half-expectation which Mrs. Gardiner had formed, of their </p>
<p>being followed by a letter from him, had ended in nothing. </p>
<p>Elizabeth had received none since her return that could come </p>
<p>from Pemberley. </p>
<p>The present unhappy state of the family rendered any other </p>
<p>excuse for the lowness of her spirits unnecessary; nothing, </p>
<p>therefore, could be fairly conjectured from THAT, though </p>
<p>Elizabeth, who was by this time tolerably well acquainted </p>
<p>with her own feelings, was perfectly aware that, had she </p>
<p>known nothing of Darcy, she could have borne the dread of </p>
<p>Lydia's infamy somewhat better. It would have spared her, she </p>
<p>thought, one sleepless night out of two. </p>
<p>When Mr. Bennet arrived, he had all the appearance of his </p>
<p>usual philosophic composure. He said as little as he had ever </p>
<p>been in the habit of saying; made no mention of the business </p>
<p>that had taken him away, and it was some time before his </p>
<p>daughters had courage to speak of it. </p>
<p>It was not till the afternoon, when he had joined them at </p>
<p>tea, that Elizabeth ventured to introduce the subject; and then, </p>
<p>on her briefly expressing her sorrow for what he must have </p>
<p>endured, he replied, "Say nothing of that. Who should suffer </p>
<p>but myself? It has been my own doing, and I ought to feel it." </p>
<p>"You must not be too severe upon yourself," replied </p>
<p>Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"You may well warn me against such an evil. Human </p>
<p>nature is so prone to fall into it! No, Lizzy, let me once in my </p>
<p>life feel how much I have been to blame. I am not afraid of </p>
<p>being overpowered by the impression. It will pass away soon </p>
<p>enough." </p>
<p>"Do you suppose them to be in London?" </p>
<p>"Yes; where else can they be so well concealed?" </p>
<p>"And Lydia used to want to go to London," added Kitty. </p>
<p>"She is happy then," said her father drily; "and her </p>
<p>residence there will probably be of some duration." </p>
<p>Then after a short silence he continued: </p>
<p>"Lizzy, I bear you no ill-will for being justified in your </p>
<p>advice to me last May, which, considering the event, shows </p>
<p>some greatness of mind." </p>
<p>They were interrupted by Miss Bennet, who came to fetch </p>
<p>her mother's tea. </p>
<p>"This is a parade," he cried, "which does one good; it gives </p>
<p>such an elegance to misfortune! Another day I will do the </p>
<p>same; I will sit in my library, in my nightcap and powdering </p>
<p>gown, and give as much trouble as I can; or, perhaps, I may </p>
<p>defer it till Kitty runs away." </p>
<p>"I am not going to run away, papa," said Kitty fretfully. "If </p>
<p>I should ever go to Brighton, I would behave better than </p>
<p>Lydia." </p>
<p>"YOU go to Brighton. I would not trust you so near it as </p>
<p>Eastbourne for fifty pounds! No, Kitty, I have at last learnt to </p>
<p>be cautious, and you will feel the effects of it. No officer is </p>
<p>ever to enter into my house again, nor even to pass through </p>
<p>the village. Balls will be absolutely prohibited, unless you </p>
<p>stand up with one of your sisters. And you are never to stir out </p>
<p>of doors till you can prove that you have spent ten minutes of </p>
<p>every day in a rational manner." </p>
<p>Kitty, who took all these threats in a serious light, began to </p>
<p>cry. </p>
<p>"Well, well," said he, "do not make yourself unhappy. If </p>
<p>you are a good girl for the next ten years, I will take you to a </p>
<p>review at the end of them." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 49. </heading>
<p>Two days after Mr. Bennet's return, as Jane and Elizabeth </p>
<p>were walking together in the shrubbery behind the house, they </p>
<p>saw the housekeeper coming towards them, and, concluding </p>
<p>that she came to call them to their mother, went forward to </p>
<p>meet her; but, instead of the expected summons, when they </p>
<p>approached her, she said to Miss Bennet, "I beg your pardon, </p>
<p>madam, for interrupting you, but I was in hopes you might </p>
<p>have got some good news from town, so I took the liberty of </p>
<p>coming to ask." </p>
<p>"What do you mean, Hill? We have heard nothing from </p>
<p>town." </p>
<p>"Dear madam," cried Mrs. Hill, in great astonishment, </p>
<p>"don't you know there is an express come for master from Mr. </p>
<p>Gardiner? He has been here this half-hour, and master has had </p>
<p>a letter." </p>
<p>Away ran the girls, too eager to get in to have time for </p>
<p>speech. They ran through the vestibule into the breakfast- </p>
<p>room; from thence to the library; their father was in neither; </p>
<p>and they were on the point of seeking him upstairs with their </p>
<p>mother, when they were met by the butler, who said: </p>
<p>"If you are looking for my master, ma'am, he is walking </p>
<p>towards the little copse." </p>
<p>Upon this information, they instantly passed through the </p>
<p>hall once more, and ran across the lawn after their father, who </p>
<p>was deliberately pursuing his way towards a small wood on </p>
<p>one side of the paddock. </p>
<p>Jane, who was not so light nor so much in the habit of </p>
<p>running as Elizabeth, soon lagged behind, while her sister, </p>
<p>panting for breath, came up with him, and eagerly cried out: </p>
<p>"Oh, papa, what news--what news? Have you heard from </p>
<p>my uncle?" </p>
<p>"Yes I have had a letter from him by express." </p>
<p>"Well, and what news does it bring--good or bad?" </p>
<p>"What is there of good to be expected?" said he, taking the </p>
<p>letter from his pocket. "But perhaps you would like to read it." </p>
<p>Elizabeth impatiently caught it from his hand. Jane now </p>
<p>came up. </p>
<p>"Read it aloud," said their father, "for I hardly know </p>
<p>myself what it is about." </p>
<p>"Gracechurch Street, Monday, August 2. </p>
<p>"MY DEAR BROTHER, </p>
<p>"At last I am able to send you some tidings of my niece, </p>
<p>and such as, upon the whole, I hope it will give you </p>
<p>satisfaction. Soon after you left me on Saturday, I was </p>
<p>fortunate enough to find out in what part of London they </p>
<p>were. The particulars I reserve till we meet; it is enough to </p>
<p>know they are discovered. I have seen them both--" </p>
<p>"Then it is as I always hoped," cried Jane; "they are </p>
<p>married!" </p>
<p>Elizabeth read on: </p>
<p>"I have seen them both. They are not married, nor can I </p>
<p>find there was any intention of being so; but if you are willing </p>
<p>to perform the engagements which I have ventured to make on </p>
<p>your side, I hope it will not be long before they are. All that is </p>
<p>required of you is, to assure to your daughter, by settlement, </p>
<p>her equal share of the five thousand pounds secured among </p>
<p>your children after the decease of yourself and my sister; and, </p>
<p>moreover, to enter into an engagement of allowing her, during </p>
<p>your life, one hundred pounds per annum. These are </p>
<p>conditions which, considering everything, I had no hesitation </p>
<p>in complying with, as far as I thought myself privileged, for </p>
<p>you. I shall send this by express, that no time may be lost in </p>
<p>bringing me your answer. You will easily comprehend, from </p>
<p>these particulars, that Mr. Wickham's circumstances are not so </p>
<p>hopeless as they are generally believed to be. The world has </p>
<p>been deceived in that respect; and I am happy to say there will </p>
<p>be some little money, even when all his debts are discharged, </p>
<p>to settle on my niece, in addition to her own fortune. If, as I </p>
<p>conclude will be the case, you send me full powers to act in </p>
<p>your name throughout the whole of this business, I will </p>
<p>immediately give directions to Haggerston for preparing a </p>
<p>proper settlement. There will not be the smallest occasion for </p>
<p>your coming to town again; therefore stay quiet at Longbourn, </p>
<p>and depend on my diligence and care. Send back your answer </p>
<p>as fast as you can, and be careful to write explicitly. We have </p>
<p>judged it best that my niece should be married from this </p>
<p>house, of which I hope you will approve. She comes to us to- </p>
<p>day. I shall write again as soon as anything more is </p>
<p>determined on. Yours, etc., </p>
<p>"EDW. GARDINER." </p>
<p>"Is it possible?" cried Elizabeth, when she had finished. </p>
<p>"Can it be possible that he will marry her?" </p>
<p>"Wickham is not so undeserving, then, as we thought </p>
<p>him," said her sister. "My dear father, I congratulate you." </p>
<p>"And have you answered the letter?" cried Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"No; but it must be done soon." </p>
<p>Most earnestly did she then entreaty him to lose no more </p>
<p>time before he wrote. </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear father," she cried, "come back and write </p>
<p>immediately. Consider how important every moment is in </p>
<p>such a case." </p>
<p>"Let me write for you," said Jane, "if you dislike the </p>
<p>trouble yourself." </p>
<p>"I dislike it very much," he replied; "but it must be done." </p>
<p>And so saying, he turned back with them, and walked </p>
<p>towards the house. </p>
<p>"And may I ask--" said Elizabeth; "but the terms, I </p>
<p>suppose, must be complied with." </p>
<p>"Complied with! I am only ashamed of his asking so </p>
<p>little." </p>
<p>"And they MUST marry! Yet he is SUCH a man!" </p>
<p>"Yes, yes, they must marry. There is nothing else to be </p>
<p>done. But there are two things that I want very much to know; </p>
<p>one is, how much money your uncle has laid down to bring it </p>
<p>about; and the other, how am I ever to pay him." </p>
<p>"Money! My uncle!" cried Jane, "what do you mean, sir?" </p>
<p>"I mean, that no man in his senses would marry Lydia on </p>
<p>so slight a temptation as one hundred a year during my life, </p>
<p>and fifty after I am gone." </p>
<p>"That is very true," said Elizabeth; "though it had not </p>
<p>occurred to me before. His debts to be discharged, and </p>
<p>something still to remain! Oh! it must be my uncle's doings! </p>
<p>Generous, good man, I am afraid he has distressed himself. A </p>
<p>small sum could not do all this." </p>
<p>"No," said her father; "Wickham's a fool if he takes her </p>
<p>with a farthing less than ten thousand pounds. I should be </p>
<p>sorry to think so ill of him, in the very beginning of our </p>
<p>relationship." </p>
<p>"Ten thousand pounds! Heaven forbid! How is half such a </p>
<p>sum to be repaid?" </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet made no answer, and each of them, deep in </p>
<p>thought, continued silent till they reached the house. Their </p>
<p>father then went on to the library to write, and the girls </p>
<p>walked into the breakfast-room. </p>
<p>"And they are really to be married!" cried Elizabeth, as </p>
<p>soon as they were by themselves. "How strange this is! And </p>
<p>for THIS we are to be thankful. That they should marry, small </p>
<p>as is their chance of happiness, and wretched as is his </p>
<p>character, we are forced to rejoice. Oh, Lydia!" </p>
<p>"I comfort myself with thinking," replied Jane, "that he </p>
<p>certainly would not marry Lydia if he had not a real regard for </p>
<p>her. Though our kind uncle has done something towards </p>
<p>clearing him, I cannot believe that ten thousand pounds, or </p>
<p>any thing like it, has been advanced. He has children of his </p>
<p>own, and may have more. How could he spare half ten </p>
<p>thousand pounds?" </p>
<p>"If he were ever able to learn what Wickham's debts have </p>
<p>been," said Elizabeth, "and how much is settled on his side on </p>
<p>our sister, we shall exactly know what Mr. Gardiner has done </p>
<p>for them, because Wickham has not sixpence of his own. The </p>
<p>kindness of my uncle and aunt can never be requited. Their </p>
<p>taking her home, and affording her their personal protection </p>
<p>and countenance, is such a sacrifice to her advantage as years </p>
<p>of gratitude cannot enough acknowledge. By this time she is </p>
<p>actually with them! If such goodness does not make her </p>
<p>miserable now, she will never deserve to be happy! What a </p>
<p>meeting for her, when she first sees my aunt!" </p>
<p>"We must endeavour to forget all that has passed on either </p>
<p>side," said Jane: "I hope and trust they will yet be happy. His </p>
<p>consenting to marry her is a proof, I will believe, that he is </p>
<p>come to a right way of thinking. Their mutual affection will </p>
<p>steady them; and I flatter myself they will settle so quietly, </p>
<p>and live in so rational a manner, as may in time make their </p>
<p>past imprudence forgotten." </p>
<p>"Their conduct has been such," replied Elizabeth, "as </p>
<p>neither you, nor I, nor anybody can ever forget. It is useless to </p>
<p>talk of it." </p>
<p>It now occurred to the girls that their mother was in all </p>
<p>likelihood perfectly ignorant of what had happened. They </p>
<p>went to the library, therefore, and asked their father whether </p>
<p>he would not wish them to make it known to her. He was </p>
<p>writing and, without raising his head, coolly replied: </p>
<p>"Just as you please." </p>
<p>"May we take my uncle's letter to read to her?" </p>
<p>"Take whatever you like, and get away." </p>
<p>Elizabeth took the letter from his writing-table, and they </p>
<p>went upstairs together. Mary and Kitty were both with Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet: one communication would, therefore, do for all. After </p>
<p>a slight preparation for good news, the letter was read aloud. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet could hardly contain herself. As soon as Jane had </p>
<p>read Mr. Gardiner's hope of Lydia's being soon married, her </p>
<p>joy burst forth, and every following sentence added to its </p>
<p>exuberance. She was now in an irritation as violent from </p>
<p>delight, as she had ever been fidgety from alarm and vexation. </p>
<p>To know that her daughter would be married was enough. She </p>
<p>was disturbed by no fear for her felicity, nor humbled by any </p>
<p>remembrance of her misconduct. </p>
<p>"My dear, dear Lydia!" she cried. "This is delightful </p>
<p>indeed! She will be married! I shall see her again! She will be </p>
<p>married at sixteen! My good, kind brother! I knew how it </p>
<p>would be. I knew he would manage everything! How I long to </p>
<p>see her! and to see dear Wickham too! But the clothes, the </p>
<p>wedding clothes! I will write to my sister Gardiner about them </p>
<p>directly. Lizzy, my dear, run down to your father, and ask him </p>
<p>how much he will give her. Stay, stay, I will go myself. Ring </p>
<p>the bell, Kitty, for Hill. I will put on my things in a moment. </p>
<p>My dear, dear Lydia! How merry we shall be together when </p>
<p>we meet!" </p>
<p>Her eldest daughter endeavoured to give some relief to the </p>
<p>violence of these transports, by leading her thoughts to the </p>
<p>obligations which Mr. Gardiner's behaviour laid them all </p>
<p>under. </p>
<p>"For we must attribute this happy conclusion," she added, </p>
<p>"in a great measure to his kindness. We are persuaded that he </p>
<p>has pledged himself to assist Mr. Wickham with money." </p>
<p>"Well," cried her mother, "it is all very right; who should </p>
<p>do it but her own uncle? If he had not had a family of his own, </p>
<p>I and my children must have had all his money, you know; </p>
<p>and it is the first time we have ever had anything from him, </p>
<p>except a few presents. Well! I am so happy! In a short time I </p>
<p>shall have a daughter married. Mrs. Wickham! How well it </p>
<p>sounds! And she was only sixteen last June. My dear Jane, I </p>
<p>am in such a flutter, that I am sure I can't write; so I will </p>
<p>dictate, and you write for me. We will settle with your father </p>
<p>about the money afterwards; but the things should be ordered </p>
<p>immediately." </p>
<p>She was then proceeding to all the particulars of calico, </p>
<p>muslin, and cambric, and would shortly have dictated some </p>
<p>very plentiful orders, had not Jane, though with some </p>
<p>difficulty, persuaded her to wait till her father was at leisure to </p>
<p>be consulted. One day's delay, she observed, would be of </p>
<p>small importance; and her mother was too happy to be quite </p>
<p>so obstinate as usual. Other schemes, too, came into her head. </p>
<p>"I will go to Meryton," said she, "as soon as I am dressed, </p>
<p>and tell the good, good news to my sister Philips. And as I </p>
<p>come back, I can call on Lady Lucas and Mrs. Long. Kitty, </p>
<p>run down and order the carriage. An airing would do me a </p>
<p>great deal of good, I am sure. Girls, can I do anything for you </p>
<p>in Meryton? Oh! Here comes Hill! My dear Hill, have you </p>
<p>heard the good news? Miss Lydia is going to be married; and </p>
<p>you shall all have a bowl of punch to make merry at her </p>
<p>wedding." </p>
<p>Mrs. Hill began instantly to express her joy. Elizabeth </p>
<p>received her congratulations amongst the rest, and then, sick </p>
<p>of this folly, took refuge in her own room, that she might </p>
<p>think with freedom. </p>
<p>Poor Lydia's situation must, at best, be bad enough; but </p>
<p>that it was no worse, she had need to be thankful. She felt it </p>
<p>so; and though, in looking forward, neither rational happiness </p>
<p>nor worldly prosperity could be justly expected for her sister, </p>
<p>in looking back to what they had feared, only two hours ago, </p>
<p>she felt all the advantages of what they had gained. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 50. </heading>
<heading>Mr. Bennet had very often wished before this period of his </heading>
<heading>life that, instead of spending his whole income, he had laid by </heading>
<heading>an annual sum for the better provision of his children, and of </heading>
<heading>his wife, if she survived him. He now wished it more than </heading>
<heading>ever. Had he done his duty in that respect, Lydia need not </heading>
<heading>have been indebted to her uncle for whatever of honour or </heading>
<heading>credit could now be purchased for her. The satisfaction of </heading>
<heading>prevailing on one of the most worthless young men in Great </heading>
<heading>Britain to be her husband might then have rested in its proper </heading>
<heading>place. He was seriously concerned that a cause of so little </heading>
<heading>advantage to anyone should be forwarded at the sole expense </heading>
<heading>of his brother-in-law, and he was determined, if possible, to </heading>
<heading>find out the extent of his assistance, and to discharge the </heading>
<p>obligation as soon as he could. </p>
<p>When first Mr. Bennet had married, economy was held to </p>
<p>be perfectly useless, for, of course, they were to have a son. </p>
<p>The son was to join in cutting off the entail, as soon as he </p>
<p>should be of age, and the widow and younger children would </p>
<p>by that means be provided for. Five daughters successively </p>
<p>entered the world, but yet the son was to come; and Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, for many years after Lydia's birth, had been certain </p>
<p>that he would. This event had at last been despaired of, but it </p>
<p>was then too late to be saving. Mrs. Bennet had no turn for </p>
<p>economy, and her husband's love of independence had alone </p>
<p>prevented their exceeding their income. </p>
<p>Five thousand pounds was settled by marriage articles on </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet and the children. But in what proportions it </p>
<p>should be divided amongst the latter depended on the will of </p>
<p>the parents. This was one point, with regard to Lydia, at least, </p>
<p>which was now to be settled, and Mr. Bennet could have no </p>
<p>hesitation in acceding to the proposal before him. In terms of </p>
<p>grateful acknowledgment for the kindness of his brother, </p>
<p>though expressed most concisely, he then delivered on paper </p>
<p>his perfect approbation of all that was done, and his </p>
<p>willingness to fulfil the engagements that had been made for </p>
<p>him. He had never before supposed that, could Wickham be </p>
<p>prevailed on to marry his daughter, it would be done with so </p>
<p>little inconvenience to himself as by the present arrangement. </p>
<p>He would scarcely be ten pounds a year the loser by the </p>
<p>hundred that was to be paid them; for, what with her board </p>
<p>and pocket allowance, and the continual presents in money </p>
<p>which passed to her through her mother's hands, Lydia's </p>
<p>expenses had been very little within that sum. </p>
<p>That it would be done with such trifling exertion on his </p>
<p>side, too, was another very welcome surprise; for his wish at </p>
<p>present was to have as little trouble in the business as </p>
<p>possible. When the first transports of rage which had </p>
<p>produced his activity in seeking her were over, he naturally </p>
<p>returned to all his former indolence. His letter was soon </p>
<p>dispatched; for, though dilatory in undertaking business, he </p>
<p>was quick in its execution. He begged to know further </p>
<p>particulars of what he was indebted to his brother, but was too </p>
<p>angry with Lydia to send any message to her. </p>
<p>The good news spread quickly through the house, and with </p>
<p>proportionate speed through the neighbourhood. It was borne </p>
<p>in the latter with decent philosophy. To be sure, it would have </p>
<p>been more for the advantage of conversation had Miss Lydia </p>
<p>Bennet come upon the town; or, as the happiest alternative, </p>
<p>been secluded from the world, in some distant farmhouse. But </p>
<p>there was much to be talked of in marrying her; and the good- </p>
<p>natured wishes for her well-doing which had proceeded before </p>
<p>from all the spiteful old ladies in Meryton lost but a little of </p>
<p>their spirit in this change of circumstances, because with such </p>
<p>an husband her misery was considered certain. </p>
<p>It was a fortnight since Mrs. Bennet had been downstairs; </p>
<p>but on this happy day she again took her seat at the head of </p>
<p>her table, and in spirits oppressively high. No sentiment of </p>
<p>shame gave a damp to her triumph. The marriage of a </p>
<p>daughter, which had been the first object of her wishes since </p>
<p>Jane was sixteen, was now on the point of accomplishment, </p>
<p>and her thoughts and her words ran wholly on those </p>
<p>attendants of elegant nuptials, fine muslins, new carriages, </p>
<p>and servants. She was busily searching through the </p>
<p>neighbourhood for a proper situation for her daughter, and, </p>
<p>without knowing or considering what their income might be, </p>
<p>rejected many as deficient in size and importance. </p>
<p>"Haye Park might do," said she, "if the Gouldings could </p>
<p>quit it--or the great house at Stoke, if the drawing-room were </p>
<p>larger; but Ashworth is too far off! I could not bear to have </p>
<p>her ten miles from me; and as for Pulvis Lodge, the attics are </p>
<p>dreadful." </p>
<p>Her husband allowed her to talk on without interruption </p>
<p>while the servants remained. But when they had withdrawn, </p>
<p>he said to her: "Mrs. Bennet, before you take any or all of </p>
<p>these houses for your son and daughter, let us come to a right </p>
<p>understanding. Into ONE house in this neighbourhood they </p>
<p>shall never have admittance. I will not encourage the </p>
<p>impudence of either, by receiving them at Longbourn." </p>
<p>A long dispute followed this declaration; but Mr. Bennet </p>
<p>was firm. It soon led to another; and Mrs. Bennet found, with </p>
<p>amazement and horror, that her husband would not advance a </p>
<p>guinea to buy clothes for his daughter. He protested that she </p>
<p>should receive from him no mark of affection whatever on the </p>
<p>occasion. Mrs. Bennet could hardly comprehend it. That his </p>
<p>anger could be carried to such a point of inconceivable </p>
<p>resentment as to refuse his daughter a privilege without which </p>
<p>her marriage would scarcely seem valid, exceeded all she </p>
<p>could believe possible. She was more alive to the disgrace </p>
<p>which her want of new clothes must reflect on her daughter's </p>
<p>nuptials, than to any sense of shame at her eloping and living </p>
<p>with Wickham a fortnight before they took place. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was now most heartily sorry that she had, from </p>
<p>the distress of the moment, been led to make Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>acquainted with their fears for her sister; for since her </p>
<p>marriage would so shortly give the proper termination to the </p>
<p>elopement, they might hope to conceal its unfavourable </p>
<p>beginning from all those who were not immediately on the </p>
<p>spot. </p>
<p>She had no fear of its spreading farther through his means. </p>
<p>There were few people on whose secrecy she would have </p>
<p>more confidently depended; but, at the same time, there was </p>
<p>no one whose knowledge of a sister's frailty would have </p>
<p>mortified her so much--not, however, from any fear of </p>
<p>disadvantage from it individually to herself, for, at any rate, </p>
<p>there seemed a gulf impassable between them. Had Lydia's </p>
<p>marriage been concluded on the most honourable terms, it was </p>
<p>not to be supposed that Mr. Darcy would connect himself with </p>
<p>a family where, to every other objection, would now be added </p>
<p>an alliance and relationship of the nearest kind with a man </p>
<p>whom he so justly scorned. </p>
<p>From such a connection she could not wonder that he </p>
<p>would shrink. The wish of procuring her regard, which she </p>
<p>had assured herself of his feeling in Derbyshire, could not in </p>
<p>rational expectation survive such a blow as this. She was </p>
<p>humbled, she was grieved; she repented, though she hardly </p>
<p>knew of what. She became jealous of his esteem, when she </p>
<p>could no longer hope to be benefited by it. She wanted to hear </p>
<p>of him, when there seemed the least chance of gaining </p>
<p>intelligence. She was convinced that she could have been </p>
<p>happy with him, when it was no longer likely they should </p>
<p>meet. </p>
<p>What a triumph for him, as she often thought, could he </p>
<p>know that the proposals which she had proudly spurned only </p>
<p>four months ago, would now have been most gladly and </p>
<p>gratefully received! He was as generous, she doubted not, as </p>
<p>the most generous of his sex; but while he was mortal, there </p>
<p>must be a triumph. </p>
<p>She began now to comprehend that he was exactly the man </p>
<p>who, in disposition and talents, would most suit her. His </p>
<p>understanding and temper, though unlike her own, would have </p>
<p>answered all her wishes. It was an union that must have been </p>
<p>to the advantage of both; by her ease and liveliness, his mind </p>
<p>might have been softened, his manners improved; and from </p>
<p>his judgement, information, and knowledge of the world, she </p>
<p>must have received benefit of greater importance. </p>
<p>But no such happy marriage could now teach the admiring </p>
<p>multitude what connubial felicity really was. An union of a </p>
<p>different tendency, and precluding the possibility of the other, </p>
<p>was soon to be formed in their family. </p>
<p>How Wickham and Lydia were to be supported in tolerable </p>
<p>independence, she could not imagine. But how little of </p>
<p>permanent happiness could belong to a couple who were only </p>
<p>brought together because their passions were stronger than </p>
<p>their virtue, she could easily conjecture. </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>Mr. Gardiner soon wrote again to his brother. To Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet's acknowledgments he briefly replied, with assurance </p>
<p>of his eagerness to promote the welfare of any of his family; </p>
<p>and concluded with entreaties that the subject might never be </p>
<p>mentioned to him again. The principal purport of his letter </p>
<p>was to inform them that Mr. Wickham had resolved on </p>
<p>quitting the militia. </p>
<p>"It was greatly my wish that he should do so," he added, </p>
<p>"as soon as his marriage was fixed on. And I think you will </p>
<p>agree with me, in considering the removal from that corps as </p>
<p>highly advisable, both on his account and my niece's. It is Mr. </p>
<p>Wickham's intention to go into the regulars; and among his </p>
<p>former friends, there are still some who are able and willing to </p>
<p>assist him in the army. He has the promise of an ensigncy in </p>
<p>General ----'s regiment, now quartered in the North. It is an </p>
<p>advantage to have it so far from this part of the kingdom. He </p>
<p>promises fairly; and I hope among different people, where </p>
<p>they may each have a character to preserve, they will both be </p>
<p>more prudent. I have written to Colonel Forster, to inform him </p>
<p>of our present arrangements, and to request that he will satisfy </p>
<p>the various creditors of Mr. Wickham in and near Brighton, </p>
<p>with assurances of speedy payment, for which I have pledged </p>
<p>myself. And will you give yourself the trouble of carrying </p>
<p>similar assurances to his creditors in Meryton, of whom I shall </p>
<p>subjoin a list according to his information? He has given in all </p>
<p>his debts; I hope at least he has not deceived us. Haggerston </p>
<p>has our directions, and all will be completed in a week. They </p>
<p>will then join his regiment, unless they are first invited to </p>
<p>Longbourn; and I understand from Mrs. Gardiner, that my </p>
<p>niece is very desirous of seeing you all before she leaves the </p>
<p>South. She is well, and begs to be dutifully remembered to </p>
<p>you and your mother.--Yours, etc., </p>
<p>"E. GARDINER." </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet and his daughters saw all the advantages of </p>
<p>Wickham's removal from the ----shire as clearly as Mr. </p>
<p>Gardiner could do. But Mrs. Bennet was not so well pleased </p>
<p>with it. Lydia's being settled in the North, just when she had </p>
<p>expected most pleasure and pride in her company, for she had </p>
<p>by no means given up her </p>
<p>plan of their residing in </p>
<p>Hertfordshire, was a severe disappointment; and, besides, it </p>
<p>was such a pity that Lydia should be taken from a regiment </p>
<p>where she was acquainted with everybody, and had so many </p>
<p>favourites. </p>
<p>"She is so fond of Mrs. Forster," said she, "it will be quite </p>
<p>shocking to send her away! And there are several of the young </p>
<p>men, too, that she likes very much. The officers may not be so </p>
<p>pleasant in General----'s regiment." </p>
<p>His daughter's request, for such it might be considered, of </p>
<p>being admitted into her family again before she set off for the </p>
<p>North, received at first an absolute negative. But Jane and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, who agreed in wishing, for the sake of their sister's </p>
<p>feelings and consequence, that she should be noticed on her </p>
<p>marriage by her parents, urged him so earnestly yet so </p>
<p>rationally and so mildly, to receive her and her husband at </p>
<p>Longbourn, as soon as they were married, that he was </p>
<p>prevailed on to think as they thought, and act as they wished. </p>
<p>And their mother had the satisfaction of knowing that she </p>
<p>would be able to show her married daughter in the </p>
<p>neighbourhood before she was banished to the North. When </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet wrote again to his brother, therefore, he sent his </p>
<p>permission for them to come; and it was settled, that as soon </p>
<p>as the ceremony was over, they should proceed to Longbourn. </p>
<p>Elizabeth was surprised, however, that Wickham should </p>
<p>consent to such a scheme, and had she consulted only her own </p>
<p>inclination, any meeting with him would have been the last </p>
<p>object of her wishes. </p>
<heading>Chapter 51. </heading>
<p>Their sister's wedding day arrived; and Jane and Elizabeth </p>
<p>felt for her probably more than she felt for herself. The </p>
<p>carriage was sent to meet them at ----, and they were to return </p>
<p>in it by dinner-time. Their arrival was dreaded by the elder </p>
<p>Miss Bennets, and Jane more especially, who gave Lydia the </p>
<p>feelings which would have attended herself, had she been the </p>
<p>culprit, and was wretched in the thought of what her sister </p>
<p>must endure. </p>
<p>They came. The family were assembled in the breakfast </p>
<p>room to receive them. Smiles decked the face of Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>as the carriage drove up to the door; her husband looked </p>
<p>impenetrably grave; her daughters, alarmed, anxious, uneasy. </p>
<p>Lydia's voice was heard in the vestibule; the door was </p>
<p>thrown open, and she ran into the room. Her mother stepped </p>
<p>forwards, embraced her, and welcomed her with rapture; gave </p>
<p>her hand, with an affectionate smile, to Wickham, who </p>
<p>followed his lady; and wished them both joy with an alacrity </p>
<p>which shewed no doubt of their happiness. </p>
<p>Their reception from Mr. Bennet, to whom they then </p>
<p>turned, was not quite so cordial. His countenance rather </p>
<p>gained in austerity; and he scarcely opened his lips. The easy </p>
<p>assurance of the young couple, indeed, was enough to </p>
<p>provoke him. Elizabeth was disgusted, and even Miss Bennet </p>
<p>was shocked. Lydia was Lydia still; untamed, unabashed, </p>
<p>wild, noisy, and fearless. She turned from sister to sister, </p>
<p>demanding their congratulations; and when at length they all </p>
<p>sat down, looked eagerly round the room, took notice of some </p>
<p>little alteration in it, and observed, with a laugh, that it was a </p>
<p>great while since she had been there. </p>
<p>Wickham was not at all more distressed than herself, but </p>
<p>his manners were always so pleasing, that had his character </p>
<p>and his marriage been exactly what they ought, his smiles and </p>
<p>his easy address, while he claimed their relationship, would </p>
<p>have delighted them all. Elizabeth had not before believed </p>
<p>him quite equal to such assurance; but she sat down, resolving </p>
<p>within herself to draw no limits in future to the impudence of </p>
<p>an impudent man. She blushed, and Jane blushed; but the </p>
<p>cheeks of the two who caused their confusion suffered no </p>
<p>variation of colour. </p>
<p>There was no want of discourse. The bride and her mother </p>
<p>could neither of them talk fast enough; and Wickham, who </p>
<p>happened to sit near Elizabeth, began inquiring after his </p>
<p>acquaintance in that neighbourhood, with a good humoured </p>
<p>ease which she felt very unable to equal in her replies. They </p>
<p>seemed each of them to have the happiest memories in the </p>
<p>world. Nothing of the past was recollected with pain; and </p>
<p>Lydia led voluntarily to subjects which her sisters would not </p>
<p>have alluded to for the world. </p>
<p>"Only think of its being three months," she cried, "since I </p>
<p>went away; it seems but a fortnight I declare; and yet there </p>
<p>have been things enough happened in the time. Good </p>
<p>gracious! when I went away, I am sure I had no more idea of </p>
<p>being married till I came back again! though I thought it </p>
<p>would be very good fun if I was." </p>
<p>Her father lifted up his eyes. Jane was distressed. Elizabeth </p>
<p>looked expressively at Lydia; but she, who never heard nor </p>
<p>saw anything of which she chose to be insensible, gaily </p>
<p>continued, "Oh! mamma, do the people hereabouts know I am </p>
<p>married to-day? I was afraid they might not; and we overtook </p>
<p>William Goulding in his curricle, so I was determined he </p>
<p>should know it, and so I let down the side-glass next to him, </p>
<p>and took off my glove, and let my hand just rest upon the </p>
<p>window frame, so that he might see the ring, and then I bowed </p>
<p>and smiled like anything." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could bear it no longer. She got up, and ran out </p>
<p>of the room; and returned no more, till she heard them passing </p>
<p>through the hall to the dining parlour. She then joined them </p>
<p>soon enough to see Lydia, with anxious parade, walk up to her </p>
<p>mother's right hand, and hear her say to her eldest sister, "Ah! </p>
<p>Jane, I take your place now, and you must go lower, because I </p>
<p>am a married woman." </p>
<p>It was not to be supposed that time would give Lydia that </p>
<p>embarrassment from which she had been so wholly free at </p>
<p>first. Her ease and good spirits increased. She longed to see </p>
<p>Mrs. Phillips, the Lucases, and all their other neighbours, and </p>
<p>to hear herself called "Mrs. Wickham" by each of them; and </p>
<p>in the mean time, she went after dinner to show her ring, and </p>
<p>boast of being married, to Mrs. Hill and the two housemaids. </p>
<p>"Well, mamma," said she, when they were all returned to </p>
<p>the breakfast room, "and what do you think of my husband? Is </p>
<p>not he a charming man? I am sure my sisters must all envy </p>
<p>me. I only hope they may have half my good luck. They must </p>
<p>all go to Brighton. That is the place to get husbands. What a </p>
<p>pity it is, mamma, we did not all go." </p>
<p>"Very true; and if I had my will, we should. But my dear </p>
<p>Lydia, I don't at all like your going such a way off. Must it be </p>
<p>so?" </p>
<p>"Oh, lord! yes;--there is nothing in that. I shall like it of all </p>
<p>things. You and papa, and my sisters, must come down and </p>
<p>see us. We shall be at Newcastle all the winter, and I dare say </p>
<p>there will be some balls, and I will take care to get good </p>
<p>partners for them all." </p>
<p>"I should like it beyond anything!" said her mother. </p>
<p>"And then when you go away, you may leave one or two </p>
<p>of my sisters behind you; and I dare say I shall get husbands </p>
<p>for them before the winter is over." </p>
<p>"I thank you for my share of the favour," said Elizabeth; </p>
<p>"but I do not particularly like your way of getting husbands." </p>
<p>Their visitors were not to remain above ten days with </p>
<p>them. Mr. Wickham had received his commission before he </p>
<p>left London, and he was to join his regiment at the end of a </p>
<p>fortnight. </p>
<p>No one but Mrs. Bennet regretted that their stay would be </p>
<p>so short; and she made the most of the time by visiting about </p>
<p>with her daughter, and having very frequent parties at home. </p>
<p>These parties were acceptable to all; to avoid a family circle </p>
<p>was even more desirable to such as did think, than such as did </p>
<p>not. </p>
<p>Wickham's affection for Lydia was just what Elizabeth had </p>
<p>expected to find it; not equal to Lydia's for him. She had </p>
<p>scarcely needed her present observation to be satisfied, from </p>
<p>the reason of things, that their elopement had been brought on </p>
<p>by the strength of her love, rather than by his; and she would </p>
<p>have wondered why, without violently caring for her, he </p>
<p>chose to elope with her at all, had she not felt certain that his </p>
<p>flight was rendered necessary by distress of circumstances; </p>
<p>and if that were the case, he was not the young man to resist </p>
<p>an opportunity of having a companion. </p>
<p>Lydia was exceedingly fond of him. He was her dear </p>
<p>Wickham on every occasion; no one was to be put in </p>
<p>competition with him. He did every thing best in the world; </p>
<p>and she was sure he would kill more birds on the first of </p>
