<p>September, than any body else in the country. </p>
<p>One morning, soon after their arrival, as she was sitting </p>
<p>with her two elder sisters, she said to Elizabeth: </p>
<p>"Lizzy, I never gave YOU an account of my wedding, I </p>
<p>believe. You were not by, when I told mamma and the others </p>
<p>all about it. Are not you curious to hear how it was managed?" </p>
<p>"No really," replied Elizabeth; "I think there cannot be too </p>
<p>little said on the subject." </p>
<p>"La! You are so strange! But I must tell you how it went </p>
<p>off. We were married, you know, at St. Clement's, because </p>
<p>Wickham's lodgings were in that parish. And it was settled </p>
<p>that we should all be there by eleven o'clock. My uncle and </p>
<p>aunt and I were to go together; and the others were to meet us </p>
<p>at the church. Well, Monday morning came, and I was in such </p>
<p>a fuss! I was so afraid, you know, that something would </p>
<p>happen to put it off, and then I should have gone quite </p>
<p>distracted. And there was my aunt, all the time I was dressing, </p>
<p>preaching and talking away just as if she was reading a </p>
<p>sermon. However, I did not hear above one word in ten, for I </p>
<p>was thinking, you may suppose, of my dear Wickham. I </p>
<p>longed to know whether he would be married in his blue </p>
<p>coat." </p>
<p>"Well, and so we breakfasted at ten as usual; I thought it </p>
<p>would never be over; for, by the bye, you are to understand, </p>
<p>that my uncle and aunt were horrid unpleasant all the time I </p>
<p>was with them. If you'll believe me, I did not once put my foot </p>
<p>out of doors, though I was there a fortnight. Not one party, or </p>
<p>scheme, or anything. To be sure London was rather thin, but, </p>
<p>however, the Little Theatre was open. Well, and so just as the </p>
<p>carriage came to the door, my uncle was called away upon </p>
<p>business to that horrid man Mr. Stone. And then, you know, </p>
<p>when once they get together, there is no end of it. Well, I was </p>
<p>so frightened I did not know what to do, for my uncle was to </p>
<p>give me away; and if we were beyond the hour, we could not </p>
<p>be married all day. But, luckily, he came back again in ten </p>
<p>minutes' time, and then we all set out. However, I recollected </p>
<p>afterwards that if he had been prevented going, the wedding </p>
<p>need not be put off, for Mr. Darcy might have done as well." </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy!" repeated Elizabeth, in utter amazement. </p>
<p>"Oh, yes!--he was to come there with Wickham, you </p>
<p>know. But gracious me! I quite forgot! I ought not to have </p>
<p>said a word about it. I promised them so faithfully! What will </p>
<p>Wickham say? It was to be such a secret!" </p>
<p>"If it was to be secret," said Jane, "say not another word on </p>
<p>the subject. You may depend upon my seeking no further." </p>
<p>"Oh! certainly," said Elizabeth, though burning </p>
<p>curiosity; "we will ask you no questions." </p>
<p>with </p>
<p>"Thank you," said Lydia, "for if you did, I should certainly </p>
<p>tell you all, and then Wickham would be angry." </p>
<p>On such encouragement to ask, Elizabeth was forced to put </p>
<p>it out of her power, by running away. </p>
<p>But to live in ignorance on such a point was impossible; or </p>
<p>at least it was impossible not to try for information. Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>had been at her sister's wedding. It was exactly a scene, and </p>
<p>exactly among people, where he had apparently least to do, </p>
<p>and least temptation to go. Conjectures as to the meaning of it, </p>
<p>rapid and wild, hurried into her brain; but she was satisfied </p>
<p>with none. Those that best pleased her, as placing his conduct </p>
<p>in the noblest light, seemed most improbable. She could not </p>
<p>bear such suspense; and hastily seizing a sheet of paper, wrote </p>
<p>a short letter to her aunt, to request an explanation of what </p>
<p>Lydia had dropt, if it were compatible with the secrecy which </p>
<p>had been intended. </p>
<p>"You may readily comprehend," she added, "what my </p>
<p>curiosity must be to know how a person unconnected with any </p>
<p>of us, and (comparatively speaking) a stranger to our family, </p>
<p>should have been amongst you at such a time. Pray write </p>
<p>instantly, and let me understand it--unless it is, for very cogent </p>
<p>reasons, to remain in the secrecy which Lydia seems to think </p>
<p>necessary; and then I must endeavour to be satisfied with </p>
<p>ignorance." </p>
<p>"Not that I SHALL, though," she added to herself, as she </p>
<p>finished the letter; "and my dear aunt, if you do not tell me in </p>
<p>an honourable manner, I shall certainly be reduced to tricks </p>
<p>and stratagems to find it out." </p>
<p>Jane's delicate sense of honour would not allow her to </p>
<p>speak to Elizabeth privately of what Lydia had let fall; </p>
<p>Elizabeth was glad of it;--till it appeared whether her inquiries </p>
<p>would receive any satisfaction, she had rather be without a </p>
<p>confidante. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 52. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth had the satisfaction of receiving an answer to her </p>
<p>letter as soon as she possibly could. She was no sooner in </p>
<p>possession of it than, hurrying into the little copse, where she </p>
<p>was least likely to be interrupted, she sat down on one of the </p>
<p>benches and prepared to be happy; for the length of the letter </p>
<p>convinced her that it did not contain a denial. </p>
<p>"Gracechurch street, Sept. 6. </p>
<p>"MY DEAR NIECE, </p>
<p>"I have just received your letter, and shall devote this </p>
<p>whole morning to answering it, as I foresee that a LITTLE </p>
<p>writing will not comprise what I have to tell you. I must </p>
<p>confess myself surprised by your application; I did not expect </p>
<p>it from YOU. Don't think me angry, however, for I only mean </p>
<p>to let you know that I had not imagined such inquiries to be </p>
<p>necessary on YOUR side. If you do not choose to understand </p>
<p>me, forgive my impertinence. Your uncle is as much surprised </p>
<p>as I am--and nothing but the belief of your being a party </p>
<p>concerned would have allowed him to act as he has done. But </p>
<p>if you are really innocent and ignorant, I must be more </p>
<p>explicit. </p>
<p>"On the very day of my coming home from Longbourn, </p>
<p>your uncle had a most unexpected visitor. Mr. Darcy called, </p>
<p>and was shut up with him several hours. It was all over before </p>
<p>I arrived; so my curiosity was not so dreadfully racked as </p>
<p>YOUR'S seems to have been. He came to tell Mr. Gardiner </p>
<p>that he had found out where your sister and Mr. Wickham </p>
<p>were, and that he had seen and talked with them both; </p>
<p>Wickham repeatedly, Lydia once. From what I can collect, he </p>
<p>left Derbyshire only one day after ourselves, and came to </p>
<p>town with the resolution of hunting for them. The motive </p>
<p>professed was his conviction of its being owing to himself </p>
<p>that Wickham's worthlessness had not been so well known as </p>
<p>to make it impossible for any young woman of character to </p>
<p>love or confide in him. He generously imputed the whole to </p>
<p>his mistaken pride, and confessed that he had before thought it </p>
<p>beneath him to lay his private actions open to the world. His </p>
<p>character was to speak for itself. He called it, therefore, his </p>
<p>duty to step forward, and endeavour to remedy an evil which </p>
<p>had been brought on by himself. If he HAD ANOTHER </p>
<p>motive, I am sure it would never disgrace him. He had been </p>
<p>some days in town, before he was able to discover them; but </p>
<p>he had something to direct his search, which was more than </p>
<p>WE had; and the consciousness of this was another reason for </p>
<p>his resolving to follow us. </p>
<p>"There is a lady, it seems, a Mrs. Younge, who was some </p>
<p>time ago governess to Miss Darcy, and was dismissed from </p>
<p>her charge on some cause of disapprobation, though he did </p>
<p>not say what. She then took a large house in Edward-street, </p>
<p>and has since maintained herself by letting lodgings. This </p>
<p>Mrs. Younge was, he knew, intimately acquainted with </p>
<p>Wickham; and he went to her for intelligence of him as soon </p>
<p>as he got to town. But it was two or three days before he could </p>
<p>get from her what he wanted. She would not betray her trust, I </p>
<p>suppose, without bribery and corruption, for she really did </p>
<p>know where her friend was to be found. Wickham indeed had </p>
<p>gone to her on their first arrival in London, and had she been </p>
<p>able to receive them into her house, they would have taken up </p>
<p>their abode with her. At length, however, our kind friend </p>
<p>procured the wished-for direction. They were in ---- street. He </p>
<p>saw Wickham, and afterwards insisted on seeing Lydia. His </p>
<p>first object with her, he acknowledged, had been to persuade </p>
<p>her to quit her present disgraceful situation, and return to her </p>
<p>friends as soon as they could be prevailed on to receive her, </p>
<p>offering his assistance, as far as it would go. But he found </p>
<p>Lydia absolutely resolved on remaining where she was. She </p>
<p>cared for none of her friends; she wanted no help of his; she </p>
<p>would not hear of leaving Wickham. She was sure they should </p>
<p>be married some time or other, and it did not much signify </p>
<p>when. Since such were her feelings, it only remained, he </p>
<p>thought, to secure and expedite a marriage, which, in his very </p>
<p>first conversation with Wickham, he easily learnt had never </p>
<p>been HIS design. He confessed himself obliged to leave the </p>
<p>regiment, on account of some debts of honour, which were </p>
<p>very pressing; and scrupled not to lay all the ill-consequences </p>
<p>of Lydia's flight on her own folly alone. He meant to resign </p>
<p>his commission immediately; and as to his future situation, he </p>
<p>could conjecture very little about it. He must go somewhere, </p>
<p>but he did not know where, and he knew he should have </p>
<p>nothing to live on. </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy asked him why he had not married your sister </p>
<p>at once. Though Mr. Bennet was not imagined to be very rich, </p>
<p>he would have been able to do something for him, and his </p>
<p>situation must have been benefited by marriage. But he found, </p>
<p>in reply to this question, that Wickham still cherished the </p>
<p>hope of more effectually making his fortune by marriage in </p>
<p>some other country. Under such circumstances, however, he </p>
<p>was not likely to be proof against the temptation of immediate </p>
<p>relief. </p>
<p>"They met several times, for there was much to be </p>
<p>discussed. Wickham of course wanted more than he could get; </p>
<p>but at length was reduced to be reasonable. </p>
<p>"Every thing being settled between THEM, Mr. Darcy's </p>
<p>next step was to make your uncle acquainted with it, and he </p>
<p>first called in Gracechurch street the evening before I came </p>
<p>home. But Mr. Gardiner could not be seen, and Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>found, on further inquiry, that your father was still with him, </p>
<p>but would quit town the next morning. He did not judge your </p>
<p>father to be a person whom he could so properly consult as </p>
<p>your uncle, and therefore readily postponed seeing him till </p>
<p>after the departure of the former. He did not leave his name, </p>
<p>and till the next day it was only known that a gentleman had </p>
<p>called on business. </p>
<p>"On Saturday he came again. Your father was gone, your </p>
<p>uncle at home, and, as I said before, they had a great deal of </p>
<p>talk together. </p>
<p>"They met again on Sunday, and then _I_ saw him too. It </p>
<p>was not all settled before Monday: as soon as it was, the </p>
<p>express was sent off to Longbourn. But our visitor was very </p>
<p>obstinate. I fancy, Lizzy, that obstinacy is the real defect of </p>
<p>his character, after all. He has been accused of many faults at </p>
<p>different times, but THIS is the true one. Nothing was to be </p>
<p>done that he did not do himself; though I am sure (and I do </p>
<p>not speak it to be thanked, therefore say nothing about it), </p>
<p>your uncle would most readily have settled the whole. </p>
<p>"They battled it together for a long time, which was more </p>
<p>than either the gentleman or lady concerned in it deserved. </p>
<p>But at last your uncle was forced to yield, and instead of being </p>
<p>allowed to be of use to his niece, was forced to put up with </p>
<p>only having the probable credit of it, which went sorely </p>
<p>against the grain; and I really believe your letter this morning </p>
<p>gave him great pleasure, because it required an explanation </p>
<p>that would rob him of his borrowed feathers, and give the </p>
<p>praise where it was due. But, Lizzy, this must go no farther </p>
<p>than yourself, or Jane at most. </p>
<p>"You know pretty well, I suppose, what has been done for </p>
<p>the young people. His debts are to be paid, amounting, I </p>
<p>believe, to considerably more than a thousand pounds, another </p>
<p>thousand in addition to her own settled upon HER, and his </p>
<p>commission purchased. The reason why all this was to be </p>
<p>done by him alone, was such as I have given above. It was </p>
<p>owing to him, to his reserve and want of proper consideration, </p>
<p>that Wickham's character had been so misunderstood, and </p>
<p>consequently that he had been received and noticed as he was. </p>
<p>Perhaps there was some truth in THIS; though I doubt </p>
<p>whether HIS reserve, or ANYBODY'S reserve, can be </p>
<p>answerable for the event. But in spite of all this fine talking, </p>
<p>my dear Lizzy, you may rest perfectly assured that your uncle </p>
<p>would never have yielded, if we had not given him credit for </p>
<p>ANOTHER INTEREST in the affair. </p>
<p>"When all this was resolved on, he returned again to his </p>
<p>friends, who were still staying at Pemberley; but it was agreed </p>
<p>that he should be in London once more when the wedding </p>
<p>took place, and all money matters were then to receive the last </p>
<p>finish. </p>
<p>"I believe I have now told you every thing. It is a relation </p>
<p>which you tell me is to give you great surprise; I hope at least </p>
<p>it will not afford you any displeasure. Lydia came to us; and </p>
<p>Wickham had constant admission to the house. HE was </p>
<p>exactly what he had been, when I knew him in Hertfordshire; </p>
<p>but I would not tell you how little I was satisfied with her </p>
<p>behaviour while she staid with us, if I had not perceived, by </p>
<p>Jane's letter last Wednesday, that her conduct on coming </p>
<p>home was exactly of a piece with it, and therefore what I now </p>
<p>tell you can give you no fresh pain. I talked to her repeatedly </p>
<p>in the most serious manner, representing to her all the </p>
<p>wickedness of what she had done, and all the unhappiness she </p>
<p>had brought on her family. If she heard me, it was by good </p>
<p>luck, for I am sure she did not listen. I was sometimes quite </p>
<p>provoked, but then I recollected my dear Elizabeth and Jane, </p>
<p>and for their sakes had patience with her. </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy was punctual in his return, and as Lydia </p>
<p>informed you, attended the wedding. He dined with us the </p>
<p>next day, and was to leave town again on Wednesday or </p>
<p>Thursday. Will you be very angry with me, my dear Lizzy, if I </p>
<p>take this opportunity of saying (what I was never bold enough </p>
<p>to say before) how much I like him. His behaviour to us has, </p>
<p>in every respect, been as pleasing as when we were in </p>
<p>Derbyshire. His understanding and opinions all please me; he </p>
<p>wants nothing but a little more liveliness, and THAT, if he </p>
<p>marry PRUDENTLY, his wife may teach him. I thought him </p>
<p>very sly;-he hardly ever mentioned your name. But slyness </p>
<p>seems the fashion. </p>
<p>"Pray forgive me if I have been very presuming, or at least </p>
<p>do not punish me so far as to exclude me from P. I shall never </p>
<p>be quite happy till I have been all round the park. A low </p>
<p>phaeton, with a nice little pair of ponies, would be the very </p>
<p>thing. </p>
<p>"But I must write no more. The children have been </p>
<p>wanting me this half hour. </p>
<p>"Yours, very sincerely, </p>
<p>"M. GARDINER." </p>
<p>The contents of this letter threw Elizabeth into a flutter of </p>
<p>spirits, in which it was difficult to determine whether pleasure </p>
<p>or pain bore the greatest share. The vague and unsettled </p>
<p>suspicions which uncertainty had produced of what Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>might have been doing to forward her sister's match, which </p>
<p>she had feared to encourage as an exertion of goodness too </p>
<p>great to be probable, and at the same time dreaded to be just, </p>
<p>from the pain of obligation, were proved beyond their greatest </p>
<p>extent to be true! He had followed them purposely to town, he </p>
<p>had taken on himself all the trouble and mortification </p>
<p>attendant on such a research; in which supplication had been </p>
<p>necessary to a woman whom he must abominate and despise, </p>
<p>and where he was reduced to meet, frequently meet, reason </p>
<p>with, persuade, and finally bribe, the man whom he always </p>
<p>most wished to avoid, and whose very name it was </p>
<p>punishment to him to pronounce. He had done all this for a </p>
<p>girl whom he could neither regard nor esteem. Her heart did </p>
<p>whisper that he had done it for her. But it was a hope shortly </p>
<p>checked by other considerations, and she soon felt that even </p>
<p>her vanity was insufficient, when required to depend on his </p>
<p>affection for her -for a woman who had already refused him-- </p>
<p>as able to overcome a sentiment so natural as abhorrence </p>
<p>against relationship with Wickham. Brother-in-law of </p>
<p>Wickham! Every kind of pride must revolt from the </p>
<p>connection. He had, to be sure, done much. She was ashamed </p>
<p>to think how much. But he had given a reason for his </p>
<p>interference, which asked no extraordinary stretch of belief. It </p>
<p>was reasonable that he should feel he had been wrong; he had </p>
<p>liberality, and he had the means of exercising it; and though </p>
<p>she would not place herself as his principal inducement, she </p>
<p>could, perhaps, believe that remaining partiality for her might </p>
<p>assist his endeavours in a cause where her peace of mind must </p>
<p>be materially concerned. It was painful, exceedingly painful, </p>
<p>to know that they were under obligations to a person who </p>
<p>could never receive a return. They owed the restoration of </p>
<p>Lydia, her character, every thing, to him. Oh! how heartily did </p>
<p>she grieve over every ungracious sensation she had ever </p>
<p>encouraged, every saucy speech she had ever directed towards </p>
<p>him. For herself she was humbled; but she was proud of him. </p>
<p>Proud that in a cause of compassion and honour, he had been </p>
<p>able to get the better of himself. She read over her aunt's </p>
<p>commendation of him again and again. It was hardly enough; </p>
<p>but it pleased her. She was even sensible of some pleasure, </p>
<p>though mixed with regret, on finding how steadfastly both she </p>
<p>and her uncle had been persuaded that affection and </p>
<p>confidence subsisted between Mr. Darcy and herself. </p>
<p>She was roused from her seat, and her reflections, by some </p>
<p>one's approach; and before she could strike into another path, </p>
<p>she was overtaken by Wickham. </p>
<p>"I am afraid I interrupt your solitary ramble, my dear </p>
<p>sister?" said he, as he joined her. </p>
<p>"You certainly do," she replied with a smile; "but it does </p>
<p>not follow that the interruption must be unwelcome." </p>
<p>"I should be sorry indeed, if it were. We were always good </p>
<p>friends; and now we are better." </p>
<p>"True. Are the others coming out?" </p>
<p>"I do not know. Mrs. Bennet and Lydia are going in the </p>
<p>carriage to Meryton. And so, my dear sister, I find, from our </p>
<p>uncle and aunt, that you have actually seen Pemberley." </p>
<p>She replied in the affirmative. </p>
<p>"I almost envy you the pleasure, and yet I believe it would </p>
<p>be too much for me, or else I could take it in my way to </p>
<p>Newcastle. And you saw the old housekeeper, I suppose? </p>
<p>Poor Reynolds, she was always very fond of me. But of </p>
<p>course she did not mention my name to you." </p>
<p>"Yes, she did." </p>
<p>"And what did she say?" </p>
<p>"That you were gone into the army, and she was afraid had </p>
<p>--not turned out well. At such a distance as THAT, you know, </p>
<p>things are strangely misrepresented." </p>
<p>"Certainly," he replied, biting his lips. Elizabeth hoped she </p>
<p>had silenced him; but he soon afterwards said: </p>
<p>"I was surprised to see Darcy in town last month. We </p>
<p>passed each other several times. I wonder what he can be </p>
<p>doing there." </p>
<p>"Perhaps preparing for his marriage with Miss de Bourgh," </p>
<p>said Elizabeth. "It must be something particular, to take him </p>
<p>there at this time of year." </p>
<p>"Undoubtedly. Did you see him while you were at </p>
<p>Lambton? I thought I understood from the Gardiners that you </p>
<p>had." </p>
<p>"Yes; he introduced us to his sister." </p>
<p>"And do you like her?" </p>
<p>"Very much." </p>
<p>"I have heard, indeed, that she is uncommonly improved </p>
<p>within this year or two. When I last saw her, she was not very </p>
<p>promising. I am very glad you liked her. I hope she will turn </p>
<p>out well." </p>
<p>"I dare say she will; she has got over the most trying age." </p>
<p>"Did you go by the village of Kympton?" </p>
<p>"I do not recollect that we did." </p>
<p>"I mention it, because it is the living which I ought to have </p>
<p>had. A most delightful place!--Excellent Parsonage House! It </p>
<p>would have suited me in every respect." </p>
<p>"How should you have liked making sermons?" </p>
<p>"Exceedingly well. I should have considered it as part of </p>
<p>my duty, and the exertion would soon have been nothing. One </p>
<p>ought not to repine;--but, to be sure, it would have been such a </p>
<p>thing for me! The quiet, the retirement of such a life would </p>
<p>have answered all my ideas of happiness! But it was not to be. </p>
<p>Did you ever hear Darcy mention the circumstance, when you </p>
<p>were in Kent?" </p>
<p>"I have heard from authority, which I thought AS GOOD, </p>
<p>that it was left you conditionally only, and at the will of the </p>
<p>present patron." </p>
<p>"You have. Yes, there was something in THAT; I told you </p>
<p>so from the first, you may remember." </p>
<p>"I DID hear, too, that there was a time, when sermon- </p>
<p>making was not so palatable to you as it seems to be at </p>
<p>present; that you actually declared your resolution of never </p>
<p>taking orders, and that the business had been compromised </p>
<p>accordingly." </p>
<p>"You did! and it was not wholly without foundation. You </p>
<p>may remember what I told you on that point, when first we </p>
<p>talked of it." </p>
<p>They were now almost at the door of the house, for she had </p>
<p>walked fast to get rid of him; and unwilling, for her sister's </p>
<p>sake, to provoke him, she only said in reply, with a good- </p>
<p>humoured smile: </p>
<p>"Come, Mr. Wickham, we are brother and sister, you </p>
<p>know. Do not let us quarrel about the past. In future, I hope </p>
<p>we shall be always of one mind." </p>
<p>She held out her hand; he kissed it with affectionate </p>
<p>gallantry, though he hardly knew how to look, and they </p>
<p>entered the house. </p>
<heading>Chapter 53. </heading>
<p>Mr. Wickham was so perfectly </p>
<p>satisfied with this </p>
<p>conversation that he never again distressed himself, or </p>
<p>provoked his dear sister Elizabeth, by introducing the subject </p>
<p>of it; and she was pleased to find that she had said enough to </p>
<p>keep him quiet. </p>
<p>The day of his and Lydia's departure soon came, and Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet was forced to submit to a separation, which, as her </p>
<p>husband by no means entered into her scheme of their all </p>
<p>going to Newcastle, was likely to continue at least a </p>
<p>twelvemonth. </p>
<p>"Oh! my dear Lydia," she cried, "when shall we meet </p>
<p>again?" </p>
<p>"Oh, lord! I don't know. Not these two or three years, </p>
<p>perhaps." </p>
<p>"Write to me very often, my dear." </p>
<p>"As often as I can. But you know married women have </p>
<p>never much time for writing. My sisters may write to ME. </p>
<p>They will have nothing else to do." </p>
<p>Mr. Wickham's adieus were much more affectionate than </p>
<p>his wife's. He smiled, looked handsome, and said many pretty </p>
<p>things. </p>
<p>"He is as fine a fellow," said Mr. Bennet, as soon as they </p>
<p>were out of the house, "as ever I saw. He simpers, and smirks, </p>
<p>and makes love to us all. I am prodigiously proud of him. I </p>
<p>defy even Sir William Lucas himself to produce a more </p>
<p>valuable son-in-law." </p>
<p>The loss of her daughter made Mrs. Bennet very dull for </p>
<p>several days. </p>
<p>"I often think," said she, "that there is nothing so bad as </p>
<p>parting with one's friends. One seems so forlorn without </p>
<p>them." </p>
<p>"This is the consequence, you see, Madam, of marrying a </p>
<p>daughter," said Elizabeth. "It must make you better satisfied </p>
<p>that your other four are single." </p>
<p>"It is no such thing. Lydia does not leave me because she </p>
<p>is married, but only because her husband's regiment happens </p>
<p>to be so far off. If that had been nearer, she would not have </p>
<p>gone so soon." </p>
<p>But the spiritless condition which this event threw her into </p>
<p>was shortly relieved, and her mind opened again to the </p>
<p>agitation of hope, by an article of news which then began to </p>
<p>be in circulation. The housekeeper at Netherfield had received </p>
<p>orders to prepare for the arrival of her master, who was </p>
<p>coming down in a day or two, to shoot there for several </p>
<p>weeks. Mrs. Bennet was quite in the fidgets. She looked at </p>
<p>Jane, and smiled and shook her head by turns. </p>
<p>"Well, well, and so Mr. Bingley is coming down, sister," </p>
<p>(for Mrs. Phillips first brought her the news). "Well, so much </p>
<p>the better. Not that I care about it, though. He is nothing to us, </p>
<p>you know, and I am sure _I_ never want to see him again. </p>
<p>But, however, he is very welcome to come to Netherfield, if </p>
<p>he likes it. And who knows what MAY happen? But that is </p>
<p>nothing to us. You know, sister, we agreed long ago never to </p>
<p>mention a word about it. And so, is it quite certain he is </p>
<p>coming?" </p>
<p>"You may depend on it," replied the other, "for Mrs. </p>
<p>Nicholls was in Meryton last night; I saw her passing by, and </p>
<p>went out myself on purpose to know the truth of it; and she </p>
<p>told me that it was certain true. He comes down on Thursday </p>
<p>at the latest, very likely on Wednesday. She was going to the </p>
<p>butcher's, she told me, on purpose to order in some meat on </p>
<p>Wednesday, and she has got three couple of ducks just fit to </p>
<p>be killed." </p>
<p>Miss Bennet had not been able to hear of his coming </p>
<p>without changing colour. It was many months since she had </p>
<p>mentioned his name to Elizabeth; but now, as soon as they </p>
<p>were alone together, she said: </p>
<p>"I saw you look at me to-day, Lizzy, when my aunt told us </p>
<p>of the present report; and I know I appeared distressed. But </p>
<p>don't imagine it was from any silly cause. I was only confused </p>
<p>for the moment, because I felt that I SHOULD be looked at. I </p>
<p>do assure you that the news does not affect me either with </p>
<p>pleasure or pain. I am glad of one thing, that he comes alone; </p>
<p>because we shall see the less of him. Not that I am afraid of </p>
<p>MYSELF, but I dread other people's remarks." </p>
<p>Elizabeth did not know what to make of it. Had she not </p>
<p>seen him in Derbyshire, she might have supposed him capable </p>
<p>of coming there with no other view than what was </p>
<p>acknowledged; but she still thought him partial to Jane, and </p>
<p>she wavered as to the greater probability of his coming there </p>
<p>WITH his friend's permission, or being bold enough to come </p>
<p>without it. </p>
<p>"Yet it is hard," she sometimes thought, "that this poor </p>
<p>man cannot come to a house which he has legally hired, </p>
<p>without raising all this speculation! I WILL leave him to </p>
<p>himself." </p>
<p>In spite of what her sister declared, and really believed to </p>
<p>be her feelings in the expectation of his arrival, Elizabeth </p>
<p>could easily perceive that her spirits were affected by it. They </p>
<p>were more disturbed, more unequal, than she had often seen </p>
<p>them. </p>
<p>The subject which had been so warmly canvassed between </p>
<p>their parents, about a twelvemonth ago, was now brought </p>
<p>forward again. </p>
<p>"As soon as ever Mr. Bingley comes, my dear," said Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, "you will wait on him of course." </p>
<p>"No, no. You forced me into visiting him last year, and </p>
<p>promised, if I went to see him, he should marry one of my </p>
<p>daughters. But it ended in nothing, and I will not be sent on a </p>
<p>fool's errand again." </p>
<p>His wife represented to him how absolutely necessary such </p>
<p>an attention would be from all the neighbouring gentlemen, on </p>
<p>his returning to Netherfield. </p>
<p>"'This an etiquette I despise," said he. "If he wants our </p>
<p>society, let him seek it. He knows where we live. I will not </p>
<p>spend my hours in running after my neighbours every time </p>
<p>they go away and come back again." </p>
<p>"Well, all I know is, that it will be abominably rude if you </p>
<p>do not wait on him. But, however, that shan't prevent my </p>
<p>asking him to dine here, I am determined. We must have Mrs. </p>
<p>Long and the Gouldings soon. That will make thirteen with </p>
<p>ourselves, so there will be just room at table for him." </p>
<p>Consoled by this resolution, she was the better able to bear </p>
<p>her husband's incivility; though it was very mortifying to </p>
<p>know that her neighbours might all see Mr. Bingley, in </p>
<p>consequence of it, before THEY did. As the day of his arrival </p>
<p>drew near: </p>
<p>"I begin to be sorry that he comes at all," said Jane to her </p>
<p>sister. "It would be nothing; I could see him with perfect </p>
<p>indifference, but I can hardly bear to hear it thus perpetually </p>
<p>talked of. My mother means well; but she does not know, no </p>
<p>one can know, how much I suffer from what she says. Happy </p>
<p>shall I be, when his stay at Netherfield is over!" </p>
<p>"I wish I could say anything to comfort you," replied </p>
<p>Elizabeth; "but it is wholly out of my power. You must feel it; </p>
<p>and the usual satisfaction of preaching patience to a sufferer is </p>
<p>denied me, because you have always so much." </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley arrived. Mrs. Bennet, through the assistance </p>
<p>of servants, contrived to have the earliest tidings of it, that the </p>
<p>period of anxiety and fretfulness on her side might be as long </p>
<p>as it could. She counted the days that must intervene before </p>
<p>their invitation could be sent; hopeless of seeing him before. </p>
<p>But on the third morning after his arrival in Hertfordshire, she </p>
<p>saw him, from her dressing-room window, enter the paddock </p>
<p>and ride towards the house. </p>
<p>Her daughters were eagerly called to partake of her joy. </p>
<p>Jane resolutely kept her place at the table; but Elizabeth, to </p>
<p>satisfy her mother, went to the window--she looked,--she saw </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy with him, and sat down again by her sister. </p>
<p>"There is a gentleman with him, mamma," said Kitty; </p>
<p>"who can it be?" </p>
<p>"Some acquaintance or other, my dear, I suppose; I am </p>
<p>sure I do not know." </p>
<p>"La!" replied Kitty, "it looks just like that man that used to </p>
<p>be with him before. Mr. what's-his-name. That tall, proud </p>
<p>man." </p>
<p>"Good gracious! Mr. Darcy!--and so it does, I vow. Well, </p>
<p>any friend of Mr. Bingley's will always be welcome here, to </p>
<p>be sure; but else I must say that I hate the very sight of him." </p>
<p>Jane looked at Elizabeth with surprise and concern. She </p>
<p>knew but little of their meeting in Derbyshire, and therefore </p>
<p>felt for the awkwardness which must attend her sister, in </p>
<p>seeing him almost for the first time after receiving his </p>
<p>explanatory letter. Both sisters were uncomfortable enough. </p>
<p>Each felt for the other, and of course for themselves; and their </p>
<p>mother talked on, of her dislike of Mr. Darcy, and her </p>
<p>resolution to be civil to him only as Mr. Bingley's friend, </p>
<p>without being heard by either of them. But Elizabeth had </p>
<p>sources of uneasiness which could not be suspected by Jane, </p>
<p>to whom she had never yet had courage to shew Mrs. </p>
<p>Gardiner's letter, or to relate her own change of sentiment </p>
<p>towards him. To Jane, he could be only a man whose </p>
<p>proposals she had refused, and whose merit she had </p>
<p>undervalued; but to her own more extensive information, he </p>
<p>was the person to whom the whole family were indebted for </p>
<p>the first of benefits, and whom she regarded herself with an </p>
<p>interest, if not quite so tender, at least as reasonable and just </p>
<p>as what Jane felt for Bingley. Her astonishment at his coming- </p>
<p>-at his coming to Netherfield, to Longbourn, and voluntarily </p>
<p>seeking her again, was almost equal to what she had known </p>
<p>on first witnessing his altered behaviour in Derbyshire. </p>
<p>The colour which had been driven from her face, returned </p>
<p>for half a minute with an additional glow, and a smile of </p>
<p>delight added lustre to her eyes, as she thought for that space </p>
<p>of time that his affection and wishes must still be unshaken. </p>
<p>But she would not be secure. </p>
<p>"Let me first see how he behaves," said she; "it will then </p>
<p>be early enough for expectation." </p>
<p>She sat intently at work, striving to be composed, and </p>
<p>without daring to lift up her eyes, till anxious curiosity carried </p>
<p>them to the face of her sister as the servant was approaching </p>
<p>the door. Jane looked a little paler than usual, but more sedate </p>
<p>than Elizabeth had expected. On the gentlemen's appearing, </p>
<p>her colour increased; yet she received them with tolerable </p>
<p>ease, and with a propriety of behaviour equally free from any </p>
<p>symptom of resentment or any unnecessary complaisance. </p>
<p>Elizabeth said as little to either as civility would allow, and </p>
<p>sat down again to her work, with an eagerness which it did not </p>
<p>often command. She had ventured only one glance at Darcy. </p>
<p>He looked serious, as usual; and, she thought, more as he had </p>
<p>been used to look in Hertfordshire, than as she had seen him </p>
<p>at Pemberley. But, perhaps he could not in her mother's </p>
<p>presence be what he was before her uncle and aunt. It was a </p>
<p>painful, but not an improbable, conjecture. </p>
<p>Bingley, she had likewise seen for an instant, and in that </p>
<p>short period saw him looking both pleased and embarrassed. </p>
<p>He was received by Mrs. Bennet with a degree of civility </p>
<p>which made her two daughters ashamed, especially when </p>
<p>contrasted with the cold and ceremonious politeness of her </p>
<p>curtsey and address to his friend. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, particularly, who knew that her mother owed to </p>
<p>the latter the preservation of her favourite daughter from </p>
<p>irremediable infamy, was hurt and distressed to a most painful </p>
<p>degree by a distinction so ill applied. </p>
<p>Darcy, after inquiring of her how Mr. and Mrs. Gardiner </p>
<p>did, a question which she could not answer without confusion, </p>
<p>said scarcely anything. He was not seated by her; perhaps that </p>
<p>was the reason of his silence; but it had not been so in </p>
<p>Derbyshire. There he had talked to her friends, when he could </p>
<p>not to herself. But now several minutes elapsed without </p>
<p>bringing the sound of his voice; and when occasionally, </p>
<p>unable to resist the impulse of curiosity, she raised he eyes to </p>
<p>his face, she as often found him looking at Jane as at herself, </p>
<p>and frequently on no </p>
<p>object but the ground. More </p>
<p>thoughtfulness and less anxiety to please, than when they last </p>
<p>met, were plainly expressed. She was disappointed, and angry </p>
<p>with herself for being so. </p>
<p>"Could I expect it to be otherwise!" said she. "Yet why did </p>
<p>he come?" </p>
<p>She was in no humour for conversation with anyone but </p>
<p>himself; and to him she had hardly courage to speak. </p>
<p>She inquired after his sister, but could do no more. </p>
<p>"It is a long time, Mr. Bingley, since you went away," said </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet. </p>
<p>He readily agreed to it. </p>
<p>"I began to be afraid you would never come back again. </p>
<p>People DID say you meant to quit the place entirely at </p>
<p>Michaelmas; but, however, I hope it is not true. A great many </p>
<p>changes have happened in the neighbourhood, since you went </p>
<p>away. Miss Lucas is married and settled. And one of my own </p>
<p>daughters. I suppose you have heard of it; indeed, you must </p>
<p>have seen it in the papers. It was in The Times and The </p>
<p>Courier, I know; though it was not put in as it ought to be. It </p>
<p>was only said, 'Lately, George Wickham, Esq. to Miss Lydia </p>
<p>Bennet,' without there being a syllable said of her father, or </p>
<p>the place where she lived, or anything. It was my brother </p>
<p>Gardiner's drawing up too, and I wonder how he came to </p>
<p>make such an awkward business of it. Did you see it?" </p>
<p>Bingley replied that he did, and made his congratulations. </p>
<p>Elizabeth dared not lift up her eyes. How Mr. Darcy looked, </p>
<p>therefore, she could not tell. </p>
<p>"It is a delightful thing, to be sure, to have a daughter well </p>
<p>married," continued her mother, "but at the same time, Mr. </p>
<p>Bingley, it is very hard to have her taken such a way from me. </p>
<p>They are gone down to Newcastle, a place quite northward, it </p>
<p>seems, and there they are to stay I do not know how long. His </p>
<p>regiment is there; for I suppose you have heard of his leaving </p>
<p>the ----shire, and of his being gone into the regulars. Thank </p>
<p>Heaven! he has SOME friends, though perhaps not so many </p>
<p>as he deserves." </p>
<p>Elizabeth, who knew this to be levelled at Mr. Darcy, was </p>
<p>in such misery of shame, that she could hardly keep her seat. </p>
<p>It drew from her, however, the exertion of speaking, which </p>
<p>nothing else had so effectually done before; and she asked </p>
<p>Bingley whether he meant to make any stay in the country at </p>
<p>present. A few weeks, he believed. </p>
<p>"When you have killed all your own birds, Mr. Bingley," </p>
<p>said her mother, "I beg you will come here, and shoot as many </p>
<p>as you please on Mr. Bennet's manor. I am sure he will be </p>
<p>vastly happy to oblige you, and will save all the best of the </p>
<p>covies for you." </p>
<p>Elizabeth's misery increased, at such unnecessary, such </p>
<p>officious attention! Were the same fair prospect to arise at </p>
<p>present as had flattered them a year ago, every thing, she was </p>
<p>persuaded, would be hastening to the same vexatious </p>
<p>conclusion. At that instant, she felt that years of happiness </p>
<p>could not make Jane or herself amends for moments of such </p>
<p>painful confusion. </p>
<p>"The first wish of my heart," said she to herself, "is never </p>
<p>more to be in company with either of them. Their society can </p>
<p>afford no pleasure that will atone for such wretchedness as </p>
<p>this! Let me never see either one or the other again!" </p>
<p>Yet the misery, for which years of happiness were to offer </p>
<p>no compensation, received soon afterwards material relief, </p>
<p>from observing how much the beauty of her sister re-kindled </p>
<p>the admiration of her former lover. When first he came in, he </p>
<p>had spoken to her but little; but every five minutes seemed to </p>
<p>be giving her more of his attention. He found her as handsome </p>
<p>as she had been last year; as good natured, and as unaffected, </p>
<p>though not quite so chatty. Jane was anxious that no </p>
<p>difference should be perceived in her at all, and was really </p>
<p>persuaded that she talked as much as ever. But her mind was </p>
<p>so busily engaged, that she did not always know when she </p>
<p>was silent. </p>
<p>When the gentlemen rose to go away, Mrs. Bennet was </p>
<p>mindful of her intended civility, and they were invited and </p>
<p>engaged to dine at Longbourn in a few days time. </p>
<p>"You are quite a visit in my debt, Mr. Bingley," she added, </p>
<p>"for when you went to town last winter, you promised to take </p>
<p>a family dinner with us, as soon as you returned. I have not </p>
<p>forgot, you see; and I assure you, I was very much </p>
<p>disappointed that you did not come back and keep your </p>
<p>engagement." </p>
<p>Bingley looked a little silly at this reflection, and said </p>
<p>something of his concern at having been prevented by </p>
<p>business. They then went away. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet had been strongly inclined to ask them to stay </p>
<p>and dine there that day; but, though she always kept a very </p>
<p>good table, she did not think anything less than two courses </p>
<p>could be good enough for a man on whom she had such </p>
<p>anxious designs, or satisfy the appetite and pride of one who </p>
<p>had ten thousand a year. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 54. </heading>
<p>As soon as they were gone, Elizabeth walked out to </p>
<p>recover her spirits; or in other words, to dwell without </p>
<p>interruption on those subjects that must deaden them more. </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's behaviour astonished and vexed her. </p>
<p>"Why, if he came only to be silent, grave, and indifferent," </p>
<p>said she, "did he come at all?" </p>
<p>She could settle it in no way that gave her pleasure. </p>
<p>"He could be still amiable, still pleasing, to my uncle and </p>
<p>aunt, when he was in town; and why not to me? If he fears </p>
<p>me, why come hither? If he no longer cares for me, why </p>
<p>silent? Teasing, teasing, man! I will think no more about </p>
<p>him." </p>
<p>Her resolution was for a short time involuntarily kept by </p>
<p>the approach of her sister, who joined her with a cheerful </p>
<p>look, which showed her better satisfied with their visitors, </p>
<p>than Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"Now," said she, "that this first meeting is over, I feel </p>
<p>perfectly easy. I know my own strength, and I shall never be </p>
<p>embarrassed again by his coming. I am glad he dines here on </p>
<p>Tuesday. It will then be publicly seen that, on both sides, we </p>
<p>meet only as common and indifferent acquaintance." </p>
<p>"Yes, very indifferent indeed," said Elizabeth, laughingly. </p>
<p>"Oh, Jane, take care." </p>
<p>"My dear Lizzy, you cannot think me so weak, as to be in </p>
<p>danger now?" </p>
<p>"I think you are in very great danger of making him as </p>
<p>much in love with you as ever." </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>They did not see the gentlemen again till Tuesday; and </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, in the meanwhile, was giving way to all the </p>
<p>happy schemes, which the good humour and common </p>
<p>politeness of Bingley, in half an hour's visit, had revived. </p>
<p>On Tuesday there was a large party assembled at </p>
<p>Longbourn; and the two who were most anxiously expected, </p>
<p>to the credit of their punctuality as sportsmen, were in very </p>
<p>good time. When they repaired to the dining-room, Elizabeth </p>
<p>eagerly watched to see whether Bingley would take the place, </p>
<p>which, in all their former parties, had belonged to him, by her </p>
<p>sister. Her prudent mother, occupied by the same ideas, </p>
<p>forbore to invite him to sit by herself. On entering the room, </p>
<p>he seemed to hesitate; but Jane happened to look round, and </p>
<p>happened to smile: it was decided. He placed himself by her. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, with a triumphant sensation, looked towards his </p>
<p>friend. He bore it with noble indifference, and she would have </p>
<p>imagined that Bingley had received his sanction to be happy, </p>
<p>had she not seen his eyes likewise turned towards Mr. Darcy, </p>
<p>with an expression of half-laughing alarm. </p>
<p>His behaviour to her sister was such, during dinner time, as </p>
<p>showed an admiration of her, which, though more guarded </p>
<p>than formerly, persuaded Elizabeth, that if left wholly to </p>
<p>himself, Jane's happiness, and his own, would be speedily </p>
<p>secured. Though she dared not depend upon the consequence, </p>
<p>she yet received pleasure from observing his behaviour. It </p>
<p>gave her all the animation that her spirits could boast; for she </p>
<p>was in no cheerful humour. Mr. Darcy was almost as far from </p>
<p>her as the table could divide them. He was on one side of her </p>
<p>mother. She knew how little such a situation would give </p>
<p>pleasure to either, or make either appear to advantage. She </p>
<p>was not near enough to hear any of their discourse, but she </p>
<p>could see how seldom they spoke to each other, and how </p>
<p>formal and cold was their manner whenever they did. Her </p>
<p>mother's ungraciousness, made the sense of what they owed </p>
<p>him more painful to Elizabeth's mind; and she would, at </p>
<p>times, have given anything to be privileged to tell him that his </p>
<p>kindness was neither unknown nor unfelt by the whole of the </p>
<p>family. </p>
<p>She was in hopes that the evening would afford some </p>
<p>opportunity of bringing them together; that the whole of the </p>
<p>visit would not pass away without enabling them to enter into </p>
<p>something more of conversation than the mere ceremonious </p>
<p>salutation attending his entrance. Anxious and uneasy, the </p>
<p>period which passed in the drawing-room, before the </p>
<p>gentlemen came, was wearisome and dull to a degree that </p>
<p>almost made her uncivil. She looked forward to their entrance </p>
<p>as the point on which all her chance of pleasure for the </p>
<p>evening must depend. </p>
<p>"If he does not come to me, THEN," said she, "I shall give </p>
<p>him up for ever." </p>
<p>The gentlemen came; and she thought he looked as if he </p>
<p>would have answered her hopes; but, alas! the ladies had </p>
<p>crowded round the table, where Miss Bennet was making tea, </p>
<p>and Elizabeth pouring out the coffee, in so close a </p>
<p>confederacy that there was not a single vacancy near her </p>
<p>which would admit of a chair. And on the gentlemen's </p>
<p>approaching, one of the girls moved closer to her than ever, </p>
<p>and said, in a whisper: </p>
<p>"The men shan't come and part us, I am determined. We </p>
<p>want none of them; do we?" </p>
<p>Darcy had walked away to another part of the room. She </p>
<p>followed him with her eyes, envied everyone to whom he </p>
<p>spoke, had scarcely patience enough to help anybody to </p>
<p>coffee; and then was enraged against herself for being so silly! </p>
<p>"A man who has once been refused! How could I ever be </p>
<p>foolish enough to expect a renewal of his love? Is there one </p>
<p>among the sex, who would not protest against such a </p>
<p>weakness as a second proposal to the same woman? There is </p>
<p>no indignity so abhorrent to their feelings!" </p>
<p>She was a little revived, however, by his bringing back his </p>
<p>coffee cup himself; and she seized the opportunity of saying: </p>
<p>"Is your sister at Pemberley still?" </p>
<p>"Yes, she will remain there till Christmas." </p>
<p>"And quite alone? Have all her friends left her?" </p>
<p>"Mrs. Annesley is with her. The others have been gone on </p>
<p>to Scarborough, these three weeks." </p>
<p>She could think of nothing more to say; but if he wished to </p>
<p>converse with her, he might have better success. He stood by </p>
<p>her, however, for some minutes, in silence; and, at last, on the </p>
<p>young lady's whispering to Elizabeth again, he walked away. </p>
<p>When the tea-things were removed, and the card-tables </p>
<p>placed, the ladies all rose, and Elizabeth was then hoping to </p>
<p>be soon joined by him, when all her views were overthrown </p>
<p>by seeing him fall a victim to her mother's rapacity for whist </p>
<p>players, and in a few moments after seated with the rest of the </p>
<p>party. She now lost every expectation of pleasure. They were </p>
<p>confined for the evening at different tables, and she had </p>
<p>nothing to hope, but that his eyes were so often turned </p>
<p>towards her side of the room, as to make him play as </p>
<p>unsuccessfully as herself. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet had designed to keep the two Netherfield </p>
<p>gentlemen to supper; but their carriage was unluckily ordered </p>
<p>before any of the others, and she had no opportunity of </p>
<p>detaining them. </p>
<p>"Well girls," said she, as soon as they were left to </p>
<p>themselves, "What say you to the day? I think every thing has </p>
<p>passed off uncommonly well, I assure you. The dinner was as </p>
<p>well dressed as any I ever saw. The venison was roasted to a </p>
<p>turn-and everybody said they never saw so fat a haunch. The </p>
<p>soup was fifty times better than what we had at the Lucases' </p>
<p>last week; and even Mr. Darcy acknowledged, that the </p>
<p>partridges were remarkably well done; and I suppose he has </p>
<p>two or three French cooks at least. And, my dear Jane, I never </p>
<p>saw you look in greater beauty. Mrs. Long said so too, for I </p>
<p>asked her whether you did not. And what do you think she </p>
<p>said besides? 'Ah! Mrs. Bennet, we shall have her at </p>
<p>Netherfield at last.' She did indeed. I do think Mrs. Long is as </p>
<p>good a creature as ever lived--and her nieces are very pretty </p>
<p>behaved girls, and not at all handsome: I like them </p>
<p>prodigiously." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, in short, was in very great spirits; she had </p>
<p>seen enough of Bingley's behaviour to Jane, to be convinced </p>
<p>that she would get him at last; and her expectations of </p>
<p>advantage to her family, when in a happy humour, were so far </p>
<p>beyond reason, that she was quite disappointed at not seeing </p>
<p>him there again the next day, to make his proposals. </p>
<p>"It has been a very agreeable day," said Miss Bennet to </p>
<p>Elizabeth. "The party seemed so well selected, so suitable one </p>
<p>with the other. I hope we may often meet again." </p>
<p>Elizabeth smiled. </p>
<p>"Lizzy, you must not do so. You must not suspect me. It </p>
<p>mortifies me. I assure you that I have now learnt to enjoy his </p>
<p>conversation as an agreeable and sensible young man, without </p>
<p>having a wish beyond it. I am perfectly satisfied, from what </p>
<p>his manners now are, that he never had any design of </p>
<p>engaging my affection. It is only that he is blessed with </p>
<p>greater sweetness of address, and a stronger desire of </p>
<p>generally pleasing, than any other man." </p>
<p>"You are very cruel," said her sister, "you will not let me </p>
<p>smile, and are provoking me to it every moment." </p>
<p>"How hard it is in some cases to be believed!" </p>
<p>"And how impossible in others!" </p>
<p>"But why should you wish to persuade me that I feel more </p>
<p>than I acknowledge?" </p>
<p>"That is a question which I hardly know how to answer. </p>
<p>We all love to instruct, though we can teach only what is not </p>
<p>worth knowing. Forgive me; and if you persist in indifference, </p>
<p>do not make me your confidante." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 55. </heading>
<p>A few days after this visit, Mr. Bingley called again, and </p>
<p>alone. His friend had left him that morning for London, but </p>
<p>was to return home in ten days time. He sat with them above </p>
<p>an hour, and was in remarkably good spirits. Mrs. Bennet </p>
<p>invited him to dine with them; but, with many expressions of </p>
<p>concern, he confessed himself engaged elsewhere. </p>
<p>"Next time you call," said she, "I hope we shall be more </p>
<p>lucky." </p>
<p>He should be particularly happy at any time, etc. etc.; and </p>
<p>if she would give him leave, would take an early opportunity </p>
<p>of waiting on them. </p>
<p>"Can you come to-morrow?" </p>
<p>Yes, he had no engagement at all for to-morrow; and her </p>
<p>invitation was accepted with alacrity. </p>
<p>He came, and in such very good time that the ladies were </p>
<p>none of them dressed. In ran Mrs. Bennet to her daughter's </p>
<p>room, in her dressing gown, and with her hair half finished, </p>
<p>crying out: </p>
<p>"My dear Jane, make haste and hurry down. He is come-- </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley is come. He is, indeed. Make haste, make haste. </p>
<p>Here, Sarah, come to Miss Bennet this moment, and help her </p>
<p>on with her gown. Never mind Miss Lizzy's hair." </p>
<p>"We will be down as soon as we can," said Jane; "but I </p>
<p>dare say Kitty is forwarder than either of us, for she went up </p>
<p>stairs half an hour ago." </p>
<p>"Oh! hang Kitty! what has she to do with it? Come be </p>
<p>quick, be quick! Where is your sash, my dear?" </p>
<p>But when her mother was gone, Jane would not be </p>
<p>prevailed on to go down without one of her sisters. </p>
<p>The same anxiety to get them by themselves was visible </p>
<p>again in the evening. After tea, Mr. Bennet retired to the </p>
<p>library, as was his custom, and Mary went up stairs to her </p>
<p>instrument. Two obstacles of the five being thus removed, </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet sat looking and winking at Elizabeth and </p>
<p>Catherine for a considerable time, without making any </p>
<p>impression on them. Elizabeth would not observe her; and </p>
<p>when at last Kitty did, she very innocently said, "What is the </p>
<p>matter mamma? What do you keep winking at me for? What </p>
<p>am I to do?" </p>
<p>"Nothing child, nothing. I did not wink at you." She then </p>
<p>sat still five minutes longer; but unable to waste such a </p>
<p>precious occasion, she suddenly got up, and saying to Kitty, </p>
<p>"Come here, my love, I want to speak to you," took her out of </p>
<p>the room. Jane instantly gave a look at Elizabeth which spoke </p>
<p>her distress at such premeditation, and her entreaty that SHE </p>
<p>would not give in to it. In a few minutes, Mrs. Bennet half- </p>
<p>opened the door and called out: </p>
<p>"Lizzy, my dear, I want to speak with you." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was forced to go. </p>
<p>"We may as well leave them by themselves you know;" </p>
<p>said her mother, as soon as she was in the hall. "Kitty and I </p>
<p>are going upstairs to sit in my dressing-room." </p>
<p>Elizabeth made no attempt to reason with her mother, but </p>
<p>remained quietly in the hall, till she and Kitty were out of </p>
<p>sight, then returned into the drawing-room. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet's schemes for this day were ineffectual. </p>
<p>Bingley was every thing that was charming, except the </p>
<p>professed lover of her daughter. His ease and cheerfulness </p>
<p>rendered him a most agreeable addition to their evening party; </p>
<p>and he bore with the ill-judged officiousness of the mother, </p>
<p>and heard all her silly remarks with a forbearance and </p>
<p>command of countenance particularly grateful to the daughter. </p>
<p>He scarcely needed an invitation to stay supper; and before </p>
<p>he went away, an engagement was formed, chiefly through his </p>
<p>own and Mrs. Bennet's means, for his coming next morning to </p>
<p>shoot with her husband. </p>
<p>After this day, Jane said no more of her indifference. Not a </p>
<p>word passed between the sisters concerning Bingley; but </p>
<p>Elizabeth went to bed in the happy belief that all must </p>
<p>speedily be concluded, unless Mr. Darcy returned within the </p>
<p>stated time. Seriously, however, she felt tolerably persuaded </p>
<p>that all this must have taken place with that gentleman's </p>
<p>concurrence. </p>
<p>Bingley was punctual to his appointment; and he and Mr. </p>
<p>Bennet spent the morning together, as had been agreed on. </p>
<p>The latter was much more agreeable than his companion </p>
<p>expected. There was nothing of presumption or folly in </p>
<p>Bingley that could provoke his ridicule, or disgust him into </p>
<p>silence; and he was more communicative, and less eccentric, </p>
<p>than the other had ever seen him. Bingley of course returned </p>
<p>with him to dinner; and in the evening Mrs. Bennet's </p>
<p>invention was again at work to get every body away from him </p>
<p>and her daughter. Elizabeth, who had a letter to write, went </p>
<p>into the breakfast room for that purpose soon after tea; for as </p>
<p>the others were all going to sit down to cards, she could not be </p>
<p>wanted to counteract her mother's schemes. </p>
<p>But on returning to the drawing-room, when her letter was </p>
<p>finished, she saw, to her infinite surprise, there was reason to </p>
<p>fear that her mother had been too ingenious for her. On </p>
<p>opening the door, she perceived her sister and Bingley </p>
<p>standing together over the hearth, as if engaged in earnest </p>
<p>conversation; and had this led to no suspicion, the faces of </p>
<p>both, as they hastily turned round and moved away from each </p>
<p>other, would have told it all. Their situation was awkward </p>
<p>enough; but HER'S she thought was still worse. Not a syllable </p>
<p>was uttered by either; and Elizabeth was on the point of going </p>
<p>away again, when Bingley, who as well as the other had sat </p>
<p>down, suddenly rose, and whispering a few words to her </p>
<p>sister, ran out of the room. </p>
<p>Jane could have no reserves from Elizabeth, where </p>
<p>confidence would give pleasure; and instantly embracing her, </p>
<p>acknowledged, with the liveliest emotion, that she was the </p>
<p>happiest creature in the world. </p>
<p>"'This too much!" she added, "by far too much. I do not </p>
<p>deserve it. Oh! why is not everybody as happy?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth's congratulations were given with a sincerity, a </p>
<p>warmth, a delight, which words could but poorly express. </p>
<p>Every sentence of kindness was a fresh source of happiness to </p>
<p>Jane. But she would not allow herself to stay with her sister, </p>
<p>or say half that remained to be said for the present. </p>
<p>"I must go instantly to my mother;" she cried. "I would not </p>
<p>on any account trifle with her affectionate solicitude; or allow </p>
<p>her to hear it from anyone but myself. He is gone to my father </p>
<p>already. Oh! Lizzy, to know that what I have to relate will </p>
<p>give such pleasure to all my dear family! how shall I bear so </p>
<p>much happiness!" </p>
<p>She then hastened away to her mother, who had purposely </p>
<p>broken up the card party, and was sitting up stairs with Kitty. </p>
<p>Elizabeth, who was left by herself, now smiled at the </p>
<p>rapidity and ease with which an affair was finally settled, that </p>
<p>had given them so many previous months of suspense and </p>
<p>vexation. </p>
<p>"And this," said she, "is the end of all his friend's anxious </p>
<p>circumspection! of all his sister's falsehood and contrivance! </p>
<p>the happiest, wisest, most reasonable end!" </p>
<p>In a few minutes she was joined by Bingley, whose </p>
<p>conference with her father had been short and to the purpose. </p>
<p>"Where is your sister?" said he hastily, as he opened the </p>
<p>door. </p>
<p>"With my mother up stairs. She will be down in a moment, </p>
<p>I dare say." </p>
<p>He then shut the door, and, coming up to her, claimed the </p>
<p>good wishes and affection of a sister. Elizabeth honestly and </p>
<p>heartily expressed her delight in the prospect of their relation- </p>
<p>ship. They shook hands with great cordiality; and then, till her </p>
<p>sister came down, she had to listen to all he had to say of his </p>
<p>own happiness, and of Jane's perfections; and in spite of his </p>
<p>being a lover, Elizabeth really believed all his expectations of </p>
<p>felicity to be rationally founded, because they had for basis </p>
<p>the excellent understanding, and super-excellent disposition of </p>
<p>Jane, and a general similarity of feeling and taste between her </p>
<p>and himself. </p>
<p>It was an evening of no common delight to them all; the </p>
<p>satisfaction of Miss Bennet's mind gave a glow of such sweet </p>
<p>animation to her face, as made her look handsomer than ever. </p>
<p>Kitty simpered and smiled, and hoped her turn was coming </p>
<p>soon. Mrs. Bennet could not give her consent or speak her </p>
<p>approbation in terms warm enough to satisfy her feelings, </p>
<p>though she talked to Bingley of nothing else for half an hour; </p>
<p>and when Mr. Bennet joined them at supper, his voice and </p>
<p>manner plainly showed how really happy he was. </p>
<p>Not a word, however, passed his lips in allusion to it, till </p>
<p>their visitor took his leave for the night; but as soon as he was </p>
<p>gone, he turned to his daughter, and said: </p>
<p>"Jane, I congratulate you. You will be a very happy </p>
<p>woman." </p>
<p>Jane went to him instantly, kissed him, and thanked him </p>
<p>for his goodness. </p>
<p>"You are a good girl;" he replied, "and I have great </p>
<p>pleasure in thinking you will be so happily settled. I have not </p>
<p>a doubt of your doing very well together. Your tempers are by </p>
<p>no means unlike. You are each of you so complying, that </p>
<p>nothing will ever be resolved on; so easy, that every servant </p>
<p>will cheat you; and so generous, that you will always exceed </p>
<p>your income." </p>
<p>"I hope not so. Imprudence or thoughtlessness in money </p>
<p>matters would be unpardonable in me." </p>
<p>"Exceed their income! My dear Mr. Bennet," cried his </p>
<p>wife, "what are you talking of? Why, he has four or five </p>
<p>thousand a year, and very likely more." Then addressing her </p>
<p>daughter, "Oh! my dear, dear Jane, I am so happy! I am sure I </p>
<p>shan't get a wink of sleep all night. I knew how it would be. I </p>
<p>always said it must be so, at last. I was sure you could not be </p>
<p>so beautiful for nothing! I remember, as soon as ever I saw </p>
<p>him, when he first came into Hertfordshire last year, I thought </p>
<p>how likely it was that you should come together. Oh! he is the </p>
<p>handsomest young man that ever was seen!" </p>
<p>Wickham, Lydia, were all forgotten. Jane was beyond </p>
<p>competition her favourite child. At that moment, she cared for </p>
<p>no other. Her younger sisters soon began to make interest with </p>
<p>her for objects of happiness which she might in future be able </p>
<p>to dispense. </p>
<p>Mary petitioned for the use of the library at Netherfield; </p>
<p>and Kitty begged very hard for a few balls there every winter. </p>
<p>Bingley, from this time, was of course a daily visitor at </p>
<p>Longbourn; coming frequently before breakfast, and always </p>
<p>remaining till after supper; unless when some barbarous </p>
<p>neighbour, who could not be enough detested, had given him </p>
<p>an invitation to dinner which he thought himself obliged to </p>
<p>accept. </p>
<p>Elizabeth had now but little time for conversation with her </p>
<p>sister; for while he was present, Jane had no attention to </p>
<p>bestow on anyone else; but she found herself considerably </p>
<p>useful to both of them in those hours of separation that must </p>
<p>sometimes occur. In the absence of Jane, he always attached </p>
<p>himself to Elizabeth, for the pleasure of talking of her; and </p>
<p>when Bingley was gone, Jane constantly sought the same </p>
<p>means of relief. </p>
<p>"He has made me so happy," said she, one evening, "by </p>
<p>telling me that he was totally ignorant of my being in town </p>
<p>last spring! I had not believed it possible." </p>
<p>"I suspected as much," replied Elizabeth. "But how did he </p>
<p>account for it?" </p>
<p>"It must have been his sister's doing. They were certainly </p>
<p>no friends to his acquaintance with me, which I cannot </p>
<p>wonder at, since he might have chosen so much more </p>
<p>advantageously in many respects. But when they see, as I trust </p>
<p>they will, that their brother is happy with me, they will learn </p>
<p>to be contented, and we shall be on good terms again; though </p>
<p>we can never be what we once were to each other." </p>
<p>"That is the most unforgiving speech," said Elizabeth, "that </p>
<p>I ever heard you utter. Good girl! It would vex me, indeed, to </p>
<p>see you again the dupe of Miss Bingley's pretended regard." </p>
<p>"Would you believe it, Lizzy, that when he went to town </p>
<p>last November, he really loved me, and nothing but a </p>
<p>persuasion of MY being indifferent would have prevented his </p>
<p>coming down again!" </p>
<p>"He made a little mistake to be sure; but it is to the credit </p>
<p>of his modesty." </p>
<p>This naturally introduced a panegyric from Jane on his </p>
<p>diffidence, and the little value he put on his own good </p>
<p>qualities. Elizabeth was pleased to find that he had not </p>
<p>betrayed the interference of his friend; for, though Jane had </p>
<p>the most generous and forgiving heart in the world, she knew </p>
<p>it was a circumstance which must prejudice her against him. </p>
<p>"I am certainly the most fortunate creature that ever </p>
<p>existed!" cried Jane. "Oh! Lizzy, why am I thus singled from </p>
<p>my family, and blessed above them all! If I could but see </p>
<p>YOU as happy! If there WERE but such another man for </p>
<p>you!" </p>
<p>"If you were to give me forty such men, I never could be </p>
<p>so happy as you. Till I have your disposition, your goodness, I </p>
<p>never can have your happiness. No, no, let me shift for </p>
<p>myself; and, perhaps, if I have very good luck, I may meet </p>
<p>with another Mr. Collins in time." </p>
<p>The situation of affairs in the Longbourn family could not </p>
<p>be long a secret. Mrs. Bennet was privileged to whisper it to </p>
<p>Mrs. Phillips, and she ventured, without any permission, to do </p>
<p>the same by all her neighbours in Meryton. </p>
<p>The Bennets were speedily pronounced to be the luckiest </p>
<p>family in the world, though only a few weeks before, when </p>
<p>Lydia had first run away, they had been generally proved to </p>
<p>be marked out for misfortune. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 56. </heading>
<p>One morning, about a week after Bingley's engagement </p>
<p>with Jane had been formed, as he and the females of the </p>
<p>family were sitting together in the dining-room, their attention </p>
<p>was suddenly drawn to the window, by the sound of a </p>
<p>carriage; and they perceived a chaise and four driving up the </p>
<p>lawn. It was too early in the morning for visitors, and besides, </p>
<p>the equipage did not answer to that of any of their neighbours. </p>
<p>The horses were post; and neither the carriage, nor the livery </p>
<p>of the servant who preceded it, were familiar to them. As it </p>
<p>was certain, however, that somebody was coming, Bingley </p>
<p>instantly prevailed on Miss Bennet to avoid the confinement </p>
<p>of such an intrusion, and walk away with him into the </p>
<p>shrubbery. They both set off, and the conjectures of the </p>
<p>remaining three continued, though with little satisfaction, till </p>
<p>the door was thrown open and their visitor entered. It was </p>
<p>Lady Catherine de Bourgh. </p>
<p>They were of course all intending to be surprised; but their </p>
<p>astonishment was beyond their expectation; and on the part of </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet and Kitty, though she was perfectly unknown to </p>
<p>them, even inferior to what Elizabeth felt. </p>
<p>She entered the room with an air more than usually </p>
<p>ungracious, made no other reply to Elizabeth's salutation than </p>
<p>a slight inclination of the head, and sat down without saying a </p>
<p>word. Elizabeth had mentioned her name to her mother on her </p>
<p>ladyship's entrance, though no request of introduction had </p>
<p>been made. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, all amazement, though flattered by having a </p>
<p>guest of such high importance, received her with the utmost </p>
<p>politeness. After sitting for a moment in silence, she said very </p>
<p>stiffly to Elizabeth, "I hope you are well, Miss Bennet. That </p>
<p>lady, I suppose, is your mother." </p>
<p>Elizabeth replied very concisely that she was. </p>
<p>"And THAT I suppose is one of your sisters." </p>
<p>"Yes, madam," said Mrs. Bennet, delighted to speak to a </p>
<p>Lady Catherine. "She is my youngest girl but one. My </p>
<p>youngest of all is lately married, and my eldest is somewhere </p>
<p>about the grounds, walking with a young man who, I believe, </p>
<p>will soon become a part of the family." </p>
<p>"You have a very small park here," returned Lady </p>
<p>Catherine after a short silence. </p>
<p>"It is nothing in comparison of Rosings, my lady, I dare </p>
<p>say; but I assure you it is much larger than Sir William </p>
<p>Lucas's." </p>
<p>"This must be a most inconvenient sitting room for the </p>
<p>evening, in summer; the windows are full west." </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet assured her that they never sat there after </p>
<p>dinner, and then added: </p>
<p>"May I take the liberty of asking your ladyship whether </p>
<p>you left Mr. and Mrs. Collins well." </p>
<p>"Yes, very well. I saw them the night before last." </p>
<p>Elizabeth now expected that she would produce a letter for </p>
<p>her from Charlotte, as it seemed the only probable motive for </p>
<p>her calling. But no letter appeared, and she was completely </p>
<p>puzzled. </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet, with great civility, begged her ladyship to </p>
<p>take some refreshment; but Lady Catherine very resolutely, </p>
<p>and not very politely, declined eating anything; and then, </p>
<p>rising up, said to Elizabeth, </p>
<p>"Miss Bennet, there seemed to be a prettyish kind of a little </p>
<p>wilderness on one side of your lawn. I should be glad to take a </p>
<p>turn in it, if you will favour me with your company." </p>
<p>"Go, my dear," cried her mother, "and show her ladyship </p>
<p>about the different walks. I think she will be pleased with the </p>
<p>hermitage." </p>
<p>Elizabeth obeyed, and running into her own room for her </p>
<p>parasol, attended her noble guest downstairs. As they passed </p>
<p>through the hall, Lady Catherine opened the doors into the </p>
<p>dining-parlour and drawing-room, and pronouncing them, </p>
<p>after a short survey, to be decent looking rooms, walked on. </p>
<p>Her carriage remained at the door, and Elizabeth saw that </p>
<p>her waiting-woman was in it. They proceeded in silence along </p>
<p>the gravel walk that led to the copse; Elizabeth was </p>
<p>determined to make no effort for conversation with a woman </p>
<p>who was now more than usually insolent and disagreeable. </p>
<p>"How could I ever think her like her nephew?" said she, as </p>
<p>she looked in her face. </p>
<p>As soon as they entered the copse, Lady Catherine began </p>
<p>in the following manner:- </p>
<p>"You can be at no loss, Miss Bennet, to understand the </p>
<p>reason of my journey hither. Your own heart, your own </p>
<p>conscience, must tell you why I come." </p>
<p>Elizabeth looked with unaffected astonishment. </p>
<p>"Indeed, you are mistaken, Madam. I have not been at all </p>
<p>able to account for the honour of seeing you here." </p>
<p>"Miss Bennet," replied her ladyship, in an angry tone, "you </p>
<p>ought to know, that I am not to be trifled with. But however </p>
<p>insincere YOU may choose to be, you shall not find ME so. </p>
<p>My character has ever been celebrated for its sincerity and </p>
<p>frankness, and in a cause of such moment as this, I shall </p>
<p>certainly not depart from it. A report of a most alarming </p>
<p>nature reached me two days ago. I was told that not only your </p>
<p>sister was on the point of being most advantageously married, </p>
<p>but that you, that Miss Elizabeth Bennet, would, in all </p>
<p>likelihood, be soon afterwards united to my nephew, my own </p>
<p>nephew, Mr. Darcy. Though I KNOW it must be a scandalous </p>
<p>falsehood, though I would not injure him so much as to </p>
<p>suppose the truth of it possible, I instantly resolved on setting </p>
<p>off for this place, that I might make my sentiments known to </p>
<p>you." </p>
<p>"If you believed it impossible to be true," said Elizabeth, </p>
<p>colouring with astonishment and disdain, "I wonder you took </p>
<p>the trouble of coming so far. What could your ladyship </p>
<p>propose by it?" </p>
<p>"At once to insist upon having such a report universally </p>
<p>contradicted." </p>
<p>"Your coming to Longbourn, to see me and my family," </p>
<p>said Elizabeth coolly, "will be rather a confirmation of it; if, </p>
<p>indeed, such a report is in existence." </p>
<p>"If! Do you then pretend to be ignorant of it? Has it not </p>
<p>been industriously circulated by yourselves? Do you not know </p>
<p>that such a report is spread abroad?" </p>
<p>"I never heard that it was." </p>
<p>"And can you likewise declare, that there is no foundation </p>
<p>for it?" </p>
<p>"I do not pretend to possess equal frankness with your </p>
<p>ladyship. You may ask questions which I shall not choose to </p>
<p>answer." </p>
<p>"This is not to be borne. Miss Bennet, I insist on being </p>
<p>satisfied. Has he, has my nephew, made you an offer of </p>
<p>marriage?" </p>
<p>"Your ladyship has declared it to be impossible." </p>
<p>"It ought to be so; it must be so, while he retains the use of </p>
<p>his reason. But your arts and allurements may, in a moment of </p>
<p>infatuation, have made him forget what he owes to himself </p>
<p>and to all his family. You may have drawn him in." </p>
<p>"If I have, I shall be the last person to confess it." </p>
<p>"Miss Bennet, do you know who I am? I have not been </p>
<p>accustomed to such language as this. I am almost the nearest </p>
<p>relation he has in the world, and am entitled to know all his </p>
<p>dearest concerns." </p>
<p>"But you are not entitled to know mine; nor will such </p>
<p>behaviour as this, ever induce me to be explicit." </p>
<p>"Let me be rightly understood. This match, to which you </p>
<p>have the presumption to aspire, can never take place. No, </p>
<p>never. Mr. Darcy is engaged to my daughter. Now what have </p>
<p>you to say?" </p>
<p>"Only this; that if he is so, you can have no reason to </p>
<p>suppose he will make an offer to me." </p>
<p>Lady Catherine hesitated for a moment, and then replied: </p>
<p>"The engagement between them is of a peculiar kind. From </p>
<p>their infancy, they have been intended for each other. It was </p>
<p>the favourite wish of HIS mother, as well as of her's. While in </p>
<p>their cradles, we planned the union: and now, at the moment </p>
<p>when the wishes of both sisters would be accomplished in </p>
<p>their marriage, to be prevented by a young woman of inferior </p>
<p>birth, of no importance in the world, and wholly unallied to </p>
<p>the family! Do you pay no regard to the wishes of his friends? </p>
<p>To his tacit engagement with Miss de Bourgh? Are you lost to </p>
<p>every feeling of propriety and delicacy? Have you not heard </p>
<p>me say that from his earliest hours he was destined for his </p>
<p>cousin?" </p>
<p>"Yes, and I had heard it before. But what is that to me? If </p>
<p>there is no other objection to my marrying your nephew, I </p>
<p>shall certainly not be kept from it by knowing that his mother </p>
<p>and aunt wished him to marry Miss de Bourgh. You both did </p>
<p>as much as you could in planning the marriage. Its completion </p>
<p>depended on others. If Mr. Darcy is neither by honour nor </p>
<p>inclination confined to his cousin, why is not he to make </p>
<p>another choice? And if I am that choice, why may not I accept </p>
<p>him?" </p>
<p>"Because honour, decorum, prudence, nay, interest, forbid </p>
<p>it. Yes, Miss Bennet, interest; for do not expect to be noticed </p>
<p>by his family or friends, if you wilfully act against the </p>
<p>inclinations of all. You will be censured, slighted, and </p>
<p>despised, by everyone connected with him. Your alliance will </p>
<p>be a disgrace; your name will never even be mentioned by any </p>
<p>of us." </p>
<p>"These are heavy misfortunes," replied Elizabeth. "But the </p>
<p>wife of Mr. Darcy must have such extraordinary sources of </p>
<p>happiness necessarily attached to her situation, that she could, </p>
<p>upon the whole, have no cause to repine." </p>
<p>"Obstinate, headstrong girl! I am ashamed of you! Is this </p>
<p>your gratitude for my attentions to you last spring? Is nothing </p>
<p>due to me on that score? Let us sit down. You are to </p>
<p>understand, Miss Bennet, that I came </p>
<p>here with the </p>
<p>determined resolution of carrying my purpose; nor will I be </p>
<p>dissuaded from it. I have not been used to submit to any </p>
<p>person's whims. I have not been in the habit of brooking </p>
<p>disappointment." </p>
<p>"THAT will make your ladyship's situation at present more </p>
<p>pitiable; but it will have no effect on me." </p>
<p>"I will not be interrupted. Hear me in silence. My daughter </p>
<p>and my nephew are formed for each other. They are </p>
<p>descended, on the maternal side, from the same noble line; </p>
<p>and, on the </p>
<p>father's, from respectable, honourable, and </p>
<p>ancient--though untitled--families. Their fortune on both sides </p>
<p>is splendid. They are destined for each other by the voice of </p>
<p>every member of their respective houses; and what is to divide </p>
<p>them? The upstart pretensions of a young woman without </p>
<p>family, connections, or fortune. Is this to be endured! But it </p>
<p>must not, shall not be. If you were sensible of your own good, </p>
<p>you would not wish to quit the sphere in which you have been </p>
<p>brought up." </p>
<p>"In marrying your nephew, I should not consider myself as </p>
<p>quitting that sphere. He is a gentleman; I am a gentleman's </p>
<p>daughter; so far we are equal." </p>
<p>"True. You ARE a gentleman's daughter. But who was </p>
<p>your mother? Who are your uncles and aunts? Do not imagine </p>
<p>me ignorant of their condition." </p>
<p>"Whatever my connections may be," said Elizabeth, "if </p>
