<p>your nephew does not object to them, they can be nothing to </p>
<p>YOU." </p>
<p>"Tell me once for all, are you engaged to him?" </p>
<p>Though Elizabeth would not, for the mere purpose of </p>
<p>obliging Lady Catherine, have answered this question, she </p>
<p>could not but say, after a moment's deliberation: </p>
<p>"I am not." </p>
<p>Lady Catherine seemed pleased. </p>
<p>"And will you promise me, never to enter into such an </p>
<p>engagement?" </p>
<p>"I will make no promise of the kind." </p>
<p>"Miss Bennet I am shocked and astonished. I expected to </p>
<p>find a more reasonable young woman. But do not deceive </p>
<p>your self into a belief that I will ever recede. I shall not go </p>
<p>away till you have given me the assurance I require." </p>
<p>"And I certainly NEVER shall give it. I am not to be </p>
<p>intimidated into anything so wholly unreasonable. Your </p>
<p>ladyship wants Mr. Darcy to marry your daughter; but would </p>
<p>my giving you the wished-for promise make their marriage at </p>
<p>all more probable? Supposing him to be attached to me, </p>
<p>would my refusing to accept his hand make him wish to </p>
<p>bestow it on his cousin? Allow me to say, Lady Catherine, </p>
<p>that the arguments with which you have supported this </p>
<p>extraordinary application have been as frivolous as the </p>
<p>application was ill-judged. You have widely mistaken my </p>
<p>character, if you think I can be worked on by such persuasions </p>
<p>as these. How far your nephew might approve of your </p>
<p>interference in his affairs, I cannot tell; but you have certainly </p>
<p>no right to concern yourself in mine. I must beg, therefore, to </p>
<p>be importuned no farther on the subject." </p>
<p>"Not so hasty, if you please. I have by no means done. To </p>
<p>all the objections I have already urged, I have still another to </p>
<p>add. I am no stranger to the particulars of your youngest </p>
<p>sister's infamous elopement. I know it all; that the young </p>
<p>man's marrying her was a patched-up business, at the expence </p>
<p>of your father and uncles. And is such a girl to be my </p>
<p>nephew's sister? Is her husband, is the son of his late father's </p>
<p>steward, to be his brother? Heaven and earth!--of what are </p>
<p>you thinking? Are the shades of Pemberley to be thus </p>
<p>polluted?" </p>
<p>"You can now have nothing further to say," she resentfully </p>
<p>answered. "You have insulted me in every possible method. I </p>
<p>must beg to return to the house." </p>
<p>And she rose as she spoke. Lady Catherine rose also, and </p>
<p>they turned back. Her ladyship was highly incensed. </p>
<p>"You have no regard, then, for the honour and credit of my </p>
<p>nephew! Unfeeling, selfish girl! Do you not consider that a </p>
<p>connection with you must disgrace him in the eyes of </p>
<p>everybody?" </p>
<p>"Lady Catherine, I have nothing further to say. You know </p>
<p>my sentiments." </p>
<p>"You are then resolved to have him?" </p>
<p>"I have said no such thing. I am only resolved to act in that </p>
<p>manner, which will, in my own opinion, constitute my </p>
<p>happiness, without reference to YOU, or to any person so </p>
<p>wholly unconnected with me." </p>
<p>"It is well. You refuse, then, to oblige me. You refuse to </p>
<p>obey the claims of duty, honour, and gratitude. You are </p>
<p>determined to ruin him in the opinion of all his friends, and </p>
<p>make him the contempt of the world." </p>
<p>"Neither duty, nor honour, </p>
<p>nor </p>
<p>gratitude," replied </p>
<p>Elizabeth, "have any possible claim on me, in the present </p>
<p>instance. No principle of either would be violated by my </p>
<p>marriage with Mr. Darcy. And with regard to the resentment </p>
<p>of his family, or the indignation of the world, if the former </p>
<p>WERE excited by his marrying me, it would not give me one </p>
<p>moment's concern -and the world in general would have too </p>
<p>much sense to join in the scorn." </p>
<p>"And this is your real opinion! This is your final resolve! </p>
<p>Very well. I shall now know how to act. Do not imagine, Miss </p>
<p>Bennet, that your ambition will ever be gratified. I came to try </p>
<p>you. I hoped to find you reasonable; but, depend upon it, I </p>
<p>will carry my point." </p>
<p>In this manner Lady Catherine talked on, till they were at </p>
<p>the door of the carriage, when, turning hastily round, she </p>
<p>added, "I take no leave of you, Miss Bennet. I send no </p>
<p>compliments to your mother. You deserve no such attention. I </p>
<p>am most seriously displeased." </p>
<p>Elizabeth made no answer; and without attempting to </p>
<p>persuade her ladyship to return into the house, walked quietly </p>
<p>into it herself. She heard the carriage drive away as she </p>
<p>proceeded up stairs. Her mother impatiently met her at the </p>
<p>door of the dressing-room, to ask why Lady Catherine would </p>
<p>not come in again and rest herself. </p>
<p>"She did not choose it," said her daughter, "she would go." </p>
<p>"She is a very fine-looking woman! and her calling here </p>
<p>was prodigiously civil! for she only came, I suppose, to tell us </p>
<p>the Collinses were well. She is on her road somewhere, I dare </p>
<p>say, and so, passing through Meryton, thought she might as </p>
<p>well call on you. I suppose she had nothing particular to say to </p>
<p>you, Lizzy?" </p>
<p>Elizabeth was forced to give into a little falsehood here; </p>
<p>for to acknowledge the substance of their conversation was </p>
<p>impossible. </p>
<heading>Chapter 57. </heading>
<p>The discomposure of spirits which this extraordinary visit </p>
<p>threw Elizabeth into, could not be easily overcome; nor could </p>
<p>she, for many hours, learn to think of it less than incessantly. </p>
<p>Lady Catherine, it appeared, had actually taken the trouble of </p>
<p>this journey from Rosings, for the sole purpose of breaking </p>
<p>off her supposed engagement with Mr. Darcy. It was a </p>
<p>rational scheme, to be sure! but from what the report of their </p>
<p>engagement could originate, Elizabeth was at a loss to </p>
<p>imagine; till she recollected that HIS being the intimate friend </p>
<p>of Bingley, and HER being the sister of Jane, was enough, at a </p>
<p>time when the expectation of one wedding made everybody </p>
<p>eager for another, to supply the idea. She had not herself </p>
<p>forgotten to feel that the marriage of her sister must bring </p>
<p>them more frequently together. And her neighbours at Lucas </p>
<p>Lodge, therefore (for through their communication with the </p>
<p>Collinses, the report, she concluded, had reached lady </p>
<p>Catherine), had only set that down as almost certain and </p>
<p>immediate, which she had looked forward to as possible at </p>
<p>some future time. </p>
<p>In revolving Lady Catherine's expressions, however, she </p>
<p>could not help feeling some uneasiness as to the possible </p>
<p>consequence of her persisting in this interference. From what </p>
<p>she had said of her resolution to prevent their marriage, it </p>
<p>occurred to Elizabeth that she must meditate an application to </p>
<p>her nephew; and how HE might take a similar representation </p>
<p>of the evils attached to a connection with her, she dared not </p>
<p>pronounce. She knew not the exact degree of his affection for </p>
<p>his aunt, or his dependence on her judgment, but it was </p>
<p>natural to suppose that he thought much higher of her </p>
<p>ladyship than SHE could do; and it was certain that, in </p>
<p>enumerating the miseries of a marriage with ONE, whose </p>
<p>immediate connections were so unequal to his own, his aunt </p>
<p>would address him on his weakest side. With his notions of </p>
<p>dignity, he would probably feel that the arguments, which to </p>
<p>Elizabeth had appeared weak and ridiculous, contained much </p>
<p>good sense and solid reasoning. </p>
<p>If he had been wavering before as to what he should do, </p>
<p>which had often seemed likely, the advice and entreaty of so </p>
<p>near a relation might settle every doubt, and determine him at </p>
<p>once to be as happy as dignity unblemished could make him. </p>
<p>In that case he would return no more. Lady Catherine might </p>
<p>see him in her way through town; and his engagement to </p>
<p>Bingley of coming again to Netherfield must give way. </p>
<p>"If, therefore, an excuse for not keeping his promise </p>
<p>should come to his friend within a few days," she added, "I </p>
<p>shall know how to understand it. I shall then give over every </p>
<p>expectation, every wish of his constancy. If he is satisfied </p>
<p>with only regretting me, when he might have obtained my </p>
<p>affections and hand, I shall soon cease to regret him at all." </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>The surprise of the rest of the family, on hearing who their </p>
<p>visitor had been, was very great; but they obligingly satisfied </p>
<p>it, with the same kind of supposition which had appeased Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet's curiosity; and Elizabeth was spared from much </p>
<p>teasing on the subject. </p>
<p>The next morning, as she was going downstairs, she was </p>
<p>met by her father, who came out of his library with a letter in </p>
<p>his hand. </p>
<p>"Lizzy," said he, "I was going to look for you; come into </p>
<p>my room." </p>
<p>She followed him thither; and her curiosity to know what </p>
<p>he had to tell her was heightened by the supposition of its </p>
<p>being in some manner connected with the letter he held. It </p>
<p>suddenly struck her that it might be from Lady Catherine; and </p>
<p>she anticipated with dismay all the consequent explanations. </p>
<p>She followed her father to the fire place, and they both sat </p>
<p>down. He then said, </p>
<p>"I have received a letter this morning that has astonished </p>
<p>me exceedingly. As it principally concerns yourself, you </p>
<p>ought to know its contents. I did not know before, that I had </p>
<p>two daughters on the brink of matrimony. Let me congratulate </p>
<p>you on a very important conquest." </p>
<p>The colour now rushed into Elizabeth's cheeks in the </p>
<p>instantaneous conviction of its being a letter from the nephew, </p>
<p>instead of the aunt; and she was undetermined whether most </p>
<p>to be pleased that he explained himself at all, or offended that </p>
<p>his letter was not rather addressed to herself; when her father </p>
<p>continued: </p>
<p>"You look conscious. Young ladies have great penetration </p>
<p>in such matters as these; but I think I may defy even YOUR </p>
<p>sagacity, to discover the name of your admirer. This letter is </p>
<p>from Mr. Collins." </p>
<p>"From Mr. Collins! and what can HE have to say?" </p>
<p>"Something very much to the purpose of course. He begins </p>
<p>with congratulations on the approaching nuptials of my eldest </p>
<p>daughter, of which, it seems, he has been told by some of the </p>
<p>good-natured, gossiping Lucases. I shall not sport with your </p>
<p>impatience, by reading what he says on that point. What </p>
<p>relates to yourself, is as follows: 'Having thus offered you the </p>
<p>sincere congratulations of Mrs. Collins and myself on this </p>
<p>happy event, let me now add a short hint on the subject of </p>
<p>another; of which we have been advertised by the same </p>
<p>authority. Your daughter Elizabeth, it is presumed, will not </p>
<p>long bear the name of Bennet, after her elder sister has </p>
<p>resigned it, and the chosen partner of her fate may be </p>
<p>reasonably looked up to as one of the most illustrious </p>
<p>personages in this land.' </p>
<p>"Can you possibly guess, Lizzy, who is meant by this?" </p>
<p>'This young gentleman is blessed, in a peculiar way, with </p>
<p>every thing the heart of mortal can most desire,--splendid </p>
<p>property, noble kindred, and extensive patronage. Yet in spite </p>
<p>of all these temptations, let me warn my cousin Elizabeth, and </p>
<p>yourself, of what evils you may incur by a precipitate closure </p>
<p>with this gentleman's proposals, which, of course, you will be </p>
<p>inclined to take immediate advantage of.' </p>
<p>"Have you any idea, Lizzy, who this gentleman is? But </p>
<p>now it comes out: </p>
<p>"'My motive for cautioning you is as follows. We have </p>
<p>reason to imagine that his aunt, Lady Catherine de Bourgh, </p>
<p>does not look on the match with a friendly eye.' </p>
<p>"MR. DARCY, you see, is the man! Now, Lizzy, I think I </p>
<p>HAVE surprised you. Could he, or the Lucases, have pitched </p>
<p>on any man within the circle of our acquaintance, whose name </p>
<p>would have given the lie more effectually to what they </p>
<p>related? Mr. Darcy, who never looks at any woman but to see </p>
<p>a blemish, and who probably never looked at you in his life! It </p>
<p>is admirable!" </p>
<p>Elizabeth tried to join in her father's pleasantry, but could </p>
<p>only force one most reluctant smile. Never had his wit been </p>
<p>directed in a manner so little agreeable to her. </p>
<p>"Are you not diverted?" </p>
<p>"Oh! yes. Pray read on." </p>
<p>"'After mentioning the likelihood of this marriage to her </p>
<p>ladyship last night, </p>
<p>she immediately, with her usual </p>
<p>condescension, expressed what she felt on the occasion; when </p>
<p>it become apparent, that on the score of some family </p>
<p>objections on the part of my cousin, she would never give her </p>
<p>consent to what she termed so disgraceful a match. I thought it </p>
<p>my duty to give the speediest intelligence of this to my </p>
<p>cousin, that she and her noble admirer may be aware of what </p>
<p>they are about, and not run hastily into a marriage which has </p>
<p>not been properly sanctioned.' Mr. Collins moreover adds, 'I </p>
<p>am truly rejoiced that my cousin Lydia's sad business has </p>
<p>been so well hushed up, and am only concerned that their </p>
<p>living together before the marriage took place should be so </p>
<p>generally known. I must not, however, neglect the duties of </p>
<p>my station, or refrain from declaring my amazement at </p>
<p>hearing that you received the young couple into your house as </p>
<p>soon as they were married. It was an encouragement of vice; </p>
<p>and had I been the rector of Longbourn, I should very </p>
<p>strenuously have opposed it. You ought certainly to forgive </p>
<p>them, as a Christian, but never to admit them in your sight, or </p>
<p>allow their names to be mentioned in your hearing.' That is his </p>
<p>notion of Christian forgiveness! The rest of his letter is only </p>
<p>about his dear Charlotte's situation, and his expectation of a </p>
<p>young olive-branch. But, Lizzy, you look as if you did not </p>
<p>enjoy it. You are not going to be MISSISH, I hope, and </p>
<p>pretend to be affronted at an idle report. For what do we live, </p>
<p>but to make sport for our neighbours, and laugh at them in our </p>
<p>turn?" </p>
<p>"Oh!" cried Elizabeth, "I am excessively diverted. But it is </p>
<p>so strange!" </p>
<p>"Yes--THAT is what makes it amusing. Had they fixed on </p>
<p>any other man it would have been nothing; but HIS perfect </p>
<p>indifference, and YOUR pointed dislike, make </p>
<p>it so </p>
<p>delightfully absurd! Much as I abominate writing, I would not </p>
<p>give up Mr. Collins's correspondence for any consideration. </p>
<p>Nay, when I read a letter of his, I cannot help giving him the </p>
<p>preference even over Wickham, much as I value the </p>
<p>impudence and hypocrisy of my son-in-law. And pray, Lizzy, </p>
<p>what said Lady Catherine about this report? Did she call to </p>
<p>refuse her consent?" </p>
<p>To this question his daughter replied only with a laugh; </p>
<p>and as it had been asked without the least suspicion, she was </p>
<p>not distressed by his repeating it. Elizabeth had never been </p>
<p>more at a loss to make her feelings appear what they were not. </p>
<p>It was necessary to laugh, when she would rather have cried. </p>
<p>Her father had most cruelly mortified her, by what he said of </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's indifference, and she could do nothing but </p>
<p>wonder at such a want of penetration, or fear that perhaps, </p>
<p>instead of his seeing too little, she might have fancied too </p>
<p>much. </p>
<heading>Chapter 58. </heading>
<p>Instead of receiving any such letter of excuse from his </p>
<p>friend, as Elizabeth half expected Mr. Bingley to do, he was </p>
<p>able to bring Darcy with him to Longbourn before many days </p>
<p>had passed after Lady Catherine's visit. The gentlemen arrived </p>
<p>early; and, before Mrs. Bennet had time to tell him of their </p>
<p>having seen his aunt, of which her daughter sat in momentary </p>
<p>dread, Bingley, who wanted to be alone with Jane, proposed </p>
<p>their all walking out. It was agreed to. Mrs. Bennet was not in </p>
<p>the habit of walking; Mary could never spare time; but the </p>
<p>remaining five set off together. Bingley and Jane, however, </p>
<p>soon allowed the others to outstrip them. They lagged behind, </p>
<p>while Elizabeth, Kitty, and Darcy were to entertain each other. </p>
<p>Very little was said by either; Kitty was too much afraid of </p>
<p>him to talk; Elizabeth was secretly forming a desperate </p>
<p>resolution; and perhaps he might be doing the same. </p>
<p>They walked towards the Lucases, because Kitty wished to </p>
<p>call upon Maria; and as Elizabeth saw no occasion for making </p>
<p>it a general concern, when Kitty left them she went boldly on </p>
<p>with him alone. Now was the moment for her resolution to be </p>
<p>executed, and, while her courage was high, she immediately </p>
<p>said: </p>
<p>"Mr. Darcy, I am a very selfish creature; and, for the sake </p>
<p>of giving relief to my own feelings, care not how much I may </p>
<p>be wounding your's. I can no longer help thanking you for </p>
<p>your unexampled kindness to my poor sister. Ever since I </p>
<p>have known it, I have been most anxious to acknowledge to </p>
<p>you how gratefully I feel it. Were it known to the rest of my </p>
<p>family, I should not have merely my own gratitude </p>
<p>to </p>
<p>express." </p>
<p>"I am sorry, exceedingly sorry," replied Darcy, in a tone of </p>
<p>surprise and emotion, "that you have ever been informed of </p>
<p>what may, in a mistaken light, have given you uneasiness. I </p>
<p>did not think Mrs. Gardiner was so little to be trusted." </p>
<p>"You must not blame my aunt. Lydia's thoughtlessness </p>
<p>first betrayed to me that you had been concerned in the </p>
<p>matter; and, of course, I could not rest till I knew the </p>
<p>particulars. Let me thank you again and again, in the name of </p>
<p>all my family, for that generous compassion which induced </p>
<p>you to take so much trouble, and bear so many mortifications, </p>
<p>for the sake of discovering them." </p>
<p>"If you WILL thank me," he replied, "let it be for yourself </p>
<p>alone. That the wish of giving happiness to you might add </p>
<p>force to the other inducements which led me on, I shall not </p>
<p>attempt to deny. But your FAMILY owe me nothing. Much as </p>
<p>I respect them, I believe I thought only of YOU." </p>
<p>Elizabeth was too much embarrassed to say a word. After a </p>
<p>short pause, her companion added, "You are too generous to </p>
<p>trifle with me. If your feelings are still what they were last </p>
<p>April, tell me so at once. MY affections and wishes are </p>
<p>unchanged, but one word from you will silence me on this </p>
<p>subject for ever." </p>
<p>Elizabeth, feeling all the more than common awkwardness </p>
<p>and anxiety of his situation, now forced herself to speak; and </p>
<p>immediately, </p>
<p>though </p>
<p>not very fluently, gave him to </p>
<p>understand that her sentiments had undergone so material a </p>
<p>change, since the period to which he alluded, as to make her </p>
<p>receive with gratitude and pleasure his present assurances. </p>
<p>The happiness which this reply produced, was such as he had </p>
<p>probably never felt before; and he expressed himself on the </p>
<p>occasion as sensibly and as warmly as a man violently in love </p>
<p>can be supposed to do. Had Elizabeth been able to encounter </p>
<p>his eye, she might have seen how well the expression of </p>
<p>heartfelt delight, diffused over his face, became him; but, </p>
<p>though she could not look, she could listen, and he told her of </p>
<p>feelings, which, in proving of what importance she was to </p>
<p>him, made his affection every moment more valuable. </p>
<p>They walked on, without knowing in what direction. There </p>
<p>was too much to be thought, and felt, and said, for attention to </p>
<p>any other objects. She soon learnt that they were indebted for </p>
<p>their present good understanding to the efforts of his aunt, </p>
<p>who did call on him in her return through London, and there </p>
<p>relate her journey to Longbourn, its motive, and the substance </p>
<p>of her conversation with Elizabeth; dwelling emphatically on </p>
<p>every expression of the latter which, in her ladyship's </p>
<p>apprehension, peculiarly denoted her perverseness and </p>
<p>assurance; in the belief that such a relation must assist her </p>
<p>endeavours to obtain that promise from her nephew which she </p>
<p>had refused to give. But, unluckily for her ladyship, its effect </p>
<p>had been exactly contrariwise. </p>
<p>"It taught me to hope," said he, "as I had scarcely ever al </p>
<p>lowed myself to hope before. I knew enough of your </p>
<p>disposition to be certain that, had you been absolutely, </p>
<p>irrevocably decided against me, you would have </p>
<p>acknowledged it to Lady Catherine, frankly and openly." </p>
<p>Elizabeth coloured and laughed as she replied, "Yes, you </p>
<p>know enough of my frankness to believe me capable of </p>
<p>THAT. After abusing you so abominably to your face, I could </p>
<p>have no scruple in abusing you to all your relations." </p>
<p>"What did you say of me, that I did not deserve? For, </p>
<p>though </p>
<p>your </p>
<p>accusations were ill-founded, formed on </p>
<p>mistaken premises, my behaviour to you at the time had </p>
<p>merited the severest reproof. It was unpardonable. I cannot </p>
<p>think of it without abhorrence." </p>
<p>"We will not quarrel for the greater share of blame </p>
<p>annexed to that evening," said Elizabeth. "The conduct of </p>
<p>neither, if strictly examined, will be irreproachable; but since </p>
<p>then, we have both, I hope, improved in civility." </p>
<p>"I cannot be so easily reconciled to myself. The </p>
<p>recollection of what I then said, of my conduct, my manners, </p>
<p>my expressions during the whole of it, is now, and has been </p>
<p>many months, inexpressibly painful to me. Your reproof, so </p>
<p>well applied, I shall never forget: 'had you behaved in a more </p>
<p>gentlemanlike manner.' Those were your words. You know </p>
<p>not, you can scarcely conceive, how they have tortured me;-- </p>
<p>though it was some time, I confess, before I was reasonable </p>
<p>enough to allow their justice." </p>
<p>"I was certainly very far from expecting them to make so </p>
<p>strong an impression. I had not the smallest idea of their being </p>
<p>ever felt in such a way." </p>
<p>"I can easily believe it. You thought me then devoid of </p>
<p>every proper feeling, I am sure you did. The turn of your </p>
<p>countenance I shall never forget, as you said that I could not </p>
<p>have addressed you in any possible way that would induce </p>
<p>you to accept me." </p>
<p>"Oh! do not repeat what I then said. These recollections </p>
<p>will not do at all. I assure you that I have long been most </p>
<p>heartily ashamed of it." </p>
<p>Darcy mentioned his letter. "Did it," said he, "did it soon </p>
<p>make you think better of me? Did you, on reading it, give any </p>
<p>credit to its contents?" </p>
<p>She explained what its effect on her had been, and how </p>
<p>gradually all her former prejudices had been removed. </p>
<p>"I knew," said he, "that what I wrote must give you pain, </p>
<p>but it was necessary. I hope you have destroyed the letter. </p>
<p>There was one part especially, the opening of it, which I </p>
<p>should dread your having the power of reading again. I can </p>
<p>remember some expressions which might justly make you </p>
<p>hate me." </p>
<p>"The letter shall certainly be burnt, if you believe it </p>
<p>essential to the preservation of my regard; but, though we </p>
<p>have both reason to think my </p>
<p>opinions </p>
<p>not </p>
<p>entirely </p>
<p>unalterable, they are not, I hope, quite so easily changed as </p>
<p>that implies." </p>
<p>"When I wrote that letter," replied Darcy, "I believed my </p>
<p>self perfectly calm and cool, but I am since convinced that it </p>
<p>was written in a dreadful bitterness of spirit." </p>
<p>"The letter, perhaps, began in bitterness, but it did not end </p>
<p>so. The adieu is charity itself. But think no more of the letter. </p>
<p>The feelings of the person who wrote, and the person who </p>
<p>received it, are now so widely different from what they were </p>
<p>then, that every unpleasant circumstance attending it ought to </p>
<p>be forgotten. You must learn some of my philosophy. Think </p>
<p>only of the past as its remembrance gives you pleasure." </p>
<p>"I cannot give you credit for any philosophy of the kind. </p>
<p>Your retrospections must be so totally void of reproach, that </p>
<p>the contentment arising from them is not of philosophy, but, </p>
<p>what is much better, of innocence. But with me, it is not so. </p>
<p>Painful recollections will intrude which cannot, which ought </p>
<p>not, to be repelled. I have been a selfish being all my life, in </p>
<p>practice, though not in principle. As a child I was taught what </p>
<p>was right, but I was not taught to correct my temper. I was </p>
<p>given good principles, but left to follow them in pride and </p>
<p>conceit. Unfortunately an only son (for many years an only </p>
<p>child), I was spoilt by my parents, who, though good </p>
<p>themselves (my father, particularly, all that was benevolent </p>
<p>and amiable), allowed, encouraged, almost taught me to be </p>
<p>selfish and overbearing; to care for none beyond my own </p>
<p>family circle; to think meanly of all the rest of the world; to </p>
<p>wish at least to think meanly of their sense and worth </p>
<p>compared with my own. Such I was, from eight to eight and </p>
<p>twenty; and such I might still have been but for you, dearest, </p>
<p>loveliest Elizabeth! What do I not owe you! You taught me a </p>
<p>lesson, hard indeed at first, but most advantageous. By you, I </p>
<p>was properly humbled. I came to you without a doubt of my </p>
<p>reception. You showed me how insufficient were all my </p>
<p>pretensions to please a woman worthy of being pleased." </p>
<p>"Had you then persuaded yourself that I should?" </p>
<p>"Indeed I had. What will you think of my vanity? I </p>
<p>believed you to be wishing, expecting my addresses." </p>
<p>"My manners must have been in fault, but not </p>
<p>intentionally, I assure you. I never meant to deceive you, but </p>
<p>my spirits might often lead me wrong. How you must have </p>
<p>hated me after THAT evening?" </p>
<p>"Hate you! I was angry perhaps at first, but my anger soon </p>
<p>began to take a proper direction." </p>
<p>"I am almost afraid of asking what you thought of me, </p>
<p>when we met at Pemberley. You blamed me for coming?" </p>
<p>"No indeed; I felt nothing but surprise." </p>
<p>"Your surprise could not be greater than MINE in being </p>
<p>noticed by you. My conscience told me that I deserved no </p>
<p>extraordinary politeness, and I confess that I did not expect to </p>
<p>receive MORE than my due." </p>
<p>"My object then," replied Darcy, "was to show you, by </p>
<p>every civility in my power, that I was not so mean as to resent </p>
<p>the past; and I hoped to obtain your forgiveness, to lessen </p>
<p>your ill opinion, by letting you see that your reproofs had </p>
<p>been at tended to. How soon any other wishes introduced </p>
<p>themselves I can hardly tell, but I believe in about half an </p>
<p>hour after I had seen you." </p>
<p>He then told her of Georgiana's delight in her </p>
<p>acquaintance, and of her disappointment at its sudden </p>
<p>interruption; which naturally leading to the cause of that </p>
<p>interruption, she soon learnt that his resolution of following </p>
<p>her from Derbyshire in quest of her sister had been formed </p>
<p>before he </p>
<p>quitted the inn, and that his gravity and </p>
<p>thoughtfulness there had arisen from no other struggles than </p>
<p>what such a purpose must comprehend. </p>
<p>She expressed her gratitude again, but it was too painful a </p>
<p>subject to each, to be dwelt on farther. </p>
<p>After walking several miles in a leisurely manner, and too </p>
<p>busy to know anything about it, they found at last, on </p>
<p>examining their watches, that it was time to be at home. </p>
<p>"What could become of Mr. Bingley and Jane!" was a </p>
<p>wonder which introduced the discussion of their affairs. Darcy </p>
<p>was delighted with their engagement; his friend had given him </p>
<p>the earliest information of it. </p>
<p>"I must ask whether you were surprised?" said Elizabeth. </p>
<p>"Not at all. When I went away, I felt that it would soon </p>
<p>happen." </p>
<p>"That is to say, you had given your permission. I guessed </p>
<p>as much." And though he exclaimed at the term, she found </p>
<p>that it had been pretty much the case. </p>
<p>"On the evening before my going to London," said he, "I </p>
<p>made a confession to him, which I believe I ought to have </p>
<p>made long ago. I told him of all that had occurred to make my </p>
<p>former interference in his affairs absurd and impertinent. His </p>
<p>surprise was great. He had never had the slightest suspicion. I </p>
<p>told him, moreover, that I believed myself mistaken in </p>
<p>supposing, as I had done, that your sister was indifferent to </p>
<p>him; and as I could easily perceive that his attachment to her </p>
<p>was unabated, I felt no doubt of their happiness together." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could not help smiling at his easy manner of </p>
<p>directing his friend. </p>
<p>"Did you speak from your own observation," said she, </p>
<p>"when you told him that my sister loved him, or merely from </p>
<p>my information last spring?" </p>
<p>"From the former. I had narrowly observed her during the </p>
<p>two visits which I had lately made here; and I was convinced </p>
<p>of her affection." </p>
<p>"And your assurance of it, I suppose, carried immediate </p>
<p>conviction to him." </p>
<p>"It did. </p>
<p>Bingley is most unaffectedly modest. His </p>
<p>diffidence had prevented his depending on his own judgment </p>
<p>in so anxious a case, but his reliance on mine made every </p>
<p>thing easy. I was obliged to confess one thing, which for a </p>
<p>time, and not unjustly, offended him. I could not allow myself </p>
<p>to conceal that your sister had been in town three months last </p>
<p>winter, that I had known it, and purposely kept it from him. </p>
<p>He was angry. But his anger, I am persuaded, lasted no longer </p>
<p>than he remained in any doubt of your sister's sentiments. He </p>
<p>has heartily forgiven me now." </p>
<p>Elizabeth longed to observe that Mr. Bingley had been a </p>
<p>most delightful friend; so easily guided that his worth was </p>
<p>invaluable; but she checked herself. She remembered that he </p>
<p>had yet to learn to be laughed at, and it was rather too early to </p>
<p>begin. In anticipating the happiness of Bingley, which of </p>
<p>course was to be inferior only to his own, he continued the </p>
<p>conversation till they reached the house. In the hall they </p>
<p>parted. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 59. </heading>
<p>"My dear Lizzy, where can you have been walking to?" </p>
<p>was a question which Elizabeth received from Jane as soon as </p>
<p>she entered their room, and from all the others when they sat </p>
<p>down to table. She had only to say in reply, that they had </p>
<p>wandered about, till she was beyond her own knowledge. She </p>
<p>coloured as she spoke; but neither that, nor anything else, </p>
<p>awakened a suspicion of the truth. </p>
<p>The evening passed quietly, unmarked by anything </p>
<p>extraordinary. The acknowledged lovers talked and laughed, </p>
<p>the unacknowledged were silent. Darcy was not of a </p>
<p>disposition in which happiness overflows in mirth; and </p>
<p>Elizabeth, agitated and confused, rather KNEW that she was </p>
<p>happy than FELT herself to be so; for, besides the immediate </p>
<p>embarrassment, there were other evils before her. She </p>
<p>anticipated what would be felt in the family when her </p>
<p>situation became known; she was aware that no one liked him </p>
<p>but Jane; and even feared that with the others it was a dislike </p>
<p>which not all his fortune and consequence might do away. </p>
<p>At night she opened her heart to Jane. Though suspicion </p>
<p>was very far from Miss Bennet's general habits, she was </p>
<p>absolutely incredulous here. </p>
<p>"You are joking, Lizzy. This cannot be!--engaged to Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy! No, no, you shall not deceive me. I know it to be </p>
<p>impossible." </p>
<p>"This is a wretched beginning indeed! My sole dependence </p>
<p>was on you; and I am sure nobody else will believe me, if you </p>
<p>do not. Yet, indeed, I am in earnest. I speak nothing but the </p>
<p>truth. He still loves me, and we are engaged." </p>
<p>Jane looked at her doubtingly. "Oh, Lizzy! it cannot be. I </p>
<p>know how much you dislike him." </p>
<p>"You know nothing of the matter. THAT is all to be </p>
<p>forgot. Perhaps I did not always love him so well as I do now. </p>
<p>But in such cases as these, a good memory is unpardonable. </p>
<p>This is the last time I shall ever remember it myself." </p>
<p>Miss Bennet still looked all amazement. Elizabeth again, </p>
<p>and more seriously assured her of its truth. </p>
<p>"Good Heaven! can it be really so! Yet now I must believe </p>
<p>you," </p>
<p>cried Jane. "My dear, dear Lizzy, I would--I do </p>
<p>congratulate you--but are you certain? forgive the question -- </p>
<p>are you quite certain that you can be happy with him?" </p>
<p>"There can be no doubt of that. It is settled between us </p>
<p>already, that we are to be the happiest couple in the world. But </p>
<p>are you pleased, Jane? Shall you like to have such a brother?" </p>
<p>"Very, very much. Nothing could give either Bingley or </p>
<p>myself more delight. But we considered it, we talked of it as </p>
<p>impossible. And do you really love him quite well enough? </p>
<p>Oh, Lizzy! do anything rather than marry without affection. </p>
<p>Are you quite sure that you feel what you ought to do?" </p>
<p>"Oh, yes! You will only think I feel MORE than I ought to </p>
<p>do, when I tell you all." </p>
<p>"What do you mean?" </p>
<p>"Why, I must confess that I love him better than I do </p>
<p>Bingley. I am afraid you will be angry." </p>
<p>"My dearest sister, now BE serious. I want to talk very </p>
<p>seriously. Let me know every thing that I am to know, without </p>
<p>delay. Will you tell me how long you have loved him?" </p>
<p>"It has been coming on so gradually, that I hardly know </p>
<p>when it began. But I believe I must date it from my first </p>
<p>seeing his beautiful grounds at Pemberley." </p>
<p>Another entreaty that she would be serious, however, </p>
<p>produced the desired effect; and she soon satisfied Jane by her </p>
<p>solemn assurances of attachment. When convinced on that </p>
<p>article, Miss Bennet had nothing further to wish. </p>
<p>"Now I am quite happy," said she, "for you will be as </p>
<p>happy as myself. I always had a value for him. Were it for </p>
<p>nothing but his love of you, I must always have esteemed </p>
<p>him; but now, as Bingley's friend and your husband, there can </p>
<p>be only Bingley and yourself more dear to me. But Lizzy, you </p>
<p>have been very sly, very reserved with me. How little did you </p>
<p>tell me of what passed at Pemberley and Lambton! I owe all </p>
<p>that I know of it to another, not to you." </p>
<p>Elizabeth told her the motives of her secrecy. She had been </p>
<p>unwilling to mention Bingley; and the unsettled state of her </p>
<p>own feelings had made her equally avoid the name of his </p>
<p>friend. But now she would no longer conceal from her his </p>
<p>share in Lydia's marriage. All was acknowledged, and half the </p>
<p>night spent in conversation. </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>"Good gracious!" cried Mrs. Bennet, as she stood at a </p>
<p>window the next morning, "if that disagreeable Mr. Darcy is </p>
<p>not coming here again with our dear Bingley! What can he </p>
<p>mean by being so tiresome as to be always coming here? I had </p>
<p>no notion but he would go a-shooting, or something or other, </p>
<p>and not disturb us with his company. What shall we do with </p>
<p>him? Lizzy, you must walk out with him again, that he may </p>
<p>not be in Bingley's way." </p>
<p>Elizabeth could hardly help laughing at so convenient a </p>
<p>proposal; yet was really vexed that her mother should be </p>
<p>always giving him such an epithet. </p>
<p>As soon as they entered, Bingley looked at her so </p>
<p>expressively, and shook hands with such warmth, as left no </p>
<p>doubt of his good information; and he soon afterwards said </p>
<p>aloud, "Mrs. Bennet, have you no more lanes hereabouts in </p>
<p>which Lizzy may lose her way again to-day?" </p>
<p>"I advise Mr. Darcy, and Lizzy, and Kitty," said Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet, "to walk to Oakham Mount this morning. It is a nice </p>
<p>long walk, and Mr. Darcy has never seen the view." </p>
<p>"It may do very well for the others," replied Mr. Bingley; </p>
<p>"but I am sure it will be too much for Kitty. Won't it, Kitty?" </p>
<p>Kitty owned that she had rather stay at home. Darcy professed </p>
<p>a great curiosity to see the view from the Mount, and </p>
<p>Elizabeth silently consented. As she went up stairs to get </p>
<p>ready, Mrs. Bennet followed her, saying: </p>
<p>"I am quite sorry, Lizzy, that you should be forced to have </p>
<p>that disagreeable man all to yourself. But I hope you will not </p>
<p>mind it: it is all for Jane's sake, you know; and there is no </p>
<p>occasion for talking to him, except just now and then. So, do </p>
<p>not put yourself to inconvenience." </p>
<p>During their walk, it was resolved that Mr. Bennet's </p>
<p>consent should be asked in the course of the evening. </p>
<p>Elizabeth reserved to herself the application for her mother's. </p>
<p>She could not determine how her mother would take it; </p>
<p>sometimes doubting whether all his wealth and grandeur </p>
<p>would be enough to overcome her abhorrence of the man. But </p>
<p>whether she were violently set against the match, or violently </p>
<p>delighted with it, it was certain that her manner would be </p>
<p>equally ill adapted to do credit to her sense; and she could no </p>
<p>more bear that Mr. Darcy should hear the first raptures of her </p>
<p>joy, than the first vehemence of her disapprobation. </p>
<p>* * * * * </p>
<p>In the evening, soon after Mr. Bennet withdrew to the </p>
<p>library, she saw Mr. Darcy rise also and follow him, and her </p>
<p>agitation on seeing it was extreme. She did not fear her </p>
<p>father's opposition, but he was going to be made unhappy; and </p>
<p>that it should be through her means--that SHE, his favourite </p>
<p>child, should be distressing him by her choice, should be </p>
<p>filling him with fears and regrets in disposing of her--was a </p>
<p>wretched reflection, and she sat in misery till Mr. Darcy </p>
<p>appeared again, when, looking at him, she was a little relieved </p>
<p>by his smile. In a few minutes he approached the table where </p>
<p>she was sitting with Kitty; and, while pretending to admire her </p>
<p>work said in a whisper, "Go to your father, he wants you in </p>
<p>the library." She was gone directly. </p>
<p>Her father was walking about the room, looking grave and </p>
<p>anxious. "Lizzy," said he, "what are you doing? Are you out </p>
<p>of your senses, to be accepting this man? Have not you always </p>
<p>hated him?" </p>
<p>How earnestly did she then wish that her former opinions </p>
<p>had been more reasonable, her expressions more moderate! It </p>
<p>would have spared her from explanations and professions </p>
<p>which it was exceedingly awkward to give; but they were now </p>
<p>necessary, and she assured him, with some confusion, of her </p>
<p>attachment to Mr. Darcy. </p>
<p>"Or, in other words, you are determined to have him. He is </p>
<p>rich, to be sure, and you may have more fine clothes and fine </p>
<p>carriages than Jane. But will they make you happy?" </p>
<p>"Have you any other objection," said Elizabeth, "than your </p>
<p>belief of my indifference?" </p>
<p>"None at all. We all know him to be a proud, unpleasant </p>
<p>sort of man; but this would be nothing if you really liked </p>
<p>him." </p>
<p>"I do, I do like him," she replied, with tears in her eyes, "I </p>
<p>love him. Indeed he has no improper pride. He is perfectly </p>
<p>amiable. You do not know what he really is; then pray do not </p>
<p>pain me by speaking of him in such terms." </p>
<p>"Lizzy," said her father, "I have given him my consent. He </p>
<p>is the kind of man, indeed, to whom I should never dare refuse </p>
<p>anything, which he condescended to ask. I now give it to </p>
<p>YOU, if you are resolved on having him. But let me advise </p>
<p>you to think better of it. I know your disposition, Lizzy. I </p>
<p>know that you could be neither happy nor respectable, unless </p>
<p>you truly esteemed your husband; unless you looked up to </p>
<p>him as a superior. Your lively talents would place you in the </p>
<p>greatest danger in an unequal marriage. You could scarcely </p>
<p>escape discredit and misery. My child, let me not have the </p>
<p>grief of seeing YOU unable to respect your partner in life. </p>
<p>You know not what you are about." </p>
<p>Elizabeth, still more affected, was earnest and solemn in </p>
<p>her reply; and at length, by repeated assurances that Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy was really the object of her choice, by explaining the </p>
<p>gradual change which her estimation of him had undergone, </p>
<p>relating her absolute certainty that his affection was not the </p>
<p>work of a day, but had stood the test of many months </p>
<p>suspense, and enumerating with energy all his good qualities, </p>
<p>she did conquer her father's incredulity, and reconcile him to </p>
<p>the match. </p>
<p>"Well, my dear," said he, when she ceased speaking, "I </p>
<p>have no more to say. If this be the case, he deserves you. I </p>
<p>could not have parted with you, my Lizzy, to anyone less </p>
<p>worthy." </p>
<p>To complete the favourable impression, she then told him </p>
<p>what Mr. Darcy had voluntarily done for Lydia. He heard her </p>
<p>with astonishment. </p>
<p>"This is an evening of wonders, indeed! And so, Darcy did </p>
<p>every thing; made up the match, gave the money, paid the </p>
<p>fellow's debts, and got him his commission! So much the bet </p>
<p>ter. It will save me a world of trouble and economy. Had it </p>
<p>been your uncle's doing, I must and WOULD have paid him; </p>
<p>but these violent young lovers carry every thing their own </p>
<p>way. I shall offer to pay him to-morrow; he will rant and </p>
<p>storm about his love for you, and there will be an end of the </p>
<p>matter." </p>
<p>He then recollected her embarrassment a few days before, </p>
<p>on his reading Mr. Collins's letter; and after laughing at her </p>
<p>some time, allowed her at last to go--saying, as she quitted the </p>
<p>room, "If any young men come for Mary or Kitty, send them </p>
<p>in, for I am quite at leisure." </p>
<p>Elizabeth's mind was now relieved from a very heavy </p>
<p>weight; and, after half an hour's quiet reflection in her own </p>
<p>room, she was able to join the others with tolerable </p>
<p>composure. Every thing was too recent for gaiety, but the </p>
<p>evening passed tranquilly away; there was no longer anything </p>
<p>material to be dreaded, and the comfort of ease and familiarity </p>
<p>would come in time. </p>
<p>When her mother went up to her dressing-room at night, </p>
<p>she followed her, and made the important communication. Its </p>
<p>effect was most extraordinary; for on first hearing it, Mrs. </p>
<p>Bennet sat quite still, and unable to utter a syllable. Nor was it </p>
<p>under many, many minutes that she could comprehend what </p>
<p>she heard; though not in general backward to credit what was </p>
<p>for the advantage of her family, or that came in the shape of a </p>
<p>lover to any of them. She began at length to recover, to fidget </p>
<p>about in her chair, get up, sit down again, wonder, and bless </p>
<p>herself. </p>
<p>"Good gracious! Lord bless me! only think! dear me! Mr. </p>
<p>Darcy! Who would have thought it! And is it really true? Oh! </p>
<p>my sweetest Lizzy! how rich and how great you will be! What </p>
<p>pin-money, what jewels, what carriages you will have! Jane's </p>
<p>is nothing to it--nothing at all. I am so pleased--so happy. </p>
<p>Such a charming man!--so handsome! so tall!--Oh, my dear </p>
<p>Lizzy! pray apologise for my having disliked him so much </p>
<p>before. I hope he will overlook it. Dear, dear Lizzy. A house </p>
<p>in town! Every thing that is charming! Three daughters </p>
<p>married! Ten thousand a year! Oh, Lord! What will become of </p>
<p>me. I shall go distracted." </p>
<p>This was enough to prove that her approbation need not be </p>
<p>doubted: and Elizabeth, rejoicing that such an effusion was </p>
<p>heard only by herself, soon went away. But before she had </p>
<p>been three minutes in her own room, her mother followed her. </p>
<p>"My dearest child," she cried, "I can think of nothing else! </p>
<p>Ten thousand a year, and very likely more! 'Tis as good as a </p>
<p>Lord! And a special licence. You must and shall be married </p>
<p>by a special licence. But my dearest love, tell me what dish </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy is particularly fond of, that I may have it to- </p>
<p>morrow." </p>
<p>This was a sad omen of what her mother's behaviour to the </p>
<p>gentleman himself might be; and Elizabeth found that, though </p>
<p>in the certain possession of his warmest affection, and secure </p>
<p>of her relations' consent, there was still something to be </p>
<p>wished for. But the morrow passed off much better than she </p>
<p>expected; for Mrs. Bennet luckily stood in such awe of her </p>
<p>intended son-in-law that she ventured not to speak to him, </p>
<p>unless it was in her power to offer him any attention, or mark </p>
<p>her deference for his opinion. </p>
<p>Elizabeth had the satisfaction of seeing her father taking </p>
<p>pains to get acquainted with him; and Mr. Bennet soon </p>
<p>assured her that he was rising every hour in his esteem. </p>
<p>"I admire all my three sons-in-law highly," said he. </p>
<p>"Wickham, perhaps, is my favourite; but I think I shall like </p>
<p>YOUR husband quite as well as Jane's." </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 60. </heading>
<p>Elizabeth's spirits soon rising to playfulness again, she </p>
<p>wanted Mr. Darcy to account for his having ever fallen in love </p>
<p>with her. "How could you begin?" said she. "I can </p>
<p>comprehend your going on charmingly, when you had once </p>
<p>made a beginning; but what could set you off in the first </p>
<p>place?" </p>
<p>"I cannot fix on the hour, or the spot, or the look, or the </p>
<p>words, which laid the foundation. It is too long ago. I was in </p>
<p>the middle before I knew that I HAD begun." </p>
<p>"My beauty you had early withstood, and as for my </p>
<p>manners--my behaviour to YOU was at least always bordering </p>
<p>on the uncivil, and I never spoke to you without rather </p>
<p>wishing to give you pain than not. Now be sincere; did you </p>
<p>admire me for my impertinence?" </p>
<p>"For the liveliness of your mind, I did." </p>
<p>"You may as well call it impertinence at once. It was very </p>
<p>little less. The fact is, that you were sick of civility, of </p>
<p>deference, of officious attention. You were disgusted with the </p>
<p>women who were always speaking, and looking, and thinking </p>
<p>for YOUR approbation alone. I roused, and interested you, </p>
<p>because I was so unlike THEM. Had you not been really </p>
<p>amiable, you would have hated me for it; but in spite of the </p>
<p>pains you took to disguise yourself, your feelings were always </p>
<p>noble and just; and in your heart, you thoroughly despised the </p>
<p>persons who so assiduously courted you. There--I have saved </p>
<p>you the trouble of accounting for it; and really, all things </p>
<p>considered, I begin to think it perfectly reasonable. To be </p>
<p>sure, you knew no actual good of me--but nobody thinks of </p>
<p>THAT when they fall in love." </p>
<p>"Was there no good in your affectionate behaviour to Jane </p>
<p>while she was ill at Netherfield?" </p>
<p>"Dearest Jane! who could have done less for her? But </p>
<p>make a virtue of it by all means. My good qualities are under </p>
<p>your protection, and you are to exaggerate them as much as </p>
<p>possible; and, in return, it belongs to me to find occasions for </p>
<p>teasing and quarrelling with you as often as may be; and I </p>
<p>shall begin directly by asking you what made you so </p>
<p>unwilling to come to the point at last. What made you so shy </p>
<p>of me, when you first called, and afterwards dined here? Why, </p>
<p>especially, when you called, did you look as if you did not </p>
<p>care about me?" </p>
<p>"Because you were grave and silent, and gave me no </p>
<p>encouragement." </p>
<p>"But I was embarrassed." </p>
<p>"And so was I." </p>
<p>"You might have talked to me more when you came to </p>
<p>dinner." </p>
<p>"A man who had felt less, might." </p>
<p>"How unlucky that you should have a reasonable answer to </p>
<p>give, and that I should be so reasonable as to admit it! But I </p>
<p>wonder how long you WOULD have gone on, if you had been </p>
<p>left to yourself. I wonder when you WOULD have spoken, if I </p>
<p>had not asked you! My resolution of thanking you for your </p>
<p>kindness to Lydia had certainly great effect. TOO MUCH, I </p>
<p>am afraid; for what becomes of the moral, if our comfort </p>
<p>springs from a breach of promise? for I ought not to have </p>
<p>mentioned the subject. This will never do." </p>
<p>"You need not distress yourself. The moral will be </p>
<p>perfectly fair. Lady Catherine's unjustifiable endeavours to </p>
<p>separate us were the means of removing all my doubts. I am </p>
<p>not indebted for my present happiness to your eager desire of </p>
<p>expressing your gratitude. I was not in a humour to wait for </p>
<p>any opening of your's. My aunt's intelligence had given me </p>
<p>hope, and I was determined at once to know every thing." </p>
<p>"Lady Catherine has been of infinite use, which ought to </p>
<p>make her happy, for she loves to be of use. But tell me, what </p>
<p>did you come down to Netherfield for? Was it merely to ride </p>
<p>to Longbourn and be embarrassed? or had you intended any </p>
<p>more serious consequence?" </p>
<p>"My real purpose was to see YOU, and to judge, if I could, </p>
<p>whether I might ever hope to make you love me. My avowed </p>
<p>one, or what I avowed to myself, was to see whether your </p>
<p>sister were still partial to Bingley, and if she were, to make the </p>
<p>confession to him which I have since made." </p>
<p>"Shall you ever have courage to announce to Lady </p>
<p>Catherine what is to befall her?" </p>
<p>"I am more likely </p>
<p>to want more time than courage, </p>
<p>Elizabeth. But it ought to done, and if you will give me a </p>
<p>sheet of paper, it shall be done directly." </p>
<p>"And if I had not a letter to write myself, I might sit by you </p>
<p>and admire the evenness of your writing, as another young </p>
<p>lady once did. But I have an aunt, too, who must not be longer </p>
<p>neglected." </p>
<p>From an unwillingness to confess how much her intimacy </p>
<p>with Mr. Darcy had been over-rated, Elizabeth had never yet </p>
<p>answered Mrs. Gardiner's long letter; but now, having THAT </p>
<p>to communicate which she knew would be most welcome, she </p>
<p>was almost ashamed to find that her uncle and aunt had </p>
<p>already lost three days of happiness, and immediately wrote as </p>
<p>follows: </p>
<p>"I would have thanked you before, my dear aunt, as I </p>
<p>ought to have done, for your long, kind, satisfactory, detail of </p>
<p>particulars; but to say the truth, I was too cross to write. You </p>
<p>supposed more than really existed. But NOW suppose as </p>
<p>much as you choose; give a loose rein to your fancy, indulge </p>
<p>your imagination in every possible flight which the subject </p>
<p>will afford, and unless you believe me actually married, you </p>
<p>cannot greatly err. You must write again very soon, and praise </p>
<p>him a great deal more than you did in your last. I thank you, </p>
<p>again and again, for not going to the Lakes. How could I be so </p>
<p>silly as to wish it! Your idea of the ponies is delightful. We </p>
<p>will go round the Park every day. I am the happiest creature in </p>
<p>the world. Perhaps other people have said so before, but not </p>
<p>one with such justice. I am happier even than Jane; she only </p>
<p>smiles, I laugh. Mr. Darcy sends you all the love in the world </p>
<p>that he can spare from me. You are all to come to Pemberley </p>
<p>at Christmas. Yours, etc." </p>
<p>Mr. Darcy's letter to Lady Catherine was in a different </p>
<p>style; and still different from either was what Mr. Bennet sent </p>
<p>to Mr. Collins, in reply to his last. </p>
<p>"DEAR SIR, </p>
<p>"I must trouble you once more for congratulations. </p>
<p>Elizabeth will soon be the wife of Mr. Darcy. Console Lady </p>
<p>Catherine as well as you can. But, if I were you, I would stand </p>
<p>by the nephew. He has more to give. </p>
<p>"Yours sincerely, etc." </p>
<p>Miss Bingley's </p>
<p>congratulations to her brother, on his </p>
<p>approaching marriage, were all that was affectionate and </p>
<p>insincere. She wrote even to Jane on the occasion, to express </p>
<p>her delight, and repeat all her former professions of regard. </p>
<p>Jane was not deceived, but she was affected; and though </p>
<p>feeling no reliance on her, could not help writing her a much </p>
<p>kinder answer than she knew was deserved. </p>
<p>The joy which Miss Darcy expressed on receiving similar </p>
<p>information, was as sincere as her brother's in sending it. Four </p>
<p>sides of paper were insufficient to contain all her delight, and </p>
<p>all her earnest desire of being loved by her sister. </p>
<p>Before any answer could arrive from Mr. Collins, or any </p>
<p>congratulations to Elizabeth from his wife, the Longbourn </p>
<p>family heard that the Collinses were come themselves to </p>
<p>Lucas Lodge. The reason of this sudden removal was soon </p>
<p>evident. Lady Catherine had been rendered so exceedingly </p>
<p>angry by the contents of her nephew's letter, that Charlotte, </p>
<p>really rejoicing in the match, was anxious to get away till the </p>
<p>storm was blown over. At such a moment, the arrival of her </p>
<p>friend was a sincere pleasure to Elizabeth, though in the </p>
<p>course of their meetings she must sometimes think the </p>
<p>pleasure dearly bought, when she saw Mr. Darcy exposed to </p>
<p>all the parading and obsequious civility of her husband. He </p>
<p>bore it, however, with admirable calmness. He could even </p>
<p>listen to Sir William Lucas, when he complimented him on </p>
<p>carrying away the brightest jewel of the country, and </p>
<p>expressed his hopes of their all meeting frequently at St. </p>
<p>James's, with very decent composure. If he did shrug his </p>
<p>shoulders, it was not till Sir William was out of sight. </p>
<p>Mrs. Phillips's vulgarity was another, and perhaps a </p>
<p>greater, tax on his forbearance; and though Mrs. Phillips, as </p>
<p>well as her sister, stood in too much awe of him to speak with </p>
<p>the familiarity which Bingley's good humour encouraged, yet, </p>
<p>whenever she DID speak, she must be vulgar. Nor was her </p>
<p>respect for him, though it made her more quiet, at all likely to </p>
<p>make her more elegant. Elizabeth did all she could to shield </p>
<p>him from the frequent notice of either, and was ever anxious </p>
<p>to keep him to herself, and to those of her family with whom </p>
<p>he might converse without mortification; and though the </p>
<p>uncomfortable feelings arising from all this took from the </p>
<p>season of courtship much of its pleasure, it added to the hope </p>
<p>of the future; and she looked forward with delight to the time </p>
<p>when they should be removed from society so little pleasing </p>
<p>to either, to all the comfort and elegance of their family party </p>
<p>at Pemberley. </p>
<footer>Ebd </footer>
<footer>E-BooksDirectory.com </footer>
<heading>Chapter 61. </heading>
<p>Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which </p>
<p>Mrs. Bennet got rid of her two most deserving daughters. </p>
<p>With what delighted pride she afterwards visited Mrs. </p>
<p>Bingley, and talked of Mrs. Darcy, may be guessed. I wish I </p>
<p>could say, for the sake of her family, that the accomplishment </p>
<p>of her earnest desire in the establishment of so many of her </p>
<p>children produced so happy an effect as to make her a </p>
<p>sensible, amiable, well-informed woman for the rest of her </p>
<p>life; though perhaps it was lucky for her husband, who might </p>
<p>not have relished domestic felicity in so unusual a form, that </p>
<p>she still was occasionally nervous and invariably silly. </p>
<p>Mr. Bennet missed his second daughter exceedingly; his </p>
<p>affection for her drew him oftener from home than anything </p>
<p>else could do. He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially </p>
<p>when he was least expected. </p>
<p>Mr. Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a </p>
<p>twelvemonth. So near a vicinity to her mother and Meryton </p>
<p>relations was not desirable even to HIS easy temper, or HER </p>
<p>affectionate heart. The darling wish of his sisters was then </p>
<p>gratified; he bought an estate in a neighbouring county to </p>
<p>Derbyshire, and Jane and Elizabeth, in addition to every other </p>
<p>source of happiness, were within thirty miles of each other. </p>
<p>Kitty, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her </p>
<p>time with her two elder sisters. In society so superior to what </p>
<p>she had generally known, her improvement was great. She </p>
<p>was not of so ungovernable a temper as Lydia; and, removed </p>
<p>from the influence of Lydia's example, she became, by proper </p>
<p>attention and management, less irritable, less ignorant, and </p>
<p>less insipid. From the further disadvantage of Lydia's society </p>
<p>she was of course carefully kept, and though Mrs. Wickham </p>
<p>frequently invited her to come and stay with her, with the </p>
<p>promise of balls and young men, her father would never </p>
<p>consent to her going. </p>
<p>Mary was the only daughter who remained at home; and </p>
<p>she was necessarily drawn from </p>
<p>the pursuit of </p>
<p>accomplishments by Mrs. Bennet's being quite unable to sit </p>
<p>alone. Mary was obliged to mix more with the world, but she </p>
<p>could still moralize over every morning visit; and as she was </p>
<p>no longer mortified by comparisons between her sisters' </p>
<p>beauty and her own, it was suspected by her father that she </p>
<p>submitted to the change without much reluctance. </p>
<p>As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no </p>
<p>revolution from the marriage of her sisters. He bore with </p>
<p>philosophy the conviction that Elizabeth must now become </p>
<p>acquainted with whatever of his ingratitude and falsehood had </p>
<p>before been unknown to her; and in spite of every thing, was </p>
<p>not wholly without hope that Darcy might yet be prevailed on </p>
<p>to make his fortune. The congratulatory letter which Elizabeth </p>
<p>received from Lydia on her marriage, explained to her that, by </p>
<p>his wife at least, if not by himself, such a hope was cherished. </p>
<p>The letter was to this effect: </p>
<p>"MY DEAR LIZZY, </p>
<p>"I wish you joy. If you love Mr. Darcy half as well as I do </p>
<p>my dear Wickham, you must be very happy. It is a great </p>
<p>comfort to have you so rich, and when you have nothing else </p>
<p>to do, I hope you will think of us. I am sure Wickham would </p>
<p>like a place at court very much, and I do not think we shall </p>
<p>have quite money enough to live upon without some help. </p>
<p>Any place would do, of about three or four hundred a year; </p>
<p>but however, do not speak to Mr. Darcy about it, if you had </p>
<p>rather not. </p>
<p>"Yours, etc." </p>
<p>As it happened that Elizabeth had MUCH rather not, she </p>
<p>endeavoured in her answer to put an end to every entreaty and </p>
<p>expectation of the kind. Such relief, however, as it was in her </p>
<p>power to afford, by the practice of what might be called </p>
<p>economy in her own private expences, she frequently sent </p>
<p>them. It had always been evident to her that such an income as </p>
<p>theirs, under the direction of two persons so extravagant in </p>
<p>their wants, and heedless of the future, must be very </p>
<p>insufficient to their support; and whenever they changed their </p>
<p>quarters, either Jane or herself were sure of being applied to </p>
<p>for some little assistance towards discharging their bills. Their </p>
<p>manner of living, even when the restoration of peace </p>
<p>dismissed them to a home, was unsettled in the extreme. They </p>
<p>were always moving from place to place in quest of a cheap </p>
<p>situation, and always spending more than they ought. His </p>
<p>affection for her soon sunk into indifference; her's lasted a </p>
<p>little longer; and in spite of her youth and her manners, she </p>
<p>retained all the claims to reputation which her marriage had </p>
<p>given her. </p>
<p>Though Darcy could never receive HIM at Pemberley, yet, </p>
<p>for Elizabeth's sake, he assisted him further in his profession. </p>
<p>Lydia was occasionally a visitor there, when her husband was </p>
<p>gone to enjoy himself in London or Bath; and with the </p>
<p>Bingleys they both of them frequently staid so long, that even </p>
<p>Bingley's good humour was overcome, and he proceeded so </p>
<p>far as to talk of giving them a hint to be gone. </p>
<p>Miss Bingley was very deeply mortified by Darcy's </p>
<p>marriage; but as she thought it advisable to retain the right of </p>
<p>visiting at Pemberley, she dropt all her resentment; was fonder </p>
<p>than ever of Georgiana, almost as attentive to Darcy as </p>
<p>heretofore, and paid off every arrear of civility to Elizabeth. </p>
<p>Pemberley was now Georgiana's home; and the attachment </p>
<p>of the sisters was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see. They </p>
<p>were able to love each other even as well as they intended. </p>
<p>Georgiana had the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; </p>
<p>though at first she often listened with an astonishment </p>
<p>bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive, manner of talking to </p>
<p>her brother. He, who had always inspired in herself a respect </p>
<p>which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object </p>
<p>of open pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which had </p>
<p>never before fallen in her way. By Elizabeth's instructions, she </p>
<p>began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with </p>
<p>her husband which a brother will not always allow in a sister </p>
<p>more than ten years younger than himself. </p>
<p>Lady Catherine was extremely indignant on the marriage </p>
<p>of her nephew; and as she gave way to all the genuine </p>
<p>frankness of her character in her reply to the letter which </p>
<p>announced its arrangement, she sent him language so very </p>
<p>abusive, especially of Elizabeth, that for some time all </p>
<p>intercourse was at an end. But at length, by Elizabeth's </p>
<p>persuasion, he was prevailed on to overlook the offence, and </p>
<p>seek a reconciliation; and, after a little further resistance on </p>
<p>the part of his aunt, her resentment gave way, either to her </p>
<p>affection for him, or her curiosity to see how his wife </p>
<p>conducted herself; and she condescended to wait on them at </p>
<p>Pemberley, in spite of that pollution which its woods had </p>
<p>received, not merely from the presence of such a mistress, but </p>
<p>the visits of her uncle and aunt from the city. </p>
<p>With the Gardiners, they were always on the most intimate </p>
<p>terms. Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they </p>
<p>were both ever sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the </p>
<p>persons who, by bringing her into Derbyshire, had been the </p>
<p>means of uniting them. </p>
